The So-called ‘Paradoxes of Validity’ of Elementary Symbolic Logic 
Consider the following five statements: 
S1: The moon has the shape of a cube. 
S2: The moon is made of non-dairy creamer. 
S3: The moon is a six-sided, three-dimensional figure. 
S4: The moon does not have the shape of a cube. 
S5: The moon is not made of non-dairy creamer. 
For right now, let us focus on S1, S2, and S3. We will discuss S4 and S5 in a moment. 
S2 obviously does not follow from S1, but S3 clearly does follow from S1. There is no way that S3 could be false if S1 were true, that being what it means to say that S3 follows from S1 (or, equivalently, that S1 ‘entails’ S3). But if S1 were true, that would obviously be perfectly compatible with S3’s being false; and S1 therefore fails to entail S3 (or, equivalently, S3 does not follow from S1). 
And yet every modern textbook on elementary symbolic logic contains the following two claims (or close variants thereof): 
K1: P.Q means “if P, then Q” 
K2: P.Q is true as long as it is not the case that P is true and Q is false. In other words, P.Q means “P and not-Q” is false. 
Ordinarily, of course, “if P, then Q” means that Q is a consequence of 
P. “If Jerry is in Paris, then Jerry is in France” is true, given, if indeed Jerry is in Paris, a consequence of that fact is that he is in France. 
But if K1 and K2 are both true, if P is false, it always entails Q, no matter Q is, and if Q is true, it always follows from P, no matter what P is. If K1 and K2 are both true, then “if the moon is the shape of a cube, the moon is made of non-dairy creamer” is true, simply because “the moon is the shape of a cube” is false. In other words, if K1 and K2 are both true, then S2 follows from S1. But this seems absurd, since, supposing that the moon had the shape of a cube, that wouldn’t require the moon to be made of non-dairy creamer. 
And it is absurd---demonstrably so, as we will now see. S5 is the negation of S3. (The negation of P is not-P. The negation of ‘snow is white’ is ‘it is not the case that snow is white.’) If K1 and K2 are true, then S5 follows form S1. In other words, if K1 and K2 are both true, then the negation of S3 follows from S1. Why? Because if K1 and K2 are true, then P.Q is true whenever P is false—and S1 is false. 
What has gone wrong here? Very simple. K1 does not have the same meaning as “if P, then Q.” The meaning of “if P, then Q” is “Q follows from P” or, equivalently, “not-Q is incompatible with P.” But this is not the meaning of “P.Q”. The meaning of “P.Q” is simply that it is not the case that both P is true and Q is false. “P.Q” contains no content relating to incompatibility/entailment. “P.Q” is a strictly truth-conditional notion. In other words, whether or not “P.Q” is true is a function only of the truth-values of P and Q (of whether they are true or false, in other words). By contrast “Q follows from P” is a non-truth-conditional notion; it is, in fact, a modal notion---meaning that it depends, not on the truth-values of P and Q, but rather on the possibility of the one’s being incompatible with the negation of the other. (“Modal” means “having to do with possibility and impossibility.”) 
So the solution to this ‘paradox’, if you wish to call it that, is simply that K1 is false. If we assume that K2 accurately states the meaning of “P.Q”, then we cannot hold that “P.Q” means “if P, then Q.” What we can do is hold that “P.Q” has some other meaning that, in some contexts, is sufficiently similar to the meaning of “if P, then Q” that in some contexts we may overlook the differences. 
The just-described ‘paradox of validity’---namely, that P, if false, entails any given Q (i.e. that nothing doesn’t follow from a false statement)--­is no paradox at all, since the meaning of “P.Q” is not that Q follows from P, but is merely that “P and not-Q” is false. 
Attempts have been made to construct formal logics in which “.” is replaced by some other symbol that really does capture the modal force of “if…then…” In most systems of so-called modal logic, there exists a symbol .. such that “P..Q” means “it is impossible for Q to be false if P is true.” And some logicians, notably Belnap and Anderson, have attempted to replace “.” with a symbol .. such that “P..Q” means “given what P and Q mean, P it is incoherent to suppose that Q is false if P is true.” Such logics are known as “relevance logics”, since they attempt to formalize the concept of one statement’s being relevant to another. But what all of these efforts have in common is that, while admitting that “if P, then Q” is not a strictly truth-conditional notion, they all try to assign a meaning to it that is as close as possible to being strictly truth-conditional. In other words, though they do not take “if P, then Q” to mean “P and not-Q is false”, they attempt to model the meaning of “if P, then Q” as closely as possible on that of “P and not-Q is false”---which for many reasons, some technical, some philosophical, is misconceived. 
As for ‘classical logic’, this so-called ‘paradox’ has no bearing on it at all. A classical logic is any system of logic in which the following two principles hold: 
No statement is both true and false (Law of Non-contradiction) 
Any given statement is either true or false (Law of Excluded Middle) 
Classical logic does not require that the concept expressed by “if” be understood in strictly truth-conditional terms. In fact, it implicitly requires the exact opposite. The motivation for the Law of Non-contradiction is that no statement can possibly be compatible with its own negation—in other words, any given statement entails (meaning that it necessarily warrants the truth of) the negation of its own negation. Therefore, the classical logic is committed, albeit implicitly, to an appropriately non-truth-conditional conception of conditionality (i.e. of the concept expressed by “if…then…”). And classical logic therefore demands a wholesale rejection of the (obviously false, as we have seen) strictly truth-conditional understanding of “if…then…” that generates the just-discussed ‘paradox of validity.’ 

The Self-defeating Nature of Utilitarianism and Consequentialism Generally 
Consider the following scenario by a contemporary philosopher: “Your good friend Erskin rests on his death bed---more accurately, the bed soon to be such. Alone with him, you have just promised that, when he passes on, you too will pass on, will pass on his stash of cash to Laetitia, a young lady friend of his. She knows nothing about the money. Erskin duly dies and in your search for Laetitia, you discover that she is a rich courtesan, as Erskin well knew. You also find---to your amazement---that Erskin has daughters and a former wife, who speak fondly of him, clearly distressed by his death. They live in poverty. Indeed, you could do with some money yourself. What ought you to do?” Argue as a utilitarian or a Kantian, and don’t forget to consider possible objections to your view. 
On his death bed, my friend Erskin asked me to make sure that his fortune be passed on to a lady friend of his. I agreed to honor that wish, but it turns out that there are others who would be more deserving of that money. What am I to do? According to the utilitarian, I should do whatever maximizes human welfare; and if that includes lying, breaking promises, or otherwise ‘using’ people, then so be it. According to the Kantian, I have but one moral obligation, namely, to treat people as ends unto themselves, and I must therefore keep my promise; for if I break it, then, by virtue of making it, I was simply manipulating Erskin out of his money and therefore treating him as a means, as opposed to an end-unto-himself. 
In this paper, I will defend the Kantian position. The basic conceit underlying my argument is that the alternative view is self-undermining: if we regard people as having so little value that they can permissibly be defrauded for a ‘greater good’, then there is no ‘greater good’, since it cannot coherently be supposed that there can be a ‘greater good’ in a world in which people lack moral worth. I will start by showing that the utilitarian position fails on its own terms, the reason being that it presupposes the existence of moral verities that it itself undermines. Then I will show that, precisely because it is thus self-undermining, utilitarianism lacks the one virtue that it is commonly thought to have and Kantianism lacks the one defect that that it is thought to have. Utilitarianism is assumed to make allowances for the welfare for the welfare of the many, and we will find this to be false; and Kantianism is thought to fail to make such allowances, and this too we will find to be false. 
Let us begin by identifying the incoherence internal to utilitarianism. When we’re told that Erskin has a wife and children who need the money, we are expected to regard their interests as having more moral weight than my promise. But there are many people on the planet who could benefit from that money: there are sick children on the other side of the planet who need money for life-saving medical procedures and brilliant entrepreneurs who need start-up capital to launch their projects. So why should be particularly concerned about Erskin’s wife and children? 
The reason is that marriage and parenthood are moral commitments. Married people have responsibilities that single people don’t, and marriage is a commitment to fulfill those responsibilities. Parents (meaning people who raise children, instead of merely having them) have responsibilities that non-parents don’t, and parenthood is a commitment to fulfill those responsibilities. Erskin did not have any such commitments to the aforementioned sick children and brilliant entrepreneurs, and that is why we don’t feel that he has any obligation to help them. Erskin did have such commitments to his wife and children, and that is why we do tend to feel that he has some obligation to help them. 
Here is the rub. I have a similar commitment to Erskin. and if it is morally permissible for me to violate my bond with Erskin, then it is equally morally permissible for Erskin to violate his bonds with his wife and children. The bonds of trust and responsibility that made Erskin responsible for taking care of his wife and children are the same as those that obligate me to keep my promise to him. It is therefore self-defeating to suppose that I should give the money to Erskin’s wife and children, instead of keeping my promise to him. For the very bonds of trust that obliged Erskin to take care of his wife and children, and that would have made it good for me to give them the money, also oblige me to keep my promise to Erskin, and therefore not to give them the money. 
The utilitarian’s position is that we should do the greatest good for the greatest number: and if this means lying, defrauding, manipulating, and the like, then so be it. But what do we mean by ‘the greatest good’? We mean a situation in which the greatest possible number of people can their exercise their autonomy, depend on parents, have truthful relations with friends and colleagues, and the like. In other words, we mean a situation in which people are not defrauding and undermining one another. But whatever it is that makes such a situation worth fighting for also makes it morally to fight for by defrauding people. 
The utilitarian position is that the needs of the many outweigh those of the view, with the consequence that it is indeed morally permissible, if not obligatory, to treat the few as nothing more than means to serving the interests of the many. At first, this seems very reasonable. The problem is that the interests of the many cannot possibly be served in a world in which any given person’s interests can be undermined for the same of some other person’s interests. A world in which the interests of the many are protected is a world in which the many can rely on others not to defraud them, and a world in which it is permissible to defraud the few for the sake of the many is not such a world. 
In conclusion, even though Kantianism initially seems to deprive people of what they deserve, it actually does the opposite, since a Kantian conception of morality is inherent in the very concept of ‘what one deserves’; and even though utilitarianism seems to give people what they deserve, it actually does the opposite, since it is only if anti-utilitarian ethical system, such as Kantianism, is correct that anyone deserves anything. Erskin’s wife and children do seem to deserve the money---and maybe they really do. But so far as they deserve, it is only because we live in a world in which people are not to be manipulated or otherwise treated as mere means and in which, in particular, promises must be kept; and because we live in such a world, my moral obligation is not to give the money to Erskin’s wife and children but is rather to keep my promise to him. 

The Altman-Z Bankruptcy-Prediction Algorithm 
Question 1 (15 Points) 
What are the benefits of utilizing a bankruptcy prediction model such as Altman’s Z score and how would you apply it to a company? (Use a specific company such as Microsoft to apply it). Read the appropriate portions of Module 4 of our Textbook for this Question. 
A company’s Altman Z-score provides a reasonably good indication of a company’s health and, in particular, of its likelihood of going bankrupt within the next two years. If X is a company, X’s Altman Z-score is the sum of the following numbers: 
3.3x(EBIT/total assets) 
1.4x(retained earnings/total assets) 
1.2x(working capital/total assets) 
.99x(sales/total assets) 
0.6x(market value of equity/total liabilities) MVE/TL 
In each case, we have a financial ratio multiplied by a coefficient that, in effect, assigns a certain degree of importance to that ratio. For example, EBIT/total assets is the ratio of a company’s total earnings (before interest and taxes) to its assets and is therefore a measure of the amount of work that those assets are doing. (EBIT is ten times larger than total assets, then total assets are operating with 1000% efficiency; if ten times smaller, then operating with 10% efficiency.) Because this is obviously such an important ratio, it is given a high degree of weight in the form of a large (3.3) coefficient. 
The next three ratios are similar to EBIT/total assets, but not as important, since they take into account taxes and interest and other factors that, although relevant to a company’s health, do not reflect how much work its assets are doing. As for market value of equity/total liabilities (MVE/TL ), this represents total value of stock divided by company-debt---in other words, the real value of equity, after debt is taken into account. This is obviously important, but it is not as important as the other ratios, since, unlike them, it says nothing about how much work company assets are doing---it speaks only to their current value. 
The higher a company’s Altman Z-score, the less likely is imminent bankruptcy, with a score of 3.0 considered the minimum score of a financially healthy company. Right now, Amazon’s Altman Z-score is 6.2--­meaning very little chance of bankruptcy. BlackBerry’s current score is negative, meaning that bankruptcy is highly possible. In 2012, Sears’ score was 2.3 which is low but almost acceptable. In 2017, it had a score of zero and declared bankruptcy that years. Historically, bankruptcy predictions based on Altman Z-scores have been accurate 90% of the time. 

Case Analysis (85 Points) 
Problems in bond ratings and the firms issuing them have led to both Standard and Poor’s as well as Moody’s modifying their ratings criteria. Included in these changes is an attempt to capture more risk elements in the rating process and to more comprehensively quantify potential areas of concern. 
The 2015 document issued by Moody’s explains their ratings criteria and the ratings they have assigned to the software industry. It can be found in the Final Exam Module. This document can be combined with corporate reporting data to apply these ratings criteria to specific companies covered by Moody’s. For this examination please use the 2018 10K for Microsoft provided earlier in the Mid-term Exam Module to apply bond ratings criteria to that company. In other words, utilize the analytics Moody’s applies to the software industry including any metrics that help in identifying and applying both the qualitative and quantitative criteria noted in the Moody’s Report on Software Companies. Please answer the following questions pertaining to the report. 

1) Discuss the five key factors important to Moody’s in assessing credit ratings for the industry. (15) 
Those five factors are: 
Scale Business Profile Profitability Leverage and Coverage Financial Policy 
Scale is cash flow, including free cash flow (cash flow minus expenditures). Obviously the bigger the cash flow, especially of free cash flow, the more robust the company. 
A company's business profile refers to its market-share and its range of goods and markets. A company that has a 100% market share is the only game in town, so to speak, and is therefore in a good position, and one that has a 0% market share is not doing any business at all. Obviously the larger a company's market share, the better. As for diversity of products/services (i.e. range of products/services), this is important because a company that offers only one product/service will go out of business if demand for that products/service dries up. That said, successful companies tend to specialize (consider A1, Goodyear, Microsoft, Coca Cola), and over-diversification of products/services can hurt a company. (This happened to A1 when it started selling seafood sauce, instead of just sticking to steak-sauce.) 
End-market diversity is the number of different markets where goods are sold. Obviously, the more such diversity, the better, other things equal, since a company whose eggs (products) are in one basket (market) is vulnerable to changes in that market. (The ‘other things equal’ rider is important, since the cost of doing business in a given market might exceed the benefits.) 
Geographic diversity is the range of venues (economically distinct areas) where it creates its products/services. As a general rule, some diversity in this respect is desirable as a hedge against contingencies. For example, if a company has all its operations in one place, then a single change could ruin it. (A tornado could destroy its facilities; a new president might decide to nationalize the corresponding industry; a new mayor might raise taxes; etc.) 
Profitability is the ratio of income to expense (‘bang for buck’, essentially). The higher this number, the better, for obvious reasons. 
A company’s leverage is the extent to which it is debt-financed, as opposed to asset-financed; and a company’s coverage is the extent to which it is able to services, in most cases, its debt. Insufficient coverage is a sign of poor financial health, as is excessive leverage. 
A company’s financial policy is the manner in which it deals with its own assets and liabilities. It is a bad sign if management is overly bearish (risk-averse), and shies away from all investment-opportunities, but it is no less a bad sign if it is overly bullish (risk-affine) and invests indiscriminately. It is a very bad sign if a company is slow to pay off its debts, and a good sign if it pays them off ahead of schedule, especially if its timeliness has to do with its having invested the borrowed assets profitably. All of this falls under the rubric of ‘financial policy.’ 

2) Why is it necessary for Moody’s to apply different weights for these factors? How are these factors measured? (10) 
Moody’s weights for these five factors are as follows: 
Leverage and Coverage 30% 
Business Profile 25% 
Financial Policy 20% 
Scale 15% 
Profitability 10% 
If a company cannot service its debt, it will, for purely legal reasons, immediately go out of existence (absent a government-bailout or some other, similarly improbable event). Of the five factors, leverage/coverage is the only one that relates to a company's legal obligations; the others relate to its economic viability. There are no laws against investing unwisely or selling mediocre products; there are laws against not repaying loans, and the penalty is typically liquidation. Consequently, leverage/coverage is weighted the most heavily (30%). 
A company’s business-profile includes its market-share and also includes its ability to survive changes in markets. Being so important, it is weighted the second most heavily (25%). 
A company’s financial policy includes the financial savviness of its management, along with the ability, or inability, to manage debt and to seize opportunities. Financial policy is therefore key to a company’s willingness and ability to grow. Financial policy is key to growth, but not as key to survival as the first two factors, and it is therefore weighted the third most heavily (20%). 
A company's scale is its total cash flow, which reflects the amount of business it is doing. Obviously, a company that is doing a lot of business is, other things equal, better off than one that is only doing a little business. The reason scale is so low on the list, with a weighting of only 15%, is that other things are not always equal. In particular, costs of materials, payroll, interest on debts, not to mention the abilities of key executives, may have critical positive or negative effects on a company’s feasibility. 
A company’s profitability is obviously important, since an unprofitable company is not worth investing in and won’t survive, this being why profitability is on the list. At the same time, it is long-term profits, not short-term profits, that matter. A company can be a pump-and-dump scheme and therefore briefly be very profitable. Consequently, profitability is not to be weighted too heavily, this being why it is ranked last, with a weighting of 10%. 
3) What are some of the assumptions, limitations and other considerations noted by Moody’s in employing this methodology? Are accounting adjustments an issue for Moody’s in applying their criteria? Please note that if so you are not required to make them but please discuss briefly what they consist of. (10) 
The most important such assumptions relate to the discount rate that a company uses when evaluating its own assets. First of all, it cannot be know with certainty at the present time how the value of money will fluctuate in the future; so discount-rates are always assumed, as opposed to known. That said, some discount-rates are more reasonable than others; and when a given company’s financials are based on overly optimistic discount-rates, Moody’s looks into the matter and, if the numbers don’t pan out, asks the company in question to revise its financials accordingly. 
One limitation that Moody’s must adjust for is that accounting and reporting requirements vary from country to country. Another is that even the most conservative financial statements are based on assumptions about interest-rates and markets in general that may prove false. 
There are two kinds of accounting adjustments, standard and non-standard, each of which is relevant to how Moody’s evaluates company­financials. Standard-adjustments include securities, pension-plans, R&D costs, and non-recurring expenses. Non-standard adjustments include debts reported at ‘fair value’ (market value), as well as likely benefits-obligations to future former employees of the company. 
4) Utilizing the data in the 10K for Microsoft please discuss the individual factor mapping results using the company’s own data (financial statements, etc.) and state whether you agree with the individual ratings assigned by Moody’s for each factor. (50) 
For the 2018 fiscal year, Microsoft’s profitability was 15%. This is considered a respectable degree of profitability. 
Its current ratio was 2.9. This figure is high, meaning that, at that time, Microsoft had a high degree of ability to meet its short-term obligations. 
Its quick ratio was 2.86. This figure is high, suggesting that Microsoft was holding to too much cash and therefore was not using its resources as efficiently as possible. 
Microsoft's cash flow was about $5 billion (extremely high), and its net income was about $16.5 billion (extremely high). 
Its total liabilities were just under $95 billion. 
Moody’s gave Microsoft AAA ratings (the highest possible) in every category, except for end-market diversity, geographic diversity, and free cash flow to debt, in which Microsoft received AA ratings. 
In every possible category, without exception, Microsoft was ranked higher than every single one of its competitors. 
In my judgments, these ratings are most part accurate, with some tentative qualifications. The first is that a AA FCF/debt ratio may be too high, given that Microsoft's total liabilities were $95 billion and its free cash flow in December 2018 was about $5 billion. Microsoft being the juggernaut that it is, this rating may well be justified; but if an assessment of Microsoft's FCF/debt were made strictly on the basis of its cash flow and debt, and not on the basis of anything else, an AA rating might be too high. 
As for end-market diversity and geographic diversity, an AA rating seems too low, given that Microsoft has a worldwide customer-base and also has operations on every continent, except for Africa. (This changed in May 2019, when it launched a $100 million facility in Kenya.) 


Application of Cobit-framework (July 24, 2019) 
To whom it may concern: 
There are many components of the COBIT framework that could be improved upon at RutgersCo. I will discuss the three that I believe to the most important. To quote my professor: “There are various components that assist with IT-governance: Culture, Ethics, and Behavior; People, Skills, and Competencies; and Services, Infrastructure, and Applications” (19_06_23_22.19.06.mp4). In this report, I will show that, in the case of RutgersCo, each of these components needs to be improved. 
Processes: One major problem is a lack of well-defined processes and procedures for carrying out tasks, especially—ominously---accounting related tasks. When I first met with the Assistant Controller (in other words, the second-in-command when it comes to managing RutgersCo’s finance’s), the first words out of his mouth were: “Boy, am I glad to see you. We need to get these books closed. The previous controller made a lot of entries himself. I have been trying to follow his work, but I just can’t quite figure out how he worked up some of the entries” (RiderCo’s Acquisition of RutgersCo). This means that procedures for keeping and sharing financial records are insufficiently defined, which is likely to lead both to loss of revenue, excessive or insufficient payments in taxes, and regulatory or even criminal sanctions. Also, the lack of such procedures in this respect leads to extra work for employees, as this quotation indicates, and also disorients employees and diminishes their respect for the company. 
This brings me to another component of the COBIT framework that could be improved at RutgersCo: People, Skills, and Competency. The accounting team at RutgersCo is feeling frustrated, because some of their best people were recently fired, making it hard for them to do their job (RiderCo’s Acquisition of RutgersCo). The purpose of the firings was to free up money for an ad campaign that totally failed, further angering RutgersCo employees. The firings diminished net competency as well as overall morale, and the ad campaign appears, at least to people in RutgersCo accounting department, to have been a callous attempt on the part of management to make quick money at the expensive of fundamentals. “People skills and competencies,” my professor observed, are integral to people “making good decisions” and thereby keeping the business going (19_06_23_22.19.06.mp4). 
Another Cobit-component that needs work here is Services, Infrastructure, and Applications. The accounting system is divided into a number of different modules, each with its own data-base and unable to communicate with one another in simple user-friendly way (RiderCo’s Acquisition of RutgersCo). This necessitates the use of ad hoc methods of communication, which require effort and increase the chance of human effort. Because of the problems with the accounting system, reporting cannot be automated and has to be done by hand, which is labor-intensive and which increases the likelihood of human error—and also increases the likelihood of deliberate falsification on the part of employees, either to minimize effort on their part or in order to commit fraud. 
These problems relate largely to poor IT-control at RutgersCo. Good IT-control requires that all employees involved in either the keep or reporting of finances be using the same system. Right now, this is not the case. There appear to be independent systems, each with its own database. The fact that multiple databases are used is likely to lead to multiple sets of company books, resulting in management having to arbitrarily choose between different sets of financial records, leading to loss of revenue and possible legal problems. 
Another IT-related problem is that there are unclear protocols relating to the cashing of checks. There should be one person or group of persons who cash checks. But right now, this is not the case, and checks are cashed by employees who happen to be in possession of them. Since these employees are in many cases not formally required to do this, the result is likely to that checks from clients simply are not cashed. Another possibility is that these employees embezzle by endorsing the checks to themselves instead of the company. There should be a well-defined company treasurer, which, at present, there is not. 
Another IT-related problem is that there are poor policies and poor communications relating to the commission-levels of sales personnel. According to one member of the accounting team, practically every single salesperson had a different agreement with management as to how much he was to receive in bonuses and as to what his commission-levels were (RiderCo’s Acquisition of RutgersCo). In fact, it appeared that different salespersons had simply gone rogue and were simply deciding for themselves what their commissions should be. This could lead to embezzlement on a large scale and, at the very least, could least to money-wastage. Also, if, as I likely under the current scenario, a given salesperson gave inconsistent reports concerning his commissions and bonuses to different members of the accounting, the result would be inconsistent records, wasted revenue, and possible regulatory and even criminal sanctions. “The culture, ethics, and behavior of individuals”, my professor said, has a significant effect on the “success of government and management activities” (19_06_23_22.19.06.mp4), and in the case of RutgersCo, the culture is one of low morale, there is a lack of internal possibly leading to loose ethics, and employee-behavior is correspondingly erratic, with an ‘every-man-for­himself’ mentality prevailing.

Introduction 
This present work is a financial analysis of Microsoft during the years 2017-2018. 2016 is included when the necessary data is available in course-documents. Other years are excluded. This analysis is not complete and it focuses only on the absolutely crucial financial indicators. Microsoft’s financials are compared to Oracle’s, so as to provide information as to Microsoft’s performance relative to its competitors. 
Our main source of information about Microsoft is Microsoft’s 2018 10-K form (specifically pages 50-53). Our main source of information about Oracle is Oracle’s income statement, accessed at 
http://financials.morningstar.com/income-statement/is.html? 
t=ORCL&region=usa&culture=en-US. 
The 
financial 
data 
provided 
in 
those 
documents rounds figures to the nearest thousandth. All specific financial data relating to Microsoft and Oracle come from these sources unless there is an indication otherwise. 
Microsoft’s performance during this time-period is positive in absolute terms. Microsoft also marginally outperforms Oracle. That said, the one companies financials parallel each other to a striking degree: Microsoft has more total assets, but its performance relative to its total assets is similar to (though marginally better than) Oracle’s performance relative to its total assets. Between 2017 and 2018, there are dips in Microsoft’s performance, but those are corresponding dips in Oracle’s performance, indicating that the issue is not with Microsoft itself but with the industry as a whole. 

Operating Cash Flow 
When conducting a financial analysis of a company, the first step is to determine its cash flow from operating activities, or operating cash flow (OCF), this being the amount of money that it generates from its activities, excluding long-term investments.[1] 
In 2016, Microsoft’s OCF was $33,325,000; in 2017, $35,507,000; and in 2018, $42,884,000. It is a good sign that Microsoft's OCF was positive during each of these years since this indicates that its products and services were generating income. A company whose OCF is zero or negative can still profit since it can make money from investments and assets, but its products and services are failing to generate income, and its position is therefore unstable. Also, Microsoft’s OCF grew over 30% during this period---also a positive sign. 
Microsoft’s net income (revenues minus expenses, i.e. profits) in 2016 was $20,539,000; in 2017, $25, 489,000; and in 2018, $16, 571,000. During each year, Microsoft’s net income was positive, but its net income fell by $8,918,000---a drop of over 34% (10K p. 52). 
Oracle’s OCF in 2016 was $13, 561, 000; in 2017, $14,126,000; and in 2018, $15,386,000. Oracle’s net income in 2016 was $8,901,000; in 2017, $9,335,000; and in 2018, $3,825,000. Oracle’s OCF was positive during each of these years, and also increased during this time period by around 13%. Between 2017 and 2018, Oracle’s net income plummeted, falling by over 50%. Since its OCF grew during this period, the fall does not indicate a decline in demand for Oracle products and services. 

Common Equity 
In 2016, Microsoft’s common equity (the amount invested by shareholders) was $68, 465, 000; in 2017, 68, 178,000; and in 2018, $69,315,000. There was therefore modest growth in Microsoft common equity during this period. In 2016, Oracle’s common equity was $164, 950, 000; in 2017, $209, 937, 000; and in 2018, $175,569,000.[2] 
Oracle’s performance here is more jagged than Microsoft’s but also better on the whole. 

Profitability 
The next few sections focus on financials relating to Microsoft’s degree of profitability. We will see that Microsoft is ‘in the black’, so to speak, and also compares better than Oracle, a representative competitor. 

Return on Equity 
In 2015, Microsoft’s return on equity (net income divided by shareholder’s equity, this being the change in the value of the shareholder’s equity) was 15.3%; in 2016, 26.9%; in 2017, 31.8%; and in 2018, 19.4% During each year, ROE is positive. At the same time, there was a decrease from 2017 to 2018. 
Oracle’s ROE in 2015 was 12.8%; in 2016, 10.3%and in 2017, 9.7%; and in 2018, 3.4%. In each year, ROE is positive, but much smaller than with Microsoft. Also, with Oracle, as with Microsoft, we see a 2017-2018 drop. This parallel suggests that in both cases the drop is a consequence not of anything company-specific but rather of some larger economic phenomenon. 
2015  2016  2017  2018  
Microsoft  15.3%  26.9%  31.8%  19.4%

Net Profit Margin 
In 2015, Microsoft’s net profit margin (net income divided by total revenue, i.e. percentage of revenue that Microsoft retained after paying expenses) was 13.9%; in 2016, 22.5%; in in 107, 26.3%; and in 2018, 15%. The profit margins are all positive, though there is a (now familiar) 2017­2018 drop. 
Oracle's net profit margin in 2015 was 27.9%; in 2016, 23.7%; in 2017, 25.3%; and in 2018, 10.2%. Again, profit margins are positive, with a 2017-2018 drop. Microsoft's profit margin appears to track Oracle's, suggesting that changes in them are due not to company-specific but rather to industry-wide factors.

Oracle  27.9%  23.7%  25.3%  10.2%  


Asset Turnover 
Asset turnover is significant because it is a good measure of a company’s health. A company with low-asset turnover is not using its assets efficiently, and a company with high-asset turnover is using them efficiently. 
In 2015, Microsoft’s asset turnover (total revenue divided by total assets) was 53%; in 2016, 44%; in 2017, 37%; and in 2018, 43%. In 2015, Oracle’s asset turnover was 34%; in 2016, 33%; in 2017, 28%; and in 2018, 
29%.[3] 
We are seeing parallels—increases in both cases, albeit higher ones in Microsoft’s case. 
2015  2016  2017  2018  
Microsoft  53%  44%  37%  43%  
Oracle  34%  33%  28%  29%  


Leverage 
In 2015, Microsoft’s leverage (debt/equity) was 34.7%; in 2016, 56.6%; in 2017, 86.7%; and in 2018, 87.3%. In 2015, Oracle’s asset turnover was 81%; in 2016, 84%; in 2017, 110%; and in 2018, 230%.[4] 
Both companies are highly leveraged, but Microsoft’s debt/equity ratio is always less than one, meaning that it has more assets than debts, and Oracles debt/equity is greater than one, meaning that is liabilities exceed its assets. A 
high degree of leverage can be a bad sign, since it can indicate deficit financing, but here it is not a bad sign, as it seems to indicate aggressive and successful investments in a growing economic sector.[5]

Liquidity Ratios Current Ratio 
The current ratio measures a company’s ability to pay its short-term obligations. The higher the ratio, the lower the likelihood of defaulting on such debts. Ratios above 1:1 (100%) are considered good, and lower ratios are considered bad. Microsoft’s current ratio is low, whereas Oracle’s is high as of 2018, though low before then. 
2015  2016  2017  2018  
Microsoft  2.5  2.3  2.9  2.9  
Oracle  3.7  4.8  3.9  5.1  


Quick Ratio 
The quick ratio is like the current ratio except that non-liquid assets are excluded from this figure. As with current ratio, a ratio of 1:1 (100%) or higher is considered good, and anything lower is considered bad. Again, Oracle’s quick ratio is better than Microsoft’s. 
2015  2016  2017  2018  
Microsoft  2.3  2.3  2.2  2.7  
Oracle  3.5  4.4  3.6  5.5  


Ebit Interest Coverage 
The Ebit Interest Coverage ratio determines how capable a company is paying the interest on its debt. Obviously should be a least one, since it will otherwise be unable to service its loan-debt. The higher the number, the better. We see here that Microsoft’s numbers compare favorably to Oracles and are also much higher than one. 
2015  2016  2017  2018  
Microsoft  24.7  16.8  11.4  14.4  
Oracle  7.4  7.4  8.9  12.3  


Average Days to Collect Receivables 
Average days to collect receivables is the average number of days that it takes a company to collect payment for services rendered. The number for Microsoft is conspicuously high, which is ambiguous but which could correspond to the fact that Microsoft’s customers are ‘captives’ who Microsoft knows will eventually pay up. 
2015  2016  2017  2018  
Microsoft  73  72  77  80  
Oracle  14  17  11  13  


Conclusion 
Microsoft and Oracle are both vigorous companies with high operating cash flows and asset-turnovers. They are highly leveraged, but this seems to be because they have thus far profited from aggressive investments in their own growth.

Eco-friendly Dieting: A Bachelor’s Story 
When I was in high school, my mother prepared most of my meals, and I ate healthy. Also, I was an athlete, and it simply wasn’t an option for me to eat junk. During college, sadly, that has changed, and I’ve gotten into the habit of eating highly processed food that comes in plastic packaging. This assignment required me to make pretty significant adjustments to my eating habits. I think I could sustain those habits, and it would actually be good for my health and possibly my mood if I did so, but it would involve my making some non-trivial life-changes. In any case, I did succeed in making my diet more eco-friendly for two days, but it wasn't easy and I had only limited success, mainly because my eating habits are currently eco-unfriendly in the extreme. 
For me, breakfast is usually a few microwaved breakfast burritos, which I wash down with Mountain Dew. I often grab a bite to eat on campus. I usually have a cheeseburger or a couple of slices of pizza, along with a soft drink. In between classes, I often eat candy bars. Dinner is usually pizza, with pepperoni and extra cheese, and a soft drink. When I’m at home, I eat a lot of plastic-wrapped snacks in between meals, mainly potato chips. If I’m not at home, I tend to have fast-food for dinner---usually a couple of big macs and a side of fries, which I wash down with a coke. 
After doing some research, I put together a two-day plan for reducing the diet-based part of my ecological footprint. I would have to forego the snacks—no potato chips or candy bars or anything else that is processed or wrapped in plastic. Plus, I couldn’t eat anything cow-based so no beef or cheese, given how much methane cows produce and also given how much petroleum is involved in feeding them. During those two days, I could not eat anything that I am used to eating. I couldn’t even drink what I’m used to drinking, since I pretty much only drink soft-drinks that come in plastic bottles. During those days, I decided, breakfast would be eggs and toast—no sausage or bacon. I didn’t know what to do about lunch, so I just had egg­and-toast sandwiches. Dinner would be pasta with homemade sauce for dinner. There would be no meat in the sauce, but I would allow myself a little bit of parmesan cheese. I knew that I wouldn’t be able to resist eating snacks in between meals; so I decided that I would eat trail-mix instead. Also, I prepared the trail-mix myself (raisins, peanuts, chocolate chips, and cashews), so as to minimize the use of plastic. Instead of drinking soda, I would drink water. I already have a Britta filter and an ice-maker, so this was not too much of a problem. 
I actually stuck with this plan for the whole two days. And I felt better. I noticed that I felt less fatigue after meals and had more energy in general, even though I was consuming fewer calories. I could not sustain that exact diet, since I need some kind of meat in my diet. The most eco-friendly—and also the healthiest—form of meat is fish. Unfortunately, I don’t know how to prepare fish (or anything else, really) in an appetizing way, and that has to change if I’m going to have an eco-friendly diet on a long-term basis. In fact, the main lesson of all this for me, at least on a practical level, is that eating healthy and eco-friendly involves knowing how to cook. If I did learn how to prepare a few healthy dishes, I could definitely sustain a relatively eco­friendly diet in perpetuity. I don’t know whether this would lower my the diet-based part of my ecological footprint to an acceptable level, but it would indeed reduce it. 

Advanced Reading and Writing Semester Project Proposal: Designer Babies 
Topic: Designer babies. 
Context: Thanks to gene editing technology, human beings will likely soon have the ability to ‘design' their own babies by altering their genetic make-up. Depending on how it is used, this technology can do great good or great harm. It can help eliminate congenital illnesses and defects. At the same time, it can be used for questionable commercial, military, or political aims. Also, because it is likely to be expensive, unequal access to it would drive a wedge between rich and poor and it might even be used as a way for the rich to tyrannize the poor. 
Research question: What kinds of public policies would help ensure that this technology would benefit the greatest number of people. 
Preliminary thesis statement: While the use of gene editing technology to create ‘designer babies’ could create problems, these problems can be 
controlled through appropriate policies, with the effect that this technology benefits everyone, and not just a select few. 
Organizational structure: Proposal (Problem-solution) 
Introduction style: Scientific and factual. 
Rough outline: 1. The scientific facts relating to the history and probable future of gene editing technology and, in particular, to its likely use to create ‘designer babies.’ 
2. 
Gene editing technology, especially its use to create defect-free children, has the potential to do a great deal of good for society. At the same time, because it is likely to be expensive, it is likely to exacerbate disparities between rich and poor, and it must therefore be determined what kinds of laws and regulations allow society to enjoy the benefits of this technology while minimizing the injustices that could arise from it. 

3. 
Gene editing technology dates back to the early 2000s, but it wasn't until 2017 that a recently discovered technology known as CRISPR made it possible to edit genes reliably and precisely. Thanks to the invention of the CRISPR, gene editing can be now be used for medical purposes, and it will is likely that it will soon be possible to use CRISPR to create ‘designer babies.’ 

4. 
Because CRISPR is expensive, only the super-wealthy can afford to use it. So the immediate question that arises is what kinds of legal and regulatory changes will help provide those who are not wealthy with access to this technology. Other problems that are likely to arise soon concern the ethical limits of gene editing: Should the use of CRISPR be restricted to preventing instances of congenital illness? Or should it be used to create ‘beautiful' and ‘smart' and otherwise ‘perfect' babies? If so, what kind of health-care reforms are needed to prevent such uses from being used in a discriminatory manner? 

5. 
It is likely that private corporations will do more than Congress to ensure equal access to CRISPR technology, given the political gridlock that prevents legislation, especially healthcare-related legislation, from going through. 

6. 
It is likely that CRISPR technology, and gene editing technology in general, will continue to develop. What cannot be predicted is whether people who need it will have access to it; nor can it be predicted whether it will be used for good as opposed to evil. Law-makers tend to lack unanimity when it 

comes to matters relating to healthcare, and a more promising avenue is to appeal to civic-minded tech-magnates. 

7. 
The bottom line is that gene editing can eliminate illness and improve human life. Also, although gene editing is now expensive, technology tends to become less expensive over time. In the 1980s, only the super-wealthy could afford computers; now everyone can afford them. This may happen with CRISPR. At the same time, policymakers should hedge by beginning to grapple with the problems discussed above. Conclusion style: Dispassionate and consensus-building. We must approach this issue in a non-divisive manner. Our objective is not to alienate the very people and corporations who have access to CRISPR with accusations but is rather to figure out how to make it in their interest to make this technology widely available.

Microsoft Five Forces Analysis 

Introduction 
Microsoft is the world’s two leading supplier of software products, the other being Apple, with the two companies being nearly equal in terms of net worth.[7] 
Microsoft is best known for the Windows Operating System, used on all PC’s, which has a 90% market-share in the digital operating system market. Microsoft is also known for, and derives enormous revenue from, a number of other products, including Microsoft Word, Excel, Skype, Bing, OneDrive, Minecraft, LinkedIn, Cortana, and PowerPoint.[8] 
Microsoft’s annual revenue (as of 2018) is $110 billion, its annual profits are $16 billion, and its market capitalization (overall value) is $775 billion (10K 62). Apple’s annual revenue (as of 2018) is $230 billion, its profits are $10 billion, and its market capitalization is $700 billion.[9] 
The third largest player in the software is IBM, with annual revenue of $15 billion, net profits of $3 billion, and market capitalization of $110 billion (IBIS 22). 
The present study is a Porter’s Five-forces analysis of Microsoft. A Porter’s Five-forces analysis examines a company in terms of the following parameters: competition within its industry, potential of new entrants into that industry, power of suppliers, power of customers, and threat of substitutes. Each of these forces, whether favorable or unfavorable to the company being analyzed, is ranked as either ‘strong’, ‘moderate’, or ‘weak.’ 

Industry Competition (Strong Force) 
Competition within the software industry is fierce. There is intense rivalry on the part of Apple to switch to its operating system. Also, Apple, as well each of the many lesser players, are highly innovative and are rapidly developing products that could chip away at Microsoft’s position. Apple, for example, is known for its smart phone, iPods, and computer-tablets. [10] 

Microsoft’s versions of these products were not successful, although it did stave off the potential economic losses by establishing lucrative partnerships with successful players in these markets, notably Samsung, whose Android phone operating system is the leading smart-phone operating system and from the sales of which Microsoft is now the largest beneficiary. 
Switching costs for existing Microsoft customers are high. It is inconvenient and expensive for users of Windows and related applications to switch to non-Microsoft products. This is because such products are easy to use and tend to be bundled together in ways that are highly functional and that are difficult or impossible to replicate with non-Microsoft products. This force favors Microsoft. 
Other firms in this industry, including but not limited to Apple, are extremely innovative and are also aggressive in marketing their products. Microsoft is not as innovative as Apple or as many of its much smaller competitors. Microsoft counters this, with some success, by buying up patents and buying into companies that offer innovations that it is not itself providing. [11] 

There is a high degree of product-diversity in the software industry, which is a strong force that works against Microsoft, since it could lead, and in some cases has led, to substitutes for Microsoft products and also to products that simply render them obsolete. 

Bargaining Power of Customers (Weak Force) 
Switching costs for Microsoft customers are high, as previously stated, mainly because Windows, Microsoft’s highest earning product, is easy to use and also bundled with a number of applications in a way that can be replicated only with difficulty using non-Microsoft softwares. Microsoft’s great strength is that it offers products (e.g. Excel, Microsoft Word, PowerPoint) at least one or two of which many simply have no choice but to use, and then links that product’s functionality with those of other products for which those same customers are likely to have at least some use. For example, all people who do anything involving writing for a living need to use a product functionally similar to Microsoft Word. If they choose an alternative to Microsoft Word, they find, first, that those products tend to be bug-infested and, second, that if they want to convert them into PDF format or embed photos in them, they are simply out of luck. 
Also, many businesses use Microsoft products and also rely on their high degree of co-functionality with one another with non-Microsoft products. This would make it difficult, and in many cases impossible, for Microsoft’s numerous existing business customers to switch brands. 

Bargaining Power of Suppliers (Moderate Force) 
Microsoft offers a range of different products, and it also relies on many different products and technologies for its own R&D. This makes it vulnerable to shifts in prices in the corresponding raw materials (IBIS 32). In particular, Microsoft is vulnerable to shifts in copper (for the copper wiring in the operating systems that it manufactures) and in petroleum (use for the plastic in the casing, which it also sells). These are both price-volatile commodities, potentially posing a problem for Microsoft. 

Threat of Substitutes (Weak Force) 
It is often difficult and sometimes impossible for Microsoft customers to switch to other brands. Non-Microsoft versions of Word and Excel do not work very well and have lower functionality than their Microsoft counterparts. Also, Microsoft makes its products easy to use with one another and with related Non-Microsoft products. For example, Microsoft Word documents are easily saved to OneDrive, which is Microsoft’s Cloud Storage service; and Microsoft Word documents can easily be saved as Adobe PDF files (and vice versa). [12] 
Also, Microsoft makes it easy to import numbers into Excel Files, and it has loaded Excel with extremely useful and easy to use accounting features. These products and integrated functionalities are not offered by other companies, making switching a non-option in most cases. 
Also, Microsoft aggressively litigates would-be rival purveyors of alternatives on patent-grounds, helping to perverse its position. At the same time, Microsoft products can be cloned and pirated. Because the technology involved in cloning Microsoft products is still rather unwieldy, people are better off simply buying them from Microsoft. But as those products get easier to use, this could potentially change. But this is not likely to happen any time soon, given that these pirated products, though functional on their own, cannot be used in conjunction with Microsoft products. So for example, while it not hard to clone Microsoft Word software, documents made with Word-clones cannot be converted into Adobe PDF’s; nor could numerical data on them imported into Microsoft Excel files. [13] 
Replicating cross-application functionalities would involve systematic pirating of Microsoft-technology, which would not be easy to do. 

Threat of New Entrants (Moderate Force) 
Any given Microsoft product can be replicated, either legally or illegally, and any such product could, on its own, be mass-produced relatively inexpensively. The problem for would-be competitors is that Microsoft products are co-functional with many non-Microsoft products and these Microsoft has exclusivity agreements with these other companies, which limit the amount of functionality that non-Microsoft products can have. For example, Microsoft word documents have a feature whereby users can dictate words, instead of having to type them. This feature is easily replicated, but it couldn’t be used on PC’s made by any existing company, since they have contracted with Microsoft to block use of such application 
with any non-Microsoft product.[14] 

At the same time, there are many small companies that already create analogues of Microsoft-products, and such a company would be a threat if it had the financing needed to market itself and to establish legally protected Microsoft-style product-integrations with related companies. The chances of this are small but not negligible. 

Conclusion 
Microsoft’s position is relatively secure. It offers useful and easy to use products for low prices, and those products have useful integrations with one another and with non-Microsoft products. In addition, Microsoft protects itself against rivals either by contracting with them, as it did with Samsung, effectively buying them out, or entering into exclusivity agreements that limit the extent to which non-Microsoft products can have the range of uses as Microsoft products. Microsoft’s vulnerability is its current lack of innovativeness. But because Microsoft hedges against this problem by simply buying out would-be competitors, it is likely to retain its position as one of the top two software providers for the foreseeable future. 
Works Cited Hadad, Jonathan. "IBISWorld Industry Report 51121 Software Publishing in the US." IBIS World. September 2018. "Microsoft Form 10-K." SEC. June 2018. 

Wittgenstein on Language and Thought (Philosophy) 
It is obvious that linguistic competence (the ability to use and understand language) enhances the ability to think. But does it constitute that ability? In other words, is it identical with that ability? 
Ludwig Wittgenstein says ‘yes’. And he supports his position with two famous arguments of his: the ‘Private Language” argument and the ‘Rule-following argument’. 
According to the ‘Private Language’, one cannot invent a ‘private language’, except by translating some pre-existing public language into private terms. Wittgenstein’s argument is that semantic rules (rules that assign meanings to expressions) are, like all rules, null and void—and therefore as good as non-existence—unless enforced. And, so Wittgenstein also holds, the semantic rules of a private language would be unenforced— and therefore null and void. Therefore, so Wittgenstein concludes, a private language (that isn’t just a re-boot of a pre-existing public language) is impossible. 
According to the Rule Following argument, there can be no such thing as a ‘private rule’; in other words, one cannot impose a rule on oneself: all genuine rules are social rules, meaning that they involve pluralities of people. Wittgenstein’s argument for this is similar to the Private Language Argument: unenforced rules are non-rules and private rules are unenforced. 
Wittgenstein also holds that thinking involves following rules—his argument, so far as he has one, being that, unless mental activity is rule-governed, it isn’t principled and, unless it is principled, it isn’t thought so much as it is mere free association or mere sensation, or some such. 
Wittgenstein thus holds that thinking involves the following rules. He also holds that rules are inherently public. So he is forced by these starting points to find some set of public rules the following of which can reasonably be identified with the act of thinking. And he understandably believes that linguistic rules are the only rules that meet this requirement. And it follows from these assumptions that, indeed, thought is inherently linguistic, i.e. that linguistic competence is constitutive of cognitive competence. 
But Wittgenstein is wrong and his argument is bad. First of all, thought is needed in order to understand or operate with semantic rules. Without thought, a creature can make noises, but it cannot speak. The difference between a coffee-maker that makes noise and a person that speaks is that the latter, but not the former, understands the rules that link meaning to sounds. So Wittgenstein is simply false. 
Plus, not all thought is linguistic. There is visual thought, musical thought, etc. And there is no plausible way to identify these with the manipulating of linguistic symbols. 
Given that Wittgenstein’s position is false, there must be a flaw in his reasoning. And indeed there is: Wittgenstein is wrong to assume that non­public rules can be violated with impunity. Suppose that I invent a private code in which I tell myself that X means ‘poisonous berries’ and Y means ‘non-poisonous berries.’ If I misinterpret my own language, I won’t be penalized by other people—the ‘language police’ won’t get on my case—but I will indeed be penalized, as I will eat the poisonous berries and die. 
So the situation here is simply. Wittgenstein gave a bad argument for a wrong and implausible idea, and that’s the end of it. 

Camus and Schopenhauer on the Meaning of Life (Philosophy) 
Instructions: 
Please, answer the following essay question. Read the question closely and make sure to address all of its parts. The recommended length for your answer is: min. 500 words – max. 600 words. 
Q. How does Schopenhauer arrive at the basic insight that life is suffering? Under these apparently frustrating conditions, what is the best (or “least bad”) way to live, according to him? Next, what does Camus mean by “the absurd” and how shall we cope with it? What do Schopenhauer’s take on life and Camus’s take on life have in common, and in what ways are they different? Whose view of life do you find more convincing, and why? 
Schopenhauer believes that life is suffering. Camus believes that life is absurd. They thus both seem to have a fundamentally negative conception of life. To this extent, their conceptions of life are similar or at least seem to be. But scrutiny of their respective positions proves them to be very different. 
First of all, one can hold that life is fundamentally an exercise in suffering without holding that life is meaningless or ‘absurd’. Steve Jobs spent most of his life struggling to achieve success. And the amount of time that he spent struggling to achieve success may well have exceeded the amount of time that he actually enjoyed it. But was his life meaningless? No. 
Second, one can hold that life is fundamentally not about suffering without holding that it is absurd. In fact, people who have a hedonistic approach to life seem to be of the view that life is absurd. Also, if life is fundamentally an exercise in suffering, then it would seem that life does have meaning, its meaning being given to it by one’s need to minimize or eliminate suffering. And life is drained of meaning to the extent that it is free of adversity. 
This actually seems to be Schopenhauer’s own view. “Life without pain has no meaning”, Schopenhauer writes. Schopenhauer’s position is that pleasure and happiness are low-probability conditions, since they are unstable and hard to achieve, and that pain and unhappiness are high-probability conditions, since they are stable and easy to achieve. From this, Schopenhauer deduces that life is more about pain than pleasure, more about unhappiness than happiness. 
But Schopenhauer does not hold that life is meaningless. As already indicated, he holds that at least some strife is necessary for meaning. Second, he holds that pain and unhappiness can be minimized. In Schopenhauer’s view, people tend to have a preconception to the effect that life is supposed to make them happy; and if one lets go of that preconception, one is allowing life to be happy in its own way. 
Camus does not hold that life is necessarily painful. At the same time, Camus does hold that life is ‘absurd.’ By this Camus means that only that life does not supply Man with a meaning: [15] 
But Camus also holds that, if life did have some pre-existing meaning, that fact would hem us in. And the fact that it doesn’t, so Camus holds, allows us to be free.”[16] 

Although Camus’ views about the meaning(fulness) of life differ from Schopenhauer’s, both views coincide in one respect. According to both, it is by accepting that which is negative in life that one stands to gain what is positive in it. Says Camus: “You will never be happy if you continue to search for what happiness consists of. You will never live if you are looking for the meaning of life.”[17] 
Says Schopenhauer: “If you accustom yourself to this view of life you will regulate your expectations accordingly, and cease to look upon all its disagreeable incidents … as anything unusual or irregular; nay, you will find everything is as it should be, in a world where each of us pays the penalty of existence in [their] own particular way.” [18] 



Are Late-term Abortions Ethical? (Philosophy) 
A late-term abortion is one that is performed in the third trimester of pregnancy. Are such abortions ethical? In this paper, I consider the work of four reputable scholars, two of whom argue that late-term abortions are ethical and two of whom argue that they are unethical. I arrive at the position that late-term abortions are unethical. 
Before we consider what these scholars say, let us make some general points of a philosophical nature. 
When an abortion is performed, two parties are involved: the mother and the fetus. The mother clearly has ethical rights, since she is indisputably a person. But does the fetus have rights? This is not clear, because it is not clear whether the fetus is a person. If the fetus is a person, then the fetus has rights. If the fetus is not a person--if it is a subhuman or a pre-person--then it either has no rights or has fewer rights than a person. 
A newborn infant is considered a person. A newborn infant does not have particularly well-developed reasoning capabilities, but it has consciousness, it can experience sensations and perceptions, and it can experience emotion. A one day old fetus is probably not a person since it has none of these attributes. 
Should it turn out that a fetus in the third trimester has all of the psychological attributes of a one-day old infant, then it would have rights. In particular, it would have the right to live. In any case, the question whether the fetus is psychologically comparable to a newborn is a medical question and can be answered only on the basis of knowledge of its nervous system and the like. 
According to Kenneth E. Himma, a professor of philosophy, late term abortions are perfectly ethical. His argument is that fetuses are not conscious, do not feel pain, do not make moral judgments and for these reasons fail to be people. 
According to Sara Miller, a journalist, late-term fetuses don’t feel pain and, indeed, don’t feel anything and are not therefore not people. 
According to Don Berkich, a professor of philosophy, fetuses will become people, even if they are not currently people, and in having an abortion, one is, as it were, killing the person that the fetus is going to be: one is robbing it of ‘a future like ours,’ as Berkich puts it. 
According to Maureen Condic, a professor of neurobiology, there is strong medical evidence to suggest that late-term fetuses can feel pain and have other psychological traits specific to human beings; and for this reason, Condic alleges, late-term abortions are ethically wrong. 
The medical evidence strongly suggests that Condic is right. Late-term fetuses are neurologically sufficiently similar to newborn infants that the former may reasonably be presumed to have the same psychological characteristics as the latter, a corollary being that the former have whatever ethical rights the latter have. The arguments put forth by Himma and Miller are therefore null and void, as they are based on false medical claims concerning the biological condition of late-term fetuses. As for Berkich’s claim that abortion robs the fetus of its future, this claim is consistent with the fact that, judging by the relevant neurobiological benchmarks, the fetus is indeed a person. 

Exploring Music Assignment 1 
This particular work---“Scared to be Lonely” by Martin Garrix and sung by Dua Lipa[19]--was probably more on the repetitive side than on the variable side, but it was, at the end of the day, a pretty healthy mix. Also, it has a nice balance of voice and instrumentation, including some Hybrid (electronic) instrumentation.
