Chapter 1 Introduction to the Study 


Introduction 
Thanks to the internet, it is dramatically easier than it used to be for consumers to know what is supplied and for suppliers to know what is demanded. Prices have dropped; quality has increased; selection has grown exponentially; efficiency has increased exponentially. The internet makes information easy to acquire and easy to send, and that is why it has revolutionized commerce (Bauer & Latzer, 2016). 
One of the few economic sectors that the internet has not benefited is education (Slimp, 2019). Since the world went online in the late '90s, tuitions have increased, and quality of instruction has fallen. This is deeply paradoxical. Educators are information-providers; so the internet should in principle be driving tuitions down and drive quality of instruction up. 
There are two different academic disciplines---Knowledge Management (KM) and Information Economics (IE)—that should both explain and solve this problem (Hawamdeh, 2007). KM is the study of how institutions can improve internal communications and, on that basis, self-optimize (Dalkir 2018). IE is the study of knowledge-markets (Stiglitz 1985), a knowledge market being a market in which information is what is bought and sold. Each of these disciplines is young, with IE coming into existence in the '80s and KM coming into existence in the '90s. Neither discipline has said much of anything about the failure of the internet to improve education (Birchler & Butler, 2007). Further, neither has to any degree improved education or even tried to do so, there being no documented cases of any school self-optimizing or even trying to self-optimize on the basis of results from each discipline. 
At the same time, each discipline is rich in results that can be used to optimize education. The basic tenet of IE is that markets must be transparent in order to function; in other words, the values of assets must be known and those assets must be priced accordingly (Stiglitz 1985). The basic tenet of KM is that organizations must be transparent in order to function; in other words, the values of employees must be known and those employees must be positioned accordingly (Dalkir 2018). How these tenets are to be operationalized depends on the context. But each has been successfully operationalized in several different contexts, and prima facie there is nothing about the field of education that would make it especially difficult to figure how to operationalize them in that context (Friedman, 2012). The only reason it has not yet been figured out is, evidently, that nobody has tried (Law, 2020). 

Background to the Study 
In traditional economic theory, it is assumed that all market participants have equal access to information. This is, of course, false, as Hayek (1945) observed. People differ radically in respect of what they know. According to Hayek, these differences will help society, rather than hurt it, so long as governments don’t manipulate prices. When prices are set by the market, Hayek reasoned, they correctly degrees of supply and demand, and anyone who knows what prices are, no matter how ignorant he might otherwise be, can service demand and profit. 
This model is known as Efficient Market Theory (Arrow, 1999). Efficient Market Theory (EMT) embodies two distinct contentions: first, that markets are efficient when assets are appropriately priced; second, that assets are appropriately priced when governments don’t alter prices. The first contention has proven to be unqualifiedly accurate (Friedman 2012, Sowell 2016). The second has proven to be true of some markets (Lo 2017) and false or only partly true of others (Arrow 1996, Sen 2006, Tirole 2017). What matters in this context is that it is an attempt to figure out how markets must be arranged if assets, including information, are to be generated and distributed with maximal efficiency. According to Milton Friedman (1955), a corollary of EMT is that education cannot be efficient without being completely deregulated. 
In his (1963) paper “Uncertainty and the Welfare Economics of Medical Care”, Kenneth Arrow proved that it is impossible for knowledge to be equitably distributed in healthcare markets and that such markets therefore tend to be inefficient. George Akerlof (1970), Michael Spence (1970) Joseph Stiglitz (1975) proved the same to be true of information-markets: in other words, information is not and cannot possibly be equitably distributed in markets in which information is itself what is bought and sold, a corollary being that such markets therefore naturally tend to be inefficient. It was with the work of these three authors that IE became an autonomous subdiscipline of economics. 
The discipline of information economics has had virtually nothing to say about education (Akinyemi, 2013). This is surprising for many reasons. First, the education industry is a textbook case of a knowledge-market. Also, given how large the US Education Industry is, the macroeconomic consequences of inefficiencies in it, especially of inefficiencies resulting from inequitably distributed information, are virtually incalculable. (According to Bryant and Sarakatsannis (2015) “US education is a $1.5 trillion industry 
and growing at 5 percent annually.”)Finally, American education is both highly regulated and highly inefficient, making it an ideal testing ground for the very kinds of deregulation that economists so vehemently advocate in other industries. 
Like IE, KM has been curiously silent about education. KM came into existence in the earlyv90's, when companies began hiring Chief Knowledge Officers. A CKO’s job is to organize and optimally deploy a company’s ‘intangible assets.’ An intangible asset is simply one that is not physical, and knowledge itself is one of the most important kinds of intangible assets. Ikujiro Nonaka’s (1991) article “The Knowledge Creating Company”, published in the Harvard Business Review, introduced the concept of Knowledge Management to the academic community. In 1998, Thomas Stewart, editor of the Harvard Business Review, published his book Intellectual Capital: The New Wealth of Organizations. This helped give additional credibility to KM, both inside and outside of academia. Within a few years, it became customary for universities to offer courses in KM. 
The central tenet of KM is that companies gain by treating knowledge as a veritable asset, comparable to machinery or land, and lose when they treat it as a mere asset-precursor (Gupta 2004, Dalkir 2018). Exactly what is involved in treating knowledge as a veritable asset depends on the context. 
Within practically any large company outside of academia, there is at least one person whose job title is ‘knowledge manager.' But this position simply doesn't exist in schools and universities, and KM-theoreticians have said quite literally nothing about how schools and universities can improve internal communications (Yueh, 2018). 
In this work, we will map what KM and IE have already said and done onto universities and instruction-markets. But there is an important preliminary point. There are two very different kinds of Knowledge Management: Reactive and Proactive (Hunter, 2016). Reactive KM involves improving communications within an organization that is already mature and whose structure is therefore largely fixed. Proactive KM involves creating organizations whose structures guarantee good internal communications. A consequence of this distinction is that there are two ways of measuring the efficacy of KM: first, by considering reactive cases of KM, i.e. by considering the consequences on the part of an already existing and mature organization to implement principles of KM; second, by considering cases of proactive KM, i.e. by studying the difference between companies that applied KM to themselves only after they were mature and companies that were so designed as to avoid having KM problems in the first place. Some examples will help clarify these points. 
The Lehman Brothers investment bank was founded in 1844 and by the early 2000s had become so large that nobody within it, including management, had more than a fragmentary knowledge of its various operations. Management became concerned about its lack of knowledge of Lehman’s operations, fearing that it might be overexposed, and hired knowledge managers to help make its operations more internally transparent (Ball, 2018). This proved impossible, since Lehman’s various different departments enjoyed the autonomy that Lehman’s poor internal communications allowed them to have and actively fought attempts to expose their workings. As a result, Lehman invested more than it could afford to lose and collapsed when, in 2008, those investments proved to be bad ones. Lehman tried to use reactive KM to solve its problems, but by then its problems were intractable. 
Compare Lehman with E-trade. Founded in 1982, e-trade offers many of the same services offered by Lehman. But it was so designed to be lightweight, with transactions being automated whenever possible and all digitally logged in a centralized database. Because it is well-organized and has minimal overhead, E-trade is easy to use and charges its clients a fraction of what its brick-and-mortar counterparts charge. By practicing proactive KM, E-trade avoided Lehman-type communications blockages, and its market share has grown steadily since its inception (Moschella, 2017). 
In general, reactive KM yields only modest results, while proactive KM often yields spectacular results (Buffet, 2015). E-trade, Amazon, Microsoft, Apple, Airbnb, and Uber are companies whose success derives from proactive KM, and these are the very companies that we will discuss when investigating how KM can benefit education. 
KM-based cases of optimization are organization-internal, whereas IE-based cases of optimization involve whole markets and are therefore company-external. But IE-based optimization often results from KM-based optimization, and we will discuss several cases of this. For example, Amazon, E-trade, and Airbnb are both models of good KM and, partly for that reason, also led to efficient exchanges of information within markets, thereby optimizing those markets. But it must be kept in mind that no case of KM-based optimization is ever actually identical with a case of IE-based optimization, even though both may involve a single organization. Having good internal communications is a cause of, but is not identical with, Amazon’s making it easier for people to exchange information and products within many different markets (Keiding, 2017). 

Problem Statement 
The problem here is how to use accepted principles of KM and IE to optimize education. Information economists have proposed ways to optimize finance and healthcare, and in some cases those proposals have been implemented with good results, but they have maintained a veritable wall of silence as to how their discipline can be used to optimize education (Stankov, 2019). 
This is deeply perplexing. The very purpose of economics is to study how variations in inputs affect outputs. When more is spent and less is produced, economists are quick to sound the alarm. In education, costs have steadily grown over the last fifty years and yet outputs have declined (Busch, 2017). And yet economists have said nothing. The situation is doubly perplexing, given how much the internet could have optimized education and given how little it has done so (Pratt & DeDionisio, 2019). Similar points hold of KM. 
At the same time, these disciplines have created the intellectual instruments necessary to solve this problem. IE has made it clear how to optimize information-markets, and KM has made it clear how to optimize organization-internal communications. Universities are organizations, and instructions-markets are knowledge-markets. The purpose of this study is to map what IE has said about knowledge-markets in general onto instruction-markets specifically and to map what KM has said about organizations in general onto universities (Bootle, 2019). 

Purpose of the Study 
The purpose of this work is to show that IE and KM can be just as successful at optimizing instruction-markets and institutions of learning as they have been at optimizing knowledge-markets in general and organizations in general. 
We will discuss several cases of KM being used to optimize businesses and several cases of IE being used to optimize markets. It will be shown that each case involved the elimination of a suboptimality similar to one that vitiates American higher education, and it will then be shown how to implement the corresponding optimizations thereto. 
When discussing KM-based cases of optimization, we will focus on four companies: Amazon, E-trade, Uber, and Airbnb. Each of these KM-success stories corresponds to an IE-success story. This is because good internal communications allowed each of these companies to improve communications with its respective market and, on that basis, to improve those markets. We will describe the structure of each of these companies, making it clear how that structure conduces to good internal communications. We will then discuss how these companies were able to dominate and optimize their respective markets. In each case, it will be made clear how these companies optimized communications within their respective markets and how their doing so was responsible for their both dominating and optimizing those markets. 
We will find that, as each of these companies came to dominate its respective market, the structure of that market and that company’s internal structure increasingly mirrored each other. Before these companies came into existence, those markets were opaque and inefficient. After they came into existence, they began to become transparent and efficient, like those companies themselves. 
We will find that these companies have several structural similarities, in particular: 
*Small staff in relation to their business-volumes, 
*Heavy reliance on contract labor, 
*High degree of automation, 
*High employee turnover, 
*High degree of willingness to employ and to fire, 
*Well-defined performance-benchmarks, 
*Minimal middle-management, 
*An emphasis reducing consumer-costs, rather than on justifying high consumer-costs, 
*An emphasis on minimizing the amount of time between purchase and receipt of products/services, 
*Minimal reliance on public funding, 
*Non-adversarial relationship to independent and small operators, 
*An emphasis on helping suppliers and consumers find one another, and 
*Each enables people to do for themselves what they previously had to pay others to do (Boyd, 2019). 
Each of these companies benefits from market-transparency and deregulation. Their predecessors benefited form market-opacity and protectionism. E-trade by makes money by allowing people to act as their own brokers. Its predecessors made money by forcing people to go through their brokers. Uber makes money by allowing anyone to give a ride to anyone. Its predecessors made money by contracting with local governments to restrict would-be competitors from operating. Airbnb makes money by allowing any given person to rent out his home for a night. Its predecessors made money by offering lodging to people who simply didn't know that they had alternatives. Amazon makes money by giving consumers and suppliers access to each other. Amazon is the first company of its kind, and the markets that it now services were opaque and inefficient prior to its existence. Each of these companies makes money because the internet makes information available, and their predecessors made money because the absence of the internet made it unavailable (Rutherford, 2017). 
When examined, universities prove to be the very antithesis of the just-described companies, being characterized by high: 
*High degree of organizational opacity, 
*Minimal turnover, 
*Low degree of willingness to employ or to fire 
*Complete non-enforcement of economically meaningful performance-benchmarks, 
*Focus on justifying ever-increasing customer-expenses, instead of reducing them, 
*Minimal automation, 
*Heavy reliance on incumbent status, 
*Heavy reliance public funding, 
*High degree of exposure to costly labor regulations, 
*Adversarial relationship to independent operators, and 
*Fierce opposition to allowing customers to self-service (McDonald, 2010). 
Despite these facts, universities are not struggling financially. On the contrary, they are flourishing. This is because universities, though often nominally private, are parastatal organizations that do not compete on the open market (Cubberly, 2017). This point will be elaborated in Chapter 3. 
It might be surmised that there are formidable legal and regulatory barriers to creating universities, or university-substitutes, that embody the transparency and efficiency of an Amazon or an E-trade. This is not the case. The government is remarkably willing to grant the requisite licenses and is remarkably slow to regulate licensees out of existence (Mazzucatto, 2017). We appear to be dealing with a true market vacuum: a case where would-be suppliers have been slow to notice demand, and the already-mentioned success-stories strongly suggest ways to service this demand. This will be discussed in detail in Chapter 4. 

Research Questions 
An ‘instruction-provider’, as we will be using this term, is an organization whose purpose is to sell instruction. Thus, a university is an instruction-provider, as is an online tutoring agency. 
Our primary research question is: 
RQ1: What kind of an internal structure must an instruction-provider if it is to benefit from a transparent instruction-market? 
We will find that companies cannot benefit from transparent markets without themselves helping to make them transparent. For this reason, the answer to RQ1 is identical with the answer to: 
RQ2: What kind of an internal structure must an instruction-provider if it is to help make instruction-markets more transparent? 
Other questions that we will address are: 
RQ3: In what ways are existing instruction-providers succeeding and in what ways are they failing? 
RQ4: To what extent can be they be so restructured as to stop failing in those ways, and to what extent does their continued financial well-being depend on their being so restructured? 
RQ5: Are there markets that existing instruction-providers are systemically incapable of servicing, and if so, how could they be serviced? 
RQ1-RQ5 are all practical questions, but the answers to them will help us answer some important theoretical questions, the main one being 
RQ6: Granting that information is necessarily unevenly distributed in instruction-markets, how can the resulting inefficiencies be minimized? 
RQ6 is a specialized form of an open question of Information Economics, namely: 
RQ7: How can information-markets be efficient? 
In an information-market, information is ipso facto unevenly distributed. In an efficient market, information is ipso facto evenly distributed. Hence the raison d’être for RQ7. 

Advancing Scientific Knowledge and Significance of Study 
The primary significance of this study is that it will show how to make education better by making it profitable. A secondary significance is that it will show how to make knowledge-markets efficient. The first significance is practical; the second is theoretical. 
The idea that education is corrupted by being monetized is deeply entrenched in the popular psyche, despite there being no empirical evidence to that effect (Busch, 2017). Also, educational institutions, unlike businesses, are often treated as cultural icons. If a business fails to perform, no one for a second believes that it should be allowed to hold onto its market share. There were cries of outrage when the big three automakers were bailed, even though they employ hundreds of thousands of Americans and are veritable cornerstones of Americana (Tilleman, 2016). But universities receive more and more public money as they charge their students more and more for educations that benefit them less and less; and yet it is seldom so much as hinted that universities should explain where that money goes. The underlying conceit seems to be that research and instruction are sacred and must therefore be insulated from market forces, lest they be ‘bought’ (Samuelson, 2011). 
This conceit is false and injurious. It is not selling out to provide non-defective products and services for an agreed upon price. It is selling out to take money for defective products and services. The open market doesn’t allow people to take money for defective or undesired products and services. If I try to sell paintings that nobody wants or I try to sell cars that don’t work, I will go out of business. But if my money comes from someone other than the people who need my services, I can provide defective or useless services and continue to be funded (Sowell, 2016). 
In any case, it is a fact that monetized information-exchanges are more efficient non-monetized information-exchanges (Hazlitt, 1985). People who are financially motivated to avail others of information, they do more quickly and inexpensively than those who are not and also provide more accurate information. This insight has been used to optimize many different kinds of markets, including information-markets, and the present work will show how it can be used to optimize instruction-markets. 

Rationale for Methodology 
The basic contention of this work is that information-markets, including instruction-markets, are transparent when to the extent that they are monetized and opaque to the extent that they are not, a corollary being that education is optimized by being monetized. 
This proposition can be established on strictly econometric grounds. Information, as well as information-quality, can be quantified. Therefore, it is possible to identify and measure the factors that conduce to information-flow. Entrepreneurs do this implicitly, and recently mathematicians and economists have done this explicitly (Roe, 2019). Further, monetary inputs and outputs can obviously be quantified and can therefore correlated with other quantifiable inputs and outputs, such as labor, time, and information (Hayashi, 2011). 
Inevitably, there will be theoretical puzzles relating to the quantification of information, but they will not affect the contexts that we will be considering. 
Consequently, our methodology is quantitative; more specifically, econometrics-based, econometrics being the branch of economics that quantitative dependencies of economics outputs on economic inputs (Hayashi, 2011). 
The mathematics involved in econometrics can be very simple or very complex, depending on the context, and fortunately are simple in the contexts that concern us. 
Nature of the Research Design 

for the Study 
We will start by comparing our target-companies---Amazon, E-trade, Uber, and Airbnb---with unsuccessful companies in similar markets. We will assign an information-efficiency quotient (IEQ) to each company, a given company’s IEQ being high if it is likely to capitalize on information and low if it is unlikely to do so. On this basis, we will then identify the structural characteristics of companies, including instruction-providers, with high IEQ’s. 
We will then compare information-efficient markets with information-inefficient markets. The efficient-markets will be those that were made efficient by our target companies, and the inefficient ones will be those same markets prior to their being thus optimized. We will then assign IEQ’s to those various markets, on that basis identifying the structural properties of high-IEQ markets, including information-markets. 

Research Questions 
What organizational structure must an instruction-provider have if it is to provide the best possible instruction at the lowest possible price? 
What organizational structure must an instruction-provider have it is to be sensitive to changes in the kinds of instruction that are demanded and is also to be able to service those changes? 
Is it possible for a profitable instruction-provider to sell results as opposed to services? If so how? 
When does research have to be insulated from market-forces in order to be good, and when does it have to be exposed to market-forces to be good? 
Does tenure lead to transparency or to opacity in instruction-markets? 
Why are universities increasingly expensive, despite being an 
increasingly bad investment? 
Under what circumstances does the information-asymmetries inherent in information-markets, including instruction-markets, drive down the price of information? 
Are there certain kinds of information-providers that benefit from data-protectionism and market-opacity? Does a company that sells information about bond markets benefit from a lack of public access to data about such markets? 
What kind of organizational structure must a company have if it is to benefit from selling instruction that enables people to self-instruct? 
Can governments help make instruction-markets more transparent, or does government-intervention tend to make such markets less transparent? 
Are information-providers necessarily put out of business if they do their job too well? 
Is there a definition of the term ‘rational actor’ that accommodates the fact that what a person wants changes as he acquires more knowledge and more intelligence? 
We will put forth and defend answers to each of these questions. Here are some anticipations of what we will find. 
Properly structured instruction-providers benefit from transparency in their respective markets and are harmed by its absence. At the same time, a belief to the contrary is embodied in the very structures of most existing instruction-providers, a consequence being that instruction is more expensive, less available, and of lower quality than it need be (Friedman, 1993). 
Instruction-providers benefit from market-transparency to the extent that they provide good instruction and are harmed by it to the extent that they don’t. 
Information-providers that are not instruction-providers fall into two very different categories: information-hoarders (companies that make money by blocking the flow of information and then charging people for it) and information-seekers (companies that make money by finding information that would be hard to obtain even in transparent markets), with information-seekers benefiting from market-transparency and information-hoarders being harmed it (Seward, 2016). 
The concept of a ‘rational actor’ is a coherent one, and has operational economic significance, despite the fact that desires vary with changes in knowledge and intelligence. The reason is that economically rational actor necessarily desires the information needed to acquire more resources, and this desire only grows as he acquires more such knowledge (Gawronski, 2016). 

Definitions of Terms 
Parity: A situation in which economic competitors are equally competent (Hazhimzade, 2017). 
Information-parity: A situation in which economic competitors are equally informed (Clapham, 2014). 
Market-transparency: Equal availability of market-relevant information to all market-participants (Hazhimzade, 2017). 
Market-opacity: Absence of market-transparency (Clapham, 2014). 
Structural market-opacity: Market-opacity due to deliberate attempts on the part of governments or market-actors to obstruct the flow of information (Blackburn, 2016). 
Non-structural market-opacity: Market-opacity due to differences in luck, intelligence, and effort on the part of market participants, and not to deliberate attempts on anyone's part to obstruct access to information (Hazhimzade, 2017). 
Information-provider: Organization whose business is to sell information (Blackburn, 2016). 
Information-hoarder: Information-provider whose business involves 
its restricting access to information that would otherwise be available and then charging for access to said information (Ferguson, 2016). 
Information-seeker: Information-provider whose business is to obtain information that is unavailable only because of limitations on time, effort, and ability on the part of those want it (Rutherford, 2019). 
Data-protectionism: Policy adopted either by governments or private entities of sequestering otherwise publicly available information for the purpose of inflating its market-value and of protecting market-positions, not for the purpose of guaranteeing organizational or public security. Data-protectionist policies are often represented as serving the interests of national or organizational security, the distinction between security-driven and protectionist-driven data-sequestration tends to be clear. Data-protectionism undermines commerce; security-driven data-sequestration does not (Upton, 2014). 
Instruction-provider: Organization whose business is to sell instruction (Upton, 2014). 
Parastatal organization: An organization that is nominally private but functions as a governmental organization. The main difference between parastatal and genuinely private organizations is not that the former are more powerful than the latter; sometimes parastatal organizations have very little power, and sometimes genuinely private organizations are very powerful. Rather, the difference is that parastatal organizations are insulated from market-forces and persist despite a failure to provide goods or services for which there is any obvious demand (Reddy, 2005). 
Equilibrium: A market in which prices equal values is said to be in equilibrium. Disequilibrium leads to inefficiency, since it involves somebody’s either paying too much or not being paid enough. When prices are above equilibrium, consumers pay too much; and when prices are below equilibrium, suppliers are not paid enough (Ferguson, 2017). 
Rigid labor market: A labor market in which slow job turnover inflates labor-costs and diminishes labor-quality (Reddy, 2005). 
Efficient Market Theory (EMT): The principle that market-relevant information tends to be equitably distributed, leading to equilibrium, in the absence of government policies preventing the flow of information (Hazhimzade, 2017). 

Assumptions, Limitations, Delimitations 
People often have beliefs for which they do not have good evidence. Such a belief is a hypothesis when it is one’s objective to prove it true, and an assumption when one has no such objective (Kuczynski, 2009). This work will be rich in hypotheses but relatively free of assumptions. We will make relatively few assumptions as the truthfulness of descriptions of markets or of organizations, since our starting-point will be that opaque markets and organizations inherently lack credibility. As for transparent markets and organizations, statements about them are readily verified or falsified and therefore need not be assumed true or false. 
The discipline of economics is proverbially rich in assumptions, and those assumptions often prove to be false or irrelevant to real-world conditions, with the result that the theories based on them prove to be false or useless. An extreme but otherwise quite standard example of this is EMT. The crash of 2008 was a consequence of market-opacities resulting from data-hoarding on the part of private institutions, coupled with credulity on the part of the public (Soros, 2009). 
In this work, economic principles will be used, but no economic models or theories will be assumed true. A guiding principle of the present work is that unregulated commerce tends to be more efficient than regulated commerce. But this is never simply assumed in the present work; in some cases, it is argued for, and in others, it is argued to be false. 
The main assumption that the present work makes is that what is true of non-information markets, and also of information-markets that are not instruction-markets, will also hold of instruction-markets. That said, there are cases of instructions being made more efficient by being monetized, and no cases of them being made inefficient by being monetized. So this ‘assumption’ of ours is not a pure assumption (Clarey, 2011). 
The most questionable assumption that we will make is that instruction-markets are not in any way ‘special’---that emotional and cultural resistances to monetizing instruction, though possible, are not inevitable. This assumption is questionable given the iconic status that the ‘Academy’ has in present-day culture, along with the obvious affection people have for their ‘alma maters’, despite exorbitant tuitions and minimal returns-on-investment (Clarey, 2009). 

Summary and Organization of the Remainder of the Study 
In Chapter 2, the histories and structures of our target-companies--­Amazon, E-trade, Uber, and Airbnb---will be given. It will be said how their structures differ from those of their predecessors and few remaining competitors. It will be shown our target-companies are more aware of and able to capitalize on market-data than their predecessor-competitors, and it will also be explained why. It will also be explained how our target-companies affected markets. This will involve explaining what these markets were like before and after the inception of these companies, especially how information-flow within changed after their inception. In some cases, notably Amazon, these companies created new markets, the nature and effects of which we will discuss. 
In Chapter 3, the structures of existing instruction-providers will be compared with those of our target-companies, and the structures of existing instruction-markets will be compared with the markets optimized or created by those companies. Tentative conclusions as to how to optimize existing instruction-markets will be drawn. Finally, actual examples of efficient instruction-providers, along with the corresponding markets, will be discussed and shown to bear out these conclusions. 
In Chapter 4, we will delineate some of the theoretical implications of the previous chapters. We will develop the distinction between reactive and proactive KM. We will also discuss the distinction between normative and descriptive IE, descriptive IE between the study of how information flows within existing markets and normative IE being the study of how it should flow. In relation to this, it will be discussed what both private actors and governments can do to improve transparency within information-markets. We will also discuss the threat of parastatal organizations to market-transparency, especially in relation to information-and instruction-markets, and will discuss how those can be managed. We will find reason to believe that, in the United States at the present time, the government is not much of a threat to transparency within such markets and that, so far as they are opaque, it is only because private organizations have not made it a priority to make them transparent.

Goldman, Rousseau and von Hayek on the Ideal State (Politics) 
What kind of state would exist in a free and just society, and why? Goldman and Rousseau give similar answers to this question; Hayek gives a very different answer. 
Goldman’s answer is---none. A free and just society is one in which there is no government and therefore no state. Goldman’s position is that government is inherently unjust--a bully that exploits and coerces and does no good: “Just as religion has fettered the human mind, and as property, or the monopoly of things, has subdued and stifled man’s needs, so has the State enslaved his spirit, dictating every phase of conduct.” (Anarchism: What it Really Stands For, p. 4) 
Of course, the question arises: If there is no government (no state), then what will keep order? Goldman’s answer is that the very crime that it is supposedly the state’s purpose to prevent is itself a product of the state: “The most absurd apology for authority and law is that they serve to diminish crime...So long as every institution of today, economic, political, social, and moral, conspires to misdirect human energy into wrong channels; so long as most people are out of place doing the things they hate to do, living a life they loathe to live, crime will be inevitable, and all the laws on the statutes can only increase, but never do away with, crime.” (Anarchism: What it Really Stands For, p. 4) 
The idea here seems to be that human beings are inherently cooperative and, left to their own devices, engage in constructive rather than mutually antagonistic activity. 
Rousseau’s views are similar to Goldman’s. According to Rousseau, people are not naturally antagonistic towards one another: “So long as men remained content with their rustic huts.....they lived free, healthy, honest and happy lives, so long as their nature allowed, and as they continued to enjoy the pleasures of mutual and independent intercourse.” (Discourse on Inequality, p. 9) 
People became antagonistic, according to Rousseau, with the advent of civilization. Civilization made it possible for people to have more than they needed. When this happened, people became wealth-and status-driven and, in order to maximize their wealth and status, took to mutual exploitation: “[F]rom the moment it appeared advantageous to any one man to have enough provisions for two, equality disappeared, property was introduced, work became indispensable, and vast forests became smiling fields, which man had to water with the sweat of his brow, and where slavery and misery were soon seen to germinate and grow up with the crops.” (Discourse on Inequality, p. 8) In other words, human beings are best off living in tribes, tribalism being intermediate between anarchy and civilization. 
There is a serious problem with Rousseau’s position. While there may be some advantages to a tribal (or pre-civilizational) mode of existence, there are some serious disadvantages: lack of choices in regards to profession and lifestyle, lack of medical care, lack of education, confinement to a small geographical area, lack of technology, and the need to work for every single meal that one consumes. Also, it is unlikely that tribal life is as placid and carefree as Rousseau suggests. Tribes attacked one another and were certainly capable of behaving cruelly towards their own members. And if tribal life is so idyllic, why don’t people opt for it today? 
Goldman’s thinking is similarly flawed. Goldman may be right that the state is responsible for some of the crime that it claims to exist to prevent, and she is surely right that states often behave very unjustly. But without the state, contracts wouldn’t be enforced and there would be no economies of scale; and the state is therefore necessary for all of the improvements to life resulting from economies of scale. 
Rightly taking it for granted that the state is necessary for crime-prevention and contract enforcement, Hayek advocates what he refers to as ‘liberalism’, this being the view that the state should protect people but should otherwise not interfere with their lives: “Liberalism is opposed, however, to supplanting competition by inferior methods of guiding economic activity. And it regards competition as superior not only because in most circumstances it is the most efficient method known but because it is the only method which does not require the coercive or arbitrary intervention of authority. It dispenses with the need for ‘conscious social control’ and gives individuals a chance to decide whether the prospects of a particular occupation are sufficient to compensate for the disadvantages connected with it.” (Road to Serfdom, pp. 45-46) 
Is Hayek right that good government is minimal government? There is certainly considerable evidence for that claim: over-regulated economies (e.g. the Soviet Union) tend to fail and minimally regulated ones (e.g. the US in its early days) tend to flourish. More importantly, people should within reason be allowed to live the way they want to live, and overly invasive governments make that impossible. This does not mean that Hayek is unqualifiedly correct, which he probably isn’t, but it suggests that his position is likely to be a respectable first approximation to the truth. 
By contrast, the views of Goldman and Rousseau are not realistic, since, as previously explained, anarchy and tribalism fail to avail people of the freedoms and amenities that they want. 

Take-home Exam on Russian History (Politics/History/Current Events) 
Discuss the sources of the revolution initiated by the Bolsheviks in 1917. Address all relevant political, social, and economic causes. Was the revolution avoidable? Did the tsar's reforms after the 1905 Revolution have any chance at preventing the revolution? Explain. 
The Bolshevik Revolution per se had little popular support. But the Tsar’s government was weak, and towards the end of World War 1, and largely because of it, more or less just collapsed, at which point the Bolshevik’s simply ‘picked up’ power. The relevant question isn’t so much “what caused the Russian Revolution?” as it is “what caused the Tsarist system of government to lose power and credibility?” And the answer to the latter is as follows. Russia was largely poor; it was divided between a small group of very wealthy people and a vastly larger group of very poor people. Also, there was a growing urban intellectual class that (with some legitimacy) saw Tsarism as antiquated and unprogressive. The Tsarist system was proverbially bureaucratic and autocratic (though not, ironically, anywhere nearly as much as the Soviet system that would replace it). Tsar Nicolas’s reforms had a ‘too little, too late’ quality. WWI decapitalized Russia and made it defenseless against the numerically small but determined Bolshevik Party, which, as previously stated, was not at that point met by strong resistance to its (successful) 1917/1918 power-grab. 
Describe and discuss Stalin's Five-Year Plans. What was the goal of these plans? Did Stalin achieve his goals? Were there any negative consequences? Explain and provide examples. 
Stalin, though in some ways a ‘strong leader’, was really just an inept bungler whose methods of statecraft were crude. And his infamous ‘Five Year Plans” were prime examples of this. The Five-Year Plans were attempts to modernize and industrialize Russia, by building damns, factories, and the like, and in this way supposedly modernizing Russia’s economy. It should be pointed out that much of the labor for the Five Year Plans was slave labor, supplied by Stalin’s burgeoning Gulag population, which, though initially created by Stalin’s attempting to hold onto power by incarcerating actual, possible and conceivable political opposition, soon became (to some extent) about providing the labor-force for Stalin’s expensive Industrialization efforts. In any case, the Five Year Plans were successful in some respects; they did turn the Soviet Union into an industrial and military power. But they were grand failures in other respects, in that, even though they made the Soviet Union militarily powerful, they made the Soviet people poor and they also decapitalized the country, which led its collapse 50 years later. 
Describe the distribution of power under the Soviet model. What institutions were most powerful? How did this differ from the way power is supposed to be distributed? Make sure that you provide examples and address the concept of democratic centralism. 
The Communist Party of the Soviet Union would elect a Central Committee, this being a kind of ‘board of directors’ of the Soviet Union. The Central Committee would elect a Politburo, this being comparable, within limits, to the President’s ‘Cabinet.’ The politburo would elect a General Secretary, who was the Supreme Leader of the entire Soviet Union. The outcomes of these ‘elections’ were, at least during the Stalin years and to a lesser extent later, pre-determined by existing political alliances. But that is the way in which those alliances were given official force. The Central Committee and Politburo, under the direction of the General Secretary, would issue edicts/recommendations to local Party Central Committees, which, in their turn, would run their respective geographical territories. 
What is civil society? Do you agree that civil society is necessary for the formation of democracy? Explain. How did the Soviet Union deal with civil society? Has Russia under Putin's leadership been any different? Do you think Russia can become democratic based on its experiences with civil society? 
Civil Society is the totality of institutions and practices and values in a given country that are not either political or strictly commercial in in nature. Thus, family, religion (except for state-imposed religion), private morality, and personal conduct (e.g. one’s conduct outside of political contexts or commercial exchanges) are aspects of civil society. Civil society is not technically necessary for democracy. In a society of economic actors---none of whom had any existence apart from their business interests—there would still be a need for, and a possibility of, democracy. In fact, democratic institutions are especially important when it comes to the protection of economic interests, mainly of one’s right to engage in trade. Under the Soviet Union, civil society was fiercely repressed. Under Putin, civil society is not particularly repressed, contrary to what it is chic to say. Russians under Putin can recreate as they wish and they are free to engage in commerce (even though they often have to deal with organized crime). 
How has Putin managed to consolidate power in Russia? Has he obtained the support of the population and the Duma? If so, how? Evaluate whether there is any chance at democracy developing under Putin. 
Putin began in the KGB. After the Soviet Union fell, he became a protégé of Boris Yeltsin and was appointed Prime minister by Yeltsin in 1999. Putin later pardoned Yeltsin, thereby indebting the latter to him. Putin showed strength and decisiveness throughout his career. He began by imprisoning Oil Oligarch Mikhail Khodorkovsky, which sent a message to the other Oligarchs—who threatened to dilute Putin’s power by having a share of it—to cease to vie for political power. Putin also handled a number of crises, including the Chechen War and also the seizure of a Moscow movie theatre by Chechen rebels—in a decisive manner, thus earning the respect, or at least the fear, of the Russian populace. Putin is in fact popular with the Russian people (even after allowances are made for likely fabrications on the part of a Putin-controlled media) because he is seen as having stabilized the country. And for similar reasons (and with similar qualifications), Putin is popular with the Duma, having recently won a ‘super-majority’ of support therein. 
What are the primary problems with the Russian economy? Provide explanations that engage economic and political factors. Based on this information, what changes need to occur for the Russian economy to improve and stabilize? Are these changes likely? Why or why not? 
Russia has three major economic problems. First, it relies too much on energy-exports. Technology is making many economic sectors less energy-dependent, and Russia’s reliance on a global energy-demand therefore does not bode well for it. 
Also, Russia suffers from political corruption, which negatively affects entrepreneurs and also discourages foreign investment in Russia. 
Finally, Russia, partly for the two reasons just mentioned, is suffering from a ‘brain-drain’: many talented members of intelligence-heavy professions are leaving Russia for greener pastures. 
In order to hedge against these problems, Russia would need to diversify its economy, which will be difficult to do unless political corruption is reigned in. It should be pointed out that there have been relatively non-corrupt autocracies and there have been, and are, highly corrupt democracies. So it isn’t necessarily the case that the demise of Putin-style autocracy is a sine qua non for the improvement of Russia’s economy. (Putinites would argue that the opposite is the case.) In any case, it doesn’t seem particularly likely that the needed changes will happen, mainly because no one—not Putin, not his opponents—seems to have any clear ideas as to how to make it happen. Nor are there are any obvious ideas as to how to do this. Nor, indeed, does there seem to be the requisite political will. My personal feeling is that there were incentive-programs for small businesses in Russia—programs that incentivized the Russian equivalent of Facebook (which, incidentally, does exist)---this might lead to the needed sort of economic diversification. But any views on these matters are necessarily speculative at this point. 
Did 
the 
founding 
fathers 
support 
or 
oppose 
democracy? 
Was 
the 
Constitution 
a 
democratic 
document 
or 
an 
anti-democratic 
document? 
(History/Politics) 
The 
US 
Constitution 
is 
at 
its 
core 
a 
democratic 
document, 
and 
since 
the 
US 
Constitution 
is 
the 
embodiment 
of 
the 
political 
views 
of 
its 
framers, 
these 
being 
the 
Founding 
Fathers, 
it 
follows 
that 
the 
Founding 
Fathers 
supported 
democracy. 
In 
this 
paper, 
it 
will 
be 
shown, 
largely 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
direct 
quotes 
from 
the 
original 
(1789) 
US 
Constitution, 
that 
the 
latter 
is 
a 
largely 
if 
not 
a 
completely 
democratic 
document. 
Then 
we 
will 
show 
that 
some 
apparently 
undemocratic 
provisions 
of 
the 
Constitution 
are 
in 
fact 
democratic. 
Finally, 
we 
will 
discuss 
the 
fact 
that 
during 
the 
early 
years 
of 
the 
republic, 
only 
propertied 
white 
males 
could 
vote, 
and 
we 
will 
show 
that 
this 
obviously 
undemocratic 
practice 
does 
not 
prove 
the 
Constitution 
or 
the 
Founding 
Fathers 
to 
be 
undemocratic. 
A 
democracy 
is 
a 
society 
in 
which 
the 
people 
elect 
their 
officials. 
In 
the 
system 
of 
government 
described 
in 
the 
1789-Constitution, 
key 
government 
officials 
are 
elected 
and 
those 
that 
are 
not 
elected 
are 
appointed 
by 
those 
who 
are. 
First 
of 
all, 
provisions 
are 
made 
for 
the 
election 
of 
representatives, 
Senators 
and 
a 
chief 
executive 
(or 
President). 
In 
Article 
I, 
Section 
2 
of 
the 
1789-Constitution 
says: 
“The 
House 
of 
Representatives 
shall 
be 
composed 
of 
Members 
chosen 
every 
second 
Year 
by 
the 
People 
of 
the 
several 
States, 
and 
the 
Electors 
in 
each 
State 
shall 
have 
the 
Qualifications 
requisite 
for 
Electors 
of 
the 
most 
numerous 
Branch 
of 
the 
State 
Legislature….” 
This 
quote 
makes 
it 
that 
“the 
People”, 
this 
being 
some 
subset 
of 
the 
general 
population, 
choose 
who 
their 
governmental 
representatives 
will 
be, 
at 
least 
within 
limits. 
Later 
in 
Article 
I, 
Section 
2 
it 
is 
written 
that: 
“Representatives 
and 
direct 
Taxes 
shall 
be 
apportioned 
among 
the 
several 
States 
which 
may 
be 
included 
within 
this 
Union, 
according 
to 
their 
respective 
Numbers, 
which 
shall 
be 
determined 
by 
adding 
to 
the 
whole 
Number 
of 
free 
Persons, 
including 
those 
bound 
to 
Service 
for 
a 
Term 
of 
Years… 
When 
vacancies 
happen 
in 
the 
Representation 
from 
any 
State, 
the 
Executive 
Authority 
thereof 
shall 
issue 
Writs 
of 
Election 
to 
fill 
such 
Vacancies.” 
The first sentence of this quotations indicates that the degree of representation given to a state is a function of the number of people in that state, and the second sentence indicates that it is the citizens of that state who decide who will represent them. 
The last sentence of Article I, Section 2 states that: “The House of Representatives…shall have the sole Power of Impeachment.” In other words, these representatives have the power to remove the President from Office (or at least to initiate proceedings to the effect), this being a way of keeping the President under the control of elected officials. 
The 1789-Constitution intends for the House of Representatives to be unqualifiedly democratic, since it provides both that the people in a given state elect their representatives and also that the number of representatives per state is proportional to that state’s population. 
In Section III of the 1789-Constitution, it is written that: “The Senate of the United States shall be composed of two Senators from each State, chosen 
by 
the 
Legislature 
thereof, for six Years; and each Senator shall have one Vote.” Thus, the 1789-Constitution intends for the Senate to be a qualifiedly democratic institution, since although it provides that the people of a given state shall elect their own Senators, it does not provide that the number of Senators per state is proportional to that state’s population. 
In Article II, Section 1, it is written that: “The executive Power shall be vested in a President of the United States of America. He shall hold his Office during the Term of four Years, and, together with the Vice President, chosen for the same Term, be elected, as follows.” Each state has a number of ‘electors.’ The number of electors per state is proportional to the population of that state. The electors for a given state unanimously vote for the Presidential candidate voted for by a majority of the people in that state. So it is these electors who directly vote for President; the people only indirectly do so. But these electors always vote in line with the votes cast by the majority of their constituents. 
Basically, a state’s citizens do not directly vote for President. Rather, their state directly votes for the President, and they vote for who their state will vote for. 
This system makes it possible for a Presidential candidate who loses the popular vote to win the election, and some might regard this system as undemocratic or only imperfectly democratic. But that is not the only possible view of the matter. The electoral system gives the vote to states, and it therefore preserves all of the democratic functions carried out by states. 
There is an important respect in which the United States in 1789 was not democratic. Only white, male land-owners could vote. Women could not. Native Americans could not. African-Americans could not. And, significantly, unpropertied while males could not. (Be it noted that the 1789­Constitution does not itself restrict the vote to propertied while males. The Constitutions was silent on the matter, it being the culture at large that limited suffrage in this way.) 
Why was this? Although an element of racism, sexism and snobbism may have played a role, this system was primarily about restricting suffrage to people who had an economic stake in the welfare of the country. Obviously, slaves did not want the country to be an economic success, since it was denying them their freedom, and if given the right to vote, they might well have voted for its destruction. As for Native Americans, even though the United States did not enslave them and even though they had commercial ties to it, their welfare did not depend on its welfare, and for this reason they could not be counted to vote for its welfare. As for women in this time period, they were not engaged in commerce and, however positive their intentions towards the United States, might have been, they could not be counted upon to vote in an informed manner. The same is true of white males who had no property: however positive their intentions might have been, they could not be counted upon to vote in an informed manner. 
Finally, the 1789-Constitution did not prevent anyone from voting, and it has proved easy to amend so as to grant suffrage to people who did not previously have it. This was by design. The Founding Fathers designed a system that had a fundamentally democratic structure. That system has not always been implemented in a maximally democratic way, but that is only because it has not always been implemented as it was meant to be. 
In conclusion, the 1789 US-Constitution describes a highly democratic system of government. Although it contains some apparently undemocratic provisions, these provisions prove upon analysis not to be undemocratic or to be only qualifiedly so. As for the fact that only white propertied males could vote during the early years of this country’s existence, that had no basis in the Constitution, which did not prevent anyone from voting, and was instead a consequence of the way in which the Constitution was implemented. 
The Two Faces of China’s Negative Soft Power: China’s Belt and Road Initiative as a Case of Negative Soft Power (Politics) 
There are two kinds of power that a nation can have: hard power and soft power. Hard power is the ability to coerce using military or economic means. Soft power is about influencing, as opposed to coercing. A nation’s soft power lies in the appeal of its culture, the sentiments that others have about its practices, and its general countenance. 
Just as there are two kinds of power, so there are two kinds of soft power: positive and negative. Positive soft power is the power to influence other nations to engage in a constructive or affirmative endeavor, e.g. to build a dam. Negative soft power is the power to influence other nations to engage in a negative or privative endeavor, e.g. to refuse to transact with some nation or to withdraw support from 
There are two kinds of negative soft power: the deliberate kind and the inadvertent kind. In other words, negative soft power can sometimes, be used deliberately, but it can also just ‘happen’, without its being intended by the nation in question. An example of deliberate negative soft power would be the U.S.’s boycotting the Soviet Olympics in 1980: this was not a case of the US’s coercing anybody, militarily or otherwise. But it sent a message to other nations that the United States took a dim view of the Soviet Union, which message in turn reinforced those other nations’ existing misgiving about the Soviet Union. 
A case of inadvertent negative soft power would be the detestation for the US in the Islamic world that have resulted from the US’s attempts to grace the Islamic world with its pop culture, which attempts were seen in the Islamic world not as acts of kindness but as acts of cultural imperialism and of religious intolerance. 
In this study, I am going to show that China, through its Belts and Roads Initiate (BRI), is exercising a number of different forms of soft power: positive, deliberate negative, and inadvertent negative. On the one hand, as we will see, some see this initiative as a sincere attempt on China’s part to build economic (and literal) bridges between nations: so to this extent, it is a case of positive soft power. On the other hand, some see it as attempts on China’s part to infiltrate other nations: so to this extent it is inadvertent negative soft power. Finally, China has deliberately represented BRI as being a conscious attempt on China’s part to rally Asian and African nations against the US and other Western powers: so to this extent, it is deliberate negative soft power. 
Interest Groups and Political Parties (Politics/Current Events) 
Describe the evolution of political parties in the United States. 
The United States is technically a multi-party system, but in practice, it is and always has been a two-party system. When the United States was founded, there were no political parties, in the United States or anywhere else. The Founding Fathers did not see American as being party-based, and the Constitution does not even refer to political parties. But political parties quickly came into existence, quickly stabilizing into the two-party configuration that we still have today. The first two parties were the Federalist Party and the Democratic-Republican Party. The Federalist party concerned with the country’s macro-economic well-being, and the Democratic-Republican Party was concerned more with the individual’s welfare and quality of life. The Federalist party corresponded (with some qualifications) to today’s Republican Party, and the Democratic-Republican Party corresponded to today’s Democratic Party. (The qualifications relate to the fact that the Federalist Party was focused mainly on economic matters and, unlike today’s Republican Party, had little or no interest, or even negative interest, in ‘religious values’ and ‘family values’ and the like.) 
In fact, for the entirety of the USA’s existence, politics has been dominated by two parties, one corresponding to today’s Democratic Party, the other corresponding to today’s Republican Party. 
The actual Democratic Party came into existence in the 1830s and was founded by Andrew Jackson, as an attempt to transfer wealth away from plutocrats and into the hands of the masses, and the actual Republican Party was founded in 1854, largely as an attempt to end slavery but also as an attempt centralize power and unify the country under sustainable macro­economic policies. 
As the just-stated facts make clear, there have been pronounced shifts within and between the parties. Someone with Andrew Jackson’s values would now being seen as the antithesis of a democratic; in fact, Jackson’s positions were strongly in line with those of current President Donald Trump. (Trump, though wealthy and though in some ways a supporter of the wealthy, is also an arch-populist, just as Jackson was.) And Lincoln, though a Republican, probably had values at least in some ways more in line with those of contemporary democrats. (That said, Lincoln was in other respects quite representative of modern Republicanism, mainly in that, despite his progressive views on race-relations, he represented the interests of the Corporatistic North over the more agrarian and in some ways pre-capitalist South.) 
But even more striking than what has changed the American Party Politics over the last 250 years is what hasn’t changed. During any given election cycle, there is one party that focuses on macro-economic policy, and another that focuses on ‘the little guy’; there is one party that wants to centralize power and another that wants to decentralize it; there is one party that wants to cleave to the Constitution, and another that regards the Constitution as being contraindicated. In some contexts, in some respects, the Republican Party represents, or is seen as representing, the interests of small businesses; in other words, the Democratic Party has that role. At certain junctures, the Republican Party is relatively isolationist and relatively uninclined to go to war, and the Democratic party, being less isolationist, is relatively inclined to do so; and at the other junctures, the roles are reversed. 
Despite the differences between the two parties, there was a kind of equilibrium or stalemate between the Republicans and the Democrats. They agreed to disagree on certain issues and to agree on others, and their back­and-forth had a rather automated, kabuki-like quality to it. But with Donald Trump that changed (whether for better or worse is obviously a matter of debate). What is distinctive about Trump is that he came into politics from the outside. He is not a politician; he is a businessman who went into politics. And this is reflected in his views, which are in some ways more disruptive to Republican orthodoxies than to Democratic orthodoxies. (Trump actually conforms quite well to Democratic presumptions about the Republican Party, and for that same reason Trump does not conform well to Republican efforts to mollify the Democratic Party.) In any case, though, American party politics was quick to come into existence and assume a form barely distinguishable from its contemporary form. Be it noted that, in his book Propaganda, Edmund Bernays, the father of modern advertising, claimed that, for reasons of psychology, people need political parties and, at the same time, that they cannot have more than two. And it would seem that Bernays was right. 
France 
France has a semipresidential system. What exactly does this mean? Specifically, address the roles of the executive and legislative branches in this system. Would you recommend the semipresidential system for other countries? Explain why or why not 
Much like Russia, France has a semi-presidential system of government, meaning that there is a prime-minister as well as President, both of whom are answerable to the nation’s legislature. A semi-presidential system differs from a parliamentary republic in that in the case of a parliamentary republic the president is a mere figure head, whereas in the case of a semi-presidential republic has real power. And semi-presidential system differs from a presidential system in that, whereas in a presidential system the cabinet is appointed by the president and can also be dismissed by him, in a semi-presidential system the cabinet is appointed by the legislature and cannot be unilaterally dismissed by the president. In France, the President names the Prime Minister. 
France’s government, like the USA’s, is divided into executive, legislative, and judicial branches. And the judicial branch, like the USA’s, is divided into a National Assembly, corresponding to the US House of Representative, and a Senate, corresponding to the US senate. 
The legislative process in France, though basically similar to its American counterpart. In the United States, the Supreme court does not determine the constitutional of a prospective law; it merely determines the constitutionality of pre-existing laws, and then only under when their constitutionality is made salient by criminal or civil litigation. But in France, in order for a bill to become law, its constitutionality must be determined in advance (by the Constitutional Adviser) and so must its consistency with existing National and European laws (by the Conseil d’état). But the largest difference between the American and the French system is that, whereas the American system is presidential, the French system is semi-presidential, so that the French system has a ‘dual executive’ branch. 
The advantage of a semi-presidential over a presidential system is that it tends to prevent political gridlock, mainly by making it impossible for the President to exercise power without the support of both the Prime Minister and the legislature. So the legislature and the executive have trouble drifting far enough apart from each other for there to be insuperable disagreements between them. At the same time, this is also, at least in some contexts, a disadvantage, since it prevents the President taking the bold initiatives that may be necessary to deal with crises. 
My personal view is that, while there should be checks and balances and that Presidential authority must be restricted, the presidential has more than enough checks and balances on the President’s power. By the same token, the semi-presidential arraignment over-limits the President and (unless, as is the case in Russia, which is really an autocracy and only formally a semi-presidential system) and prevents him from taking bold and (in some contexts) necessary action. 
Russia 
How has geography influenced Russian political development over the centuries? 
The Russian Empire was physically extremely large. Also, much of it, being Arctic or Sub-arctic, is very cold. Further, even though it has the largest coastline of any country, that coastline is on the Arctic Ocean and is therefore of limited use for either commerce or for Russia’s self-management. Russia’s size, though in some respects a strength, left it exposed to invaders and made it difficult to protect, this being why, over the centuries, it sustained a series of nasty blows at the hands of numerous Central Asian and East Asian countries. 
Also, whereas the United States is replete with high-quality arable land, the land in Russia is relatively non-arable and the climate is inhospitable to many forms of agriculture. Russia’s geographical isolation, coupled with enormous size and the difficulties involved in Russia-internal (and therefore largely land-based) travel, had the result that Russia as a whole was in some ways more backwards than other European nations, being slower than most other European nations (including some Eastern European nations) to receive the benefits of political enlightenment. Also, the difficulties involved in traveling from Russia’s urban centers (e.g. St. Petersburg and Moscow)—all of which are located in the far West of the country and in which Russia’s aristocracy as well as its intelligence tended to reside---created an unusually large cleavage in outlook and in standard of living between the elite and the non-elite of Russia, which helped make Russia vulnerable to the 1917 Revolution. 
Additionally, Russia, being large and being difficult to travel within, was (and is) difficult to govern and to control, which is probably part of what made Russia slow to adopt democratic rule and which, in turn, was part of what it made relatively easy for the Bolsheviks to take over. To this day, Russia is ruled in a semi-autocratic manner, and its lack of arable manner has resulted in an economy that over-relies in petrol exports. 
Further, its harsh climate and vast internal distances make it difficult (but not impossible) to develop medium sized business. 
What led to the collapse of the Soviet Union? (Politics/History/Current 
Events) 
The proximal cause of the collapse of the Soviet Union was the lowering of gas prices that occurred in the late 80s. Russia, being a defunct, bureaucratic communist nation had very little economy, and its revenue came almost entirely from petroleum exports. So when petroleum prices fell in the late 80s, Russia was completely decapitalized, and its economy literally ground to a halt: trains containing food didn’t even leave the station, since worker’s weren’t being paid. 
The fall in prices was deliberate, being the workmanship of strongly anti-Soviet US President Ronald Reagan, who had already done much to deplete Soviet coffers by engaging in it a Nuclear Arms Race. (It was unclear whether it was Reagan’s intention in engaging in the Arms Race to decapitalize the Soviet Union. Some believe Reagan’s motivations were of a knee-jerk anti-Communist kind. Others believe that, indeed, Reagan was deliberately driving the Soviet Union to poverty and subsequent collapse.) 
Because Russia had no legitimate free market on the basis of which to generate funds, a return to Communism was not an option. At the same time, Russia had a vast grey-market economy and—as the next few years would make clear—no shortage of entrepreneurial talent. So (after a years of economic catastrophe, courtesy of Jeffrey Sachs and other Western Economic advisers) the Iron Hand of State-planning quickly made way for the Invisible Hand of the free market. 
What kind of political system does Russia have now? Is it effective? Legitimate? Explain. 
Russia is a Constitutional (constitution-based) Federal, Semi-presidential Republic. It is Federal in that, like the United States, it consists of a plurality of states that have their own individual governments but that are in their managed by a single (Federal) government. It is semi-Presidential, as opposed to presidential, in that, although the President is the de jure head of government, he shares power with a Prime Minister. (In actuality, the Prime Minister, Vladimir Putin, has more power than the President, Dmitry Medvedev.) 
In practice, Russia is a semi-autocratic state with democratic elements —but more of an autocracy than a democracy. This configuration is legitimate in the Russian people, though far from happy with every aspect of the job Putin is doing, seem to prefer strong, centralized governance, as opposed to decentralized, democratic governance. 
So relative to the awesome tasks faced by any possible government of Russia, the semi-autocratic, semi-democratic approach seems indicated, in that it combines the decisiveness and crisis-reading of a strong, autocratic government with the responsiveness to the people’s needs of a democratically elected government. 
Is it possible for Russia to break its dependence on strong leaders? If so, how will it get rid of Putin? And should it? 
While it is an article of faith in the West that ‘democracies’ are good and ‘autocracies’ are bad, autocracies can sometimes function better. An example is China: China’s massive economic growth and economic liberalization over the last 30 years were consequences of sapient, but strong centralized management. Russia’s economic growth during the last few decades, though not quite as good as China’s, has still been considerable. And this seems, at least in part, to have been made possible by the political stability created by Putin’s rather autocratic ruling style. 
Also, Russia, consisting as it does of a plurality of oftentimes mutually belligerent nationalities, might be better off, at least for the time being, having decisive strong rulers, instead of politically driven weak ones. Be it noted that, whereas only 1/5th of Russians express support for the democratic leaders they have had (basically Gorbachev and Yeltsin), ½ of Russians still have positive feelings about Brezhnev and Stalin![50] 

So while Putin’s future is unclear and while there is legitimate disagreement as to his merits, it seems unlikely that Western style democracy in Russia is particularly likely or desirable any time soon. 
Plato’s Republic as Pol Potist Bureaucracy (Politics/Philosophy) 
For Plato, justice is identical with people staying in their ‘rightful place.’ (“[T]o do one’s own business, in some shape or other, is justice.” [433 
b] ) Plato has a very narrow and hierarchical conception of what a person’s ‘rightful place’ is. He believes that a person’s social position should be determined by his ‘nature.’ He further believes that one’s ‘nature’ cannot in any relevant respects change. (“You are, all of you in this community, brothers. But when god fashioned you, he added gold in the composition of those of you who are qualified to be Rulers (which is why your prestige is greatest); he put silver in the Auxiliaries, and iron and bronze in the farmers and other workers.” [412 e] ) 
A corollary is that, for Plato, social strata should be rigidly defined, it being necessary that the barriers between them be virtually impenetrable. (““If they [the non-ruling classes] follow these rules, they will be safe themselves and the saviors of the city; but whenever they come to possess lands, and houses, and money of their own, they will be householders and cultivators instead of guardians, and will become hostile masters of their fellow-citizens rather than their allies.” [416 b] ) 
Further, Plato doesn’t acknowledge the legitimacy of a person’s private life. For him, the entirety of a person’s life should be subordinate to the interests of the state. Marriages should be arranged. (“We must arrange for marriage, and make it as sacred an affair as we can. And a sacred marriage is one that produces the most beneficial results.” [458 e]) And people must never marry or reproduce with people outside of their social classes. (“Guardians must ‘mate the best of our men with the best of our women as often as possible, and the inferior men with the inferior women as seldom as possible, and bring up only the offspring of the best.’” [459 e] ) 
In Plato’s ‘ideal republic’, life is so micromanaged by the state that there is practically no such thing as private life. Every aspect of each citizen’s life must be in alignment with the interests of the state, and existence is completely collectivized. (“Our men and women Guardians should be forbidden by law to live together in separate households, and all the women should be common to all the men; similarly, children should be held in common, and no parent should know its child, or child its parent.” See also: “They shall have no private property beyond the barest essentials. Second, none of them shall possess a dwelling-house or storehouse to which all have not the right of entry. Next, their food shall be provided by the other citizens as an agreed wage for the duties they perform…” [416 d] ) 
Plato correctly states that such a rigidly hierarchical social arrangement cannot be 
sustained unless the masses are kept ignorant and constantly lied to. (“And no one but the rulers must know what is happening, if we are to avoid dissension in our Guardian herd.” [459 a] ) 
So with respect to the question “To what extent does justice (for Plato) depend upon the state propagation of falsehoods?”, the answer is: Completely. For Plato, justice depends on the state’s constantly lying to its own citizenry. Not only that. It depends on its keeping them ignorant and also in the resulting gaps in its citizens’ knowledge with carefully constructed propaganda. Plato himself says this. Plato clearly states that the state must constantly subject its own citizenry to propaganda (pro-state lies) if the state is to remain intact and unified, it being Plato’s contention that nothing is more important than unity and intactness of the state. (“Is there anything worse for a state to be split and fragmented, or anything better than cohesion and unity?” [462 b] ) 
As for the question “Insofar as deception is necessary to maintain justice, can Plato’s distinction between benign or beneficial lies and improper or dangerous ones be sustained?”, the answer is: No--not at all. Of course, the answer to this question is tautologously ‘yes’ if one accepts Plato’s conception of ‘justice.’ But according to Plato’s conception of justice, nobody is entitled to any freedoms of any kind. Since there is no independent reason to accept such a conception of justice, and every reason to reject, the actual answer to the above question is indeed: No. The lies that, in Plato’s view, the state should require its citizens to accept are ones that the state must tell if it is to continue to enforce Plato’s conception of justice, According to that conception of justice, people do not have the right to listen to the kind of music they enjoy or marry the people they love or indeed do much of anything that they personally would like to do. Since such a restrictive conception of justice is false and evil, any lies that are told to uphold it are ipso facto evil, and there is therefore to principled distinction between good and evil lies in relation to such a conception.
