Political Parties in the United States: A Brief History 
Describe the evolution of political parties in the United States. 
The United States is technically a multi-party system, but in practice, it is and always has been a two-party system. When the United States was founded, there were no political parties, in the United States or anywhere else. The Founding Fathers did not see American as being party-based, and the Constitution does not even refer to political parties. But political parties quickly came into existence, quickly stabilizing into the two-party configuration that we still have today. The first two parties were the Federalist Party and the Democratic-Republican Party. The Federalist party concerned with the country’s macro-economic well-being, and the Democratic-Republican Party was concerned more with the individual’s welfare and quality of life. The Federalist party corresponded (with some qualifications) to today’s Republican Party, and the Democratic-Republican Party corresponded to today’s Democratic Party. (The qualifications relate to the fact that the Federalist Party was focused mainly on economic matters and, unlike today’s Republican Party, had little or no interest, or even negative interest, in ‘religious values’ and ‘family values’ and the like.) 
In fact, for the entirety of the USA’s existence, politics has been dominated by two parties, one corresponding to today’s Democratic Party, the other corresponding to today’s Republican Party. 
The actual Democratic Party came into existence in the 1830s and was founded by Andrew Jackson, as an attempt to transfer wealth away from plutocrats and into the hands of the masses, and the actual Republican Party was founded in 1854, largely as an attempt to end slavery but also as an attempt centralize power and unify the country under sustainable macro­economic policies. 
As the just-stated facts make clear, there have been pronounced shifts within and between the parties. Someone with Andrew Jackson’s values would now being seen as the antithesis of a democratic; in fact, Jackson’s positions were strongly in line with those of current President Donald Trump. (Trump, though wealthy and though in some ways a supporter of the wealthy, is also an arch-populist, just as Jackson was.) And Lincoln, though a Republican, probably had values at least in some ways more in line with those of contemporary democrats. (That said, Lincoln was in other respects quite representative of modern Republicanism, mainly in that, despite his progressive views on race-relations, he represented the interests of the Corporatistic North over the more agrarian and in some ways pre-capitalist South.) 
But even more striking than what has changed the American Party Politics over the last 250 years is what hasn’t changed. During any given election cycle, there is one party that focuses on macro-economic policy, and another that focuses on ‘the little guy’; there is one party that wants to centralize power and another that wants to decentralize it; there is one party that wants to cleave to the Constitution, and another that regards the Constitution as being contraindicated. In some contexts, in some respects, the Republican Party represents, or is seen as representing, the interests of small businesses; in other words, the Democratic Party has that role. At certain junctures, the Republican Party is relatively isolationist and relatively uninclined to go to war, and the Democratic party, being less isolationist, is relatively inclined to do so; and at the other junctures, the roles are reversed. 
Despite the differences between the two parties, there was a kind of equilibrium or stalemate between the Republicans and the Democrats. They agreed to disagree on certain issues and to agree on others, and their back­and-forth had a rather automated, kabuki-like quality to it. But with Donald Trump that changed (whether for better or worse is obviously a matter of debate). What is distinctive about Trump is that he came into politics from the outside. He is not a politician; he is a businessman who went into politics. And this is reflected in his views, which are in some ways more disruptive to Republican orthodoxies than to Democratic orthodoxies. (Trump actually conforms quite well to Democratic presumptions about the Republican Party, and for that same reason Trump does not conform well to Republican efforts to mollify the Democratic Party.) In any case, though, American party politics was quick to come into existence and assume a form barely distinguishable from its contemporary form. Be it noted that, in his book Propaganda, Edmund Bernays, the father of modern advertising, claimed that, for reasons of psychology, people need political parties and, at the same time, that they cannot have more than two. And it would seem that Bernays was right. 
France’s Political System 
France has a semipresidential system. What exactly does this mean? Specifically, address the roles of the executive and legislative branches in this system. Would you recommend the semipresidential system for other countries? Explain why or why not 
Much like Russia, France has a semi-presidential system of government, meaning that there is a prime-minister as well as President, both of whom are answerable to the nation’s legislature. A semi-presidential system differs from a parliamentary republic in that in the case of a parliamentary republic the president is a mere figure head, whereas in the case of a semi-presidential republic has real power. And semi-presidential system differs from a presidential system in that, whereas in a presidential system the cabinet is appointed by the president and can also be dismissed by him, in a semi-presidential system the cabinet is appointed by the legislature and cannot be unilaterally dismissed by the president. In France, the President names the Prime Minister. 
France’s government, like the USA’s, is divided into executive, legislative, and judicial branches. And the judicial branch, like the USA’s, is divided into a National Assembly, corresponding to the US House of Representative, and a Senate, corresponding to the US senate. 
The legislative process in France, though basically similar to its American counterpart. In the United States, the Supreme court does not determine the constitutional of a prospective law; it merely determines the constitutionality of pre-existing laws, and then only under when their constitutionality is made salient by criminal or civil litigation. But in France, in order for a bill to become law, its constitutionality must be determined in advance (by the Constitutional Adviser) and so must its consistency with existing National and European laws (by the Conseil d’état). So the legislature and the executive have trouble drifting far enough apart from each other for there to be insuperable disagreements between them. At the same time, this is also, at least in some contexts, a disadvantage, since it prevents the President taking the bold initiatives that may be necessary to deal with crises. 
My personal view is that, while there should be checks and balances and that Presidential authority must be restricted, the presidential has more than enough checks and balances on the President’s power. By the same token, the semi-presidential arraignment over-limits the President and (unless, as is the case in Russia, which is really an autocracy and only formally a semi-presidential system) and prevents him from taking bold and (in some contexts) necessary action. 
Russia’s Geography in Relation to its Political Development 
How has geography influenced Russian political development over the centuries? 
The Russian Empire was physically extremely large. Also, much of it, being Arctic or Sub-arctic, is very cold. Further, even though it has the largest coastline of any country, that coastline is on the Arctic Ocean and is therefore of limited use for either commerce or for Russia’s self-management. Russia’s size, though in some respects a strength, left it exposed to invaders and made it difficult to protect, this being why, over the centuries, it sustained a series of nasty blows at the hands of numerous Central Asian and East Asian countries. 
Also, whereas the United States is replete with high-quality arable land, the land in Russia is relatively non-arable and the climate is inhospitable to many forms of agriculture. Russia’s geographical isolation, coupled with enormous size and the difficulties involved in Russia-internal (and therefore largely land-based) travel, had the result that Russia as a whole was in some ways more backwards than other European nations, being slower than most other European nations (including some Eastern European nations) to receive the benefits of political enlightenment. Also, the difficulties involved in traveling from Russia’s urban centers (e.g. St. Petersburg and Moscow)—all of which are located in the far West of the country and in which Russia’s aristocracy as well as its intelligence tended to reside---created an unusually large cleavage in outlook and in standard of living between the elite and the non-elite of Russia, which helped make Russia vulnerable to the 1917 Revolution. 
Additionally, Russia, being large and being difficult to travel within, was (and is) difficult to govern and to control, which is probably part of what made Russia slow to adopt democratic rule and which, in turn, was part of what it made relatively easy for the Bolsheviks to take over. To this day, Russia is ruled in a semi-autocratic manner, and its lack of arable manner has resulted in an economy that over-relies in petrol exports. 
Further, its harsh climate and vast internal distances make it difficult (but not impossible) to develop medium sized business. 
The Collapse of the Soviet Union? 
What led to the collapse of the USSR? 
The proximal cause of the collapse of the Soviet Union was the lowering of gas prices that occurred in the late 80s. Russia, being a defunct, bureaucratic communist nation had very little economy, and its revenue came almost entirely from petroleum exports. So when petroleum prices fell in the late 80s, Russia was completely decapitalized, and its economy literally ground to a halt: trains containing food didn’t even leave the station, since worker’s weren’t being paid. 
The fall in prices was deliberate, being the workmanship of strongly anti-Soviet US President Ronald Reagan, who had already done much to deplete Soviet coffers by engaging in it a Nuclear Arms Race. (It was unclear whether it was Reagan’s intention in engaging in the Arms Race to decapitalize the Soviet Union. Some believe Reagan’s motivations were of a knee-jerk anti-Communist kind. Others believe that, indeed, Reagan was deliberately driving the Soviet Union to poverty and subsequent collapse.) 
Because Russia had no legitimate free market on the basis of which to generate funds, a return to Communism was not an option. At the same time, Russia had a vast grey-market economy and—as the next few years would make clear—no shortage of entrepreneurial talent. So (after a years of economic catastrophe, courtesy of Jeffrey Sachs and other Western Economic advisers) the Iron Hand of State-planning quickly made way for the Invisible Hand of the free market. 
Russia’s Current Political System 
What kind of political system does Russia have now? Is it effective? Legitimate? Explain. 
Russia is a Constitutional (constitution-based) Federal, Semi-presidential Republic. It is Federal in that, like the United States, it consists of a plurality of states that have their own individual governments but that are in their managed by a single (Federal) government. It is semi-Presidential, as opposed to presidential, in that, although the President is the de jure head of government, he shares power with a Prime Minister. (In actuality, the Prime Minister, Vladimir Putin, has more power than the President, Dmitry Medvedev.) 
In practice, Russia is a semi-autocratic state with democratic elements —but more of an autocracy than a democracy. This configuration is legitimate in the Russian people, though far from happy with every aspect of the job Putin is doing, seem to prefer strong, centralized governance, as opposed to decentralized, democratic governance. 
So relative to the awesome tasks faced by any possible government of Russia, the semi-autocratic, semi-democratic approach seems indicated, in that it combines the decisiveness and crisis-reading of a strong, autocratic government with the responsiveness to the people’s needs of a democratically elected government. 
Russia’s Dependence on Strong Leaders 
Is it possible for Russia to break its dependence on strong leaders? If so, how will it get rid of Putin? And should it? 
While it is an article of faith in the West that ‘democracies’ are good and ‘autocracies’ are bad, autocracies can sometimes function better. An example is China: China’s massive economic growth and economic liberalization over the last 30 years were consequences of sapient, but strong centralized management. Russia’s economic growth during the last few decades, though not quite as good as China’s, has still been considerable. And this seems, at least in part, to have been made possible by the political stability created by Putin’s rather autocratic ruling style. 
Also, Russia, consisting as it does of a plurality of oftentimes mutually belligerent nationalities, might be better off, at least for the time being, having decisive strong rulers, instead of politically driven weak ones. Be it noted that, whereas only 1/5th of Russians express support for the democratic leaders they have had (basically Gorbachev and Yeltsin), ½ of Russians still have positive feelings about Brezhnev and Stalin![51] 

So while Putin’s future is unclear and while there is legitimate disagreement as to his merits, it seems unlikely that Western style democracy in Russia is particularly likely or desirable any time soon. 
Summary of Politics of Economic Growth in the States (Economics) 
In the 1980s, rates of economic growth among the various different states of India were relatively uniform. But in the 1990s, this changes. Whereas some states (e.g. Bihar, Orissa, UP, Punjab, Rajasthan) stagnated economically or even declined, rates of economic growth increased in other states (e.g. Gujarat, Kerala, West Bengal). 
How are these growth differentials to be explained? Let us start by identifying two plausible but false answers to this question. 
First liberalization in the states in question attracted investment-capital, which led to growth. Though plausible, this analysis is false, as Table 1A shows there to be no correlation between growth-rates and state-income during the period in question. 
Second, the states that had high rates of growth during the 1990s were those that had rates of growth during 1980s, and the high-rates of growth in the states in question were therefore simply the result of economic inertia. But this is simply false, as Table 1D shows. 
The actual answer is this: In each case, economic growth was a combination both of investment-capital along with pre-existing infrastructure; in other words, if a given Indian state exhibited pronounced economic growth during the 1990s, it was because of a combination of a high-degree of infrastructure on its part and a large amount of outside investment. 
Thus, states that were given a large amount of capital but had poor infrastructure did not appreciably grown, and neither did states that had good infrastructure were given little investment capital. Only those states grew that satisfied both conditions. And not only is this a priori reasonable; it is the only explanation not ruled out by the data (see Tables 1A-1D). 
Should the US have entered WWI? (History) 
The U.S. had two prima facie reasons for entering WWI on behalf of the Allies. First, the American economy stood to gain much if America fought in the War, specifically if it fought on behalf of the Allies. Second, the 
U.S. had reason to regard Germany as a military threat. In this context, it is necessary to distinguish reasons that the US had to go to war from the actual causes of its going to war, and it is necessary to distinguish each from what the US alleged to be the reasons for its going to war. We will see that although the US had legitimate economic and (to a lesser extent) military reasons for going to war, these probably were not the actual causes of its going to War. We will also see that what the US alleged to be its reasons for going to war were probably identical neither with the legitimate reasons it had for going to war nor with the actual causes of its doing so. 
WWI began in 1914 when a Serbian nationalist assassinated the Archduke Ferdinand. This led to a series of territory-disputes among the nations of Europe, which turned to into a full-blown international war. The two sides of this war were the Allies and the Central Powers. The Allies consisted of France, Britain, Russia, Japan and Italy. The Central Powers consisted Germany, Austria-Hungary, Turkey and Bulgaria. 
For the first 3 years of the War, the US was not involved. President Wilson urged neutrality and issued a ‘neutrality-proclamation.’ (Wilson had been re-elected on an anti-war platform with the slogan ‘he kept us out of war.’) Most Americans supported him in this. 
Both sides wanted the US to join them. The British had cultural and linguistic ties to the United States and al allowing them to drench the US with ant time, German Americans were one of th Kaiser Wilhelm II, the leader of German

part on Standardized Test Scores (Public Policy) 
In her article “Is it finally time to get rid of the SAT and ACT College Admissions Tests”, Valerie Strauss claims that by basing admissions-decisions on SAT and ACT scores, colleges and universities are somehow perpetuating some kind of systemic bias against those who are underprivileged. To this end, Strauss cites the fact that people from higher socioeconomic strata outperform those from the lower socioeconomic strata on SAT’s and ACT’s, the implication being that those tests ‘rig’ college admissions in favor of those who are already well-to-do. In this paper, a case will be made that Strauss’s suggestion, if implemented, would have disastrous consequences: instead of resulting a fair college admissions system, it would eliminate a metric that helps educators evaluate their own effectiveness and would consequently result in worse education for everybody, rich and poor alike. 
The first point is that if economically underprivileged people are underperforming on a test, the right response is to educate them better; it is not to get rid of the test. Consider the following hypothetical. In country X, each citizen must, upon turning 18, to two years of some kind of physical labor; and tests of physical ability are used to determine exactly who does what. If a given person cannot do a single push-up, then that person is not given a job that requires enormous amounts of physical strength. If someone cannot run more than ten feet without passing out, that person is not assigned a job that involves great physical endurance. In country X, wealthy people are more healthy than poor people, simply because wealth people in X have better medical care than poor people, are better nourished, drink cleaner water, and so on. Consequently, wealthy people in X vastly outperform poor people on these tests of physical aptitude. How should the government respond? Should it declare these physical tests to be ‘unjust’? Should it get rid of all tests that expose the differences in fitness and health between rich and poor? No. That would be the worst possible course of action, as it would hide the injustices in question. The right response is to keep using those metrics and to improve the performance of those whose performance is currently substandard. 
Strauss’s reasoning, if relativized to this hypothetical, would require the government of X to get rid of these fitness tests. This would make it hard to know whose fitness-levels had to improved and would perpetuate the whatever it was that was lowering the fitness levels of poor people. It would also make it hard to know whether attempts improve health and fitness were effective. Similarly, if the SAT’s and ACT’s ceased to be used, the result would simply be to cloak—not to fix—whatever it was that was responsible for the differences between rich and poor in respect of their scores on these tests. Hiding inequalities does not eliminate them; it exacerbates them and makes them harder to correct. 
Further, Strauss’s suggest that we scrap the SAT’s and ACT’s would deprive universities of an enormously valuable metric. On average, people who score high on these tests stay in college and do well in college, whereas people who score low do poorly and are highly likely to drop out. See Figures 1-3. 
Figure 1 First Year GPA by SAT Score 

Adapted from “The Use of Clustering Models and Constructive Methods to Explain Professional Trajectories and Other Economic Drivers,” by June Gerard, 2016, Proceedings of the Scottish Mathematical Society 119, 
p. 61. Reprinted with permission. 
Figure 2 Graduation Rates by SAT Score 

Adapted from: “Regression Models in Educational Statistics,” by Barry Posner, 2017, Modern Statistics and Statistical Methods 54, p. 66. Reprinted with permission. 
Figure 3 

Adapted from: Belnap, Noel. Towards Higher Graduation Rates. Community Scholar, 16(7), 13. 
Figures 1-3 show that those who do well on the SAT tend to well in college and also to graduate, and they also show that those who do poorly on the SAT tend to do poorly and not to graduate. For what it is worth, this is consistent with my personal experience. To my knowledge, the people I know from high school did well on the SAT’s and ACT’s are flourishing in college, and those who did poorly are struggling and some have even dropped out. 
Straus writes: “For years, questions have been raised about the validity and value of SAT and ACT scores in college admissions. The College Board has paid for research touting the “predictive value” of SAT scores in forecasting how students will do in college and beyond, but critics have questioned the research.” We have just seen statistical proof of the claim that they are predictive of academic success. To be sure, there are enormously gifted people who do poorly on standardized tests and there are people who lack substance who do well on them. These are not perfect tests by any stretch of the imagination. But given a thousand people who scored in the top 5% on the SAT’s and a thousand who scored in the bottom 5%, the former group is much more likely to yield college-ready students than the latter, and universities would be foolish to spurn the use of SAT’s and other such metrics. 
Also, granting that standardized fail to measure creativity and insight and granting that their diagnostic value is indeed quite limited, they are one of the few meaningful data-points that college admissions have. Grades often reflect biases, either positive or negative, on the part of instructors, and ‘extra-curricular activities’, though laudable, are not little relevance to scholarly potential. 
I have a much older sister, and when she was growing up, my parents did not have enough money to send her to a good school. As a result, her education suffered and her SAT scores were mediocre. When I was growing up, my parents had the resources to send me to a good school, with the result that I did well on the SAT’s and am currently doing respectably in college. Part of what my motivated my father to earn more was his knowledge that he needed to do so to give me a good education, and my sister’s less than optimal test scores. 
Strauss tries to muddy the waters by linking the SAT’s to cheating on the part of people of means: “Is it finally time for colleges and universities to stop requiring applicants to take the SAT and ACT college admissions exams?” Strauss asks at the beginning of her article. “The question, long asked by testing critics, is being revived with new urgency amid the explosive college admissions bribery scandal rocking the world of higher education. As part of an investigation they called Operation Varsity Blues, federal prosecutors last week charged some 50 people, including famous Hollywood actresses and wealthy financiers. The alleged schemes included hiring impostors to take SAT and ACT exams, or rigging the test by asking for additional time to take it even when that wasn’t necessary.” 
This is not good reasoning. Obviously, these people were wrong to cheat, but the right response is not to get rid of the SAT but simply to make sure that people don’t cheat on it. Suppose that people bribed officials to be given life-saving medication that was in short supply. By Strauss’s reasoning, we should get rid of the medication. By my reasoning, we should prevent fraudulent use of it. 
In the United States, College admissions are to a large extent determined by one’s performance on the SAT or ACT. To be sure, one’s scores on these tests are not the only relevant factor, but admissions-committees give them enormous weight. Someone with low board scores will have a tough time getting into a top school, let alone being scholarship money. Is this right? Should one’s performance on a four-hour multiple-choice test determine where one goes to college or even whether one does so? Author and education-writer Strauss answers with a resounding "no", and she brilliantly defends her answers in her Washington Post Article "Is it finally time to get rid of the SAT and ACT college admissions test?" According to Strauss, SAT and ACT scores are no less a reflection of socioeconomic privilege than of actual merit. In defense of this, she cites the statistical fact that people from impoverished backgrounds do worse on these tests than people from wealthy backgrounds. Lest it be thought that wealthy test-takers might simply be more intelligent than poor ones, Strauss points out that the wealthy, unlike the poor, have the option of hiring private instructors to boost their performance on such tests. “[K]ids from poor families do worse than kids with money”, Strauss writes. “Wealthy parents can provide benefits that many poor families can’t, such as tutors [and] learning opportunities.” For good measure, Strauss reinforces this already weighty claim by quoting Bob Schaeffer, Director of Public Education at Fair Test. “How”, Schaeffer asks, “can any college admissions office tell whether the ACT/SAT score they receive reflects an applicant’s actual test-taking skills or whether it was inflated by test-coaching ‘steroids’?” (Strauss, 3) This is consistent with my personal experience. When raising my oldest sister, who is twelve years older than I am, my parents had little money and therefore little to invest in her education. When raising me, my parents did have resources and invested heavily in my education. As a result, I outperformed her on the SAT by a hefty margin, her Math score being 450 and mine being 750. My sister is actually mathematically gifted; but because of her straitened circumstances, she was forced to teach herself material that I had the privilege being taught by qualified instructors. My sister's low SAT scores closed off a lot of opportunities that would have been available to her had she had better instruction. She is obviously not an isolated case, and I am therefore inclined to agree with Strauss that SAT and ACT scores are quite as much a measure of wealth as they are of merit. Less than one month ago, Strauss points out, the FBI arrested over 50 wealthy financiers and famous actors for either hiring impostors to take their SAT's for their children and for other forms of SAT-related fraud. This shows us that people with money have the option not only of inflating but of falsifying their children’s test-scores; and according to Strauss, it constitutes an additional reason to marginalize the relevance of SAT and ACT scores to college admissions. Once again, Strauss quotes Bob Schaeffer, according to whom the wealthy have used a wide variety of ruses to falsify their children’s scores: “Those…schemes”, writes Schaeffer, “have included hiring impersonators to take the exams; making phony “disability’ claims to gain extra test-taking time; paying to change wrong answers or fill in missing responses; and bribing proctors and test-site supervisors to ignore these illegal acts.”(Strauss). I attended an elite private school in Thailand, and many of my classmates were given unlimited amounts of time to take tests because their parents had bribed psychiatrists to claim that they had medical conditions, such as ADHD, that warranted such dispensations. It was disturbing to me to see friends of mine cheat their way into having high test scores. 
“In some cases,” Strauss writes, “test administration in an entire country has had to be canceled at the last minute because of credible cheating allegations.” (Strauss, 2). In 2017, the SAT's were canceled in Thailand, when it was discovered that a number of students had been using high-tech glasses to have the answers fed to them. I remember a friend of my sister’s sobbing when she found out that she would not able to take the test. In situations where cheating is rampant, those who choose not to engage in it are penalized for their own integrity; and this, as Strauss points out, is yet another reason why SAT and ACT scores should be optional for college applicants. 
Strauss's arguments are compelling. Money can be used to inflate or even falsify SAT and ACT scores. So long as college admissions are based on such scores, people from wealthy families have an unfair advantage and those who have the integrity not to cheat are at an unfair disadvantage. The poor will continue to be unfairly deprived of the blessings of a college education so long as college admissions are based on SAT and ACT scores, and Strauss is therefore right that making these tests optional would do much to level the playing field. 
Being a Voter in Texas (Current Events) 
Why it is important to know about who represents you in the Texas Legislature. Be specific. 
I live in Houston Texas (specially, in 77017), which is in many respects a diverse and dynamic city. In a city of this size, there are inevitably many pressing issues; and in a city as culturally rich and diverse as Houston —as blessed by a wealth of different cultures and as cursed by the manifold difficulties striking equitable balances between so many different viewpoints­--state legislators have awesome responsibilities. It is their responsibility to make sure that the school board does its job, that city workers do their jobs, that hospitals, parks and basic public services are in working order. Further, state representatives must see to it that municipal workers do their job while also seeing to it that their district is hospitable to business. And it is proverbially difficult for representatives (or officials of any kind, for that matter) strike this balance. Our state representative in 77071 is Mary Ann Perez. Frankly, I myself have very little information about Representative Perez, so I cannot pass judgment. But her website (http://votemaryannperez.com/) provided next to no information about her views, which I found disconcerting, especially since one’s state representative is in many ways one’s go-to when one has problems within one’s own community. Obviously senators and congresspersons, having as they do both local and nation-level responsibilities, cannot be expected to respond promptly to strictly local problems. And precisely because state-representatives are there to step into that breech, it is especially helpful to know who one’s state representative is and how well he/she represents your interest. 
Three Takeaways from The Young Turks’ ‘Iceland does the Unimaginable’ (Current Events) 
Takeaway 1: In the US, bankers seem to have an almost special status. This may be a consequence of our nation’s high regard for wealth. Or it may be a consequence of the fact that they use their immense wealth to influence legislation and law-enforcement. 
Takeaway 2: In the US, regulators rationalize their failure to prosecutor bank-fraud on the grounds that, because banks are so big, doing so could disrupt the economy. But the real reason is likely that these regulators are ‘on the pad’, i.e. have been bought off. 
Takeaway 3: The spuriousness of this excuse on the part of regulator’s is evidenced by that fact that in Iceland, where they sent their top bankers to jail for fraud, the economy picked up as a result. 
Three Takeaways from WSJ Video about the European Union 
One reason that the Economic Union was created in order to bind nations together in such a way that it would not be in their interest go to war with each other. Thus, it was at least in part the traumatic experience of World Wars I and II that led Europe to see itself as needing to be economically unified. 
Another reason for establishing the EU was to consolidate the various European economies into a single super-economy that would be competitive no the world market. 
Because the EU comprises so many different economies and because it requires various different governments to agree on monetary policy, it takes a very long time to make decisions, with the result) that it cannot act quickly enough in crisis situations. And this is what happened with Greece: Greece couldn’t pay its debts, and because the EU couldn’t act quickly enough to contain the situation, Greece’s economy dragged down the other economies of the EU with it.

Divan Japonais A Paradigm of the Art Nouveau Movement 
“Art Nouveau is an international style of art, architecture and applied art, especially the decorative arts, that was most popular between 1890 and 1910. A reaction to the academic art of the 19th century, it was inspired by natural forms and structures, particularly the curved lines of plants and flowers.” 
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art_Nouveau 
There is much truth, but also much falsehood in this definition of ‘Art Nouveau’, as we will now see in our analysis of a classic case of that movement Divan Japonais. 
Created by Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec in 1892, Divan Japonais is a poster for a fashionable nightclub in Paris. Divan Japonais is an example of art nouveau. Like all artistic movements, art nouveau is better defined through examples than through words, but works belonging to this movement tend to be characterized by the use of “long, sinuous, organic lines” (https://www.britannica.com/art/Art-Nouveau). Here is an example of such a work: 

This work bears obvious similarities to Divan Japonais: 

And each work bears obvious similarities to other paradigms of this movement, for example: 

Interestingly, all of these works are similar have a decidedly ‘Japanese’ quality, as we can see when look at paradigms of classic Japanese paintings: 

Or, to take another example: 

What all these works have in common is a curious mixture of starkness, representational realism, two-dimensionalism, along with the fact that they depict people who are affecting very deliberate poses, much like actors in Kabuki theatre. 
Let us now focus on Divan Japonais: 

The iconic elements in this print are the graphic elements---the actual drawings. (Iconic representation is representation by virtue of resembling. A drawing of a hand resembles a hand and represents a hand for that reason. The express ‘Abo’s hand’ does not resemble Abod’s hand and represents said hand conventionally, or ‘symbolically’, and therefore non-iconically.) So the woman is an iconic representation (of a woman); her hat is an iconic representation; the blackness of her dress is an iconic representation (of the blackness of a dress); and so on. 
The words at the top of the poster are symbolic (conventional) representations (they denote a Parisian hotspot). There are no indexical representations (unless the painting itself is considered to be an indexical representation of the artist’s activity of depositing paint/ink on a canvass). 
The tension in Divan Japonais is more in the narrative (the message) than in either the form (the structure of the message) or the coding (use of encrypted messaging techniques). The narrative involves a highly well dressed woman, who clearly sees herself as an elegant grand dame, sitting next to a rather defeated looking man, who does not quite self-conceptualize as a member of the upper class and who does not have the upper hand in this situation. In the background there is another woman, similar to the first, with a proudly and unnaturally upright posture. The tension, I therefore surmise, lies primarily in the narrative. The fact that the woman is in the foreground, with the defeated looking man in the background, is arguably a coded message to women that at this nightclub, they will be treated as VIP’s. The fact that the defeated looking man is sandwiched between two hyper-proud women reinforces this message. 
The color-schema is hyper-coded, in that the one woman is dressed in black and the other in white, with a beige man in between them, as though the women were tag-teaming him or, alternately, as though the one woman’s seeming innocence (represented by the whiteness of her clothes) was a mask covering up manipulativeness (represented by the blackness of the other woman’s clothes). The shapes of the man and two women are literal and therefore under-coded; and the color of the clothes of the background woman —the white of her dress and the contrasting blackness of her gauntlets—is moderately cooded, in that it is both literal and also symbolic (of her being both good and bad or, more likely, of her appearing good while being a kind of predator, whose prey is this man or men of his ilk). 
This work, with its clear lines and clear visual message, overlaid on a sinister undercurrent, is characteristic of much work in the Art Nouveau 
tradition, for example: and also, interestingly, of classical Japanese art, for example: 


This last painting has clean lines and a forthright mode of exposition, but the figure depicted in it is cartoonish and contorted, almost a mockery of a person. much the same is true of the figures in Divan Japonais the same way, though in a more understated way. 
New Statendam 
Analysis of a piece of an Art Deco Work 
Art Deco, sometimes referred to as Deco, is a style of visual arts, architecture and design that first appeared in France just before World 
War 
I. 
[1] 
Art Deco influenced the design of buildings, furniture, jewelry, fashion, cars, movie theatres, trains, ocean liners, and everyday objects such as radios and vacuum cleaners.[2] 
It took its name, short for Arts Décoratifs, from the Exposition 
internationale 
des 
arts 
décoratifs 
et 
industriels 
modernes(International 
Exhibition 
of 
Modern 
Decorative 
and 
Industrial 
Arts) 
held in Paris in 1925.[3] 
It combined modernist 
styles with fine craftsmanship and rich materials. During its heyday, Art Deco represented luxury, glamour, exuberance, and faith in social and technological progress. 
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art_Deco 
This definition of ‘Art Deco’ is weak and vague, and we shall do much to improve it right now, by analyzing a classic example of Art Deco work, often referred to as “New Statendam.” 
Created in 1928 by Fernand Leger in 1928, this poster is an advertisement is for the New Statendam Holland-American shipping line. This work is an example of the art deco movement and also has heavy cubist influences. Art deco is a style of art that involves the use of clear lines, well-defined and often exaggerated geometric forms, crisp and starkly mutually contrasting colors, and an emphasis on functionality and purpose, as opposed to subtleties of meaning. In this respect, this posters (henceforth ‘New Statendam’) is a paradigm of the Art Deco movement: 

It becomes clear why this poster is so quintessentially Art Deco when 
we compare with other Art Deco paintings and illustrations, for example: 
And: 



And, finally: 
In all four of these works, we see an emphasis on clearly defined concepts, heavy use of well-defined geometrical figures, and an unflinching emphasis on functionality. Each of these paintings omits nuances of color, as well as nuances of meaning, so as to focus on shapes and on shape-related functions. Note how well-defined the Empire State building is and how crisply it contrasts with the background. Notice in the illustration of the gentleman that his facing is missing, even though his body-shape and the cut of this clothes are done with geometrical precision: the message being sent there is that function is what matters, not subjectivity. Much the same is true 

What is striking about New Statendam is that although it is an advertisement for a cruise ship, it emphasizes the functional aspects of the vessel in question: the pipes, with smoke gushing out of them, and what appear to be fuel tanks---all represented in a proudly geometrical manner, as though it were a factory that were being sold, or a freight vessel, not a cruise-line. This seems to reflect a certain optimism of this age and a faith that it had in mechanization. 
The only symbolic (purely conventional and therefore non-iconic) representations in this poster are the words. There are no indexical elements, unless the work itself is regarded as an indexical representation (i.e. as a representation by way of being an effect) of the artist’s activity. There is of course plenty of iconic representation, the most conspicuous ones being the drawings of the pipes belching smoke, all uniformly jutting up, as though they were soldiers fighting for the cause of industrial progress. 
The narrative seems to be: Hop on board on the future by booking a cruise with us. The form is scarcely distinguishable from the narrative, in that the narrative concerns the industrial efficacy of this vessel and the form consists of well-defined, seemingly functional geometrical figures. The coded message seems to be that if one doesn’t book passage on this ship, the future will pass one by. 
The smoke bellowing out of the smokestacks seems to be heavily coded, in that it has multiple messages, including the future is with this ship and also the future is toxic and noxious. The smokestacks (if that is the right word) seem to be moderately coded, in that while they are serving largely as literal representation, they also have ‘ulterior’ meanings, the main one being the vessel in question is a kind of method of transport to modernity. The color and shading on the smokestacks seem to be literal representations of actual color and shading and are therefore examples of undercoding. 
Art Deco is similar to cubism, in its heavy use of geometry and, more subtly, in its nihilistic undercurrents. But Art Deco, involving as it does clear illustrations of shapes and of other purpose-related aspects of objects, is ideal for advertisements, often being used to this end, with results that, despite their starkness, are logical and elegant, for example: 

As this last piece illustrates, Art Deco is ultimately less about nihilism than it is about progress and functionality. 
Lawn Tennis Analysis of a piece of an Art Deco Work 
As I’ve said before, “the Mod generation”, contrary to popular belief, 
was not born in even 1958, but in the 1920s after a steady gestation from about 1917 or so. Now, Mod certainly came of age, fully sure of itself by 1958, completely misunderstood by 1963, and in a perpetual cycle of reinvention and rediscovery of itself by 1967 and 1975, respectively, but it was born in the 1920s, and I will maintain this. I don’t care who disagrees with me, and there are dozens of reasons that I do so —from the Art Deco aesthetic, to flapper fashions (complete with bobbed hair), to androgyny and subtle effeminacy, to jazz.” 
- Ruadhán J. McElroy 
https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/tag/art-deco 

According to this quotation, Art Deco is characterized by ‘subtle effeminacy’ and ‘androgyny’ and, so it is suggested, lacks the dignity of a ‘serious’ artistic movement. In this paper, we will see how false this characterization is. Indeed, we need only so much as glance at some paradigms of Art Deco illustrations to see as much, for example: 

Created by A.M. Cassandre in 1932, this work (henceforth “LT”, short of “Lawn Tennis”) is a poster-advertisement for a tennis tournament: 
Hence the wording on LT: “Grande Quinzaine Internationale de Lawn-Tennis” (“Grand Two week Lawn-tennis Tournament”), these words, along with the supplementary words in the header and footer positions, being the only symbolic (conventional, non-narrative, non-indexical) elements in LT. 
LT is quintessentially ‘Art Deco’, with its crisp, futuristic lines and proudly geometry-based, mathematical mode of representation. Notice that the centerpiece of this work is the tennis ball---appropriately represented by a perfect sphere---with the player, the only human being represented in this work, being reduced to a geometrical shape, stripped of a face and of a distinctive identity. This is typical of Art Deco work. (See Paper 2, specifically the representations of Bat Man and the well-dressed gentleman, neither of whom has a face but each of whom is otherwise represented with geometrical precision.) 
Note also the line of the tennis net---how it forms a perfectly uniform horizon that practically bisects the ball. 
The narrative here is that tennis, and by extension all human endeavor, is a mathematical game, not a vague spiritual endeavor. 
As previously stated, the symbolic (narrative) components of this work are confined to the words. And, as is the case with all art, there is no indexical component, unless the work itself is taken to be an indexical representation of the activities of the artist. As for symbolic representation, there is plenty: the ball resembles and thereby represents a ball; the man resembles and thereby represents a man; and so on. 
In the case of this work, the tension seems to be concentrated in the form, with the ball sphere being almost perfectly bisected by the horizon formed by the top of the next, all juxtaposed with a blank-faced, automaton-like human being. The narrative is fairly innocuous, its message basically being that tennis is being played. The coding seems to be to the effect that the ‘game’ of tennis is not so much a game as it is a matter of utter seriousness, comparable in its purposiveness to rocket-building or mathematical discovery. There is a dark energy—a mixture of optimism about industry and economics, coupled with spirituality—that informs this work and that informs Art Deco work in general. Consider the following four Art Deco paradigms: 


They all involve perfect symmetries, the absence (sometimes by way 
of omission, sometimes by way of deliberate effacement) of human subjectivity, clearly defined lines, an emphasis on functionality, sometimes to the exclusion of human welfare, an extreme confidence in science and industrialization, and an attitude of energetic, mathematically tinged optimism towards the future. What is striking about LT is that it treats a leisurely pursuit, the playing of tennis, as though it were comparable to industrial construction or scientific research, and it does this by using starkly geometrical forms and by omitting or anonymizing any human beings who might be involved, these techniques being representative of Art Deco work in general, granting that not all such work is quite as rigidly geometrical or as correspondingly inhuman. 
Art Deco is very much a product of its time, this being the early 20th Century. During this time, there was great faith in industrial progress and in the power of science and technology to improve both the technology and human life. There was also a decreased faith in religious faith and in subjectivism generally. Also, Art Deco, with its clean lines and generally utilitarian orientation, is ideal for advertising and has therefore often been 

Art Deco is an enduring style, because it doesn’t flood the mind with extraneous information and appeals to a clean-burning aesthetic, similar to, and possibly also exemplified by, the work of architect Frank Lloyd Wright: 

Student-passage and my revision thereof (Pedagogy) 
hi i need editing this passage please and rewording things 
There are many times in life where we run into a problems trying to figure things out whether at work or at school.and psychologist have come up with many different ways as to how to get over the stumbleing block and address the problem. If I was the head of a problem solving team and my team members ran into a problem. The frist stragety I would try to use to help get my team around the block is the heuristic stragety. This stragety is reffered to as the thinking strategie . where we reduce the number of options to make solving the problem much faster and easier. Another type of stragie I would try to use is an algorithm. An algorthem is stragety that uses step-by­step intructions to get to the answer. An algorthem would be useful in helping solve the problem because you are using step-by-step intruscutions and it can mostly likely get you the right answer. Trial and error can also be useful because it requires trying a number of different ways to get to the solution and helps rule out the soultions that don’t work. 
In life we have to do deal with many different kinds of problems. Some of these problems are external, meaning that they have to do with our circumstances, not with our personalities. But many of these problems are internal, meaning that they do have to do with our personalities and our ways of handling difficult situations. Psychologists help us with situations of the second kind. And even those of who are not psychologists often have occasion to put into practice the lessons that psychologists have to teach us. For example, psychologists have shown that seemingly unsolvable problems can be solved if, instead of being treated as indivisible units, they are broken down into small problems with known or easily identified solutions. This way of thinking is an example of a heuristic strategy—a strategy for dealing with complex or difficult situations. Psychologists have helped us identify many such strategies, and they have shown that our minds employ many such strategies, even without our consciously knowing it. A related concept is that of the algorithm. An algorithm is a well-defined step-by-step procedure for generating the solution to a problem. (The rules that we learn in grade school for the division and multiplication of multi-digit numbers are examples of algorithms.) The concept of an algorithm has its origins in mathematical logic, specifically in the work of Turing and other logicians. But psychologists have discovered that we unconsciously use algorithms in order to learn and to problem-solve. Sometimes more creative or informal methods are needed to solve problems, one example being the proverbial method of trial and error. Trial and error obviously doesn’t always lead to success. But oftentimes it is the only road to success, and we often learn much from our failures. One of the benefits of this method is that, precisely because it is so uncertain to lead to success, it leads to valuable experiences that inform and enrich us. The discipline of psychology, like all scientific disciplines, develops largely (though not entirely) through the use of the trial-and-error method, and psychologists have done much to clarify its importance in our lives.

Summary and Analysis of Tomatoland (Politics/Current Events) 
Tomatoland, by Barry Estabrook, is about the tomato industry in Florida, where about 70% of American tomatoes are grown. The book carefully describes the inhuman conditions under which workers in this industry labor, and it also discusses the manifold health consequences for these workers (and to a lesser extent all tomato-consumers in the US) of the heavy-duty chemicals and pesticides involved in the growth of tomatoes in Florida. The author repeatedly points out that Florida tomatoes are extremely bland and unsavory, especially as compared with tomatoes grown without pesticides, the point being that all of this loss and suffering is for naught. The author also describes the failure of the legal system to improve any aspect of this situation: it ignores the fact that slave labor is involved (and, indeed, heavily relied on); it ignores the abysmal working conditions of non-enslaved workers; and it ignores the adverse health effects of the industrial chemicals used in the process, which include sky-high cancer rates and limbless offspring. Finally, the author discusses some attempts that politicians and activists have made to remedy the situation, and he also discusses cases where people have produced quality-tomatoes without dehumanizing their workers and without using lethal chemicals. 
The book starts out by describing the origins of the American tomato industry. Tomatoes, like potatoes, were unknown to Europeans until the Spanish were introduced to them by the Aztecs and Incas. These tomatoes were small and did not have the smooth, near-spherical shape had by the tomatoes we currently use. The large, round tomatoes that we are familiar with are the handiwork of Alexander Livingston, an American entrepreneur and horticulturalist. Livingston first cultivated such tomatoes in the 1820s. Livingston’s distinctively round and succulent-looking tomatoes were a hit, and the American tomato-industry was born. 
In the 1870s, Florida became a tomato-producer, owing mainly to the fact that, thanks to recently built railroads, it was possible to quickly transport produce from Florida to the Northeast. Because of Florida’s climate, tomatoes can be grown there year-round. But with this qualification, so the author says, Florida is in many ways a terrible place to grow tomatoes. Because Florida is so hot, tomatoes have to be drenched in chemicals to keep them from being eaten by vermin. (In cooler climates, by contrast, chemicals need not be used, at least not in such abundance, granting that year-round tomato-growth is not a possibility in such places.) 
The author then shifts his discussion to the various chemical used in the growth of Florida tomatoes, going into great detail about the staggering costs both to human life, especially (but not exclusively) those of the workers involved, and to Florida’s ecosystem. After discussing the damage done to various native species (e.g. alligators and herons unable to reproduce and dying in droves), the author carefully describes the plight of the workers involved. (In this context, the author is discussing people who ‘choose’ to have these jobs.) If it rains, the workers cannot work and therefore aren’t paid. If there is moisture on the crops, they have to wait, without pay, until it dries before they can start working. On a good day, after working 12 hours, they are lucky to take home $70. Because they don’t have automobiles, they have no choice but to live near the tomato-fields, and landlords charge them outrageously high-rents, which eat up most of their salaries. They are often made ill by the chemicals they are handling and oftentimes cannot afford gloves or other basic protective gear. Because of these toxins, their children are unusually likely to be born with severe defects, including not having limbs or genitalia. But these workers don’t have insurance and therefore can’t pay for medical treatment for themselves or their children. And if a worker so much as complains of illness while on the job, he is likely to be fired and left without any source of income. Because the workers are undocumented, they are not protected by unions and, indeed, have minimal legal protections of any kind. 
The workers’ lack of legal protections brings the author to what I found to be the most poignant and informative part of the book: the discussion of the huge role of slave labor involved in the Florida tomato-industry. Many of the people picking tomatoes in Florida are veritable slaves. The companies that own the fields have agreements with human-traffickers who supply them with these workers, the latter having been abducted and forced to work, with no benefits and minimal pay, under the most brutal conditions. If they try to escape, they are beaten or killed. 
In the last part of the book, the author describes attempts that people have made to improve the situation. He talks about various civil rights and legal activists who are trying to improve working conditions, as well as purge the entire industry of slave labor. He also discusses the case of a Princeton graduate who started a successful tomato-business in Pennsylvania that, although not as lucrative as its sinister Florida counterparts, is relatively successful financial and also produces higher-quality tomatoes. 
I found this book to be readable and engaging, mainly because it was so loaded with very specific and very striking (usually strikingly grim) information about a relatively large sector of the American economy. There are very few statistics and very little analysis, but there is a wealth of carefully described, first-hand facts, and that this is this book’s greatest strength. 
It is also its greatest weakness. The author doesn’t explain why the Florida tomato-industry is so fraught with injustice. Why are conditions for workers so poor in this particular industry, and why is slave-labor used in this particular industry? Does this industry require the use of slave labor? Are its profit-margins so thin? If so, why? And why does the US government look the other way? Is the Florida tomato industry such an important part of the US economy that the government won't adequately police it? Is the United States actually without the financial resources to defray the costs of not dehumanizing workers in this industry? 
The author does mention one or two cases of reasonable successful companies that grew tomatoes without resorting unfair labor practices and without using ecocidal amounts of toxic chemicals. But each of these ‘companies’, as the author also points, though only in passing, was really just a single person, and that person did not have an operation of scale. The reader is thus left without an answer to the question: What is the alternative to the current state of affairs? 
At no point does the author explicitly put forth an economic explanation of the situation. So far as any such analysis is provided, it is confined to the usual mantra that ‘corporations’ are to blame. Nor does the author say how the situation can be improved. He spends a great deal of time talking about the efforts of various activists, along with the politicians (e.g. 
Bernie Sanders) who are sympathetic to them. But from what we can gather, these activists and politicians have done very little to improve the situation. 
This is not surprising. It isn’t activists and politicians who improve labor-conditions. It is economic development. When the industrial revolution first began in Britain, working conditions were appalling and injury-and fatality-rates were sky-high. Thanks to industrialization itself, Britain eventually became wealthy enough that it no longer needed to rely on exploitative labor practices, at which point labor unions became powerful and labor laws were put in place. Regulation can only do so much. If companies don’t have the money to pay their workers, they will either go out of business or use slave-labor, and there is nothing that regulation by itself can do to change this. Regulators can shut down companies that have unfair labor practices—and they should do so. But regulators cannot magically give these companies the funds that they need to pay their employees an honest wage. And if an industry-sector is sufficiently large, the government may choose not to regulate, especially if regulation will cause that sector to implode. Therefore, it has to be figured out how this industry-sector can be so modified that it doesn’t need to rely on unfair labor practices---and Estabrook says nothing about how to do this. Nor does Estabrook say anything as to how tomatoes can be mass-produced without using toxic chemicals. 
In sum: I enjoyed this book enormously, because it was so rich in first­hand facts, as opposed to empty statistics and abstract analysis. The problem discussed by the author is malfeasance on the part of tomato-growers in Florida, specifically in connection with grossly exploitative labor practices and also in connection with their use of environment-and health-destroying chemicals. The author doesn’t put forth an argument at all: he states facts. These facts make it clear that there is a problem, and they make it clear what that problem is (slave labor, underpaid workers, illness among workers and to some extent among the general public, and destruction of wildlife). What the author doesn’t say that is that people responsible for these horrors are obviously aware of them, and they wouldn’t continue to perpetrate them unless they had extremely strong financial incentives for doing so. And the author also doesn’t say why they have these incentives or how they honorable conduct on their part can be incentivized. So yes—Tomatoland is replete with solid evidence for specific factual claims, e.g. claims relating to worker-illness and worker-exploitation. But explanations are proposed and a fortiori no evidence or reasoning is adduced in their favor. The author did do a creditable job of explaining the history of Florida’s tomato-industry, but he doesn’t even try to identify the financial pressures that are responsible for its continued existence or for its abhorrent practices. As earlier stated, what I found most striking about this book was the discussion of slave labor in this industry-sector: I do not believe that the author is exaggerating. This is not an acceptable situation, and we as a nation must address it. But it seems to be entirely off of our national radar—a fact that I also find striking (and disturbing). 
A Demographic Analysis of Dropout-likelihood and Dropout-rationality 
Abstract 
In the fall of 2017, approximately 5 million people enrolled in college in the United States, and 56% of them had dropped out by the end of the school year (National Center for Education Statistics, 2019). In the present work, we will find that, contrary to what is generally assumed, people who drop out tend to be rational to do so. First, we will establish (1) that the greater the likely financial pay-off of a college degree, the less likely one is to drop out. Then we will establish (2) that the more likely a college degree is to lead to an emotionally satisfying career, the less likely one is to drop out. 
Claim (1) will be established on the basis of analysis of existing statistics. IQ is directly correlated with SAT-scores and SAT-scores are directly correlated with graduation-probability. The higher a given college­graduate’s SAT-scores, the greater the probable financial benefits of a college degree and the smaller the probable costs. The costs of college to those who score in the top percentile on the SAT are negligible compared to the gains. It therefore tends to be irrational for such people to drop out, and their drop-out rate is less than 1%. Strikingly, the dropout rate for those who score in the top percentile on the SAT are invariant with respect to gender, ethnicity, and personal finances. For those who score in the 40th percentile or below, the costs of a college degree a exceed the benefits. It therefore tends to be rational for such people to drop out of college, and over 60% of them do so. Dropout-rates within the class of low IQ dropouts are not invariant with respect to gender, ethnicity, and personal finances, minorities and males being marginally more likely Caucasians and females to drop out, and the socioeconomically underprivileged being non-marginally more likely than the socioeconomically privileged to do so. The higher the SAT, the more invariant dropout-rates become with respect to these variables. 
Within the class of low (<110) IQ graduates, those whose careers are based on vocational training received outside of college earn approximately 20% more than those whose careers are based on their college degrees. Within the class of low IQ persons who do not have vocational certifications, those with college degrees earn only marginally more than those without college degrees and earn less after student-debt is adjusted for. Moreover, the jobs that members of the one group are likely to have coincide for the most part with those that members of the other group are likely to have. 
Earnings therefore depend on IQ, vocational training, and college-degree. For those on the lower end of the IQ spectrum, vocational training is positively dispositive of high net earnings in those on the lower end of the IQ spectrum and a college degree is dispositive of low net earnings. In those on the upper end of the IQ spectrum, a college degree is dispositive of high net earnings and vocational training does not in general have an appreciable effect either way. More precisely, the aforementioned variables generate the following earnings hierarchy: 
(i) 
Low IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(ii) 
High non-graduates without vocational training 


(iii) Low IQ graduates without vocational training 
(iv) 
High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(v) 
Low IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

(vi) 
High IQ non-graduates with vocational training 


(vii) High IQ graduates with vocational training 
(viii) High IQ graduates without vocational training 
Claim (2) cannot be established on the basis of existing statistics. This 
is not statistics are inherently incapable of settling the matter; it is simply because the existing statistical data does not exist. Consequently, the present researcher had to generate that data on his own, by administering a carefully constructed questionnaire the just-mentioned eight-groups of people, the defeasible presumption being that career-satisfaction levels will, to at least some degree, track, earnings levels.

Keywords: College Retention, Economic Rationality, Vocational Training, Life-Satisfaction
