Chapter 1: Introduction to the Study Introduction 
College dropout rates are currently over 50% and have been for over a decade (Pevin, 2015). This is often described as a ‘tragedy’ (Wolf, 2017) or a ‘scandal’ (Kirp, 2019). On the face of it, this is understandable. Dropping out is a form of quitting, and quitting is in many contexts a form of failure. Also, a college degree has come to be associated in the popular psyche with ‘the good life’---two cars in the driveway, a house in the suburbs, and so on (Corn, 2017). Also, just as Hillary Clinton said, college graduates do on average earn approximately $1,000,000 during their lifetimes than non-graduates (Bartik, 2016). Finally, it is obvious to many people that if they hadn’t gone to college, they wouldn’t have the careers that they do have: they wouldn’t be investment bankers or physicians or attorneys (Altoni et al., 2016). 
The truth is, however, that the dropout rates are not high enough. There is relatively definitive evidence of this of a purely objective, statistical nature. We will discuss this evidence in detail in the present work, but the upshot of it is easily stated: Those with high IQ’s economically benefit from college, and those with low IQ’s don’t. The reason for this, quite simply, is that people with low IQ’s derive little economic benefit from academic courses of study and are economically better off learning and practicing a trade. Not only is this the obvious, off-the-cuff explanation: it is, as we will go to great lengths to show, a matter of demonstrable statistical fact. These claims will be established on the basis of rigorous statistical analysis. 
Of course, money isn’t everything. Given only that someone with a low IQ stands to earn more money practicing a trade than he does using his college degree, it doesn’t follow that he is more fulfilled if he takes the first path. That said, we will show that he is in fact more fulfilled. We will show on the basis of survey-data that low IQ college graduates, not including those whose careers are based on vocational training received outside of college, are among the least fulfilled sub-populations in the country. The happiest sub-population, as those same surveys indicate, consists of high IQ college graduates, and the second happiest is vocationally trained college non-graduates. 
We will establish this by administering a carefully constructed questionnaire to eight groups of people. The groups in question are: 
(i) 
Low IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(ii) 
High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 


(iii) Low IQ graduates without vocational training 
(iv) 
High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(v) 
Low IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

(vi) 
High IQ non-graduates with vocational training 


(vii) High IQ graduates with vocational training 
(viii) High IQ graduates without vocational training 
2. 
The survey uses a seven-point Likert-scale, making it possible to condense questionnaire-results into a single figure and therefore making it possible to establish quantitative comparisons between survey-results. 

3. 
Survey results will establish the following ‘fulfillment­hierarchy’: 


(ix) 
Low IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(x) 
High non-graduates without vocational training 

(xi) 
Low IQ graduates without vocational training 


(xii) High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 
(xiii) Low IQ non-graduates with vocational training 
(xiv) High IQ non-graduates with vocational training 
(xv) High IQ graduates with vocational training 
(xvi) High IQ graduates without vocational training The reasons for targeting these specific groups is that, as we will see, dropout-likelihood and dropout-rationality depend on IQ and vocational-training. 
Background of the Study 
There is plenty of statistical data concerning college­dropoutism, but this data has not been properly interpreted. Attempts have been made to correlate dropout-rates with ethnicity (Shapiro et al., 2017), distance from home (Patterson Silver Wolf et al., 2017), relationship-status (Adams et al., 2016), eating disorders (Lipson et al., 2017), mental health (Eisenberg, 2016), video game usage (Schmitt et al., 2015), and personal finances (Britt et al., 2017)---all to little or no avail. What the statistical data makes absolutely clear is that people with extremely high SAT/ACT scores (henceforth ‘test-scores’) seldom drop out and that people with low test-scores usually drop out. The data also makes it clear that test-scores correlate with IQ (Armstrong et al., 2017), warranting the conclusion that dropout-likelihood correlates inversely with IQ. This is invariant across genders, ethnicities, relationship-statuses and the like. Further, the statistical data makes it clear towards the lower end of the IQ spectrum, the economic benefits of a college degree become marginal at best and also those benefits are directly dependent on IQ, with those in the gifted range economically benefitting enormously from college. 
Be it noted that the statistical data does not directly disclose any of this, even though, when scrutinized, it clearly does indicate as much. The correlation between dropout-likelihood and IQ is readily enough established. The correlation between dropout-rationality and IQ is not as easily established, granting that the difficulties in establishing it are ultimately purely technical in nature. Neither correlation is particularly hard to establish given the available statistical data, making it hard (but also, given our purposes, unnecessary) to explain why these correlations have not yet been established. 
A consequence of the fact that it has not thus far been established exactly who drops out is that it also has not been established how economically rational it is for those who drop out to do so. A consequence of this latter fact, in its turn, is that economic dropout-rationality has not yet been correlated with emotional dropout-rationality. In other words, the degree to which it is economically rational for a given person to drop out of college has not been correlated with the degree to which his doing so conduces to his having an emotionally fulfilling career. 
Problem Statement 
Prior to this study it was not known exactly who dropped out or why; nor, therefore, was it known to what extent it was rational for those who dropped out to do so. The purpose of this study is to answer each of these questions, with special attention being paid to the relationship between dropoutism and career-satisfaction. An expected consequence of this study will be a need to reframe our thinking about college-dropouts and college­dropoutism. Thus far, we have regarded dropouts as being the failures (Hollander et al., 2017) and those trying to prevent them dropping out as the heroes (Moriña et al., 2017; Bullard, 2018). 
This study will show that it is the other way around. Forcing people to go to college who are not inclined to do so deprives them of income and professional fulfillment and is also enormously expensive for society as a whole. There are many reasons for this. First of all, there are many people who are happy and financially successful as auto-mechanics---or electricians or plumbers or as practitioners of some other trade---who would be miserable if, instead of learning their trade, they had gone to college. Many such people are gifted at their trade, and many people need their services and pay handsomely for them. Those same people would in many cases not be faring as well had they opted for a college-based path. They would often struggle with the coursework and either drop out, in which case they are likely to self-identify as failures, or choose a major that gives them no marketable skills, in which case they are likely to be failures. 
Also, trades (e.g. auto-repair, plumbing, carpentry) are inherently useful. A person with an IQ can become a master plumber or auto-mechanic; and if they do, they will never have to worry about employment. Also, many people who learn trades launch successful businesses of their own based on those trades, and 70% of successful small to medium-sized business are started in this way. But liberal arts degrees, however much intrinsic value they may have, tend to provide those who have them with very little in the way of marketable skills. There is no obvious way for an intellectually average or even moderately intelligent person to monetize a degree in English or History or Philosophy. If someone is intellectually gifted, he may be able to find a way to leverage into a successful career of some kind; he may become a journalist or professor or financier. But these are long-shots even those who are intellectually gifted, and for most they are simply out of the question. The result is that most people with liberal arts degrees find themselves in an extremely competitive job-market with little to offer to prospective employers and no skills on the basis of which to become self-employed. 
It is assumed that STEM degrees, unlike degrees humanities and social sciences degrees, are inherently useful. This position is misconceived. First of all, most STEM are intellectually out of reach for most of the population, including most of the college population. Second, most STEM degrees, including those that are within the intellectual reach of the average college student, are not inherently useful. A degree in biology does not by itself qualify anyone to do anything. In the case of someone who is intellectually gifted, a degree in biology may eventuate in that person’s being a physician or researcher, but usually only on the condition that the person in question spend years in graduate school or medical school. But these are low-probability outcomes for someone of average or even moderately high intelligence, and a degree in biology opens up very few professional avenues for such a person. To be sure, somebody with a bachelor’s degree in petroleum engineering or computer science is for that reason alone likely to have excellent professional prospects, the same being true, albeit to a lesser extent, of someone with a bachelor’s in chemistry. But less than 20% of college students are able to complete even the first year of any given one of these majors. 
As for mathematics and physics, degrees in these fields, in and of themselves, do very little to make those who have them employable. To be sure, people with bachelor’s degrees in these fields are relatively likely to achieve professional success, but their becoming successful usually involves their spending years after college becoming proficient in areas (e.g. programming, architecture, finance) that are only tangentially related to their undergraduate degrees. But all of this is moot, given that less than 10% of all college students are able to complete even the first year of either of these majors. 
Today (July 3, 2019), the Washington Post reported that only 27% of people with college degree have jobs in areas relating to their college degrees (Plummer, 2019). By contrast, over 90% of those with vocational degrees have jobs in the areas of their degrees (Wladawsky-Berger, 2018). This is understandable. A credential in HVAC or plumbing or computer­maintenance makes one very useful; a degree in English or Religious Studies does not. A consequence is minor economic downturns greatly effect employment-and income levels of college graduates but have little effect on those of tradespersons (Alonso, 2015; Jahn et al., 2015; Friedman, 2018; Arnold, 2018; Levin-Waldman, 2018). 
Purpose of the Study 
The purpose of this study is to determine how rational it is for those who drop out of college to do so. This will involve figuring how exactly who drops out of college, i.e. we must first identify the characteristics that dispose someone to drop out of college. Having done this, we must correlate dropout-likelihood with probably average future earnings and also with probably average degree of career-satisfaction. 
Ultimately, what we refer to as ‘rationality’ coincides not with economic rationality but with emotional rationality. Stated simply, for a person to be rational is for him to make decisions that conduce to his emotional well-being. This often involves making decisions that conduce to one’s financial well-being. But economic rationality cannot be identified with rationality since someone who makes decisions that make wealthy but suicidally depressed is ceteris paribus less rational than someone who makes decisions that make him fulfilled but only moderately wealthy (Bennett, 2016). 
This is not a purely theoretical point. Setting aside those who simply don’t earn enough to satisfy basic needs, there is surprisingly little correlation between happiness-levels and income (Easterlin, 2015). Happiness is more easily correlated less with how much they earn than with how they earn it (Oishi et al., 2015). Lawyers who earn under $100,000 have extremely low rates of career-satisfaction, and most entrepreneurs who earn more than $25,000/year have extremely high rates of career satisfaction (Hessels et al., 2018). 
Because rationality clearly involves being able to make decisions that conduce to one’s own emotional well-being, we cannot go so far as to identify rationality with economic rationality (Christiansen, 2017). But there are important reasons not to disregard the concept of economic rationality. First of all, if people who are dropping out of college are rational to do so, that suggests that instead of wasting resources trying to keep them in college, we should use resources to identify and improve access to alternative professional paths. Second, economic rationality is completely objective: whereas people’s emotional self-assessments, though prima facie credible, may be replete with rationalizations, there is no denying how much a given person earns or what kind of work he does to earn it (Ervas, 2017). In this study, consequently, we will investigate both the economic and the emotional rationality of college-dropoutism. 
Research Questions and/or Hypotheses 
This study will investigate in what ways and to what degrees the consequences of dropping out of college depend on IQ and receipt of vocational training. Given the fact that many ‘high-IQ’ careers (e.g. physician, investment banker, professor) are simply out of the question for those who do not have college degrees, there exists a reasonable, if defeasible, presumption to the effect that it is in general more rational than not for those in the intellectually gifted range to graduate from college. What is less obvious is whether the same is true of those on the other end of the IQ spectrum. Day-to-day experience suggests that, where such people are concerned, a college degree tends to lead to a tedious and low-level office job. Day-to-day experience also suggests that, when such people receive vocational training and careers based thereupon, their work, though arduous, tends to be both lucrative and fulfilling. To put it bluntly and informally, auto-mechanics, super-intendents, welders, Emergency Medical Technicians, and Web-designers seem to enjoy their work, whereas secretaries, probation officers, intake-coordinators, low-level administrators, and office-workers do not seem to enjoy their work. Of course, these claims, being based only on personal experience, cannot be asserted with confidence, it being the purpose the present study to subject them to systematic scrutiny. 
Consequently, the following research question guides this qualitative study: 
RQ1: To what extent are those who drop out of college behaving rationally in doing so? 
There are two versions of RQ1, namely: 
RQ1F: To what extent are those who drop out of college being 
financially rational in doing so? In other words, to what extent does their doing so lead to increased earnings? 
And: RQ1E: To what extent are those who drop out of college being 
emotionally rational in doing so? In other words, to what extent does their doing so lead to increased career-satisfaction? 
In order to answer RQ1, we must first answer some other questions, in particular: 
RQ2: Who drops out of college? In other words, what characteristics are dispositive of a person’s dropping out of college? (Is it poverty, mental illness, physical illness, unstable home life, low IQ, etc.?) 
RQ2 can be answered on the basis of existing statistical data, the answer being: 
HQ2: The higher a given person’s IQ, the less likely he is to drop out of college. 
RQ1F is also to be answered on the basis of statistical analysis. The 
null-answer is: HQ1F0: A person’s IQ does not in general have any bearing, positive 
or negative, on whether it is financially for that person to drop out of 
college. 
The expected answer is: 
HQ1F: The higher a given person’s IQ, the less financially rational it is 
for him to drop out of college. 
RQ1E: is not to be answered on the basis of existing statistical data. 
We will attempt to answer it on the basis of survey data. The null answer is: HQ1E: A given person’s dropping out of college may conduce to 
increased career-satisfaction or to decreased career-satisfaction; and whether it does the one as opposed to the other is completely random and of his major, his intelligence level, and indeed any other given fact about him. 
The expected answer 
HQ1E: Categorizing people by IQ and college degree status generates 
the following career-satisfaction hierarchy: 
(i) 
Low IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(ii) 
High non-graduates without vocational training 


(iii) Low IQ graduates without vocational training 
(iv) 
High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(v) 
Low IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

(vi) 
High IQ non-graduates with vocational training 


(vii) High IQ graduates with vocational training 
(viii) High IQ graduates without vocational training HQ1E is the ‘expected’ answer in three senses. First, it is consistent 
with statements made, but not adequately supported, in credible publications (Looseley et al., 2019). Second, it is consistent with my personal and professional experience. Third, it is consistent with small, informal studies that I have conducted in anticipation of the present study. 
Advancing Scientific Knowledge and Significance of the Study 
This study has manifold practical and theoretical significances. First of all, if the hypotheses stated above are correct, then the enormous resources that are being spent trying to prevent people from dropping out of college should instead be spent trying to build alternatives to college (Bok, 2017). Also, right now, k-12 education is essentially college-prep education (Harbour, 2015), and the current study is likely to show that this is misconceived: where many students are concerned, possibly most, the focus should not be on getting them into college, but on constructing alternatives to college. Also, as we will see, when low persons in the non-gifted IQ range have careers that they find fulfilling, those careers are based on vocational training, not on college degrees, and, what is more, those careers service vital and otherwise insufficiently serviced economic sectors. Right now, thanks to the exclusive focus on getting people, regardless of their talents, into and through college, many people are being steered away from careers that they would enjoy and be good at and that would benefit society. Governments and government-related bureaucracies being what they, might cannot necessarily expect the existing educational establishment to right-size itself, and the private sector may therefore have to step into this breech. 
This study also has significance of a purely theoretical kind for the fields of psychology and economics. According to the hypotheses stated above, entrepreneurs have higher levels of career satisfaction than ‘corporate wage-slaves’ who earn three times as much, and people who practice traces, such as plumbing and auto-repair, are happier than probation officers and administrators who earn twice as much. If correct, this tells us a great deal about human motivations, and it also tells us that what drives economic progress is not the desire for money so much as it is the desire for money that is acquired in a way that is consistent with one’s values and personality (Pagenelli, 2015). 
Rationale for Methodology 
There are three different investigative methodologies: quantitative, qualitative, and mixed. Quantitative methodologies attempt to assign numerical values to relevant factors and to investigate relations of dependence and predictability holding among such values (Hempel, 1965; Curtis et al., 2016); Salmon, 2017). Suppose for example that violent crime in Small Town USA has increased 500% over the last decade. A quantitative analysis of this phenomenon would try to find some numerically determinate variable (or variable-set) x (e.g. consistently decreasing funding for law-enforcement) such that changes in x correlated with the rise in crime in Small Town. Qualitative methods investigate causal relations holding among forces that either have not yet been quantified or cannot be quantified in any principled way. A qualitative analysis of the rise in crime in Small Town might involve case-studies of specific criminals, or interviews with criminals or victims of crime or law-enforcement personnel, the purpose being to elicit relevant information (e.g. information about changes in cultural norms). A mixed methodology is one that involves elements of both qualitative and quantitative methodologies (Rudestam & Newton, 2015). When applied to the rise in crime in Small Town, such an approach might involve a combination of the methods must described. 
There are two components to this project: a purely statistical component, in which the demographics of dropoutism are investigated, and a survey-based component, in which the psychology of dropoutism is investigated. In each case, the methodology used is quantitative. But there are important differences between the methodologies used, owing to the fact that, whereas the statistics needed to investigate the financial aspects of dropoutism already exist, the statistics needed to investigate its consequences for career-satisfaction do not yet exist, requiring us to generate those statistics ourselves. Consequently, the methodology used in the one case related only to the analysis of statistics, whereas the methodology used in the other case related both the analysis and to the generation of statistics. 
Nature of the Research Design of the Study 
Both parts of the inquiry were quantitative and causal-correlational in nature. The statistical part correlates dropout rates with IQ and subsequent earnings; the psychological part correlates earnings with levels of career-satisfaction. The statistical part involved nothing more than finding correlations so tight that they were obviously causal and then using commonsense to infer what the operative causal relations were. For example, there is an extremely tight connection between low SAT score and high freshman dropout-likelihood, but it is obvious that a low SAT score is not itself the cause of someone’s dropping out of college. An inability to do academic work is the cause, and this inability is manifested both in low SAT scores and also in poor performance in first-year college classes, the latter often being the immediate precursor of (voluntary or involuntary) permanent departure from college (Mabel et al., 2018). 
The psychological part involved administering surveys whose results could be represented as a single variable. Our objective here is to compare earnings with levels of career-satisfaction. Earnings are given by a single figure (e.g. $170,000/year), and we therefore need career-satisfaction to be represented by a single quantity if we are to correlate it with earnings and, consequently, if we are to determine how emotionally rational those who drop out are in doing so, i.e. if we are to determine degrees of dropoutE­
rationality (Chen et al., 2016) 
Definitions of Terms 
Dropout. In this context, a ‘dropout’ is somebody who drops out of college and does not return. Somebody who takes a year off is not a dropout. 
Dropoutism. The phenomenon of people dropping out of college. 
Dropout-likelihood. The probability that a given person will drop out of college. 
Dropout-probability. Synonymous with “dropout-likelihood.” 
Dropout-rationality. The degree to which it is rational for a given person to drop out of college. 
DropoutE-rationality. The degree to which it is economically rational 
for a given person to drop out of college. DropoutF-rationality. The degree to which it is emotionally rational 
(i.e. conducive to career-satisfaction) for a given person to drop out of college. Dropout-non-vocational. Somebody who drops out of college and then receives vocational training Dropout-vocational. Somebody who drops out of college and then receives vocational training. Dropoutism. A neologism used to denote the phenomenon of people dropping out of college. 
Economic Rationality. Ability to make decisions that maximize earnings. The concept of economic rationality is well defined, given that earnings can be quantified. Some scholars have attempted to broaden the concept of economic rationality to include other variables, such as quality of life, but these extensions of the concept of tend to strip it of operational significance. Importantly, economic rationality, narrowly construed, correlates with other quantifiable variables, such as health and longevity, which in their turn bear on quality of life. 
Graduate. In this context, a ‘graduate’ is someone who graduates from college. 
Graduation-likelihood: The probability that a given person will graduate from college. 
IQ. Short of ‘intelligence quotient.’ Existing ways of determining IQ are deeply flawed, as is evidenced by the quite routine occurrence of people who apparently do poorly on IQ tests but whose professional accomplishments unambiguously indicate extremely intelligence. That said, however unreliable the standard IQ tests may be with respect to geniuses and other outliers, they have proven reliable and useful and reliable when dealing with large populations, and IQ scores generated by such tests are therefore useful in the context of this study. 
Graduation-probability: Synonym of “graduation-likelihood.” IQ-comparable (adjective). x and y are IQ-comparable if they are in the same IQ range. IQ-comparables (noun). x and y are IQ-comparables if they are IQ-comparable. 
Non-dropouts. A neologism used to denote someone who has either graduated or is currently in college and has not dropped out of college thus far. 
Non-attendant. A neologism used to denote someone who simply doesn’t attend college at all. 
Rationality. Ability to make decisions that maximize one’s welfare. The concept of rationality simpliciter is of limited utility, owing to the absence of clear benchmarks, and often gives way to the better-defined concept of economic rationality. 
RQ. Given a particular IQ, the corresponding RQ is the ratio of average lifetime earnings of college graduates with that IQ to average lifetime earnings of non-graduates with that IQ. So RQ corresponding to IQ 103 is the ratio of average lifetime earnings of graduates with an IQ of 103 to average lifetime earnings of non-graduates with an IQ of 103. 
Test-score. Refers only to SAT and ACT scores. Oftentimes, the terms “SAT score”, “ACT score” and “SAT/ACT score” will be used; but whenever “test-score” is used, it is used to refer only to SAT/ACT scores. 
Assumptions, Limitations, Delimitations 
It is assumed that the statistical data that we are not ourselves generating is reliable. This assumption is warranted, given that in each case that data was collected by a reliable agency, such as the National Center for Education Statistics. We are also assuming that is administering the survey that we are using will in fact administer it to populations of the appropriate types. For example, when we task the survey company with administering our survey to people who have vocational certifications, we are assuming that they are in fact doing so. This assumption is warranted given that we are using a reputable survey-service. 
There is one very serious delimitation, namely that it cannot be ruled out that survey-results express wishful-thinking and rationalization (Daviaux et al. 2016). This is inherent in all psychological surveys (Taswell, 2015). That said, there are three ways in which we are hedging against this possibility. First, we will always have information about the respondent’s economic-situation, simply the company that is directly administering the survey will target people according to their demographics, including their earnings. Second, each population will be issued multiple different surveys; and if there is a tendency for a given population to respond disingenuously on a given survey, its answers on that survey are likely to conflict with its answers on the other surveys (Cosco et al., 2016). Finally, the surveys being used are already widely used and their efficacy has been established. 
Summary and Organization of the Remainder of the Study 
Chapter 2 will review the existing literature relating to the topic of this study, along with the relevant statistics. The statistical data is abundant. The literature, however, tends to be inconsistent with or non-explanatory of the statistical data. In Chapter 2, we will state the relevant statistics in detail and provide creditable models of them. In Chapter 3, methodology will be discussed. We will identify the underpinnings of the statistical work done in Chapter 2 and will also explain exactly how we will be using surveys to elicit information relating to career-satisfaction. In Chapter 4, we will analyze the survey results, and we will also restate, this time in more detail, the results of our statistical analysis of dropoutism; and we will compare those two bodies of data. In Chapter 5, we will identify the changes in public policy and also in personal conduct that are indicated by the contents of Chapter 4. 
Chapters 1-3 are already complete. The survey has already been constructed; the survey-company has verified that it can send the requisite number of surveys to the relevant populations; and the researcher has already familiarized himself with the survey software. Chapter 4 should be complete before October 2019, and Chapter 5 by January 1, 2010. 
Chapter 2: Literature Review Introduction to the Chapter 
It is easy to find out how many people don’t graduate from college (NCES, 2019a). It is also easy, though not quite as easy, to find out how many people within a given demographic fail to graduate from college (NCES, 2019b). It is not possible to find information to the effect that a person's likelihood of dropping out is a function of his having characteristic X, for some appropriate value of X. Blacks are less likely to graduate from college than whites (Shapiro et al., 2017), but 43% of blacks have college degrees and 43% of whites don't (Stout et al., 2018), and being as opposed to white, or vice versa, therefore cannot be the missing value of X in “X is why people drop out.” Similarly, males are more likely to drop out than females (Strumbos et al., 2016); but the differences are marginal, and “being male” is therefore not the missing value of X. Dropout-rates cannot even be explained in terms of poor financials. High-performing college students from impoverished backgrounds are less likely to drop out than poor-performing students from wealthy backgrounds (Korn et al., 2015; Yeager et al., 2016), and extremely high performing students from impoverished backgrounds have only marginally higher rates of dropoutism than comparably performing students from wealthy backgrounds (Hoxby et al., 2013). So poor financials, though obviously a contributing factor, are not the or even a principle factor. Religious people are less likely to graduate than non-religious people (Tentsho et al., 2019); and people who have formally diagnosed as having a serious mental illness are less likely to graduate than those who have not been so diagnosed (Naslund et al., 2015). Even if we form the intersection of each these ‘less-than' groups---even if we consider the class of socioeconomically disadvantaged, religious, mentally ill, minority males--the resulting group still has a graduation rate of around 25%, showing that none of the characteristics is more than marginally responsible for the phenomenon of dropoutism. 
Given that we don’t know what causes people to drop out, we don’t know how economically rational it is for people who drop out to do so. We are given extremely broad statistical claims, e.g. ‘college graduates out-earn non-graduates by $1,000,000’ (Witteveen et al., 2017), that supposedly settle the issue—but clearly don’t. Does staying in college always conduce to higher earnings? If not, when does it do so? We do not have answers to these questions. 
Nor do we have an answer to the question: How emotionally rational is it for people who drop out of college to do so? In other words, to what extent does their doing so conduce to their fulfillment or career-satisfaction? The presumption is that dropouts tend to end up slaving away at menial, low-paying jobs, whereas graduates tend to end up having intellectual demanding, high-paying careers. But this presumption is indeed just that. In this chapter, we will review the literature, along with the statistics, bearing on these matters. The literature is of little use, we will find, but the statistical data is of use, if properly interpreted. 
Identification of the Gap 

Who drops out of college and why, and what are they gaining and foregoing by doing so? These questions have not been answered. There is an abundance of literature concerning the demographics of dropoutism, but the claims therein made, even if correct, have little or no explanatory force. 
Within the last five years, it has been alleged, by scholars publishing in reputable journals, that dropoutism is ‘linked’ to: 
*Ethnicity (Boyraz, 2016) 
*Drug/alcohol abuse (Ho, 2016) 
*Mental illness (Mitchell, 2016) 
*Religious belief (Li, 2017) 
*Video game usage (Cochran, 2018) 
*Absence of easily accessed academic advisers and psychotherapists (Lovelace, 2017) 
*Poor dormitory and living conditions (Li, 2019) 
*Level of parents’ education (Stewart, 2015) 
*Parents’ combined income (Stewart, 2015), and 
*Relationship status (DeLuca, 2016). 
Only 30% of African Americans graduate from college. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between race and dropout-likelihood (Tate, 2017). 
Dropouts are more likely than non-dropouts to play video-games. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between video-game usage and dropout-likelihood (Dunckley, 2016). 
Dropouts are more likely than non-dropouts to have experienced serious childhood trauma. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between childhood-trauma and dropout-likelihood (Dube, 2018). 
Dropouts are more likely to be religious than non-dropouts. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between religious belief and dropout-likelihood. 
Dropouts often cite relationship issues when explaining why they dropped out. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between relationship-status and dropout-likelihood (Legg, 2017). 
Dropouts more likely than non-dropouts to self-describe or be formally described as depressed or otherwise mentally ill. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between depression/mental illness and dropout-likelihood (Marsh, 2017). 
People from low-income homes are more likely than those from high­income homes to drop out. Therefore, there is a ‘link' between parents' income and dropout-likelihood (Jaschik, 2015). 
People whose parents did not complete college are more likely to drop out than those whose parents did complete college. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between parents’ education-level and dropout-likelihood (Casey, 2018). 
Dropouts are (in 2019) more likely than to be male than female. Therefore, there is a ‘link’ between gender and dropout-likelihood (Samuels, 2017). 
Let us scrutinize these claims. 30% of African Americans do graduate from college. So the ‘link’ between ethnicity is at best weak (Paul, 2018). 
Non-dropouts are as likely as dropouts to claim to be suffering from ‘serious relationship issues.’ (Greenburg, 2011). 
Although may cases of dropping do appear to be precipitated by mental illness, depression and suicidal ideation are in fact markedly more common among non-dropouts, being particularly high in those who enroll in doctoral programs (Wong, 2018). 
Students from “low-income” homes are indeed 600% more likely high income students to drop out, if ‘low income’ and ‘high income’ are taken to mean, respectively, “annual income of less than $30,000” and “annual income of more than $250,000”—but only because the graduation rate in the second group is less than 3%. Therefore, the ‘low parental income’ theory at most describes an ancillary factor. 
Religious students are only around 10% more likely than non-religious students to drop out. Also, this figure varies enormously depending on the religious denomination. Mormons have an exceedingly low drop-out rate, as do Orthodox Jews (Saxe et al. 2013). 
Non-dropouts are as likely as dropouts to be dissatisfied with dormitory-conditions, with their social lives, and with the degree of access to student-advisers and psychotherapists (Beiter et al. 2015). 
Dropouts are at most only marginally more likely than non-dropouts to play video-games excessively or to abuse drugs/alcohol, and there is some indication that video-game usage helps prevent those with severe depression from dropping out of college (Parker, 2019). 
There are deeper problems with these studies. Maybe people who drop out drink/do drugs/play video games because they don’t want to be in college. In other words, maybe their desire to drop out is the cause, and their engaging in these maladaptive behaviors is the effect. This is not a mere theoretical possibility, as we are about to say. 
Also, maybe soon-to-be dropouts are right not to want to be in college. Maybe they are depressed about being in college because they suspect that it is a waste of their time and money. Indeed, this is likely the case, we will find. 
African Americans with test-scores (SAT/ACT scores) in 90th percentile and above are minimally likely to drop out and are less likely to do so than their white counterparts. Students in the 95th percentile above have less than a one percent change of dropping out, regardless of gender, ethnicity, religious denomination, drug/alcohol use, mental illness/depression, relationship-status, and indeed any other given variable. 
A related fact is that students who drop out are overwhelmingly likely to be struggling academically, which is consistent with what we have said about IQ vis-à-vis dropout-likelihood and also with the fact, noted by Allen et al. (2017), that ACT-scores are predictive of graduation-likelihood. 

Figure 1 ACT vis-à-vis Likelihood of Having a 4.0 Average Freshman Year 
Adapted from: Jeff Allen and Justin Radunzel, “Relating ACT Composite Score to Different Levels of First Year College GPA.” ACT Table 4, May 2017. 
Approximately 80% of students with test scores in the 40th percentile or below drop out of college (Rothwell, 2017); and this figure is almost invariant with respect to gender, ethnicity, religious denomination, drug/alcohol use, mental illness/depression, relationship-status, and indeed any other given variable (Cooper, 2014). The totality of this data suggests that ethnicity, relationship status, etc. contribute to dropoutism only in those in the middle-range, suggesting that they function only as contributory causes. 
Also, there is a complete absence of data concerning the immediate aftermath of a person’s dropping out. It may be that their rates of depression, drug-use, etc. decrease. This is an open empirical question. If the answer is ‘yes’, then college was, to so speak, the disease, and dropping out the cure. 
More importantly, what are the economic prospects of those who drop out? Do they lose opportunities or gain them? We can’t answer that until we can identify some variable that correlates tightly with high dropout rates and then correlate that same variable with economic prospects. The literature has not identified this X-factor. 
Another deeply striking fact about the scholarly literature is that it always takes it as a given that dropping out is failure. It is not asked why students drop out. Nor is it asked what they gain by doing so. Sometimes it is asked what they would have lost by not dropping out; but the answers subsequently given are strictly financial in nature and, more importantly, don’t involve consideration of important sub-divisions within the class of non-graduates, in particular, the distinction between those who receive vocational training and those who do not. 
Identification of the Gap (Continued): The Statistical Data 
The academic literature is disappointing. The statistics are more illuminating. There are two statistics that are especially important: those relating IQ to test-scores (SAT/ACTS scores) to graduation rates, and those relating test-scores to IQ. The higher the test-score, the higher the likelihood of graduation (See Figures 1-9); and the higher the IQ, the higher, the test-score (See Figures 10-11). In each case, we are dealing with strictly monotonic correlations; and these nearly monotonic correlations explain many of the weak correlations earlier discussed.

The statistical data relating to ACT/SAT scores is independently corroborated by the fact that the graduation rate of a given college is a function of the average SAT scores of its students: the higher the average SAT score, the higher the graduation rate. To be sure, we are not dealing with strictly monotonic correlations. First of all, schools falling into special categories, e.g. theological seminaries, may have different graduation-rates from test-score-comparable liberal arts colleges. Also, there are cases average test-scores at colleges differ marginally (i.e. by less than 15 percentiles) but where the one has a higher graduation-rate than the other. But within these limits, average SAT/ACT-score correlates strictly monotonically with higher graduation-rates. See Figure 9. 
Once it becomes clear who drops out and why, it becomes possible to answer important questions that have not yet been systematically investigated, let alone answered, namely: What are the prospects for dropouts? What do they stand to gain by dropping out and what do they stand to lose? What kinds of careers do they subsequently have? Are they satisfied with those careers? How do their levels of career-satisfaction compare with those of non-dropouts? Answering these questions involves dealing with important theoretical issues, which we will now discuss. 
Theoretical Foundations and/or Conceptual Framework 
The phenomenon of dropoutism has thus far been studied from cultural, political, and emotional perspectives. It has been taken for granted that high dropout rates are a ‘tragedy’, and scholarship has consisted, with few or no exceptions, to find someone or something to blame. In some cases, the blame is placed on psychopathology: dropouts are alleged to be drug addicts, alcoholics, victims of childhood trauma, or simply mentally ill. In other cases, the blame is placed on classism or racism: dropouts do not have the right skin color or do not come from the right background. In other cases, blame is placed on inhospitable university environments: there are not enough student counselors or dormitories are too crowded. 
Universities have vigorously implemented policies based on these analyses. They have built new and better recreational facilities and dormitories (Taylor, C. G., 2017); they have hired additional academic advisers and, in many cases, have made round-the-clock tutoring services available to students (Han et al., 2018); and they have hired extra psychologists and substance-abuse specialists (Uddin et al., 2019). Meanwhile, dropout rates are not only climbing but accelerating (Facchini et al., 2019). 
The reason is that dropoutism is not a political or psychological or cultural issue; it is an economic issue. The less a person stands to benefit from a college degree, the more likely he is to drop out. The 
dropout rate for those with SAT’s above the 85th percentile is less than 25%, and the economic and professional benefits to such a person of a college degree are extremely high. The dropout rate for someone with the SAT’s below the 40th percentile is almost 75%, and the benefits of a degree to such a person are minimal. See Figures 12-15. 
Strikingly, philosophy majors are the only humanities majors whose earnings are comparable to those of upper end STEM-majors, and economics majors are the only social science majors to have such earnings. This is consistent with the high IQ of those with degrees in these disciplines. See Figures 15-20. 

Figure 6 Income by SAT 
Adapted 
from: 
https://pumpkinperson.com/2016/02/11/the-incredible­
correlation-between-iq-income/ 

High IQ students who graduate are overwhelmingly likely to be financially successful and to have rewarding careers. The outcomes for low IQ graduates are very different. If they do not get vocational training, they end up unemployed, intermittently employed at menial jobs, or continuously employed in low-level administrative positions. The less someone stands to gain by staying in college, the more likely he is to drop out. If they do get vocational training and have careers based thereupon, they have moderately high incomes and rewarding careers, but are worse off than IQ-comparable people who skipped college altogether and started their vocational careers earlier. 
Outcomes for low IQ non-graduates are highly polarized. Those who do not get vocational training are much more likely than IQ-comparable graduates without vocational training to be unemployed or turn to crime. Those who do get vocational training are far less likely than IQ-comparable graduates without vocational training to be unemployed and under-employed, and they out-earn such graduates by a wide margin. Job-satisfaction for low-IQ graduates without vocational training is extremely low, and it is extremely high for vocationally trained low IQ graduates and non-graduates. Low IQ graduates vocationally trained graduates earn less than low-IQ vocationally trained non-attendants. Low IQ vocationally trained non-attendants are more likely than IQ-comparable vocationally trained dropouts to earn more $100,000+ annually, and the latter are more likely than IQ-comparable non-vocationally trained graduates to do so. 
In order to understand these results, we must know what low IQ graduates are likely to major in, since this affects how they are likely to be employed. Let us now turn to this matter and to the corresponding body of literature. 
Review of the Literature 
In order to understand the economic prospects of dropouts, we must know what people of a given intelligence-level are likely to major. Fortunately, this information is readily available and does not have to be inferred.

This information makes it possible to identify where low IQ college graduates are likely to be employed. Low IQ graduates are obviously likely to have had low IQ majors, these being Criminal Justice up to Psychology (inclusive). Choosing psychology as the cut-off was not arbitrary, since it is much more internally IQ-differentiated than psychology and since the career-outcomes of business-majors are correspondingly more diverse, economically and otherwise. 
Non-vocationally trained graduates with degrees in low IQ majors who find stable employment are overwhelmingly likely to end up in the following professional areas (Abel et al., 2014 and Nunley et al., 2016): 
*Probation and parole services *Corrections and prison administration *Social work *Elementary school teaching and administration *Flight-attendant and airport ground-attendant *Secretarial *Human resources *Drug-counseling *Convenience store management *Intake-coordination *Customer service *Sales and telemarketing *Military and Coast Guard *Restaurant-management

Averages annual salaries in these professions are approximately $38,000; adjusted for debt, $32,000; adjusted for debt and taxation $19,000. 
Most people with degrees in low IQ majors who do not have jobs falling into one of these categories do not have stable employment. 
Many of the just-mentioned career-paths require credentialing, in some cases a master’s degree, additional to the bachelor’s degree. 
The presence of people from high IQ majors (religious studies and above) in these professions is less than 1%. 
Non-vocationally trained recipients of low-IQ degrees make up less than 1% of all successful (earning at least $20,000/annually) entrepreneurs.

Literature Review: Earnings on the Part of Non-vocationally trained Non-graduates 
The presumption is that those with neither college degrees nor vocational training are in the worst economic position possible. This statistic provide little support for this position. First of all, the probable careers of low IQ graduates overlap to a high degree with those of low IQ non-vocationally trained non-graduates. 70% of employed non-graduates with no vocational certifications, as well as 40% of low IQ graduates, have jobs falling into one of the following categories (Kroeger et al., 2016): 
*Server 
*Cashier 
*Warehouse/factory worker 
*Insurance-sales 
*Custodial 
*Agricultural worker 
*Police Officer/Sheriff’s deputy 
*Military (infantry/non-commissioned officer) 
*Security guard 
*Hotel desk-worker/bellhop 
The average for these positions is $40,877. 
Approximately 15% of low IQ graduates go into education and social workers, and 15% go into various other professions (e.g. lab technician) that either formally or informally require college degrees (Howell et al., 2015). 
SAT/ACT-scores in these professions are on average slightly lower than those in low IQ majors (Hutchinson).
