Literature Review: Financial Costs and Benefits of Low IQ Degree 
The lowest IQ majors are social work, education, psychology, criminal justice, and variants thereof. People graduating with degrees in these fields on average have around $25,000 in student-loan debt (Schmeiser et al., 2016). Since work in the corresponding fields often involves getting a master's degree or some other advanced degree, they usually have to take on more debt before they can be employed in those fields (Webber et al., 2017). Consequently, the average amount of student loan debt for licensed social workers is approximately $60,000; for licensed psychologists, $65,000; for schoolteachers, $45,000; probation and parole officers, $32,000 (Bowen, 2018). 
The median monthly amount of payments on student loan debts in these fields are $600, and it takes people employed in these fields an average of 25 years to pay off their student loan debts. This makes sense when it is taken into account that these are interesting bearing loans and also that median annual salaries in these fields just slightly $20,000 (Popescu, 2018). 
Student loan debt for non-attendants with vocational training is $8,000; for dropouts with vocational training, $13,450; and for graduates with vocational training, $31,000. The average amount of time needed to repay student loans for non-attendants with vocational training is two years; for vocationally trained dropouts, four years; and for vocationally trained graduates, 11 years (Odinet, 2019). 
Literature Review: Career Satisfaction 
How fulfilling are careers based on low IQ majors in comparison with careers based on vocational training? There is very little literature, and very few statistics, bearing directly on this question. There is considerable literature and statistical data indirectly bearing on it. 
First of all, there is literature, as well as statistical data, concerning levels of career satisfaction among probation and parole officers, social workers, and the other careers that graduates with low IQ majors tend to end up with. 
This body of literature is sparse and internally contradictory, with some authors saying that career-satisfaction is very low and others saying that it is moderately high, with each school of thought having its own statistics. No definitive conclusions can be drawn from it. 
The higher the average SAT scores at a given college, the lower its dropout rate and the higher the earnings of its graduates. Harvard’s average board scores are in the top 1%; its dropout rate is less than 1%; and annual 
graduate earnings are in the top 1%. The average SAT scores at Fort Valley State are below the 45th percentile for the nation; the dropout rate is over 70%; and annual graduate earnings are below the 45th percentile. Within these 
two extremes, dropout rates and graduate-earnings are strictly ordered according to average student test-score. 
Also, the higher the average test-scores at a given college, the smaller the percentage of students who earn degrees in low IQ majors. In fact, some high IQ majors, notably philosophy, are not even offered at the lower-end schools; and many low IQ majors are not offered at higher-end schools. 
When surveyed, dropouts seldom cite difficulty with coursework as their reason for dropping out. They are more likely to cite financial difficulties, trouble balancing schoolwork with family and personal relationships, homesickness, and inability to focus (Daley, 2010; McDermott et al., 2019). 
The statistics tell a different story. Less than one percent of students with test scores in the 85th percentile and above drop out; more than 95 percent of students with test scores in the 40th percentile and below drop out. Students in the first category take on 250% debt for every year spent in college than do students in the second category, but 80% of students in the first category pay back their student loans within 10 years, whereas only 20% of students in the second category do so (Webber, 2016). A student’s dropout-likelihood is therefore a function not of the size of his financial obligations but of his eventual ability to meet those obligations, and it is therefore a function of the financial benefit to him of a college degree. The greater than benefit, the lower the dropout-likelihood. 
Summary 
The people who stand to lose money by dropping out almost never do, the dropout rate being next to zero for those with test-scores above 85th percentile, these being the people who benefit the most from a college degree. For people in this category, the financial benefits of a college degree are unambiguous. 
For those who do drop out, those benefits are ambiguous. The lower a person’s IQ, the more likely he is to drop out of college, and the greater are the financial benefits to him of vocational work. 
This is not to say that those who drop out are always making the right choice. In many cases, they are not. Although unemployment among vocationally trained non-graduates is lower than that of college graduates, unemployment among non-graduates, on the whole, is more than twice as high. For those in the middle of the bell curve, the situation is objectively ambiguous: they stand to gain as much by staying in college as they do by opting for a vocational career. That said, the more economically rational it is for a given person to drop out, the more likely he is to do so. Dropout-likelihood tracks dropoutF-rationality. 
What is not clear is whether dropout-likelihood tracks dropoutE­
rationality. This is not easily deduced from information about the financial implications of dropping out. It is in principle deducible from other objective data, such as suicide rates, rates of drug abuse, and the like. But, firstly, supposing that low-IQ college graduates had the same rates of suicide, drug use, etc. as vocationally trained IQ-comparables, it wouldn't necessarily follow that they were equally satisfied with their careers. Second, the relevant data simply isn't available. For these reasons, we will ascertain relative degrees of career satisfaction through the use of surveys. 
Chapter 3 Methodology Introduction 
The purpose of this quantitative-correlational study was to determine the causes of dropoutism. This involved finding a principled way of determining the financial and emotional prospects for those dropping out, the reason being people considering dropping out likely make their decision on the basis of at least some kind of awareness of the advantages and disadvantages of doing so. When scrutinized, the statistical data clearly indicates that dropout-likelihood is a direct function of dropoutF-rationality. 
This finding is at odds with the vast majority of scholarly and non-scholarly literature on the matter, the premise of which is that dropping out is a form of failure is therefore a consequence of intellectual, emotional, or financial deficits of some kind (Xiao et al., 2017; Welton et al., 2015; Vasquez et al., 2015; Lavine et al., 1029; Swidey, 2016; Rivera-McCutchen, 2016). A strikingly high percentage of scholarly articles on this matter attribute dropoutism to mental illness (Bishop, 2016), even though the actual data is totally inconsistent with this (Kosyluk et al., 2016). In fact, those who are behaving the most rationally are those who are either maximally likely to drop out or maximally likely not to do so, since in those cases the financial consequences of dropping out are unambiguous; and people at those extremes categorically make the rational decision. The more remote a given student is from those extremes, the less the consequences of dropping out differ from those of not doing so and, consequently, the less obviously rational that person’s decision must necessarily be. 
Researchers and journalists tend to focus on the middle-of-the-road cases and are quick to pounce on even the feeblest indication of pathology on the student’s part. They blame it on depression (Ho et al., 2016), alcoholism (Kiciman et al., 2018), general lack of self-restraint (Nevarez, 2017), and the 
like, even though there is literally no indication that these mid-range dropouts are more depressed or debauched than those who don’t drop out. 
Researchers and journalists are quick to seize upon ‘findings’ on the part of university psychologist to the effect that 40% of college students are “binge drinkers” and that upwards of a third of college students are “seriously depressed” (Sharma et al., 2018). What isn’t reported is that if these figures were correct, then universities would have rates of depression comparable to those of most Federal prisons and alcoholism rates higher than among those who end up there (Holliday et al., 2016). Most importantly, if such a higher percentage of students are mentally ill drug addicts, the first question to ask is whether universities are the right place for them. Finally, we have found that college students are strikingly sane, at least when it comes to deciding whether or not to drop out. Also striking is that those at the lower end of the IQ spectrum are quite as rational in this regard as those at the upper end. 
Prior research has not correctly explained why students drop out or whether they are behaving in a financially rational manner in doing so. Nor, therefore, has it been explained whether they are behaving in an emotionally rational manner. In the pages to come, we will address this issue. 
Statement of the Problem 
Prior to this study, it was not known to what extent people who drop out of college are in doing so behaving in either a financially or emotionally rational manner. We have found that they are behaving in a financially rational manner. This suggests that they are also behaving in an emotionally rational manner. In the pages to come, we will determine whether or not this is in fact the case. 
Enormous resources are expended trying to prevent people from dropping out of college. Given that students tend to be serving their own financial interests by dropping out, those resources should be invested in college-alternative educational and professional tracks, so as to maximize the pay-off for them of leaving college, instead of being used, as they are now, to delegitimate their doing so. Should it turn out that students who choose vocational training over college higher degrees of career satisfaction than IQ-comparable college graduates, then there is that much more reason to reallocate resources in this way. 
Research Questions and Hypotheses 
Our objective in this study is to answer the following question: 
RQ1: To what extent are those who drop out of college behaving rationally in doing so? 
There are two versions of RQ1, namely: 
RQ1F: To what extent are those who drop out of college being 
financially rational in doing so? In other words, to what extent does their doing so lead to increased earnings? 
And: 
RQ1E: To what extent are those who drop out of college being 
emotionally rational in doing so? In other words, to what extent does their doing so lead to increased career-satisfaction? 
We have already answered RQ1F. the answer being: 
HQ1F: On balance, those who drop out of college are to a statistically 
significant degree improving their levels of career satisfaction. Now must now answer RQ1E. There are three answers to this question: 
HQ1ER: Those who drop out of college are thereby doing more to 
increase their level of career-satisfaction than to diminish it. HQ1EN: On balance, those who drop out of college are not thereby 
appreciably increasing or diminishing their levels of career-satisfaction. HQ1EI: Those who drop out of college are thereby doing more to 
diminish their level of career-satisfaction than to increase it. 
For reasons already discussed, we will answer this question on the basis of data generated by surveys that we administer. Whenever we administer a survey to a given group, we must also administer it to the appropriate complement-group. For example, if we were interested in levels of emotional satisfaction among people who drink tomato juice, we would want to survey consumers of tomato-juice as well as non-consumers. In the context of this study, there are several pairs of complement-groups. 
Given that we are comparing dropouts to graduates, we must administer surveys to members of both groups and compare the results. 
Given that a person’s dropout-likelihood is inversely proportional his IQ, the most important comparison to make is between low IQ dropouts and low IQ graduates, and we must administer surveys to both populations and compare the results. 
Given that dropouts with vocational training considerably out-earn dropouts without vocational training, and also out-earn IQ-comparable graduates without vocational training, we must administer surveys to dropout-vocationals and dropout-non-vocationals and compare the results. 
Given that low IQ graduates often receive vocational training and then have careers based on that training, we must administer surveys to members of that group and compare the results with those from surveys administered to low IQ non-graduate vocationals. 
The groups just mentioned are those on whose members a college degree confers relatively little benefit. In order to understand the results of the just-mentioned surveys, we must also administer surveys to high IQ college graduate and then compare the results with those of the above-mentioned surveys. We will also want to administer surveys to high IQ non-graduates. The results will directly help us understand how people with high IQ’s benefit from college, and it may shed indirect light on the benefits, or lack thereof, to other populations. 
The relevant independent variables are therefore (i) IQ, (ii) graduate or non-graduation, and (iii) vocationally trained or not. The independent variable in this case is degree of career-satisfaction. 
The researcher had no choice but to construct his own questionnaire, owing to the fact that no existing questionnaire sufficed for the purposes of this investigation. There exist many time-tested questionnaires that measure satisfaction with one's job, i.e. with one’s current position. But one’s level of satisfaction with one’s career is often very different from one’s level of satisfaction with one’s career. (A given physician can love being a physician but hate his current position.) Nor were any of the many existing life-satisfaction questionnaires usable, since career-satisfaction and life-satisfaction, though linked, are distinct. (A given person can have love his career but have relationship or family-difficulties that leave him unfulfilled.) There exist many questionnaires relating to satisfaction with certain specific career-paths, but these were obviously too narrow for our purposes. Further, it was necessary that our questionnaire not assume that the respondent actually had a career, given that many of the respondents were likely to be unemployed, intermittently employed, or menially employed. So even if there did exist reputable career-satisfaction questionnaires, we might not have been able to use them. Finally, we needed a questionnaire that could be used effectively with each of the following populations: 
* 
Low IQ non-graduates without vocational training, 

* 
Low IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

* 
Low IQ graduates without vocational training 

* 
Low IQ graduates with vocational training, 

* 
High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

* 
High IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

* 
High IQ graduates without vocational training *High IQ graduates with vocational training This fact imposed significant constraints on the structure of the 


questionnaire to be used. It had to be intelligible to people who varied widely in education-level, intelligence, and profession. It also had to elicit relatively specific information without leading the respondents towards a given answer. No existing questionnaire of the appropriate kind satisfied these constraints, leaving it to the researcher to construct one. 
A professional service was used to administer the survey to people according to custom variables, College Degree Status, Vocational Training Status, SAT/ACT-score, and college-major. Therefore, respondents did not have to answer questions relating to their test-scores or college majors and only had to answer questions relating to career-satisfaction. All questions were required. 
The possible to answers to each question were: 
1=Strongly Disagree 
2=Disagree 
3=Slightly Disagree 
4=Neither Agree Nor Disagree 
5=Slightly Agree 
6=Agree 
7=Strongly Agree 
The answers generated seven-point Likert-scale representing career-satisfaction. 
The minimum sample size was 115 for each of the eight populations surveyed, with a total of 920. 

For the purposes of our G*Power calculations, we operated on the assumption that we were generating a parametric two-tailed Pearson’s correlation. Technically, this is not the case. The variables in our case are, respectively, (a) membership in one of the eight populations in question and 
(b) survey-score. Neither of those variables is continuous, the reason being that, as is the case with any survey-generated correlation, (DiNunno, 2011) both of the variables range over finite sets of integers. That said, this inaccuracy is innocuous when computing sample sizes for surveys, provided that, for computational purposes, the correlation in question is treated as two-tailed Pearson correlation (Nardi, 2019). 
Surveys will be professionally administered online.The survey-service estimated the completion rate to be 76%. Consequently, surveys have to be sent to at least 151 people/target-population, making for a total of 1,208 surveys to be sent, with approximately 850 of them likely to be completed. 
Research Methodology 
The methodology used in this study is quantitative. When determining the economic rationality of dropoutism, we used a relatively strictly statistical method. We found a numerical variable, namely SAT/ACT score, this being a proxy for IQ, that statistically correlated to an extremely high degree (.<.02) with graduation-probability. We then looked at future earnings of both graduates and dropouts. We then correlated IQ with the financial costs and benefits of a college degree. Benefits vary directly with IQ, we found, and costs vary indirectly, and they cancel each other out in the middle range of the IQ spectrum. 
When determining the emotional rationality of dropoutism, our methodology was also quantitative. Having correlated dropout-probability with dropoutF-rationality, it remained only to see whether 
dropoutF-rationality correlated with dropoutE-rationality. Given that dropoutF­
rationality is expressed as a dollar amount and therefore as a single number, it is necessary that dropoutE-rationality also be expressed by a single number if 
it is to be compared with dropoutF-rationality. Prior to this study, dropoutE­
rationality had never even been ascertained, let alone expressed as a single figure, and we ourselves had to figure out (i) how to assign dropoutE­
rationality quotients to people and (ii) whose dropoutE-rationality was what. 
This was readily done. First of all, dropping out is emotionally rational to the extent that doing so makes one more fulfilled than similar people who graduated. Given that the benefits of a college degree reliably correlate with IQ and IQ alone, two people are to be regarded as ‘similar’ just in case they are IQ-comparable. Therefore, for people of a given intelligence level, dropoutE-rationality is hewed to, and may therefore be identified with, the extent to which they are more fulfilled than IQ-comparable graduates. 
Having thus operationally defined ‘dropout-rationality’, we must find a way to correlate degrees of career-satisfaction with graduation-status. Given the veritable absence of career-satisfaction surveys, apart from ones that were extremely demographic-specific, we have no choice but to construct a survey of our own, which we will administer, using a professional survey service, to randomly chosen samples of each of the eight populations in question. (SAT/ACT-scores served as proxies for IQ.) The results are expected to enable us to rank the following the groups by average level of wellness and consequently to rank groups (1), (2), (5) and (6) according to dropoutE­
rationality. 
(i) 
Low IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(ii) 
High non-graduates without vocational training 


(iii) Low IQ graduates without vocational training 
(iv) 
High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(v) 
Low IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

(vi) 
High IQ non-graduates with vocational training 


(vii) High IQ graduates with vocational training 
(viii) High IQ graduates without vocational training 
Research Design 
The methodology used in this phase of the study is both correlational and non-experimental. The study is correlational because we were investigating the dependence of one set of variables (personal and financial fulfillment) on another. An experimental approach is neither possible (given that the variables, e.g. IQ and educational status, were ones that we could not alter) nor necessary (given how many people there are who differ in the relevant respects but are otherwise comparable). 
Eight different groups will be surveyed, and respondents therefore must be pre-screened for test-scores (this being the best available proxy for IQ), college degree, and vocational training. Since IQ, college degree, and vocational training are the independent variables in this study, they must be screened for. Apart from those variables, sampling must be random, so that results are in no way predetermined by selection-bias. 
The minimum sample size for each of the seven groups was 115. Since eight groups were surveyed, the total number surveyed (meaning that they complete a survey) will be 920. Given the predicted response-rate of 76, the number of people to whom surveys will have to be given is 1,208. 
The objective of the surveys is to rank populations in terms of career-satisfaction, where the populations are defined by (i) their average IQ’s, (ii) their either having or not having college degrees and (ii) their either being or not being vocationally trained. Our concern is not ‘absolute’ but rather relative levels of career of satisfaction. Should it turn out that low IQ graduates without vocational training have moderate degrees of career-satisfaction, that would not be as meaningful as it would be should it turn out out that low IQ dropouts with vocational training have high levels of career satisfaction. 
Since the survey uses a seven-point Likert scale, survey results for individuals will expressed in a single number. Results for populations will be averaged, and these averages will be used to generate rankings among the populations surveyed. 
Population and Sample Selection 
There are eight targeted populations: 
(1) 
Low IQ non-graduates without vocational training, 

(2) 
Low IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

(3) 
Low IQ graduates without vocational training 

(4) 
Low IQ graduates with vocational training, 

(5) 
High IQ non-graduates without vocational training 

(6) 
High IQ non-graduates with vocational training 

(7) 
High IQ graduates without vocational training 

(8) 
High IQ graduates with vocational training 


Within the United States, each of these populations is in the millions. The general statistical rule of thumb is that given a population of over 1,000,000, if n% group of 115 randomly chosen members of that population has a given characteristics, then n±5% of the population have that characteristics (Nardi, 2018). For example, given population of 1,000,000, if 115 members of that population are randomly chosen and 75 (65%) of them like chocolate ice-cream, then 65±10% of the original population like chocolate ice-cream. In other words, between 60% and 70% of the original population like chocolate ice-cream. 
But these points only hold when the information that is sought about the population in question can be given with a simple “yes” or “no” (Becker et al., 2016). For example, if the question is "do you prefer to chocolate to vanilla?", then, if 115 randomly chosen members from a population of 1,000,000 are surveyed, there is a 5% error margin. But this error margin may shrink even if sample-size and population-size are held fixed. It shrinks if the answers to the survey questions involve rankings (e.g. “Strongly agree, Agree, Slightly Agree…”) and the answers are bunched around one of the extremes. Suppose the survey question is “how good a job do you feel Senator X is doing?”, and 115 people are randomly chosen, with all political affiliations, economic strata, ethnicities, etc. being evenly represented; and suppose that the respondent has to answer with a number between 1 and 10 (1=completely bad and 10=perfect). If 98% of the answers are 1’s, or if 99% are 10’s, then the error margin drops to under 5% (Hopkins et al., 2018). 
Also relevant is the case where a survey is asking for a ranking, as opposed to a simple ‘yes’ or ‘no’, and, moreover, the survey is attempting to elicit that information through multiple different versions of the same query. If answers on individual questionnaires tend to be bunched around one of the extremes and if, moreover, averages of survey-responses in their turn are bunched around extremes, then the error-margin is lowered considerably. Suppose, as before, that we are trying to find out how Senator X’s constituents feel about the job he is doing, except that this time, instead of just asking “is he doing a good job?”, we ask a number of different versions of this question, e.g. “has your financial situation improved since X took office?”, “has the quality of tap water improved?”, “is your neighborhood more safe?”, etc., with the possible answers, as before, being a ranking of 1 through 10, with 1 being maximally bad and 10 being maximally good. Under this circumstance, if answers on a given survey tend to be bunched around one of the extremes and if, moreover, the overwhelming majority of survey-responses are bunched around the same extreme, then the error-margin is much lower than ceteris paribus it would otherwise be (Benjamin et al., 2018). 
Finally, and perhaps most importantly, a 5% error margin may be more than acceptable in some contexts. Here is an example of such a context. The objective of a given survey is to establish a ranking of some kind among different populations. The survey is administered and survey-results are in. It turns out that, even after taking the error-margin into account, those results generate unambiguous ranking. Under these circumstances, the error-margin is of no consequence, even if it is wide in absolute terms. Contrariwise, if the error-margin has to be extremely narrow to establish the ranking in question, then the ranking is that much less worth establishing, since its extremes are that much closer to each other. We have seen how dropoutF-rationality depends on the variables in question; if dropoutE-rationality depends on them 
in a similar way, then wide error-margins can be tolerated. 
Instrumentation 
Since there no pre-existing career-satisfaction questionnaires, we had to come up with our own. That said, we had two sources of support in the existing literature. First, there is an abundance of cogent literature as to what constitutes a ‘dream job’, which is a tolerable synonym for ‘ideal career’ (Li et al., 2016). Second, there were reputable job-satisfaction surveys with questions that, when answered affirmatively, are consistent with the respondent’s having a position that he regards as being a career and a fulfilling one at that (Khamisa et al., 2015). 
The best and most research suggests that a ‘dream job’ for a given person is one that satisfies three conditions: The job-holder is good at it; it is work that he regards as benefiting the world; conditions are supportive and conduce to a ‘state of flow’, including reasonable pay, schedule that fits one’s lifestyle, non-antagonistic co-workers, and the like. 
Importantly, although reasonable pay is necessary for a dream job, satisfaction-returns on pay over and above that amount quickly diminish (Türkoglu et al., 2017; Deeba et al., 2015). This entails that compensation is not what makes a job reasonable: if the person likes the job, he will do it for a reasonable amount; and he doesn’t like it, no amount of money will make it fulfilling for him, even though enough money induce to keep doing it. Another interesting fact is that a job's being a dream-job doesn't depend on its not being stressful. If X is John's dream job, it will continue to be his dream job even it becomes increasingly stressful, provided the stress-levels are not incapacitating; and if X is not John's dream job, decreasing job-related stress, even to the point of non-existence, won't turn X into a dream job. 
In terms of questionnaire-design, the basic desiderata for psychometric tests, such as this one, are as follows (Sijtsma et al., 2017; Nardi, 2018). 
Questions must be clear. “Are you paid enough?” is clear. “Would it not necessarily be so bad if you were better remunerated than you currently are?” is not clear. 
Questions must not be tendentiously phrased. “Do you find your work engaging?” is non-tendentiously and non-judgmentally phrased. “Does your work, despite its menial and insignificant character, manage to interest you?” 
is tendentiously and judgmentally phrased. 
Questions should not be vague or overly theoretical. “Are your clients happy with your work?” is non-vague. “In the long-term, does the economy benefit from your work?” is vague and overly theoretical. 
Questions should not be circuitous. The respondent should not feel that games are being played. “Do you get along with your boss” is direct. “If your boss became a spokesperson for a fast-food chain, would that make you want to stop eating there?” is circuitous and manipulative. 
Also, the questionnaire should not be too long. Otherwise, response rates will decline and answer-quality will deteriorate in those who complete the questionnaire. 
Finally, when information about the respondent’s attitudes is being sought, it is necessary to ask questions relating to different ways in which that attitude might actually guide his thought and behavior. Simply asking “do you like your career?” will not elicit fine-grained information about the respondent’s attitude towards his career and may even elicit a defensive response. Fine-grained and sincere responses are more likely to be elicited by questions such as the following: 
“Do you regard difficult assignments as inconveniences or as challenges?” 
“Do you find yourself thinking about how to improve your professional skills even when you are not on the job?” 
“Do you tend to throw yourself into your work when you have personal problems?” 
“Do you work even when don’t have to?” 
The researcher constructed a test satisfying these desiderata. Also, the test is sufficiently similar to proven and reputable job-satisfaction questionnaires as to minimize the risk of its failing of its purpose, while also, unlike existing questionnaires, eliciting information specific to the respondent’s level of career-satisfaction (as opposed to job-satisfaction). 
Validity 
Validity is being ensured in the following ways. 
First, a reputable professional survey-service has already been retained to administer the survey to the eight demographics in question. This service provides automated, reliable analytics and always complies with legal and professional protocols. 
Second, questions are clearly and modeled on those of reputable questionnaires, within the limits set by the differences in subject-matter. Also, the survey company has a grading system whereby survey-efficacy is assessed before the survey is administered; and this surveyed a score of 100%. 
Third, questions will initially administered to small samples, and then scaled up when results from initial surveys show that error margins were unlikely to vitiate results. Tight, demographically-driven clusterings of responses diminish error-margins and warrant upscaling; and the absence of such clusterings warrant changing the survey, so as to increase confidence-intervals, before re-administering. 
Finally, we only need error-margins to be as narrow as was consistent with their establishing unambiguous rankings. The fact that one of the variables is discontinuous made any other standard of precision irrelevant. Therefore, once initial surveys established that error-margins are narrow enough to yield an unambiguous ranking, we will be free to administer the survey to a large population. 
Reliability 
The survey consists of 20 questions. Four of the questions are concerned with the respondent’s perception of his own abilities at his work. Four are concerned with his feelings as to his level of enthusiasm for his work. Four concern his beliefs as to how much good for the world he believes himself to do. Four concern his beliefs as to whether he is adequately compensated. Four concern his beliefs as to whether his clients, co-workers, and supervisors adequately appreciate his work. No two questions within any given one of these five question-groups are identical, but answers to questions within a given group should elicit similar responses. Consequently, consistency of responses within groups indicates a high-degree of reliability and validity, and low consistency indicates low degree of reliability and validity. 
The survey-company software approved the survey for clarity and ease of use and probable high (76%) completion of rate. At the researcher’s request (and expense), a manual review of the questions was conducted, in light of information concerning the nature of the research being conducted. No changes were recommended. 
Data Collection and Management 
The survey-service will prescreen for the relevant variables (test-score, college graduation, vocationally trained). Respondents will not be prescreened for income, since surveys must be distributed randomly within each population group. Potential respondents will be sent an email with a link to the survey. 
The email contains a description of the survey, the number of questions, and the nature of those questions. It is stated that the questions were multiple choice. It is also stated that no information would ever be publicized concerning an individual respondent’s answers. It is made clear that the survey had no relation to their work or any aspect of their life and that their responses, or decision not to do or to complete the survey, will have no effect on any aspect of their lives. It is also stated that survey results would be kept for a minimum of three years. The purpose of the survey is also stated, along with the disclaimer that this is research being conducted by a doctoral candidate and is not an official government action of any kind. The identity of the researcher is not disclosed, nor is any other identifying information. 
No ‘frill material’ is included. Itis not said that responses would ‘enrich scholarship’ or anything of the sort. It is not suggested or stated that negative consequences will follow from non-responses or certain kinds of responses or that good consequences will follow from completed surveys or from certain kinds of responses. The tone is neutral and no imagery or suggestive fonts, italicizations, capitalizations, colorings, or other iconographies are used. 
If email recipients click on the link, they are taken to page where this information is repeated and where they are presented with two boxes: I CONSENT and I DO NOT CONSENT. If they click on the first box, they are taken to the survey proper. 
Data Analysis Procedures 
Our research question is: 
RQ1E: To what extent are those who drop out of college being 
emotionally rational in doing so? In other words, to what extent does their doing so lead to increased career-satisfaction? 
In order to answer RQ1, we first had to answer: 
RQ2: Who drops out of college? In other words, what characteristics are dispositive of a person’s dropping out of college? (Is it poverty, mental illness, physical illness, unstable home life, low IQ, etc.?) 
We found the answer to be: 
HQ2: The higher a given person’s IQ, the less likely he is to drop out of college. 
In the course of generating HQ2, we discovered that: 
HQ1F: The higher a given person’s IQ, the less financially rational it is 
for him to drop out of college. This established the following earnings-ranking (from bottom to top): 
(1) 
Low IQ, non-vocationally trained non-graduates 

(2) 
High IQ non-vocationally trained non-graduates 

(3) 
Low IQ, non-vocationally trained graduates 

(4) 
Low IQ vocationally trained graduates 

(5) 
Low IQ vocationally trained non-graduates 

(6) 
High IQ vocationally trained non-graduates 

(7) 
High IQ vocationally trained graduates 

(8) 
High IQ non-vocationally trained graduates 


The need to discover whether career-satisfaction generated a similar hierarchy governed our data-analysis procedures. Questionnaires pre-screen for the dependence variables (test-scores, graduation-status, vocational-status). The survey-software will be set to do the following: 
(i) 
Provide data for each respondent 

(ii) 
Find average value for each question for each target-population 


(iii) Average these averages by population, as to generate an overall career-satisfaction score for each population. 
We are operating on the assumption that answers are up to 10% inaccurate, even though that actual error-margin is less than 5%. 
Ethical Considerations 
We will administer surveys by way of a reputable company that will not spam anyone, invade their privacy, offer incentives, or otherwise violate the rights of respondents or skew responses. The questions are phrased in such a way that respondents will know that their responses, whatsoever they might be, are genuinely valued. 
Limitations and Delimitations 
The results generated will not have validity across any society at all times or across all societies at a given time. This study is not intended to establish universally applicable sociological principles. It would be important to know how existing economic conditions and government policies affected the realities discussed in this study. 
Another limitation is that this study does not examine the internal structure of any of the populations involved. For example, the population of high IQ non-vocationally trained college graduates is internally stratified, with some of its member loving their careers and others hating them, and it would be worth how it was stratified and why. The same is true of each of the sub-populations. 
Finally, it would be worth seeing if the results we obtain, whatever they might be, hold up when larger populations are concerned. 
Summary 
In the first two chapters, we discovered extremely tight connections between IQ and both dropout-rate and dropout-rationality. With those in the upper IQ range, a college degree was more significant than vocational training. With those in all IQ ranges, dropout-behavior tracked dropout-rationality. It is unambiguously rational for those in the very low IQ range to drop out and that is they do. It is unambiguously rational for people in the upper IQ range to graduate from college, and that what they do. It is ambiguously rational for those in the lower-medium range to drop out, and that is what half of them do. 
Given this information, the question to ask is whether the degree to which is emotionally rational to drop out, in the sense of being conducive to career-satisfaction, tracks the degree to which it is financially rationally to do so. We figured out how to answer this question. Having already discovered that IQ, college-graduation status, and vocational status generated an earnings hierarchy, we simply had to find out whether they also generated a career-satisfaction hierarchy. 
Because there did not exist any statistics that explicitly or implicitly answered this question, we have to generate those statistics ourselves. Because there do not exist any questionnaires that we could use, we had to design our own questionnaire; and we did so, taking care to model it, as closely as its distinctive subject-matter allowed, on existing questionnaires. Because we designed our own questionnaire, we had to make sure that it covered the relevant variables, and we also had to see to it that it generated credible information. This was labor intensive but well worth it.

Is the Turing Test a good test of intelligence? (philosophy) 
The statement ‘the Turing Test is a good test of intelligence’ is ambiguous. On the one hand, it can be taken to mean that a given entity’s acting like an intelligent being is evidence of its having intelligence, meaning that such behavior is good but not definitive reason to ascribe intelligence to the being in question. On the other hand, it can be taken to mean a given entity’s acting like an intelligent creature is constitutive of its having intelligence, meaning that there is no difference between its being intelligent and its behaving (or able to behave) like an intelligent creature. The first disambiguation, so I will argue, is correct only if subject to heavy qualifications, and the second disambiguation, so I will argue, is unqualifiedly incorrect. 
Turing introduced the concept of the Turing Test through the following hypothetical. A written but rapid exchange between two parties is occurring. One of those parties is a machine, and the other is a person. A second person is monitoring the exchange. This person knows that one of the parties involved is a person and that the other is a machine, and he also knows what each party writes and when it writes it, but he has no additional information as to either one’s identity. According to Turing, if this person is consistently able to figure out which of the two parties is the machine and which one is the person, then the machine does not have intelligence. On the other hand, if that person cannot consistently determine which of the parties is the machine, then that machine has intelligence. 
We can use a variant of Turing’s own scenario to demonstrate the spuriousness of Turing’s own reasoning. Let X be the person involved in the exchange just described and let Y be the machine; and suppose that the exchange they are having concerns arithmetic. X is making arithmetical statements (e.g. “1+1=2”, “1+2=3”, “1+2.3”), and Y is evaluating those statements. If X says “23=8”, Y responds by saying “that is correct”; and if X says “23=6”, Y responds by saying “that is incorrect.” As it happens, let us suppose, X, though an extremely gifted playwright and poet, has a blind spot for mathematics. He cannot for the life of him understand why “23=6” is false or why “23=8” is true. Consequently, when ‘conversing’ with Y, the statements that he makes are often false. But he never generates those statements mechanically. In some cases, he draws on poetic inspiration; in other cases, he considers the aesthetic properties of the inscriptions involved; sometimes he grasps the mathematical principles but misapplies them; and occasionally he grasps those principles and correctly applies them. In each 
case, a great of intelligence is involved, but it is usually either the wrong kind of intelligence or the right kind of intelligence but applied in the wrong way. 
Turing would have us believe that, in this exchange, Y is the intelligent one and X is the unintelligent one. But that is the exact opposite of the truth. The reason X is so often wrong is that he is thinking too much, albeit the wrong ways; and the reason Y is always right is that its behavior is completely reflexive, with the qualification that, thanks to the intelligence of Y’s creator, the reflexes in question track the relevant body of truths. What makes a creature intelligent is not what it does, contrary to what Turing assumes, but why it does it. In some contexts, intelligence leads to the wrong behaviors and its absence leads to the right ones. To be sure, intelligence is more likely than its absence to generate the right behaviors (including the right statements). But given a sufficiently constricted context, an unintelligent or minimally intelligent being can outperform one that is hyper-intelligent: someone with little mathematical intelligence can be trained to produce sums and products faster than Gauss could and will therefore do a better job of passing the corresponding Turing Tests. In certain contexts, certain behaviors are relatively likely to be engaged in only by intelligent creatures, and certain behaviors may therefore be relatively good evidence of intelligence; but there is no single form of conduct engaged in by intelligent beings that cannot be mimicked by unintelligent ones, and for this reason intelligence is never identical with a tendency to engage in a certain kind of overt behavior in a certain kind of context. So yes—Turing Tests are sometimes good tests of intelligence, but only in the sense that they may provide reasonable strong evidence of intelligence, not in the sense they provide definitive proof of it. 
Like us, John Searle holds that overt behavior is nothing more than evidence of intelligence, and he therefore rejects Turing’s contention to the contrary. Unfortunately, Searle’s defense of his own position is a failure and actually gives undeserved credence to Turing’s position. Searle asks to suppose that, inside of a giant robot (or robot-like carapace), there lives a man who speaks no Chinese but who has access to Chinese dictionaries and pronunciation manuals, and the like. Despite not speaking a word of Chinese himself, this man has become extremely adept at using these resources to generate decrypt Chinese statements and respond to them with content-appropriate Chinese utterances of his own, which are electronically broadcast in a metallic voice to the external world, making it seem to those in the external world that there exists a Chinese-speaking robot. According to Searle, a consequence of Turing’s position is that there really does exist a Chinese-speaking robot; and Searle takes it for granted that those who hold this are simply wrong. 
The problem is that the behavior of this ‘robot’ (i.e. the metallic husk with the man inside it) is intelligent. After all, the sounds that it produces are guided by an understanding of what linguistic symbols mean and of what those meanings make it appropriate to say, and those who believe the ‘robot’ to be intelligent are not entirely off the mark. True—those people are very wrong as to the mechanisms involved. They believe electrical circuity, as opposed to biological wetware, to be doing all the work. But they are right about intelligence being responsible for the robot’s behavior. What about their belief that the robot speaks Chinese? Searle takes it for granted that this belief is false. But is it? There is somebody inside the metallic husk who is intelligently mapping English statements onto Chinese statements and vice versa, and this person’s behavior is responsible for the noises leaving the robot-husk. The essence of a given person’s supposedly mistaken belief that the ‘the robot’ speaks Chinese is the correct belief the sounds that come out of the robot are intelligent, as opposed to mechanical, responses to the sounds that are directed at it. 
In conclusion, although a tendency to engage in certain forms of behavior in certain contexts can obviously be evidence of intelligence, such a disposition cannot itself be identical with intelligence, the reason being that any behavior that an intelligent creature is likely to engage in can be replicated by an unintelligent creature. Consequently, if Turing’s position is taken to be that overt behavior can be evidence of intelligence, then it is correct but trivial, and if it is taken to be that overt behavior can itself be intelligence, then it is simply false. Though motivated by an awareness of these truths, John Searle’s miscarried attempt to refute Turing ends up giving the latter’s an undeserved sheen of legitimacy. 
Is the Turing Test a good test of intelligence? Second Version (philosophy) 
To say that a given entity ‘passes the Turing Test’ is to say that, in some context or other, its behavior is indistinguishable from that of a being whose conduct is guided by intelligence. Turing believed, for reasons to be identified forthwith, that there is no difference between acting like an intelligent entity and being an intelligent being. Turing’s answer to the question ‘is the Turing Test a good test of intelligence?’ is therefore ‘yes.’ 
In this paper, Turing’s position will be examined and refuted. I will start by examining the hypothetical scenario through Turing himself attempts to motivate what he believes the answer to this question to be, and I will use a variant of that same scenario to show that Turing’s reasoning is spurious and, moreover, that his position is false. Then I will examine John Searle’s attempt to refute Turing’s position. It will be shown that, although Searle is right to regard Turing’s position as false, Searle’s attempt to refute Turing’s position has little force. 
Turing introduced the concept of the Turing Test through the following hypothetical. A written but rapid exchange between two parties is occurring. One of those parties is a machine, and the other is a person. A second person is monitoring the exchange. This person knows that one of the parties involved is a person and that the other is a machine, and he also knows what each party writes and when it writes it, but he has no additional information as to either one’s identity. According to Turing, if this person is consistently able to figure out which of the two parties is the machine and which one is the person, then the machine does not have intelligence. On the other hand, if that person cannot consistently determine which of the parties is the machine, then that machine has intelligence. 
We can use a variant of Turing’s own scenario to demonstrate the spuriousness of Turing’s own reasoning. Let X be the person involved in the exchange just described and let Y be the machine; and suppose that the exchange they are having concerns arithmetic. X is making arithmetical statements (e.g. “1+1=2”, “1+2=3”, “1+2.3”), and Y is evaluating those statements. If X says “23=8”, Y responds by saying “that is correct”; and if X says “23=6”, Y responds by saying “that is incorrect.” As it happens, let us suppose, X, though an extremely gifted playwright and poet, tends to overthink trivial matters and therefore has trouble producing correct statements of arithmetic. Consequently, when ‘conversing’ with Y, the statements that he makes are often false. But he never generates those statements mechanically. 
Turing would have us believe that, in this exchange, Y is the intelligent one and X is the unintelligent one. But that is the exact opposite of the truth. The reason X is so often wrong is (so we may suppose) that he is thinking too much, albeit the wrong ways; and the reason Y is always right is that its behavior is completely reflexive, with the qualification that, thanks to the intelligence of Y’s creator, the reflexes in question track the relevant body of truths. What makes a creature intelligent is not what it does, contrary to what Turing assumes, but why it does it. In some contexts, intelligence leads to the wrong behaviors and its absence leads to the right ones. Given a sufficiently constricted context, an unintelligent or minimally intelligent being can outperform one that is hyper-intelligent: someone with little mathematical intelligence can be trained to produce sums and products faster than Gauss could and will therefore do a better job of passing the corresponding Turing Tests. There is no single form of conduct engaged in by intelligent beings that cannot be mimicked by unintelligent ones, and for this reason intelligence is never identical with a tendency to engage in a certain kind of overt behavior in a certain kind of context. So a given Turing Test provides, at most, highly defeasible, context-internal evidence of intelligence, and under no circumstances can a given creature’s passing such a test be regarded as definitive proof of intelligence; and the Turing Test is therefore not in any significant sense a good test of intelligence. 
Like us, John Searle holds that overt behavior is nothing more than evidence of intelligence, and he therefore rejects Turing’s contention to the contrary. Unfortunately, Searle’s defense of his own position is a failure and actually gives undeserved credence to Turing’s position. Searle asks to suppose that, inside of a giant robot (or robot-like carapace), there lives a man who speaks no Chinese but who has access to Chinese dictionaries and pronunciation manuals, and the like. Despite not speaking a word of Chinese himself, this man has become extremely adept at using these resources to generate decrypt Chinese statements and respond to them with content-appropriate Chinese utterances of his own, which are electronically broadcast in a metallic voice to the external world, making it seem to those in the external world that there exists a Chinese-speaking robot. According to Searle, a consequence of Turing’s position is that there really does exist a Chinese-speaking robot; and Searle takes it for granted that those who hold this are simply wrong. 
The problem is that the behavior of this ‘robot’ (i.e. the metallic husk with the man inside it) is intelligent. After all, the sounds that it produces are guided by an understanding of what linguistic symbols mean and of what those meanings make it appropriate to say, and those who believe the ‘robot’ to be intelligent are not entirely off the mark. Although these people are wrong about the specific mechanisms involved, they are right about intelligence being responsible for the robot’s behavior. What about their belief that the robot speaks Chinese? Searle takes it for granted that this belief is false. But is it? There is somebody inside the metallic husk who is intelligently mapping English statements onto Chinese statements and vice versa, and this person’s behavior is responsible for the noises leaving the robot-husk. The essence of a given person’s supposedly mistaken belief that the ‘the robot’ speaks Chinese is the correct belief the sounds that come out of the robot are intelligent, as opposed to mechanical, responses to the sounds that are directed at it. 
In conclusion, although a tendency to engage to engage in certain forms of behavior in certain contexts can obviously be evidence of intelligence, such a disposition cannot itself be identical with intelligence, the reason being that any behavior that an intelligent creature is likely to engage in can be replicated by an unintelligent creature. Consequently, if Turing’s position is taken to be that overt behavior can be evidence of intelligence, then it is correct but trivial, and if it is taken to be that overt behavior can itself be intelligence, then it is simply false. Though motivated by an awareness of these truths, John Searle’s miscarried attempt to refute Turing ends up giving the latter’s an undeserved sheen of legitimacy. 
Should Caffeine be a Controlled Substance? 
Abstract 
Average daily caffeine intake among pre-adults in the United States has increased more than 100% in the last twenty years, from 30 mg/day to 70 mg/day, almost entirely due to the popularity, especially among children, of candy-flavored, highly caffeinated energy-drinks, such as Rock Star and Monster. This development is a concern for many reasons. First, caffeine is a psychoactive substance, like alcohol or marijuana, and for that reason alone, so it is felt, is not appropriate for children. Second, there is reasonably compelling that caffeine use among children is linked to temporary episodes of ADHD-like behavior. Most ominously, there have been recent studies on rats showing that massive daily doses of caffeine can impair brain-development. For these reasons, the question arises whether the selling of energy-drinks or other caffeinated substances should be criminalized or otherwise heavily regulated. The answer to that question, so it is argued in this paper, is “no.” There is no demonstrated evidence that, when consumed in moderate doses, caffeine has any effect on brain development in humans, and there is in fact evidence that, in low doses, it has neuro-regenerative and neuro-protective qualities. Second, although there is reasonably good evidence that caffeine-intake in children can temporarily cause ADHD-like symptoms, such as distractedness and fidgeting, there is no evidence that it ever leads to antisocial or violent behavior. Third, granting that children should not in general be allowed to use psychoactive substances to recreate or self-medicate, there is no evidence that caffeine has become a gateway drug for children, or that caffeine use among children is dangerously high, or that such caffeine use as there is has to any degree impaired the psychological or physical development of children or adolescents. Also, because caffeine is the active ingredient in a number of over the counter medications, including all medications for PMS and Migraine headaches, and because caffeine naturally occurs in high doses in many comestibles, including chocolate, caffeine-related regulations would be unusually burdensome to businesses and expensive for the general public. Consequently, the benefits of caffeine-related regulations, including regulations relating to the sale of energy drinks, would be far too small to justify the considerable financial and societal costs. 
Introduction 
Caffeine is the most widely used psychoactive substance in existence. For the most part, caffeine is harmless, and there is growing evidence of the health benefits of moderate caffeine consumption, especially on cognitive performance (Brice, 2016). At the same time, caffeine is not entirely innocuous. It is toxic when consumed in large doses. Also, studies have shown that proper brain development does not occur in rats that are fed astronomically high quantities of caffeine over a long period, suggesting, albeit weakly, that moderate consumption of caffeine could inhibit proper brain development in human children (Doepker et al., 2016). Additionally, although low quantities of caffeine actually have a calming effect in children, there is reasonably strong evidence that it can induce temporary ADHD-like symptoms when consumed in large quantities (França, A. P., 2018). This last fact is significant, owing to the fact that caffeine consumption among children and adolescents more than doubled over the last thirty years. The reason for this spike is the popularity among children and adolescents of highly caffeinated energy drinks, such as Rock Star and Monster. Loaded with sugar, these carbonated beverages are as easy to guzzle down as any soft-drink, making them popular with people of all ages, and they have a candy-like taste, making them especially popular with children (Chen et al., 2019). 
What should the government’s response be? Should the government take a completely hand’s off approach and do nothing to restrict the sale of caffeine, even to minors? Or should it intervene and criminalize or at least penalize the sale of caffeinated products to minors and possibly other vulnerable groups? 
It is the purpose of this paper to answer this question. After considering all of the relevant facts, we will come to the conclusion that, although parents should be mindful of possible caffeine use on the part of their children, the sale and use of caffeine should not be any more regulated than they currently are. Although caffeine can be abused, the benefits of treating caffeine as a controlled substance, even in a highly circumscribed manner, would not be worth the costs. 
The structure of this paper is as follows. We will start by identifying the relevant statistical and economic data relating to caffeine-use. Then we will discuss the effects of caffeine on both children and adults. We will discuss its various benefits, including its medicinal uses, and we will also discuss the adverse effects that it can have, especially when consumed in large doses. We will then discuss the financial and logistical complexities involved in regulating the sale and use of caffeine, many of which derive from the fact that caffeine is present not just in coffee, but also in tea, soft-drinks, and chocolate, as well as in numerous over the counter medications, including Excedrin and Midol. We will conclude by synthesizing all of this data, our conclusion being that the sale and use of caffeine should be unregulated or minimally regulated. 
Important Facts and Statistics Relating to Caffeine-use 
Caffeine is the most widely used psychoactive substance in existence. In the United States, for example, approximately 450 million cups of coffee are consumed every day (Baron, 2019). In other countries, coffee consumption is much higher. For every three cups of coffee consumed by the average American, the average German consumes five, the average Slovenian six, the average Norwegian seven, and the average Finn nine (Baron, 2019. 
Importantly, coffee-drinking, though the primary way of ingesting caffeine, is not the only way of doing so. High concentrations of caffeine are present in tea and moderate concentrations are present in soft-drinks and in chocolate. Caffeine can also be consumed ‘directly’, in the form of caffeine pills, such as Vivarin and NoDoz. Also, caffeine is the primary active ingredient in so-called ‘energy drinks’, such as Red Bull and Rock Star, which, non-coincidentally, are also loaded with sugar, giving them a candy-like taste, and are increasingly popular with adolescents and, to a lesser extent, children. Coffee-drinking accounts for 70% of caffeine-consumption; tea, chocolate, and soft-drinks, including energy drinks, for 28%; and caffeine pills, for 2% (Brice, 2016). 
Unlike opiates, tobacco, alcohol, and almost all other controlled substances, caffeine naturally occurs in many foods and beverages. Moderate to high levels of it are present in chocolate, colas, sunflower seeds, and various other staples; and it is therefore present, sometimes in high quantities, in foods that contain these ingredients, including ice-cream, trail mixes, and health bars (Brice, 2016). 
Importantly, caffeine is the active ingredient in many different over the counter (OTC) medications. It is the active ingredient in Excedrin, Anacin, and every other OTC remedy for migraines, and it is the active ingredient in Midol, Pamprin and every other OTC remedy for premenstrual syndrome. Consequently, restricting the sale of caffeine to minors would involve restricting the sale of all such items to minors. Though theoretically possible, this would be expensive, with the costs being passed onto to consumers, taxpayers, and businesses; and it would also lead to irksome expansions of regulatory bureaucracies. In order to know whether these costs would be worth bearing, we must know how caffeine affects people, in both the short and long terms (França et al., 2018). Let us now turn to this topic. 
Health-benefits of Caffeine 
First of all, caffeine increases cognitive performance. Studies have shown that people do better on standardized tests when they are under the influence of caffeine (Grosso et al. 2017). Studies have also shown that caffeine boosts manual dexterity and, in particular, that physicians performing surgery, including microsurgery, perform better when they are under the influence of caffeine (Spiller, 2019). This is obviously linked with the fact that almost all surgical operations are performed by physicians under the influence of caffeine. This is true even of physicians belonging to religious faiths, such as Mormonism, that prohibit the use of caffeine (Kelly et al., 2016). 
There also appear to be long-term health-benefits to caffeine use. Several studies have shown that caffeine use correlates with improved long­term memory. Studies also show an inverse correlation between heavy caffeine-consumption and likelihood of succumbing to Alzheimer’s and other forms of dementia. Strikingly, studies show much the same to be true of Parkinson’s Disease (PD): “26 studies found that the overall risk of developing PD fell by 24-32% per 300 mg increase in caffeine intake (i.e. with every 3 cups of coffee, approximately)” (Spiller, 2019). There is also evidence that coffee-consumption reduces the likelihood of developing certain kinds of cancer, including oral cancer and colon cancer (Endesfelder et al., 2017). This is likely a consequence not of caffeine but of the antioxidants in coffee. 
Adverse Health-effects of Caffeine 
Caffeine can also have adverse health-effects. Here it is important to note that the effects of caffeine on adults differ in some respects from its effects on children and adolescents. In adults, the adverse effects of caffeine tend to be short-term, the most common ones being insomnia and irritability. Caffeine-intake also temporarily increases heartrate and blood pressure, but these effects subside within a few hours. Contrary to what used to be thought, caffeine does not cause arrhythmia or coronary heart disease; nor does it otherwise adversely affect cardiovascular health. In adults, the main reason not to consume caffeine is that it can cause insomnia, which in turn can have serious effects on quality of life. Also, people with mental illnesses, such as schizoaffective disorder and generalized anxiety disorder, often have adverse emotional responses to caffeine (Doepker et al., 2016). 
The effects of caffeine on children and adolescents are not entirely clear, but there is evidence that it can slow down brain development if consumed in large quantities on a consistent basis. Significantly, this conclusion is based on experiments involving rats; it is not based on direct evidence, such as the performance-metrics of children known to consume caffeine regularly. Moreover, the rats in question were given astronomically high quantities of caffeine (the equivalent, after adjusting for their body-mass, of 100 cups of coffee) every day of their pre-adult lives. Finally, because those rats experienced acute sleep deprivation, it is possible that their slow brain development was a consequence of their lack of sleep, not, except indirectly, of their caffeine intake (Kolahdouzan et al. 2017). Nonetheless, the study unambiguously showed that caffeine use in some way or other retarded brain development in rats, and the similarities between rat brains and human brains make it extremely likely that, if consumed in large enough quantities over a long enough period of time, caffeine can slow down brain development in humans (Mohan et al., 2015). 
This last point is subject to some important qualifications. First of all, when administered to rats in moderate doses, caffeine actually offset the damage done by brain-injuries and, in cases when the brain-injured rats were still developing, permitted the resumption of normal brain-development. Something analogous seems to hold of human beings, in that persons who take caffeine pills during a stroke minimize the brain-damage subsequently done by that stroke. Finally, studies have shown that caffeine helps offset the damage done to neonatal rats by instances of hypoxia (Meeusen et al., 2018). 
Importantly, when taken in low to moderate doses, caffeine actually has a calming effect on children. This is why it is often prescribed by physicians to children who have ADHD, the standard daily dose being 200 mg to 400 mg. When children do not have ADHD, 300 mg or more of caffeine may temporarily cause ADHD-like symptoms, mainly fidgeting and increased anxiety. It is extremely unusual for pre-adolescents to consume this much caffeine and there are no reported cases of caffeine consumption in children leading to anti-social or self-destructive behavior or of their having any adverse health-consequences (Meeusen et al., 2018). 
For ethical reasons, there have been few studies of how caffeine affects the cognitive abilities of children or adolescents. The only studies that have been conducted concern children with ADHD who have been prescribed caffeine by a licensed physician. Those studies show that caffeine improves test-scores in children with ADHD and also improves manual dexterity. It is not clear whether the same would be true of children who do not have ADHD; nor is it otherwise clear whether those results in any way generalize to other populations. That said, there have been studies of how caffeine affects cognitive performance in college students and other populations, and those studies all show that it enhances cognitive performance (Schmidt, 2017). 
As a general rule, caffeine temporarily increases blood-pressure and heartrate, granting that, when taken in low to moderate doses, it tends to have the opposite effect on children, especially children with ADHD. For this reason, people are inclined to suspect that caffeine-use increases the likelihood of stroke and heart-attack. This has been thoroughly studied and proven to be totally false. Setting aside people with heart-conditions, even high doses of caffeine do not increase the likelihood of heart-attack or stroke. The effects of high doses of caffeine on those with coronary conditions have not been thoroughly studied. There have been no documented cases of caffeine consumption causing a cardiac incident, even in people with coronary conditions; but because caffeine is a stimulant whose effects on such people have not been studied, physicians recommend that they moderate or eliminate their use of caffeine (Spiller, 2019). 
Pros and Cons of Caffeine-regulation 
Granting that the long-term effects on children of heavy caffeine use are still unclear, their short-term effects are moderately well-known and are sometimes negative. Whereas adults respond to moderate amounts of caffeine by becoming focused, children sometimes respond by becoming confused and agitated. Caffeine makes it easy for an adult to get through a boring meeting, but it can make it hard for a child or even an adolescent to get through a boring class. (This is subject to the qualification that it helps children with ADHD to focus and behave properly.) 
This is significant because, over the last twenty years, caffeine use among pre-adults has risen from an average of 30 mg/day to 70 mg/day. Almost all of this extra caffeine comes from carbonated, candy-flavored, and highly caffeinated so-called ‘energy drinks’, such as Red Bull, Monster, and Rock Star. A single ‘Red Bull’ or ‘Rock Star’ contains around 200 mg of caffeine—the same as a strong cup of coffee. But these beverages, unlike coffee, are palatable to children; and unlike coffee, they go down smooth and can easily be guzzled down. And this is just what is happening, especially with adolescents and pre-adolescents. Currently, there are no laws preventing children from purchasing energy drinks, and it must therefore be asked whether such laws should be introduced (Spiller, 2019). 
In the present author’s judgment, the answer to this question is ‘no.’ First of all, we must distinguish the question ‘should children drink caffeinated beverages?’ from the question ‘should the sale of caffeinated beverages to children be criminalized?’ Children shouldn’t spend hours watching TV; but if they do, neither they nor their guardians should be fined or jailed for it. When asking whether such and such should be illegal, it is not enough to know whether X is morally good. It must also be taken into account whether the cure would be worse than the disease. Laws are expensive; people have to be paid to enforce them; and before we decide to criminalize such and such, we must be sure that the financial costs of doing so do not outweigh the benefits (Friedman, 1962). 
We must also know that, in criminalizing such and such, we are not thereby criminalizing things that shouldn’t be criminalized. It’s well and good to prohibit minors from purchasing energy drinks and caffeine pills. But what if the minor is a 17-year-old who is suffering an acute migraine and needs Excedrin? What if the minor is a 14-year-old female who is suffering from wrenching pains due to PMS and needs Midol? In terms of its caffeine-content, a single dose of Midol or Excedrin is comparable to the average energy drink. (A single Excedrin has 65 mg of caffeine; a single Midol has 60 mg., the recommended dosage of each being two to four pills daily.) 
Of course, one option would be to be to allow minors to purchase caffeinated medications but prohibit them from purchasing energy drinks or other caffeinated non-medicinal items. This seems reasonable at first, but it starts to seem less reasonable when financial realities are considered. This law cannot be enforced unless police officers and prosecutors are given special training and then dedicate at least some of their time to investigating and prosecuting allege offenders. Either the public defrays these costs, in which case it has that much less money to invest in education and business and the like, or it doesn’t, in which case police and prosecutors have one more law to enforce but no extra money with which to enforce it, the inevitable result being that they cease to enforce some other law. This does not mean that it would necessarily be wrong to enact the law in question, but it means that before deciding to do so, we must know what we are gaining and what we are losing (Friedman 1962). 
The gains would be modest. Fatal caffeine overdoses are extremely rare. In order to die from a caffeine overdose, it is necessary to ingest over 5 grams of caffeine within a few seconds. This can be done, but only by ingesting caffeine pills or powder; it cannot be done by drinking caffeinated liquid, since the caffeine being ingested is metabolized before it can reach fatal levels (Turnbull et al., 2017). 
What about the effects of caffeine on brain development? Do they not warrant banning the sale of energy drinks to youths? They do not. The rats in the studies in question were given 300 mg of caffeine every day of their lives until they reached adulthood. The average rat weighs under 10 oz; the average person weights around 175 lbs. or 2800 oz. This means that these rats were given the equivalent of 84000 mg of caffeine every single day of their pre-adult lives. The average American middle-schooler, by contrast, consumes 70 mg of caffeine, making it unclear whether that experiment has any bearing at all on the effects of caffeine on human brain development (Winerdal et al., 2017). 
What about the ADHD-like symptoms that caffeine elicits from pre­adolescents? Do they not demand that we criminalize the sale of energy drinks to minors? No. Children obviously shouldn’t consume energy drinks; but that doesn’t mean that we should criminalize their doing so. Children do not respond to caffeine by becoming violent or otherwise ‘acting out.’ Their responses are internal: anxiety, restlessness, irritability, and the like. These emotional changes may lead to expressions of discomfort, such as fidgeting and squirming, but there are no documented cases of their leading to violent or confrontational behavior (Brice, 2016). 
The most serious consequence for children of caffeine-consumption is difficulty sleeping. Sleep-deprivation, especially in children, is a serious matter. But, first of all, there are no documented cases of children suffering from chronic insomnia as a result of caffeine consumption, and even if there were, the right response would not be to criminalize their behavior. Setting aside cases of violent or excessively disruptive behavior, substandard behavior on the part of children can and should be dealt with by parents and teachers, through instruction and discipline. If we try to solve non-criminal problems by criminalizing them, we will only create worse problems (Friedman, 1962). 
Conclusion 
There is no evidence that caffeine consumption among adults does them harm, and there is considerable evidence that it does good. There are studies that show that daily mega-doses of caffeine impair brain development in rats; but the doses given, when adjusted for weight, were over hundreds of times higher than any dose that any human being could possibly consume. Also, those studies do not make it clear whether the caffeine directly impaired brain-development in rats or whether it did so by causing sleep-deprivation. 
Children with ADHD who are given moderate to high doses of caffeine respond with improved test-performances and improved conduct. Children without ADHD who take comparable doses respond by temporarily having ADHD-like symptoms, such as fidgeting, but they do not act out or act violently, and the effects last only a few hours. 
Although there has been a large percentage-increase in caffeine consumption among children, due almost entirely to the popularity with that demographic of energy drinks, the absolute levels of caffeine consumption among pre-adults remains low. Moreover, there has been no evidence that caffeine consumption has adversely affected children’s health or psychological development, and there is definitive evidence that it is helpful in these respects to children with ADHD. 
Government regulations are expensive. They are expensive to implement and expensive to enforce. Oftentimes, these expenses are justified. For example, the laws that prohibit minors from purchasing alcohol, tobacco, and firearms are justified, given the obvious health-problems that such regulations prevent. In the case of caffeine-related regulations, such expenses would not be justified. Currently the sale of caffeine is almost completely unregulated, and there is no evidence of any kind that children or anyone else is being harmed as a result. Also, the introduction of such regulations would be especially thorny, given the fact that it is the active ingredient in many medications, including every single OTC medicine for migraine headaches and premenstrual syndrome. It is a distinct possibility that adolescents wanting a ‘caffeine fix’ might resort to buying Excedrin or Anacin, which, unlike straight caffeine, are far from harmless. Of course, the government could counter-respond by prohibiting the sale of Excedrin and other such medications, but nothing but suffering and inconvenience would come of any such prohibition, as anyone suffering from a migraine or from premenstrual syndrome knows all too well. 
Further problems arise from the fact that caffeine is available to consumers in a wide variety of forms, including snack-bars, teas, and health-drinks. Restrictions on the sale of caffeine, if enforced, would require a veritable army of regulators and would also debilitate a wide variety of businesses. The result of such restrictions would be an unwelcome and useless encroachment of government on the private sector, with no foreseeable gain for any demographic. It must therefore be concluded that, at present, any dialing up of caffeine-related regulations are contraindicated.
