Annotated Bibliography 
Brice, M. (2016). Seven Surprising Health Benefits of Coffee. Medical Daily. Discusses recent research that proves caffeine to have a number of important health benefits, including longer life, low likelihood of contracting Alzheimer’s, and improved long-term memory. 
Meeusen, R., & Decroix, L. (2018). Nutritional supplements and the brain. International journal of sport nutrition and exercise metabolism, 28(2), 200-211. A survey of numerous recent studies concerning the effect of caffeine on the bloodstream’s ability to transport oxygen and on the consequent long-and short-term health-effects of caffeine. 
Spiller, G. A. (2019). Caffeine. CRC Press. A comprehensive, non­partisan analysis of recent scientific work concerning the health-effects of caffeine, as well as the economics of the coffee-and tea-industries. Special attention is paid to recent findings concerning the probable link between increased neural plasticity and elevated levels of caffeine intake. 
Turnbull, D., Rodricks, J. V., Mariano, G. F., & Chowdhury, F. (2017). Caffeine and cardiovascular health. Regulatory Toxicology and Pharmacology, 89, 165-185. Describes a series of studies in which the effects of caffeine on cardiovascular health were shown to be positive and, consequently, not to be similar to those of other agents, such as sodium and nicotine, that increase heartrate and blood-pressure. 
Winerdal, M., Urmaliya, V., Winerdal, M. E., Fredholm, B. B., Winqvist, O., & Ådén, U. (2017). Single dose caffeine protects the neonatal mouse brain against hypoxia ischemia. PloS one, 12(1), e0170545. A detailed discussion of striking findings on the part of researchers concerning the use of caffeine to minimize hypoxia-induced brain-damage in neonatal mice. 
Yousefi-Nooraie, R., Akbari-Kamrani, M., & Mortaz-Hedjri, S. (2018). Caffeine for cognition. The Cochrane database of systematic reviews, 2018(8). A discussion of recent studies concerning the alleged neuro-protective properties of caffeine. Provides clear, quantitative data to the effect that caffeine improves performance on standardized tests. 
Who started the Cold War? 
The Soviet Union’s political ideology was inherently aggressive and expansionistic, and that—and that alone—is why the Cold War happened. It is obviously more politically correct to take the view that both of the two major parties involved in the Cold War were equally responsible for its genesis. But that isn’t at all true, for reasons to be discussed. Nor is it true that, once the Cold War had begun, the situation was morally symmetrical, with both parties behaving equally morally and equally immorally. The situation was fundamentally asymmetrical. This was because communism—especially, the particularly invidious form of it on which the USSR was based—was not simply one political system among others. Bolshevik communism was not intended to bring peace or prosperity either to the Russian people or to the rest of the world; and the founders of the Soviet Union had every intention of bringing about a war, or some kind or other, with the ‘decadent West’, especially the United States. 
“It is better than half the world perish and the other half be communist”, said Lenin, founder the Soviet Union, “than the entire human race live and communism perish.” Before World War II, the Soviet Leadership, headed by Joseph Stalin, had already deliberately starved one third of the Ukrainian population to death, as a way of breaking Ukrainian resistance to Soviet rule. In additions, millions of people in all of the Soviet Republics had been shot, imprisoned in the Gulag, or forced to relocate en masse. Freedom of speech was non-existent; private property was equally non-existent; living conditions were wretched; there were virtually no personal, religious, or commercial freedoms of any kind. Although some people obviously had more power than others, nobody had any legal rights of any kind. Stalinist Russia was the purest example in human history of a state that was inimical to its own people. 
In popular culture, we are often told that Stalin corrupted the revolution. “The revolution began with noble ideals”, we are told. “Trotsky and Lenin wanted to create a just and egalitarian social order, but Stalin seized power and derailed the Revolution.” Lenin and Trotsky had deliberately created a hyper-authoritarian state, and they used it to break the Russian the middle class, as well as most of the rest of Russian society. Lenin’s great ‘theoretical innovation’ as a Marxist was his belief that that, in order for a Marxist state to be created, it was necessary that a ‘revolutionary vanguard’, operating without any other party’s consent, autonomously take power and, once in power, forcibly impose communism on society. As for Trotsky, his great “innovation” as a Marxist was his belief in “permanent revolution” in all countries. In Trotsky’s view, the rightful objective of a Marxist “revolutionary vanguard” was to impose Marxist communism on all nations, not just on this or that specific nation. 
Lenin and Trotsky were in complete agreement on all of these points. In fact, their only significant points of disagreement concerned matters that were internal to Marxist theory and proved to be of no significance. For example, Trotsky, unlike Lenin and Marx, held that labor unions were not inimical to the long-term interests of the ‘proletariat.’ But since there were no labor unions in Russia, this disagreement was not important. Also, Lenin and Trotsky made it their objective to strip all Russians, including peasants, of all of their assets and to kill those who resisted. They rationalized this by saying that anyone who had any assets and did not immediately relinquish them was for that very reason a ‘kulak’ (meaning an exploitative landowner, comparable to a feudal lord) who, as such, had to be ‘exterminated.’ During World War 1, when the Red Cross entered Russia to provide food and medicine to the sick and malnourished Russian peasants, Lenin and Trotsky organized troops to drive them out of Russia, which they succeeded in doing and which they rationalized by saying that the Russian peasantry had to suffer as much as possible so that they would come to hate the Tsarist order and join them in overthrowing it. 
Stalin did indeed seize power; but he was only able to do so because the Communist Party had total power and he belonged to it. The Communist Party had total power because Lenin and Trotsky fought for decades to give it total power. Stalin and Trotsky were indeed foes, but not because they had important ideological disagreements. Quite the contrary, they were in perfect agreement as to what kind of government Russia was to have, their only disagreement concerning who was to be in charge of it. 
Stalin exiled Trotsky in 1928; and by the start of WWII, Stalin had long since had total power. Stalin used World War II to expand his power base to other countries, his first move in this direction being to absorb all of Eastern Europe into the Soviet Union. It is generally believed that this was the beginning of the Cold War. Not so. Before the Soviet Union had even been established, Lenin and Trotsky had agitated for Communist revolution in the United States and Britain and had already established a network of ‘fellow travelers.’ After WWII ended, the Soviet Union had real power; and Stalin, and the many others who wanted to spread the gospel of Marxist communism, had soviet military power behind them and therefore no longer had to rely solely on agitation and other such grass-roots measures. But the so-called ‘Cold War’ was merely a militarized continuation of a campaign to take control of the West that had been occurring since the first decade of the 20th century. This led to the wholesale Marxist takeover of China, North Korea, Cuba, and Cambodia. 
The United States’ role in the Cold War was entirely defensive, in every sense. To be sure, it made many terrible mistakes. It fought communism where it wasn’t a significant threat (e.g. Vietnam, Angola) and didn’t fight it where it was a significant threat (e.g. Eastern Europe, right after the end of WWII). But the Soviet Union was the aggressor. Also, the Soviet Union’s economy was completely broken; so even though its post-Stalinist leaders lacked the Marxist ardor of their predecessors, they had no choice but to expand, since their economy wasn’t self-sufficient. (When the Soviet Union fell in 1989, it was because it simply ran out of money.) The United States had a booming economy during the Cold War—and before it, except during the early 1930s—and stood to gain little, from its wars-by-proxy with the Soviet Union. It did not gain resources or land or trading partners; and it also lost good will and credibility, with its own citizens and with the rest of the world. But the Soviet Union had everything to gain from its engagements abroad; and those engagements where what kept it economically afloat during its short existence. 
In conclusion, the Cold War was a war of Soviet aggression, fueled by a commitment to aggressive expansionism that was built into its very system by its founders and catalyzed by its desperate need for resources. For the United States, the Cold War was a losing proposition; the United States did not profit from its engagements in Vietnam or Korea or Cuba or Guyana or Angola. Moreover, those engagements were public relations disasters. The only reason the United States undertook them is that it was trying to keep Soviet aggression in check. 
Who was a better President? 
Eisenhower or JFK? 
Ultimately, Eisenhower was a better president than JFK. This is not because JFK failed to accomplish anything during his presidency: he accomplished a great deal; and his list of presidential accomplishments is longer and more illustrious than Eisenhower’s list. It isn’t because he did too little that JFK was less of a President than Eisenhower, but rather because he did too much. The American people take it for granted that their President is supposed to solve their problems. This is largely JFK’s doing: thanks to him, government is now expected to be, for lack of a better term, a ‘big brother’— a righter of wrongs, as opposed to a neutral administrator, with the result that government is now so large that nobody’s rights are secure. Eisenhower’s presidency, by contrast, was emotionally neutral and, unlike JFK’s, did not pave the way for the situation we have now, in which the government is omnipresent. 
Let us start by listing Eisenhower’s main presidential accomplishments[161]: 
*He balanced the budget. 
*He created the highway system. 
*He kept America out of war. 
*He ended the Korean war and did so on terms that were at least reasonably consistent with American interests. 
*He signed the first Civil Rights Acts into law. 
Now let us state his main presidential failures (or what are taken to be such)[162]: 
*He failed to end the Cold War. 
*He failed to help the American farmer. 
*Economic growth, though steady, was slow. 
*He did not combat the hard-right, McCarthyite wing of the Republican Party. 
*He did not promote Civil Rights sufficiently aggressively. 
Notice that Eisenhower’s supposed ‘failures’ as a President were really about his not taking an interventionist, moralizing role in American 
affairs. When we say that Eisenhower ‘should’ have done more to combat McCarthyism, what is the justification? McCarthy had a position; other people had different positions. It may have been Eisenhower’s responsibility to curb criminal misconduct on McCarthy’s part, but it was not his responsibility to extirpate all support for McCarthy’s political views. So this ‘failure’ of Eisenhower’s was a failure only relative to an activist, Kennedyesque conception of government. 
Now let us look at Kennedy’s presidential achievements and failings, starting with his (alleged) achievements[163]: 
*He averted nuclear war. *He founded the peace corp. *He encouraged public service on the part of Americans. *He helped usher in affirmative action, equal pay, and civil rights. *He started the US space program. *He gave a temporary boost to the economy. 
Now let us look at his failures: 
*He gave arms to many (then) US allies, many of which are now dangerous actors. 
*He deepened our involvement in Viet Nam. 
*He failed to remove Castro from power, emboldening the Soviets to threaten us with Nuclear war. 
JFK’s achievements are impressive—but dubious. The theme we see running through his presidency is that it is the President’s job to be a hero: a righter of wrongs, as opposed to a mere administrator---which sometimes has positive effects (e.g. civil rights), sometimes has negative ones (e.g. Viet Nam), and sometimes has ambiguous effects (e.g. expansion of money supply and credit, leading to temporary economic spike), but has led to over-expansions of government in both domestic and foreign policy. 
Sadly, unless there is an actual crisis, it is the government’s job to govern—not to inspire, not to create a utopia, not even to eliminate all injustices. Eisenhower’s Presidency was a blank compare to Kennedy’s, his main accomplishment being the construction of the national highway system. Kennedy’s system was certainly not a blank, and he was obviously in many respects a singularly good president; but his legacy was that Presidents are supposed to be heroes, not mere civil servants, which has caused politics to become much more ideological and divisive than it has to be and which, consequently, has impaired the effectiveness of government. 
Who was a better President? Nixon or LBJ? 
Richard Nixon is now made out to be a bigoted right-wing extremist, and LBJ is made out to be the exact opposite, the conventional wisdom that Richard Nixon was an oppressor and LBJ a champion of the oppressed. But this position is not consistent with their actual political legacies. Under LBJ, and to a large extent because of him, the United States government engaged in aggressive campaigns to improve education and to fight poverty, unemployment, and racial discrimination. But the aftermath of these measures is increased unemployment among African Americans, a dysfunctional educational system for everybody, and a bloated and hyper-authoritarian Federal government. Richard Nixon, by contrast, did have at least some unambiguous successes as President and must therefore be regarded as better President than LBJ. 
Let us start by listing what many would regard as LBJ’s greatest accomplishments: 
*He created the welfare system, with its general financial provisions for those in dire economic straits. 
*He signed into law measures that aggressively racism towards ethnic minorities illegal in all but the most completely personal contexts. 
*He increased Federal funding, as well as oversight, of education by a factor of ten. 
LBJ’s greatest mistake as President is generally believed to be his decision to deep American involvement in Viet Nam. 
What about Richard Nixon? What were his greatest accomplishments 
as President? 
*Nixon founded the EPA. 
*Nixon officially recognized Communist China, which, by his 
intention, hastened the demise of the Soviet Union. 
*Nixon ended the Viet Nam war 
What were Nixon’s greatest mistakes? 
*He used the instruments of government to cover up a crime that he committed. 
*He deepened American involvement in Viet Nam before ending the war. 
*He started the War on Drugs. 
*He took America off the gold standard. 
With regard to the last item, I do not myself know that this was a mistake; but it is sufficiently often mentioned that I felt obliged to include it. As for the War on Drugs, for various reasons (the main being that penalizes lifestyles, as opposed to crimes, and therefore turns the police into thought police), I believe this to be one of the worst mistakes ever made in the history of public policy, and it is, in my judgment, the blackest spot on Nixon’s legacy. 
Given only what we’ve said thus far, LBJ appears to be the obvious front-runner. But not so fast! LBJ’s ‘War on Poverty’ created more poverty, especially among the very groups that it was intended to help. Prior to LBJ’s great reforms, 
*Unemployment among African Americans was lower than it is. 
*African Americans were considerably more likely to own their 
businesses than they are now 
*African Americans were more likely to be employed as engineers and to have trades. 
*Crime among African Americans was lower, as were incarceration rates. 
*Income-gaps between Caucasians and African Americans were 
smaller, as were education-gaps. 
*Public education was better and less expensive. 
*The Federal government was smaller. 
*The cost of living was lower. 
*Rates of depression and mental illness were lower. 
*Rates of homeownership, including among African Americans, were higher. 
*Levels of well-being, including among African Americans, were higher. 
In a word, each of LBJ’s signature policies, though admirable in intent, seriously backfired. Also, LBJ’s decisions concerning Viet Nam cost lives on both sides, while failing to advance American interests. 
By contrast, Nixon’s good deeds as Presidents have stood the test of time. By recognizing China, Nixon created infighting within the Communist bloc and thereby paved the way for the demise of Soviet Communism. AS for the EPA, it does water it is supposed to do: it protects the environment, including wildlife, and helps keep water clean and water drinkable. As for Nixon’s behavior vis-à-vis Vietnam, this, like many other aspects of his presidency, was ambiguous. But he did end the war, despite being under pressure from his own base to continue it; and that is more than can be said for Johnson. 
In conclusion, LBJ clearly meant well, but his policies to a large extent did the opposite of what they were supposed to do. According to economists, this is because those policies replaced the invisible hand of supply-and-demand with the dead hand of government policy, leading to a weakened economy and a consequent loss of wealth across the board, especially for those on the bottom economic rung. I suspect that there is more to the story. I personally do not believe that welfare is particularly destructive, economically or otherwise, and I don’t believe LBJ was wrong to implement generous welfare policies. But his expansion of government was too fast and too large, its aftermath being a government that is too large and is too involved in people’s lives. 
Technology-caused Job-shortages and the Need for Universal Welfare 
How, if at all, will artificial jobs affect employment in the future? Are you an optimist on this score, or a pessimist? 
Artificial intelligence eliminates several jobs for each job that it creates. This is what has been happening since AI’s very inception, and this is what will continue to happen. Some people claim that AI creates as many jobs as it eliminates. This is false, and it is the purpose of this essay to prove that it is false. I will start by showing that it is inherent in what technology is that technological advancement decreases the amount of work that there is for people to do. I will then explain why this is doubly true of AI. I will end by discussing how government should deal with the job-shortage that AI will inevitably lead to. 
Technology reduces the need to work, and it makes the work that it doesn’t eliminate less difficult. Thanks to tractors, sewing-machines, and gas-powered drills, it takes little effort to do in an afternoon what it would take months of back-breaking labor to do by hand. What is true of cars, tractors, and sewing machines is true of AI-based technologies: thanks to smartphone-apps and computer-programs, it takes little effort, as well as little training, to do in a single afternoon what would otherwise be impossible or would require months of intense intellectual work on the part of a trained professional. 
Many people have either lost jobs to technology or fear that they soon will. Right-wing economists have argued that such people are wrong. According to such economists, technology creates a job for every job that it takes away. In their view, technology took jobs away from cobblers, but it created new jobs for the engineers needed to build and maintain the machines that made cobblers unnecessary. Similarly, so they argue, AI is taking jobs away from stockbrokers, but it has created new jobs for the programmers needed to service the e-platforms that eliminated the need for stockbrokers. In general, so it is argued, technology needs people to service it and therefore creates jobs that take the place of those that it eliminates. 
This position is false and always has been. In societies that have little or no technology, the unemployment rate tends to be zero. There was no unemployment in Cambodia under Pol Pot; nor was there any in an Eskimo fishing community in the year 1600. The reason is the same in each case: where there is no technology, labor is inefficient, and everybody must therefore work. To be sure, new technology does indeed create new kinds of work: before there were cars, there were no auto-mechanics; and before there were smartphones, there were no app-developers. But the purpose of technology is to minimize how much work we have to do; it is not to alter the nature of what we have to do while keeping its quantity the same. If cars, tractors, and fork-lifts didn’t lighten workloads, we wouldn’t have much use for them. Consequently, there is no truth whatsoever to the idea that technological advancement creates as much work as it eliminates. 
Why was such an absurd position ever argued for? The main reason is that the economists who advocate this position want to minimize the amount of government-interference in the economy. Their position is that government regulation restricts commerce and thwarts economic development: and in most contexts, they are right, since a society cannot function for long unless its members are allowed to exchange goods and services. But even if unregulated societies economically outperform regulated ones, it doesn’t follow that the government should never regulate. Second, unemployment benefits are not a form of government regulation and don’t have the same invidious economic effects as regulations. The law that prevents people from teaching unless they have a master’s degree in education is a regulation, since it prevents the market from deciding who works and gives that power to the government. The laws prevent people from killing and robbing are not regulations, since they don’t do this, and neither, for the same reason, are the laws that give people welfare benefits. To be sure, welfare benefits are not economically innocuous, since they have to be paid for out of people’s taxes; but granting that excessive taxation hurts an economy, moderate taxation is sometimes a necessity, this being a case in point. 
Technology has always eliminated jobs; but in times past, the contrary position, though false, was at least defensible. Pre-AI technology did not run itself, and its effect was not so much to eliminate employment as it was to make employment less about physical labor and more about knowing how to operate labor-saving machines. When the tractor came into existence, agricultural work became that much less about physical labor and that much more about knowing how to operate a tractor. But the story is very different when tractors are replaced by self-operating tractors and when, more generally, machines are replaced by robots. True—people will still be needed to maintain those robots, but there will be very few such jobs in relation to the number of labor jobs lost, and those jobs will also require unusually high levels of training and intellectual acumen. In general, a slight improvements in technology creates massive gains in efficiency and a corresponding drop in the demand for a given person’s labor. 
This is cause for alarm if people are still required to work for food, lodging, and other necessities, since new technology, especially AI-based technology, makes most people economically redundant. But it is not a cause for alarm if people are not forced to work for basic necessities. And thanks to the surpluses of wealth created by the very technologies that make most people economically redundant, there is no reason for most people to work. 
The standard response to this is that, unless people are forced to work, they will become parasites and society will fall apart. But the truth is that most people right now are parasites: most jobs are not necessary and cost society more than they are worth. Somebody who receives $20,000 in welfare is quite harmless. But a redundant police officer or government bureaucrat is not harmless, since, unlike the welfare recipient, that person has power. This is not to say that all government employees and police officers are useless. But there is no way to create jobs for people without giving them at least some power. Even the lowliest and most unnecessary clerk can jam up legitimate business—and is likely to do so to make himself feel important. Creating jobs for unemployable people gives them the ability to prevent employable people from contributing, and it is therefore best simply to give them money for basic necessities. Ironically, the belief of right-wing economists that everyone should have to compete in the arena of commerce, with its consequent refusal on the part of government to give welfare to people who simply cannot do so, leads to the creation of vast numbers of jobs that benefit nobody, including those who have them. I therefore suggest that a universal welfare policy be instituted. In this I am in agreement with many distinguished people, including economically informed right-wing figures, such as Richard Nixon, who was one of the first public figures to advocate such a policy, as well as technology-wise left-wing figures, such as Mark Zuckerberg. 
My Reaction to Schoenberg’s A Survivor from Warsaw https://www.youtube.com/wa 
v=DFXkc9AGoeU 

The ‘point’ of this work, so to speak, is that the events in question were not simply dark but fell outside of what human beings are able to process; and this work conveys that point by being atonal and, on that basis, deriding and caricaturing pre-existing musical norms, rather than deploying them in any ordinary way. The first sounds that we hear are of an almost triumphant-sounding trumpet (01:-04). The motif is almost like that of a national anthem (e.g. the Chinese National Anthem, which is based on what sounds like a tonal version of this same trumpet-riff). But the modal is askew, being atonal, and is quickly met with a dark, semi-atonal orchestral background. Incidentally, the cello motif immediately heard thereafter sounds like Philip Glass, and I suspect that he may even have consciously borrowed it. (Cf. https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6Stu7h7Qup8 
32:09) 
The rest of this work followed suit, all of it being on the edges of tonal music and all of it being, as it were, derogatory of it and, so it seems, of the cultural norms thereby represented, the work’s overall point seemingly being that what was happening in the world could no longer be understood in the usual rationality-based ways. What was a little hard to understand—or at least to fit into this way of understanding this work---was Chorale part, sung in Hebrew, as to the Jewish people’s future responsibilities to itself. The lyrics were straightforward and clearly non-ironic; but the accompanying music was atonal and, so it seemed, derisive, making it unclear whether the words being sung were to be taken literally, on the one hand, or, on the other hand, dismissed, in light of recent events, as naïve. I suspect that they were meant to be taken literally and were accompanied by atonal, ambiguous-sounding music only because that was the genre in which Schoenberg happened to composer. 
I chose to listen to the video that did not feature Holocaust pictures. This was because I wanted to listen to the music without being ‘told’, so to speak, how to react. (The singing, especially the solo singing in English, did, however, tell me, quite literally, what I was to be thinking and feeling; and I was a bit surprised at this brazen violation of the ‘show, don’t tell’ rule governing theatrical performances.) Frankly, I found this work easy to listen to, precisely because it doesn’t use conventional tonality. I was able to listen to each bar on its own, without having to feel that I had to embed in a larger musical narrative. Also, even though this music is classified as ‘atonal’, it is not, literally speaking, atonal; it is only relatively atonal. Relatedly, I think that contemporary audiences would find this piece much less jarring than Schoenberg’s contemporaries, mainly because so much film music sounds like this, probably because, so I suspect, Schoenberg’s music itself influenced film-composers. 
And this last point, I think, is central to what is going with Schoenberg’s music. Bach’s music is not meant to represent anything; neither is Mozart’s or Beethoven’s. These composers did, of course, compose masses and operas and other story-accompanying works; but their music is always, ultimately, to be listened to by itself. Schoenberg’s music, by contrast, sounds like a movie score and is easy to listen if listened to in that way. But if listened to on its own, in the way in which Mozart or Beethoven are listened to, it is a bit grating. I would conjecture that part of what allowed Schoenberg to compose atonal music was that his music was, at least by his intention, to be listened to as an accompaniment to a play or some such, and not on its own, so that the dissonances in it as comments on an independent story-line, and not as musical ends unto themselves.

Identify an argument in an article, book etc. that does poorly on TWO or more of the 7 standards Paul & Elder say help assess critical thinking. Briefly discuss. 
The book I chose is 'Being and Time' by Martin Heidegger. I have been told that it is a great work; but I found scarcely a single intelligible sentence in it. Plus, I also found repeated use of the same undefined terms ("existential", "authenticity", "throwness"), often with confusing variations. So far as I could understand it, it seemed to be saying that human beings are being true to themselves when they work with their hands but not when they work for companies or bureaucracies, the idea being that the latter are soul-robbingly parasitical organizations. Which--if that is what Heidegger is saying-is fine, but could have been, and has been said, better and certainly more concisely. I'd have to give this work an F on clarity, logic, and organization. 
Here is a characteristic quotation: 
"inquiring or in choosing. This holds true-and by no means least-for that understanding which is rooted in Dasein's ownmost Being, and for the possibility of developing it-namely, for ontological understanding. When tradition thus becomes master, it does so in such a way that what it 'transmits' is made so inaccessible, proximally and for the most part, that it rather becomes concealed. Tradition takes what has come down to us and delivers it over to self-evidence; it blocks our access to those primordial 'sources' from which the categories and concepts handed down to us have been in part quite genuinely drawn'! Indeed it makes us forget that they have had such an origin, and makes us suppose that the necessity of going back to these sources is something which we need not even understand. Dasein has had its historicality so thoroughly uprooted by tradition that it confines its interest to the multiformity of possible types, directions, and standpoints of philosophical activity in the most exotic and alien of cultures; and by this very interest it seeks to veil the fact that it has no ground of its Own to stand on. Consequently, despite all its iological interests and all its zeal for an Interpretation which is philologically 'objective' ["sachliche"], Dasein no longer understands the most elcmentary conditions which would alone enable it to go back to the past in a positive manner and make it productively its own. We have shown at the outset (Section I) not only that the question of the meaning of Being is one that has not been attended to and one that has been inadequately formulated, but that it has become quite forgotten in spite of all our interest in 'metaphysics'. Greek ontology and its history -which, in their numerous filiations and distortions, determine the conceptual character of philosophy even today-prove that when Dasein 22 understands either itself or Being in general, it does so in terms of the 'world', and that the ontology which has thus arisen has deteriorated to a tradition in which it gets reduced to something self-evident -merely material for reworking, as it was for In the Middle Ages this uprooted Greek ontology became a fixed body of doctrine. Its systematics, however, is by no means a mere joining together of traditional pieces into a single edifice. Though its basic conceptions of Being have been taken over dogmatically from the Greeks, a great deal of unpretentious work has been carried on further within these limits. With the peculiar character which the Scholastics gave it, Greek ontology has, in its essentials, travelled the path that leads through the Disputationes metaphysicae of Suarez to the 'metaphysics' and transcendental philosophy of modern times, determining even the foundations and the aims of Hegel's..."

Response to Posts about Descartes’ Evil Demon (my material in italics)

Great post! You made a profound point, namely, that the Evil Genius scenario is concerned not just with epistemology but also with political and even personal life. Ordinarily, the Evil Genius scenario is taken as a way of motivating skepticism, its purpose being to show that our sensory experiences could in principle be hallucinations. But if Descartes’ objective had been to make a strictly epistemological point, he wouldn’t have used such a pregnant metaphor; he would have attributed the hallucinations in question to impersonal forces, such as a chemical balance, not to a sentient being, let alone a malicious God-like one. 
During this time, science was proving the erroneousness of Scripture and the Catholic Church was being outed as a malicious deceiver. And as this was happening, the Church responded maliciously and defensively, sometimes simply killing the scientists in question. I believe that the ‘demon’ of which Descartes speaks was a metaphor for the Catholic Church. I do not believe that this was by Descartes’ conscious intention, but it was nonetheless intentional, albeit unconsciously so. 
Madisen Domayer posted Oct 14, 2019 4:48 PM 
The argument of the evil genius is confusing. Trying to understand that we “live” in a world where everything is full of deceptions is hard to grasp. It hard to imagine everything I think or see as being false, or possibly false. I think this relates to his argument of conscious vs. consciousness. Conscious refers to what we believe while consciousness is what is true. According to the evil genius theory, our conscious and consciousness are not the same, for our conscious is full of deception. One way to disprove this theory is finding ideas that we can ensure exist without doubt, such as counting numbers and knowing the alphabet. This concept relates to the Cogito Ergo Sum argument. Since I think something is true, it has to exist, despite the evil genius theory. If I can think it, it is real. I think these two theories can become confusing, but if you think of them as two separate idea, even two that contract teach other, they seem to make more since. 
I don’t think the Evil Genius scenario is quite as opaque as you make it out to be. The idea is simply that our sense-perceptions could be hallucinations. I grant that Descartes muddies the waters a bit by attributing those hallucinations to an ‘evil demon’, which introduces potentially confusing political and emotional resonances into the scenario. But the point is clear enough: what we regard as accurate awarenesses could be delusions. 
In response to which, Descartes rightly asks: What is it that I could not fail to be right about? What is my Archimedean point in my question for knowledge? Supposing that I am the victim of deliberate and systematic deception, what is that, for all that, I could not possibly be deceived about? Descartes’ answer is—I know that I doubt, and therefore think, and therefore exist. In a word—I think, therefore I am. The mere existence of doubt proves the existence of thought and, consequently, of one’s own existence. And Descartes is right about this: one’s own thought, even if that thought assumes the form of doubt, affirms one’s own existence. 
Descartes also believes that, on this slender basis, he can prove that we in fact know what we think we know—that, in particular, we can prove that, as a general rule, our sensory experiences are accurate, the same being true, with some qualifications, of the inferences we draw from them. Although I agree with Descartes that we do in fact have much of the knowledge that we attribute to ourselves, I don’t know that ‘I think, therefore I am’ is, or even could be, the basis of that knowledge. The actual basis of our knowledge of the external world is derived from considerations of continuity and coherence, as in, we must posit an external world answering to our ‘perceptual narrative’ in order to render the gaps in that narrative non-anomalous. Elasticity of demand formula Elasticity = %change in demand/%change in price

Summary of Video about Milgram Experiment 
https://www.youtube.com/watch? 
v=f97VYdkHZK8 

Critical Viewing Form 
BA 3102 
Instructions: Please address each item below for each movie. The total length of your critical viewing form responses should be approximately one-half to one page, single spaced. This form will be graded on a pass/fail basis. To pass, you need to provide reasonably detailed and insightful answers to the items below. 
1. Briefly summarize the basic plot, or issue that the movie addresses. 
The video concerned the Milgram experiment, along with a recent recreation thereof. In these experiments, volunteers were ordered to commit acts that, so they were led to believe, caused intense pain to human beings. (In reality, the people being tortured were actors and were not experiencing pain, but the volunteers did not know this and had every reason to believe otherwise.) 90% of the volunteers followed orders to a tee, their ‘excuse’ (or ‘justification’, depending on how you look it) being ‘I had my orders; the rest is not my responsibility.’ The point of these videos seems to be that people don’t take responsibility for their behavior unless they have to. 
2. What do you think is the most interesting point in the movie? 
I found it interesting that there was little or no difference between men and women in respect of how willing they were to follow orders even when those orders involved inflicting pain. Unlike the commentators, however, I did not find the result particularly surprising. Supposing for argument’s sake that women are more empathic, this empathy can either play out in the form of empathic connectedness to one’s supervisor or to one’s victim; and, as it turns out, it is more likely to play out in the former way. 
3. What is the most controversial statement you’ve heard? 
It may be that my own life-experiences have biased me, or it may be that I have already internalized and accepted the results of the Milgram experiment; but what I found most controversial was the assumption, made by all parties involved, that a human being’s go-to is just behavior and that unjust behavior requires that special rationalizations be operative. In my own experience, people tend to take the path of least resistance; they do what is expedient, not for the most part what is principled. To be sure, setting aside the odd psychopath, people choose expediency within the limits set by social acceptability; but, in my own experience, people who act with integrity despite strong social incentives to act without it are more the exception than the rule; and I found the frequently made assertion to the contrary to be particularly controversial, at least relative to my own starting assumptions. 
4. What is the most important ethical issue that the movie is addressing? Please explain. 
Do people do what is right or what is expedient? If the movie is to be trusted, which it is, the answer is: they do what is expedient. It may not have been the intention of Stanley Milgram or of the movie-makers to make this point; perhaps it was their intention to make a case that people’s moral judgment is easily skewed, with the result that, despite having the best of intentions, they act wrongly. And maybe that is what it shows: but I think the reality uncovered here is simply that people are more self-interested than ethical. 
Racism and Sexism 
BA 3102 Online Reflection Paper #2 Instructions 
Please take the Implicit Association Test and reflect on your results. The test is on the web, located at www.understandingprejudice.org/iat. Take the race and gender/career IAT tests. You need not report your scores. In your paper, please answer these questions: 
1. 
Do you think that the tests measure unconscious bias with respect to race and gender? Please be specific in discussing aspects of the test that you think are or are not valid for measuring unconscious bias. 

2. 
Either way, do you think unconscious bias exists in society (regarding race and/or gender)? If not, why not; if you think it does, how might it show itself in the organizational context? 


If you have questions about how the test works, go to https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/demo/background/faqs.html; it may be able to answer them. 
Please note that research shows that the order in which the faces are presented to you does have an effect, but the effect is very small and usually does not have a significant effect on your results. Also, please note that there are other tests in addition to the race and gender/career tests. If you wish to take one or more of these, go to this site: https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/demo/takeatest.html. You need not log in. Just enter as a guest. 
Your paper should be one to two double spaced pages, using 1 inch margins and a normal sized font. Your reflection paper will be graded on a pass/fail basis. To pass, you need to make a reasonable case for your point of view and meet the page requirement. 
There were two tests: one concerning gender, the other concerning race. The test on gender definitely did a good job of measuring the degree to which the test-taker associates career with masculinity and family with femininity. According to the test, I myself do strongly associate masculinity with work and femininity with family. I do not regard this as a bias. I used to be an ardent feminist; but, ironically, it was female acquaintances of mine who convinced to soften my pro-feminist attitudes. They told me that feminism forced them to have to work, which left them little time for family and children, which, so they told me, they deeply resented. 
When in public, I always defer to politically correct positions; and I also think that gender-roles are in fact easily overvalued. I know many men who are dismal professionals and many women who are highly competent ones; and the issue is not, frankly, one that greatly exercises me. In any case, the test did accurately gauge my feelings about traditional gender roles, with the qualification that I would not refer to those feelings as ‘biases’, since, when confronted with a woman who clearly feels at home in the workplace, or a man who clearly feels at home at home (pun intended), I strongly feel that the woman belongs in the workplace and the man belongs at home. 
As for the test on racism, it did a good job of measuring one’s feelings concerning one’s feelings about blacks (if one is white) or whites (if one is black). That said, even if one unconsciously regards whites as superior to blacks or vice versa, that is not, in and of itself, racism, since it is actions that are racist, not feelings. Somebody who has an anti-black bias but resists it when making decisions—when deciding who to hire, for example---is not racist, even though he may have the emotional underpinnings of racism. 
Humans should not be condemned if, at a given moment, they have a politically incorrect sentiment, since feelings are fluid and will tend to right­size themselves as the occasion demands. I used to have preconceptions about Chinese culture; then I got involved with a Chinese woman and, as a result, developed a pro-Chinese bias, which in its turn led to an anti-Western bias. I do not think such reversals are unusual; and I also think that, although people inevitably have preferences, some of them not entirely well-informed, they tend at the end of the day to be reasonably fair-minded when it comes to matters of race. 
Are their unconscious racial biases in our society? Of course there are. There are three hundred million people in this country, and practically any given American, including those who pose as completely politically correct, inevitably have ‘in-group’ biases. But, first of all, the people who go out of their way to show how politically correct they are often, in my experience, have politically driven and unnatural views about members of other groups. They treat a given black person as a black person, not as a black person. Individual blacks, like individual whites, want to move forward professionally; they fall in love; they fall out of love; and so on. The same being true of individual members of any other given group. And in my experience, people who are excessively concerned with political correctness seem to be focused, ironically, on group-affiliations, as opposed to individual characteristics. 
More importantly, what is important is not how individual members of a society feel but how they act. Behaviors can be managed, through laws and policies and the like, but emotions cannot be directly managed—and should not be directly managed. And whatever the private sentiments of individual American may be—and those sentiments vary widely, even within a given demographic---I don’t see that there is systematic denial of opportunity or of justice to people based on gender or race. There are laws and institutions that are intended to protect would-be targets of discriminatory conduct—and, as far as I can tell, do a creditable job of doing so. Finally, I think that the personal sentiments of individual Americans tend to follow official policy in this regard, with the consequence that we are not on the whole a particularly racist or sexist or otherwise bigoted group. 
Answers to (somebody else’s) Questions about Orwell’s “Shooting and Elephant” (My content in italics) 
1. 
Who Orwell represents in the story? Does he represent the empire? How native people see him? 

2. 
What decision did Orwell make about the elephant? Why? To what extent was the correct decision? 

3. 
Why the elephant deserves to die? or live? Why Orwell shoot the elephant? What was his motivation? 

4. 
What is the symbol of the elephant? Does the elephant symbolize the imperialistic British empire? 

5. 
Did Orwell feel peer pressure? Does Orwell was a powerful man? 


Beyond Questions 
1. 
Orwell said, there is a mask, and your face grows to fit it. In our life, How many times we decide to wear a mask to fit in? Are we looking to be accepted by others? 

2. 
Are we ever becoming ourselves? Are we always fulfilling the role that is expected of us? 

3. 
Is social media influencing our role of who we are? Are we in the 

puppet role? 

4. 
Do we have a responsibility in our homes, jobs, and community to do what is right? 

5. 
What am I going to do to become true to myself? Am I going to keep doing what others expected me to do? 


Hi Sahillys! I am a big Orwell fan, and I have read “Shooting an Elephant.” So I will try to answer your questions: 1*. First of all, “Shooting an Elephant” (SAE) is not a story; it is non­fiction. Orwell was a civil servant in Burma, and this is a true story about one of his experiences there. And yes, Orwell represents the British Empire. Actually, both he and the elephant represent the British Empire, each of them being, its own way, wounded and faltering, like (so it is suggested) the British Empire. 2*. Orwell had a legal and moral duty to kill the elephant, given that it was on a rampage. So he did in fact behave correctly. 3*. I don’t know that the elephant deserved to die, but it was killing people; so it had to be dispatched. 4*. Yes, the elephant symbolizes the British Empire---or is meant to. Orwell was a liberal anti-colonialist; and the point of SAE is to depict the British Empire as bombastic and oppressive. 5*. I believe that Orwell’s behavior in this context was not so much about personal pressure as it was about professional obligation. And no, Orwell was not a particularly powerful person. He never had much money or status and died in a poor house. 1*. Some people wear masks; others don’t. Those who wear masks tend to so most of the time, if not all of the time. Those who don’t do so are rarely phony. 2*. Some people are who they are and live honestly; others aren’t and don’t. We are not all in the same boat, as far being honest and living authentically are concerned. Some people embrace who they are and don’t buckle under peer pressure; others collapse like a house of cards. Anthropologists say that, given a group of twenty people, 19 do what the rest do and one decides for himself what to do. 3*. People who are real in real life are real in social media; people who are fake in real life are fake in social media. The former are less drawn to social media than the latter and are likely to use it primarily for reasons of 
a practical as opposed to emotional nature. 4*. Yes, tautologously so. If it is right, then we have a responsibility to do it; and if it is wrong, we have a responsibility not to do it. These are definitional truths. 5*. Nothing is preventing you from being you except you. People are what they choose to be. 
When is it a good for a company to decentralize? 
Given only the information provided, Derek should fight the holacracy initiative. Given additional information, the verdict might be different, but none of the information provided warrants decentralizing Contect; in fact, that information suggests that the opposite path is the appropriate one. Also, the changes involved in the holacracy initiative would be far-reaching and would have unpredictable consequences; and such an initiative would therefore be contraindicated in this context, since Contect’s growth has been steady and might well be jeopardized by such an initiative. 
Contect is a global construction company with many regional offices. Given that Contect is global, it might seem to be a good idea to decentralize. After all, each of its branch offices knows better than central management what it has to do and what constraints it is operating within; and it might therefore seem reasonable to implement a Holacracy-style decentralization-initiative. Also, decentralization has been proven to be the way to go in many contexts. One way for a corporation to decentralize is for it to become a franchise, and sometimes this is extremely profitable, as the case of McDonald’s shows. 
This does not appear to be one of those times. “Without centralized oversight of projects,” it says in the Case Study, “any single subsidiary’s missteps risked taking down the entire company,” the reason being that Contect is a construction-company. Construction-projects are magnets for civil and criminal litigation. There is a high probability of deliberately or accidentally violating local laws. If a Contect regional manager decides to cut costs by flouting some regulation, the result could be large numbers of deaths and injuries, a likely result being that Contect as a whole is targeted for criminal and civil actions and either shut down because of a legal action or bankrupted out of existence. Also, we know about a case where poor decisions on the part of a Contect regional office led to losses of approximately $700,000,000 as well as loss of reputation. 
It’s one thing to make Big Macs. It is quite another to make buildings. There is a single uniform recipe for all Big Macs. There cannot possibly be anything similar in the case of buildings. In addition, success in the construction-business is largely about not making mistakes. Suppose that Contect builds 100 buildings that don’t fall down and one building that does fall down. That one building is more than enough to shut off Contect’s lights and possibly put its leaders in prison. Of course, if there is a case of food-poisoning at a single McDonald’s, that too could cause major problems for the McDonald’s corporation. But McDonald’s can hedge against that by insisting that all franchises strictly follow a script; and this is not possible in the case of Contect, since no two buildings are the same. 
In some contexts, decentralization is appropriate. A personal acquaintance of mine runs an extremely successful online consulting business; and although he is the boss, each employee has almost complete autonomy. This person (let’s call him “Smith”) is himself very talented and he hired five or six very talented people; he finds them business and in exchange they ‘kick up’ a percentage, but nobody gives orders to anybody. His motto is “hire good people and leave them alone.” Smith’s overhead costs are minimal; there is no bureaucratic redundancy; and everyone who works for him is happy, since they have complete freedom. At Smith’s firm, there are no group meetings. When Smith meets with people, it is always one-on-one. And those occasions are rare. If an employee is underperforming, he is given a brief warning or just let go; and if he is performing adequately, he is not likely to hear anything. 
I know another person (let’s call him “Brown”) who started a consulting business in a similar professional area. Brown’s business-model is very different from Smith’s. Brown wants rules and predictability; and he insists on regular group-meetings and group-outings, and the like. The problem is that Brown’s company is small, and its employees find it hard to bring clients in, or to service existing clients, without getting blowback of some kind from Brown. Brown managed to borrow millions of dollars to start his business, which is why his business is still up and running, but it has been losing money since day one, unlike Smith’s business, which began with a $20,000 loan and is making money since day one. 
The kind of anarchical approach to management that Brian Robertson is talking about makes a lot of sense when profits depend on creativity, as is the case with Smith’s firm; and it also makes a lot of sense when well-defined procedures can take the place of central management, as is the case with McDonald’s. It makes less sense with a company, such as Contect, whose product is buildings, as opposed to ideas, and whose production-process cannot in any way be predetermined by some McFormula. Implementing Holacracy might benefit Contect, but it would also be extremely risky; and the risk is not worth taking, since Contect is currently doing well. 
A Tough Business Decision 
Instructions: Please answer the questions below. The length of your written case assignment form responses should be approximately one to one and a half pages, single spaced. This form will be graded on a pass/fail basis. To pass, you need to provide reasonably detailed and insightful answers to the questions below. 
You have a decision to make as Nash. To simplify things, assume that you can either (A.) sell the property to Fledgling (you won’t face legal repercussions), or (B.) not sell the property (delaying the sale while you investigate the toxic waste will likely kill the sale). 
1. What/who are the main stakeholders to your decision? List them with a brief explanation. 
The stakeholders are all of the parties who stand to be affected by my (Nash’s) decision. Nash. Therefore, *I myself am a major stakeholder, since I stand to lose my job and even my career if I make the wrong decision (and also, indeed, if I make the right one). *DAC as a whole is another stakeholder---and probably the largest one, since its solvency is at stake, as well as its reputation. *All employees of DAC are stakeholders, with higher ranking employees having commensurately more at stake than lower ranking ones. *The people who are planning on purchasing the land are stakeholders. And finally *Anyone whose health might be adversely affected by the deal is a stakeholder, since, of course, one’s health is one’s most important asset. 
2. What would you do? Sell it or not sell it? Why? 
The question is not whether or not to sell, but whether or not to make all parties involved aware of the relevant facts. And the answer to that question is: yes. I know that there are threats to people’s health on that land, and I have a moral, if not a legal, obligation to apprise any people who might be either selling or buying that land of those facts. 
I have a moral obligation to the people who are buying it, because the deal will not be in good faith if I don’t do so and also because, if they aren’t given this information, their own health and those of their tenants will be jeopardized, not to mention that they may well be lost to criminal and civil liability. 
Also, if I don’t make Williams, my boss, aware of the relevant facts, then, first of all, he won’t be in a position to make a morally informed decision and, what’s more, he could blame the entire situation on me if all goes awry, which is very likely. 
3. Please indicate whether your decision in 2. is more consistent with utilitarianism, profit maximization, or universalism, and explain why. 
I would make Williams aware of the facts. For practical reasons, I would tell him that I was doing this to protect the airline’s reputation and to protect him civil and criminal fallout. I would also point out to him that the airline’s current cashflow problems would be negligible compared to the problems to which not disclosing the situation might lead. Finally, we have no idea how the buyers will react: for all we know, they will still want the land, granting that they will probably be able to bargain down the price. 
We are dealing a potential case of serious criminal negligence. Further, I don’t even know that I would lose out financially by coming clean with Williams and also with the buyers. Finally, if Williams is smart, then he knows that, if he greenlights the deal without disclosing what he knows, I will be a witness to what he did. This does not mean that Williams let me keep my job. But if he fires me, I will still have my reputation and my integrity; and if I don’t tell him, I will very likely lose both. And the situation being what it is, the probability is that I will eventually lose both. Conclusion: Tell Williams and give him the chance to come clean with the buyers; and if he doesn’t, play the hero and go them directly, which, as Williams knows, would make it hard for him to fire me without turning himself into a villain and which, in addition, is the right thing to do. 
An Ethical Quandry 
Instructions: Please answer the questions below. The length of your video case assignment form responses should be approximately one page, single spaced. This form will be graded on a pass/fail basis. To pass, you need to provide reasonably detailed and insightful answers to the questions below. 
1. After part one if the video case, Julia is confronted with a 
decision: Should she turn left, into town, and report the accident to the 
police? Or should she turn right and go home? What do you think is 
the most ethical decision? 
Obviously, the ethical decision is to take responsibility. In a way, the fact that it was ‘accidental’---in the sense that it was caused by negligence—makes the situation less morally complicated, not more. Suppose that, for some reason or other, had targeted this person, perhaps because of a vendetta or perhaps simply because she was just plain evil. In that case, of course, her act of killing the other person would have been unambiguously evil, but her subsequent act of not turning herself in, though itself evil in its right, would at least have not been hypocritical. Moreover, she would not under that circumstance have been guilty of negligence---that is, of simply not taking full responsibility for her own actions before committing them. It is not too much to demand of a person that they not drink and drive and also that they not, while driving, rummage around items that they drop. I myself constantly have occasion to drive while tired or under the influence of Ambien, and I myself also frequently have occasion to change the radio while driving or to pick up items that I drop. But I don’t---not because I morally perfect, but simply because I am not a complete degenerate. 
But Julia was negligent, and her behavior was hypocritical. She had spent the entire evening reveling in accolades given to her for her civic mindedness. But then when the very same social system that conferred those honors demanded that she take care to drive carefully, she failed to do so; and when it then required her to take responsibility for this lapse of hers, she again failed to take responsibility. 
Julia’s failure to turn herself, though understandable, bespoke a lack of integrity as well as a lack of regard for the very community-norms that she allowed herself to be so praised for upholding, and she therefore cannot be said to have behaved rightly in not turning herself in. 
Of course, the de rigueur response is that such considerations are irrelevant, since we have to consider the hypothetical as it is presented to us, not as it might be were it real. But moral injunctions derive their force from their ability to hold up when the facts continue to pile on. It is easy to fabricate some context in which it would benefit one more to skip out on a debt than to pay it; but the moral injunction derives its practical forces from the hard-learned lessons about the consequences of not paying one’s debts, the same being true of the moral injunction to turn oneself in for crimes for which one simply ‘knows’ one would otherwise not be held responsible. 
2. After watching the second part of the video case (i.e., “part 3), do you think you made the right choice for Julia? That is, does part 3 change anything for you? 
It does not. If anything, the fact that the other driver was drunk means that, from a purely utilitarian perspective, Julia should have turned herself in. For had she done so, she would have been commended for her honesty and probably also legally exonerated. But as it stands, she is a liar and law-breaker, her decision puts on the wrong side not only of universalist but also quite possible of utilitarian conceptions of morality. 
3. Please indicate whether your decision in 1. is more consistent with utilitarianism or universalism, and explain why. 
Assuming for argument’s sake that the various beneficiaries of Julia’s teaching and charitable deeds really did depend on her specifically for assistance, then her decision to skip out taking responsibility for her crime is justifiable from a utilitarian perspective, the reason being that the more would suffer than would benefit from her going to jail or otherwise losing her ability to continue her charitable work. From a Kantian or Universalist perspective, according to which moral laws are inviolable and are not mere means to desired ends, then, of course, her conduct was immoral. That said, if psychological and sociological realities are taken into account, it is less than clear that the utilitarian and universalist verdicts would differ. In all likelihood, the loss of self-respect that Julia would like experience might well undermine her moral fiber and, for that reason, her ability and willingness to continue her work. Also, in all likelihood, there would be people who could step into the breech if she had to stop her work. People have great respect for people who take responsibility, and had Julia done so, she might well have been given a pass, and also retained her self-respect, none of which will happen if she continues the cover-up and is then found out. 
Legitimate Line Extensions as an 
Alternative to Front-businesses 
The Solution to the Pizza Puzzle 
Section I. There are two ways to implement this proposal: relative to one of them, there may be a moral dilemma; and relative to the other, there is no moral dilemma. Taken one way, Brittany is proposing that high-quality pizza be given to the guests at competitive prices through a ‘front’ of sorts, this front being a hotel-service that is posing as an independent business. Taken another way, Brittany is proposing that the hotel go into the Pizza business. In other words, she is proposing a line-extension: instead of simply providing lodging and other strictly hotel-related services, the hotel will also provide pizza, doing so not just for hotel-guests but for the general public. 
Regardless of how her proposal is interpreted, the primary stakeholders are the same, these being: the hotel-owners, including shareholders and creditors, the hotel-employees, the hotel guests, and the general pizza-consuming public. If the hotel launches an independent pizza business, which sells and delivers pizza to the general public, then the general public is more of a stakeholder than it would be if the pizza-service is implemented in a purely in-house way; but it is a stakeholder either way, since it is either being given or denied a valuable service. Also, regardless of how the proposal is carried out, competitor pizza-providers are also stakeholders, with the qualification that, unlike the other stakeholders, they are negatively affected if the venture in question is a success. 
Of course, Brittany’s proposal can simply be rejected, which means that there are three options, each with distinctive effects on the various stakeholders involved. Importantly, the ethical complexion of the case depends very much on the quality and prices of the pizza being provided. But in keeping with the terms of the hypothetical, we will operate on the assumption that the pizza continues to be reasonably priced and of high 
quality.
