Section II. There are three frameworks within which to evaluate the available options in this context: utilitarian, universalist, and profit-maximization. It will be argued that, in this particular case, all of these frameworks yield the same verdict, the reason being that in the long-term it is hard for a business to be profitable if it isn’t benefiting its consumers. 
Let us consider each of the three possible decisions, beginning with the decision to reject Brittany’s proposal. In that case, the hotel loses money. It probably has to raise costs in order to compensate for these losses. Customers end up being gouged, not to mention that they miss out on a nice dining experience, and the hotel loses business. 
Now let us suppose that the hotel has an inhouse pizza-service that poses as an independent business. First of all, a business’s obligation is to provide agreed upon services for agreed upon prices. If you pay me to build you a swimming pool, what matters is whether the job gets done at the agreed upon price. If I pretend to subcontract to my cousin Jerry, while in fact doing the job myself, that is irrelevant, so long as you are not being deceived as to the quality of the work being done. The only material considerations in this sort of context are (i) quality of work and (ii) payment-related agreements. (In some contexts—for example, with lawyers or financial-service providers­--there can be conflict-of-interest issues; but that is not an operative consideration in the pool-case or the pizza-case. Also, conflicts of interest are only material because they impact quality of service: a lawyer can’t effectively represent opposing parties, after all.) 
Also, in-house businesses of this kind are very common. I personally have never been to a hotel where there weren’t restaurants and sometimes other services that had their own names and branding but clearly had no existence outside of the hotel in question. Plus, if the hotel actually invests in special uniforms for the delivery-personnel and otherwise enhances branding, the effect is not so much to deceive customers as to enhance their experience. People will happily pay $200 for a $10 steak if they are also given nice service. The mark-up becomes unambiguously ethically problematic only when it is used to hide low-quality. If I charge you $200 for a steak and a show (possibly in the form of a well-attired delivery-person) and I only give you a steak, then I am in breech; but if I give you both, I am not in breech. 
From a utilitarian standpoint, this would be a respectable option; from a universalist perspective, this would be questionable, given the element of dishonesty involved; and from a profit-maximization perspective, it would be questionable, since it is not clear whether the expenses incurred would be offset by the hotel’s own pizza-consuming base. 
Consequently, the ideal path here would be to create a pizza service that also services the general public. This would eliminate any hint of dishonesty and would also broaden the service’s customer-base, which would help defray the various sunk costs involved in purchasing the uniforms, doing in-house promotion, and the like. Also, there would be little reason not to go this route, since most of the work, including the branding as well as product-creation, would already have been done. This approach, assuming that it was properly carried out, would therefore be optimal from all three of the perspectives in question, granting, of course, that no business venture can be known with certainty to succeed. 
As for the null path, that of simply rejecting Brittany’s offer, that would be safe in the short-term but would eat into the hotel’s bottom line and would also cut the hotel out of a potentially lucrative market, while also depriving the public of a great pizza. 
This case helps us weigh in on the debate between John Mackay and Milton Friedman, Friedman’s position being that a business’s sole concern is to profit, since the ‘invisible hand’ of the market will take care of any ethical problems involved, and Mackay’s position being that a business has to meet the market half-way when it comes to holding itself to ethical norms. Ultimately, Mackay’s position is the right one. Businesses do not profit by being ethical; they profit by giving their customers what they want. If the general public wants toxic waste and assassins-for-hire, businesses can profit by providing these unethical services. 
Friedman seems to hold that transactional ethics are identical with ethics in general, which they often are but sometimes are not. Economists, including Kenneth Arrow and Amartya Sen, have focused a great deal in the last few decades on negative ‘externalities’ which the market’s invisible hand does not do a very good job of mitigating, an externality being an unintended by-product of an economic transaction. Heavy industry generates pollution; for-profit prisons incentivize the manufacturing of crime; and unregulated agriculture incentivizes the creation of cheap but unhealthy foods. These are all examples of externalities, and the free market has not been proven to do as good a job of mitigating them as Friedman and other free-marketeers hold. This means that it is incumbent either on government regulation or the moral integrity of business-owners to do damage control. 
Another problem that Friedman overlooks is that, when not restrained either by ethics or law, businesses tend to collude with government. The government is staffed by human beings who need to eat and pay rent, the same being true of businesses; and it is only to be expected that, given the opportunity, businesses will do their best to use the government to shut out competitors and also to insulate themselves from the law. Realistically speaking, if someone is running a $20,000,000,000 business, they are likely to make donations to politicians, who in their turn will be expected to curb regulatory and law-enforcement agencies. Without either government regulation or personal integrity on the part of executives, it is likely that such collusion will rise to unacceptable levels. 
All of this said, Friedman’s position is still more true than false and isn’t as opposed to Mackay’s position as it initially seems to be. A business that is focused only on the bottom line is likely in the long run to do shoddy work and therefore to fail financially. If the public detests you and your product, which they will if you are ill-serving them and also squeezing out competitors, it will be hard to stay afloat, even with government cooperation. The reason is simply that the economy is much too large even for the government to control, this being why Enron and Bernie Madoff and Lehman Brothers, came to a screeching and disastrous halt. Each was ‘chummy’ with regulators, but there is only so much regulators can do to pacify shareholders who have lost everything. So yes—Friedman is probably right that economic rationality probably does eliminate many negative externalities, but he also probably overestimates the extent to which economic actors are long-term rational. 

Section III. I would choose to create the pizza service, complete with new branding, and would at least offer it to the general public, so as to offset the possible element of dishonesty and also to test market-demand for what might prove to be a lucrative product. The hotel has already gone to the trouble of creating a pizza that is as good as that of its competitors. If it takes no action, it will continue to lose money, which decision would be a loser relative to each of the moral frameworks in question. 
At the same time, if it goes to the trouble of buying special outfits for delivery personnel, giving them the requisite training, and so on, then it might as offer the pizza to the general public, since it would at that point have created the product and branding; and if it wanted to offer the product to the public, it would be all but ready to do so, give or take some non-trivial but manageable concerns relating to delivery and production-capacity. 
This would be 100% honest and, if successful, would maximize the benefit to all of the stakeholders. Further, there is already proof-of-product, meaning that the hotel’s pizza is already known to be good; and, of course, all of the investment in machinery and personnel is already at this point sunk, making a maximum-value approach advisable. Of course, the do-nothing approach is an option, but businesses that shirk from opportunities to grow tend to die, and it also isn’t clear that it is an option for the hotel, given how much it is already losing in pizza-based revenue. In conclusion, the line-extension option, involving a real pizza business servicing both hotel guests and non-guests, is from all the relevant perspectives the indicated path. 
Why is Sartre’s later work so bad? Response to “Human all too human: Sartre” (http://www.youtube.com/watch? v=4G4R_iAZWbQ) 
The video-series (“Human all too human: Sartre”) made a number of striking facts about Sartre’s life clear. First, he initially very much seemed to try to live in accordance with his own existentialism. He very much tried to live freely and not to surrender his identity to cultural norms or to other people’s expectations of him. I have read Being and Nothingness many times, and the purity and simplicity of its message is striking: Be free or be fake. That is its message. And it’s not just asserting it; it is providing a credible metaphysical defense of it. 
The idea is that mind, being representational, must be a kind of cavity­--or ‘nothingness’—into which the rest of the world is able to reflect itself. Were the mind as much a ‘fullness’ as other beings, so it is argued, then it wouldn’t have the reflectiveness (or ‘intentionality’, to use Brentano’s term, which Sartre also uses) characteristic of mind. Being a kind of non-being, so to speak, mind is not subject to the usual determinisms that govern the rest of reality and is therefore inherently free, attempts on our part to believe otherwise being so much ‘bad faith’, as Sartre puts it. 
This reasoning, though obviously questionable, is impressive; and even if it does not itself go through, the proposition that Sartre is attempting to establish—namely, that if we don’t live freely, then we aren’t really living at all—can and should be accepted. 
The video seemed to suggest that, for a while, Sartre himself did accept this, his acceptance of it being manifested in the creativity and originality of his brilliant early works, such as Nausea (1938) and Being and Nothingness (1943). But then, it seems to me, he ceased to accept it. This happened in two phases. First, his acceptance of it assumed degraded forms: instead of continuing to be an iconoclastic thinker, he became a rather garden-variety debauched party-boy, drinking to excess and carrying on with, and therefore presumably manipulating, multiple women at the same time. Many philosophers, including very ancient ones such as Confucius, have been quick to distinguish between genuine freedom, on the one hand, and mere self-indulgence, on the other. At some point, Sartre’s behavior down­shifted from the one to the other. The cause, I suspect, was the extreme success of his early work, which turned him into an icon, which in turn made him self-important and soft. Note that all of the works of his that are still read were published at the very beginning of his career: Nausea (1938), The Imagination (1940), Being and Nothingness (1943), and No Exit (1940). In addition to being his best works and to being the ones for which he is still famous, they are also the ones that catapulted him to fame. 
But after he became famous, his work deteriorated. Although he published quite a lot during the 50s, the only work of his from that period that is at all widely read is Existentialism is a Humanism, which, though it makes some good points, is just a 20 page, watered down version of Being and Nothingness. To its credit, it does contain the famous phrase: existence precedes essence, which, as Star Trek fans know, was paraphrased as “existence precedes programming” by a robot that became self-aware and consequently became free. In any case, Sartre’s 1950s work simply didn’t have the same ‘zip’ as his earlier work. 
The second phase of Sartre’s degeneration was his descent into Marxism. First of all, Marxism seems to be the opposite of Sartre’s existentialism. The basic precept of existentialism is: you are free to choose your own fate—indeed, you have no choice but to do so. The basic precept of Marxism is: You are not free to choose your own fate, since your socioeconomic status has already decided it for you. (Later versions of Marxism would say that your gender, sexual orientation, and ethnicity, as well as your socioeconomic status, co-determine your life-situation.) So it is deeply unclear how an existentialist can also be a Marxist. 
Sartre spent the 1960s and 1970s trying to reconcile existentialism and Marxism, his main effort in this direction being a giant work called Critique of Dialectical Reasoning. I personally don’t know whether this work has merit. I have made several attempts to read it, but simply found it boring and unintelligible; and I was struck by the contrast with his fast-paced and highly readable early works. 
The video does a good job of discussing this part of Sartre’s career. The one interviewee really nailed it when he said that Sartre’s Marxism was really about his ceasing to be the upstart that he once was and becoming a member of a tired old establishment. In other words, Sartre’s Marxism, though superficially about him being a rebel, was about him selling out. And it was this loss of his old existentialism-based identity that made his later work so boring. His early work had one focus: existentialism. His later work had two foci: existentialism and Marxism—which meant it had no focus, which made it incoherent and therefore boring. 
Summary and Analysis of The Yellow Wallpaper 
This story is told from the viewpoint of a highly emotionally sensitive woman, who in times past would have been classified as ‘neurasthenic’, meaning, in effect, that she had a persistent case of ‘raw nerves.’ The woman lives in a room by herself. She never seems to leave the room, even though it is a part of a large house, which is also occupied by her husband. Her husband is a medical doctor and is also functioning, ominously, as her personal medical doctor—more specifically, as her psychiatrist. (And, like many psychiatrists, he is not, it turns out, a particularly good one.) It is clear from the beginning of the story that the woman is emotionally troubled and, at the very least, extremely neurotic. 
But there is a great deal about her condition that is not clear. It is not clear whether she is basically a normal, if highly sensitive woman, who has experienced a bit of emotional throttling or, on the contrary, whether she has a had full-on psychotic break with reality. At the beginning of the story, we are led to believe that she is more on the neurotic side of the spectrum, but at the end it is revealed by that she is very much at the psychotic extreme. 
The story consists for the most part of the woman’s pondering her husband’s alleged failure to have a genuinely emotional, as opposed to narrowly clinical, understanding of her condition. Her husband has very clearly written her off as a demented woman, who, for reasons of protocol, must be tolerated and treated with a certain degree of civility but not sympathized with or respected. The woman’s grievances about her husband prove to be justified, and as the story progresses, it begins to emerge that her complaints about him are seriously understated, as he is driven not by cold professionalism but by ill-will and hatred towards his wife. 
At the same time, the woman’s mental condition is increasingly clearly that of somebody who is completely psychotic, as is evidenced by her fixation with, and delusions concerning, the peeling yellow wallpaper in her room, which represents her unravelling soul. 
At the end of the story, it is revealed that, contrary to what the woman herself has believed all along and also to what the reader has assumed, the woman is a prisoner and is simply trapped in that room—much as, so it is suggested, she is trapped in her own madness. 
Summary and Analysis of James 
Baldwin’s Sonny’s Blues 
This is a story about a schoolteacher, who is the narrator, and his younger heroin-addicted brother Sonny. The story starts with the narrator reading in the newspaper that Sonny had been arrested in a drug-sting. The narrator later finds out from mutual acquaintances that Sonny will be released from jail after he ‘detoxes.’ 
It is clear from the very beginning of the story that the narrator’s relationship with Sonny is weak and ambiguous, with occasional spasms of concern and interest. With the passage of time, the narrator’s heart has softened, partly because his own daughter died and left him with an emotional need for family, and he writes a letter to Sonny, asking to reunite. They do so, but constantly fight, mainly because the brother wants to be allowed to continue to live his bohemian life, heroin-addiction included, and the narrator, being a by-the-book schoolteacher, sees little legitimacy in such a lifestyle. The narrator eventually finds out that Sonny is a talented pianist who has made a name for himself in the local jazz scene. This greatly increases his respect for Sonny and makes him more willing to accept Sonny into his life. 
Dialogue about Sonny’s Blues and The Yellow Wallpaper 
Smith: Brown! How are you, my friend? How did you like those two stories? 
Brown: I very much liked The Yellow Wallpaper (YP). I’m not sure how I felt about Sonny’s Blues (SB). 
Smith: When you say “not sure…”, do you actually mean you’re not sure? Or are you politely saying that it didn’t work for you? 
Brown: It didn’t work for me. 
Smith: That’s surprising, because I know you like non-fiction, or non-fiction-like, stories about wayward lifestyles and the gritty realities they involve. 
Brown: I do. But SB didn’t work for me. 
Smith: Why not? 
Brown: First of all, the main character---the narrator—was boring. He had a boring outlook, and I got the feeling that his job was just a kind of prop. And I didn’t really know what he was supposed to represent. 
Smith: I can kind of see that. 
Brown: On the one hand, he was supposed to be this square schoolteacher, which I can certainly understand. But then there were these weird flashes of bitterness about racial injustice, among other things, that, although morally justified, had no real foundation in the character. They just came out of the blue. 
Smith: I got that feeling myself. The main character was supposed to be a blank—a mirror that reflected reality---but sometimes he would stop being a blank and would go into an angry political mode, which, however justified historically, didn’t cohere with the rest of the character’s personality. 
Brown: Yes, frankly, I felt that way too. I didn’t really know what the story was about. I mean, there were really only two characters: the main one, who was just kind of a run-of-the-mill narrator, and Sonny, who, apart from his piano-talent, had no particularly good or interesting qualities. 
Smith: Yes, and there seemed to be an understanding to the effect that all of this was a consequence of racism, or some such, and maybe it was. But if the story is just considered on its own terms—if it’s just read as a story about a man and his brother, rather than as a political diatribe—it doesn’t really work very well. 
Brown: No, it doesn’t. Not in my view, anyway. And there was undischarged premise there, something to the effect that Sonny was a lost soul who would not have been lost in a non-racist society. Which may or may not be true and which, in any case, didn’t really make for very engaging reading, I’m sorry to say. 
Smith: What about YP? What did you think of that? 
Brown: That one I very much liked. 
Smith: Which is ironic, given that you’re a male and the main character isn’t. 
Brown: Yes, that’s an irony, I suppose. But I still liked it. 
Smith: Because..? 
Brown: Because it was realistic. Especially the way the woman’s delusions were intertwined with accurate emotional perceptions. 
Smith: Explain please. 
Brown: Ok. So obviously the woman’s belief that she lived in the wallpaper, or whatever her delusion was, was indeed a delusion. But her beliefs about her husband’s emotional disposition towards her was accurate. 
Smith: That makes sense. 
Brown: And I think what drove her insane was her awareness, her unconscious awareness, of her husband’s actual feelings. 
Smith: So was it the fact that she was aware of her husband’s feelings that drove her mad, or was it the fact that she was unconsciously aware of her husband’s feeling? 
Brown: The latter. If she had known, consciously and lucidly, what her husband was really about, it would not have driven her mad, since frank acceptances of ugly realities, though unpleasant, don’t undermine mental health. 
Smith: So it was the fact that she was aware of her husband’s actual disposition towards her but couldn’t admit it. 
Brown: Right, she couldn’t admit it---except by going into a kind of perpetual dream-like state. 
Smith: You’re referring to her psychosis. 
Brown: Yes, psychosis being, as Freud said, a prolonged waking dream. 
Smith: Hoo boy! 
Brown: Yes, it’s heavy. That’s why I liked the story. 
Smith: You liked it because it was heavy, or because it was accurate? 
Brown: It wouldn’t have been heavy if it had not been psychologically accurate. 
Smith: Good point, Brown. Good point. 
The Purpose of Fiction 
Albert Camus is reported to have said that “fiction is a lie through which we tell the truth.”[170] 
Before taking this class, my position on fiction was that it was just a lie, albeit a harmless and entertaining one. I have always enjoyed reading science fiction, for example; and I have always been an avid 
movie buff, albeit of the unsophisticated type who prefers Spielberg to Fellini. And had it not been for this class, this would still be my position. 
But it isn’t. To be honest, I took this class because I had to. But after I read the first few stories, I experienced a major shift. I remember the very moment when it happened. It was when I was reading “Young Goodman Brown” that it occurred to me: fiction isn’t fiction! Fiction is a way that we confront that which we could not otherwise confront. What is “Young Goodman Brown” about? There is a young man who has absurdly simplistic views about his wife and, indeed, the rest of humanity. He assumes that they are all magically free of the impulses that he knows himself to have. Goodman Brown is, by all accounts, a decent person; but he is indeed a person—a biological organism, with aggressive and libidinal urges—and he is surely guilty of a certain degree of self-deception in assuming his wife and other community members to be the paragons of purity that they make themselves out to be. 
And that is the whole point of the story. He sets out on a journey and discovers that his wife and fellow villagers are all ‘Satanists.’ Then he wakes up! It was all a dream! But, of course, it wasn’t. What does it mean, after all, to say that his (appropriately named) beloved wife Faith is a ‘Satanist’? What does it mean, really, to say that anybody is a ‘Satanist’? It means that they are a human being. People have religions that force them to be pure because they are not pure; and they are not pure not because they are categorically evil, but because human beings are four parts animal and one part human (if that makes any sense); and the human side of us knows that, if we are to live in harmony, we must suppress our animalistic side, this being one of the functions of religion and also of law. A ‘Satanist’ is somebody who makes a point of publicly acknowledging that we are more animal than we care to admit; and since each of us has no choice but to admit this, if only to ourselves, we are all, after a fashion, Satanists. 
So the point of “Young Goodman Brown” is really that human beings are indeed more ‘human’---or sub-human, depending on how you look at it— then people care to admit. It was when I was finishing reading this story that I realized---that the point of fiction is to disclose, not to hide. Playing Devil’s Advocate, I asked myself: ‘But if that is true, why didn’t Hawthorne just come out and say what I just said?’ 
‘Suppose he had done so,’ I answered. ‘If he had done that, he would have just produced a boring treatise that wouldn’t resonate with people.’ 
People don’t respond to theories particularly well, sometimes because they are emotionally resistant to them but usually because they are too abstract and don’t connect with people in the same experiential way as stories. 
But then I realized there was another objection to my theory: ‘If writing is about baring the depths of the human psyche,’ I asked myself, ‘why don’t writers just stick to true crime and other non-fiction genres?’ This one stumped me for a while, partly because I myself used to be an avid true crime reader. But then it hit me: true crime describes what only a few people do, but fiction describes how everybody feels. The people described in true crime books are mentally ill, and usually quite devoid of morality, and their acts are not merely about garden-variety impulses coming to the surface; they are also about a lack of morality specific to such people. So when we read about criminals and serial killers, we aren’t really getting the whole story: we are certainly witnessing the deprave side of humanity, but we aren’t seeing the conflicts to which it gives rise in most people. 
But that is what we are seeing in “Young Goodman Brown.” Faith is not Ted Bundy; she is not a monster. She is a normal person, with an animalistic side and a moral side, the same being true, to varying degrees, of Goodman Brown’s townsfolk and of Goodman Brown himself. And if we just focus on non-fiction—on history, on what people have done—we don’t see the conflicts; we don’t see the really important part of the story. It is only through fiction that we see the inner story. 
Many years ago, when I was in high school, I read “Bartleby the Scrivener.” I had not the slightest idea what it was about, the reason being that, back then, the whole idea that fiction was about exposing psychological realities was alien to me. But this time, I understood it. In the years since high school, I have known people on the autism spectrum; and I have known people who, though not autistic, retreated into Bartleby-like cocoons of isolation. The people I am thinking of actually ended up having Bartleby-like jobs, one as a paralegal, the other as a statistician in the bowels of an investment bank. Both acted like Bartleby; they were rigid and unbelievably stubborn. When I read “Bartleby”, I realized that they were deeply angry; and since they were so powerless, all they could do was refuse to do as told. 
If I had been told this before re-reading Bartleby in the context of this class, I would simply have laughed. But I now see it as a simple truth—one that I had to be told through fiction to grasp. 
The story that struck me the most in this class was Kafka’s “The Hunger Artist.” Like the other two stories just discussed, this one is realistic enough that we can relate it to personal experience while being just so skewed that we see the emotional realities beneath the familiar surfaces. We are all familiar with the term ‘starving artist’ and also with the corresponding concept. And in many ways, what is being described in Kafka’s story is very normal: there is an artist—or ‘freak’, depending on how you look at it---being jeered at by an unappreciative public. So far, so good; and at first, I thought the point of this story was just that artists are not understood by their peers. But then when I read it a second time, and then a third, I realized that Kafka, though himself an artist, was not on the side of the artist in his story. Kafka seemed to have contempt for him. Kafka’s attitude towards the Starving Artist is similar to his attitude towards Gregor Samsa: he sees the latter as a cockroach, and that is how he sees the former. He sees both as fussy and small, and his story exposes a streak of narcissism and grandiosity in the so-called ‘starving artist’---none of which he could have done, at least not so poignantly, had he expressed himself through theories or through non­fictional descriptions of actual human beings. 
What I have learned in this class, therefore, is that, whereas non­fiction tells the truth about the outer, fiction tells the truth about the inner. Fiction is not, I now realize, mere entertainment. It is the most transparent window we have into the soul, and that is why all civilizations hold their myths and fables in such high regard. 
Summaries with Analysis of Ibsen’s A Doll’s House and Hawthorn’s Young Goodman Brown 
Summary of A Doll’s House: Nora Helmer, wife of banker Torvald Helmer, is living grandly, spending lavishly on Christmas presents and other non-necessaries. Her husband takes note and is put off by this, but on the surface continues to seem loving. 
Ms. Linde, a friend of Nora’s, comes by the house and asks Nora to 
persuade Torvald to get her (Ms. Linde) a job at his bank. She needs the money, she explains, because her recently deceased husband left her penniless. Nora discloses to Ms. Linde that, with the collusion of a bank employee named Krogstad, she (Nora) had fraudulently obtained a loan, which she used to help Torvald, who was ill at the time, go on a medically necessary vacation. 
Krogstad drops by and reveals that he knows that Torvald, who is his boss, is going to fire him; and he says that unless Nora convinces him not to do so, he will reveal her crime to Torvald. Despite Nora’s best efforts, Norvald fires Krogstad and he sends a letter outing Nora to Torvald. 
It turns out that Krogstad was previously in a serious relationship with Ms. Linde and is still in love with her, despite being angry that she dumped him for a (now deceased) wealthier man. Ms. Linde nonetheless manages to reactivate her relationship with Krogstad. It is not entirely clear why she does so, but the supposed reason is to convince Krogstad not to bring the law down on Nora. 
Nora succeeds in softening Krogstad’s heart, and he drops the whole matter. But the damage is done, since Torvald has read the letter and, without formally divorcing Nora, emotionally disowns her. 
Nora then realizes that her marriage with Torvald was a fraud, as he never had any real emotional connection to her and kept her on only as a trophy-doll for his perfect dollhouse-life. She then realizes that she must be the master of her own life if she is to be happy. 
Summary of Young Goodman Brown: Recently married Goodman Brown, an earnest young man, announces to his wife, Faith, that he must go on a long journey on foot in the forest. She begs him not to go, telling him how dangerous such an expedition might be; but he goes anyway. In the woods, he crosses paths with Satan. While walking and talking, Goodman Brown and the Devil cross paths with many friends of Goodman’s from back in his home village, all of whom, it turns out, are Satanists who are en route to a Luciferian gathering of some kind. When it turns out that Faith is one of these Satanists, Goodman wakes up: It was all a dream! 
Analysis of both stories: Both stories are about growing up and parting with childish fictions about other people’s motivations. More specifically, each story is about a member of the one gender finding out about the motivations of members of the other gender. Torvald cares about status and he wants a trophy wife. Nora idiotically thought that he loved her, but he was actually just using her. For Torvald, Nora was simply a kind of animate ‘doll.’ 
Goodman Brown thought that his wife was pure and innocent. But she wasn’t--not because she is a bad person, but because she is indeed a person, and a ‘pure’ person is a biological impossibility. Some people are more ethical than others, but people are people---not ‘dolls’ that do and think what we want them to. 
Goodman Brown, being a male, wants a woman who burns with passion for him but is otherwise pure. But this is not possible, not because Faith is a bad person, but because passion of its very nature follows its own dark ways. Nora Helmer wants a husband who, in addition to being a money-and status-obsessed banker, also loves and cherishes Nora. But this is not possible, not because he has no love to give, but because love, being an emotion, necessarily brings many other emotions in its wake and therefore has no place in the Kabuki-theatre, dollhouse-life that Nora wanted. 
BCG Matrix Bulletin Points 

Stars 

*Grow rapidly in high-growth markets and are therefore by far the best possible investment, if you are lucky enough to identify one. *Facebook, Microsoft, Amazon, and E-trade used to be stars, but are now cash cows (see below), owing to maturation of their respective markets. *Tend to be monopolies or semi-monopolies. Consider Amazon/Microsoft. *Because they are on the bleeding edge of growth, they tend to have aggressive and original marketing schemes, unlike cash cows, which tend to stick with the tried and true. 
Cash Cows 

*Grow predictably and slowly. *Good place to park assets, since they have reliable rates of return that exceed the usual savings account rates. *They focus on retention more than acquisition, since they are already in mature, low-growth markets. *Examples: Kellogg’s, Coca Cola, Disney. 

Question Marks *Rapid growth companies with small market shares: hence their ambiguous status. *Examples might be Coinbase, Express VPN, and TuneCore. *Smart investors scrutinize these, since they are likely to be overlooked but are promising. Likely to be miscategorized as dogs by mediocre investors. *A question mark is best advised to find a way to carve out a niche for itself, since there is no way that it can compete on size. 

*Low growth companies in low growth markets. *Examples: Sears, Kodak, any local cab business, any mom and pop that tries to compete with Jiffy Lube. *Often on the wrong side of technological change; for example, Sears is on the wrong side of the e-commerce revolution. *Investors are advised to stay away.

Dennett and Skinner on the Human 
Condition 
Response to “Reading Minds” 
(http://www.youtube.com/watch?

and “Neuroscience and Free Will” 
(https://www.youtube.com/watch?

For me, the take-home from the B.F. Skinner video (“Operant Condition”) was how shallow a thinker Skinner was. True: gambling is compulsive, addiction-mediated behavior, and such behavior is indeed reflexive. That is why we regard it as animalistic, and that is also why B.F. Skinner was discussing it. Skinner’s theory is that all behavior is reflexive: no intelligence or rationality is involved. And compulsive, addiction-driven behavior is certainly more consistent with that theory than is non-addiction­driven, non-compulsive behavior. But the very fact that Skinner had to use a degenerate and reflexive form of behavior, such as gambling, in order to substantiate his theory shows how bankrupt that theory is. 
As for the other video---“Consciousness and Free Will”---I was underwhelmed. Dennett goes on and on about how ‘people’ think that ‘consciousness is just so wonderful’, and he repeatedly says how stunted and misguided such people are. And maybe he’s right. But he doesn’t ever explain how to explain consciousness—at least not in that video and also not in his book consciousness Explained—which, though an extremely intelligent and informative book, does not so much explain consciousness as attempt to deny it. Which, of course, is implausible as well as self-refuting, given that, without consciousness, there are no denials or hallucinations or mistakes.

The obvious response to have to the first video (“Reading Your Mind”) is to ask alarmist questions about the police using this technology to read our minds and destroy any last vestiges of freedom and privacy that we have. But in my view, the really relevant questions concern the educational (and, to a lesser extent, diagnostic) possibilities of this technology. If we knew exactly what kinds of thoughts people had in response to a given quantum of information, we could streamline education, and also customize it to fit a given person’s cognitive profile; and it looks as though this technology, when developed, will make this possible. 
As for the concerns about the state using this technology to raid our psyches—that’s certainly possible, but it’s also extremely unlikely. The reasons for this have nothing to do with human nature: human beings obviously can and will do whatever they can to control people and would gladly use this technology. The reasons have to do entirely with the enormous costs involved in doing so, coupled with the latest projections about the world’s oil supply. The relevant question, I stress, is: how can we use this technology to optimize teaching? 
As for the video about free will (“Neuroscience and Free Will”), that too was illuminating, with the qualification that it wasn’t the topic of free will that was illuminated. The question ‘do we have free will?’ is very vague, and the short answer seems to be ‘no.’ The long answer seems to be something along the lines of: ‘no…but if your various psychological faculties are working with one another, instead of grinding up against one another, then you can have something that, although not quite what we mean when we talk about “free will”, has many of the same virtues.’ All of that said, the video made it clear just how much of our mental activity is unconscious or semi­conscious, and that is the respect in which it was illuminating.

Response to : “The Pre-Socratics: 
Part 1” 
(http://www.youtube.com/watch? 
v=83kUMEjaCUA) 
“The Pre-Socratics: Part 2” 
(http://www.youtube.com/watch? 
v=_IRy3E-BZhY) 

“The Pre-Socratics: Part 3” (http://www.youtube.com/watch? 
v=qVrwRHSIRIg) 

It seems to me that there are many different versions of the so-called “problem of the one and the many.” From what I can gather, the original version of this problem is given by the question: “What is it that unifies the various different parts of the universe? In other words, in virtue of what fact are the various different objects in the universe—the various animals and particles and planets and so on—components of a single universe, as opposed to multiple different universes?” 
Physics has already answered this question. Any two events are causally connected. (Any two events either have common causal origins or one is part of a causal chain that is responsible for the other.) If there is absolutely no conceivable causal connection between two events---if there is no possible causal process either directly linking them or linking them by way of some third event---then it simply makes no sense to say that they belong to the same universe. 
Relativity Theory made this position popular, at least among physicists, the reason being that, in Relativity Theory, chronological relations are defined in terms of causal relations. (e1 is said to pre-date e2 if and only if e1 can have a causal influence on e2; and e1 and e2 are said to be simultaneous if neither can have a causal influence on the other.) But it seems to me that, at some level, all people must accept it, since it seems incoherent to suppose that two events could be totally causally divorced from each other but belong to the same universe. 
In any case, when I was watching the videos about Parmenides and Thales, it occurred to me that, when people refer to “the problem of the one and may” there is a second problem that they sometimes have in mind. Thales said that everything is water, and Parmenides said that, despite appearances, nothing changes. The first view is false, and the second is not only false but absurd. But it seems to me that both of these hypotheses embody the insight that a few immutable natural laws are responsible for the various changes that occur in the universe; and when people refer to “the problem of the one and the many”, they are sometimes referring to the question: What are these laws? In other words, what are the unchanging principles underneath and responsible for the various changes that occur?” Once again, this question, though presented in this context as a philosophical question, is one that can only be answered on scientific grounds, through observation and experimentation and the like, and that has been answered to a considerable extent. 
What do we have that chimps don’t? Response to : “Ape Genius” (https://www.youtube.com/watch? v=wg-mPjhCnc8) 
The purpose of the video was supposedly to explain exactly how human beings were superior to chimpanzees. As it itself put it, it was to answer the question: Why do human study chimps, not the other way around? 
The video didn’t really answer this question, though it did hazard a guess, albeit 45 minutes into it. The mike-dropper of an answer that it gave was: 
(TA) “Humans teach each other, but chimps don’t, and chimps therefore have to learn everything they know on their own.” 
“TA” is short for ‘their answer.’ 
TA is not plausible. Chimps don’t teach each other because they can’t. Human beings do we because we can. So TA takes a mere consequence of the relevant difference between people and chimps to be the very the essence of that difference. 
If TA were correct then, if chimps started teaching one another, they would come to be human-like in their intellectual proportions and configuration---even if everything else about them were held constant. But, of course, such a counterfactual is either trivial or absurd, depending on how it is looked at. It’s trivial if it is supposed that chimps magically acquired the ability to teach one another, and it is absurd if it is supposed that they could have this ability without acquiring the various abilities—had by us and lacked by them--underlying it. 
So yes—chimps are smarter than most people are inclined to believe. The video proved this much. But no---it isn’t because people do, whereas chimps don’t, teach one another that we are able to study them but they are not able to study us. It’s the other away around: it is because we are able to study them, whereas they cannot study us, that we can teach one another, whereas chimps cannot. 
So what is the difference between us and them? One point the video made that was important is that the difference is not strictly quantitative. It isn’t just that we have more brain-cells. There is some kind of existential divide that we are on one side of and that chimps are on the other side of. 
That divide, I would suggest, is the ability to interact with reality through symbolism. Consider what we are doing in this very class and indeed in the context of this very assignment. We are exchanging symbols with one another. Most of us don’t even know what most of the rest of us even look like. But that doesn’t matter, because, unlike chimps, we don’t need to look at one another’s facial expressions and bodily behavior to communicate, since we, unlike chimps, have language at our disposal. And not just language, but a plurality of representational media: different kinds of visual art, different kinds of non-visual art, specialized mathematical and technical notations, not to mention ad hoc codes---none of which chimps have. 
The video obscured this point when it showed the chimp supposedly ‘adding one’, which, supposing that it did in fact do so, it did by tapping on a now blank part of the screen that previously had a numeral one. The problem with this supposed proof is that the ability to ‘add one’ is one of the only forms of mathematical competence that can be mimicked by pretty much any creature. Could the chimp have multiplied by three? Or divided by four? What about exponentiation? No. Of course, not. Unless a creature can interact through symbols, as opposed to mere gestures, it doesn’t even make sense to suppose that it is computing square roots or multiplying by fractions. Contrariwise, if one wants to prove that one’s St. Bernard is a mathematician, the best one can do is prove that it can ‘add one.’ 
Chimps have plenty of intelligence, as do cats and dogs and squirrels. Anyone who has had a cat knows how incredibly intuitive they are, not to mention how surgically precise their movements are. And the same can be said of chimps and terriers and pretty much all mammals, as well as many non-mammals. But there is some line that separates us from them, and that line is not the ability to teach one another; it is the ability to grasp meanings, as opposed to objects, which is a conconmitant of the ability to understand symbols. A consequence of this ability is the ability to engage in certain forms of mutual instructions, but it is not mutual instruction that separates us from them. 
Also, chimps do teach one another, at least so far as they non-symbolic relation to reality allows them to. In the following article, published in 2014 and therefore after the video, it is shown that chimps do teach 
one 
another: 
https://www.livescience.com/48078-chimpanzees-learn­
behaviors-socially.html 
“Humanity's 
closest 
living 
relatives”, 
it 
says. 
“can 
pass on culture and customs just as humans do, shedding light on the possible capabilities of the last 
common 
ancestor 
of 
both 
humans 
and chimps, the researchers say.” 
In conclusion, the video, though informative, did not answer the question that it set out to answer, and, despite its attempt to show-case the intellectual abilities of chimpanzees, failed to give them credit for one of their most astonishing intellectual powers. 
My Questions about “Dum Dum Boys”, by Cameron Joy, pp. 109-119 
I read “Dum Dum Boys”, by Cameron Joy, a very sad story about a young man who has a good heart but also has psychological problems, which he deals with by taking drugs, causing him to overdose and die as a teenager. 
About Questions 
What was the point of this story? (Was it to illustrate the evils of drug use? Or was it rather to illustrate the fragility of human beings? Or am I wrong to 
try to find a moral agenda in it?) Was this story about adolescence or was it about 1980s-style decadence? What was Cameron’s medical condition? Was he depressed? Psychotic? What did Cameron’s death teach the narrator about life? What were the narrator’s feelings about Cameron’s death? 
Beyond Questions 
Does mental illness lead to drug use or is it the other way around? What was happening in American society when this was written that caused the sort of personal disintegration described in this story? Would this story still have as much of an impact on readers today? This story seems to be in the same genre as Less than Zero, meaning that it is a story about 1980s-style lack of moral fiber, which makes me wonder: Will people today understand this story or find it relevant? 
The Meaning of Plato’s Cave Allegory Response to (http://www.youtube.com/watch? 
v=69F7GhASOdM) 

There are two ways to understand the Allegory of the Cave. On the one hand, it can be seen as putting forth a hypothesis concerning the structure of reality. On the other hand, it can be interpreted as an attempt to ‘red pill’ people about the structure of society. 
Taken the first way, the Allegory of the Cave is saying that, although our senses give us raw data, intellect is needed to model this data appropriately and therefore to see the true structure of reality. Thus taken, this allegory has no political or social overtones and is merely a point about how 
the world is structured (it consist of visible phenomena governed by underlying and therefore invisible laws), along with an epistemological corollary (in order to understand the world, it is not enough to observe it: we must also interpret those observations, and the intellect is therefore at least as much a source of knowledge as our senses). 
Taken the second way, this allegory seems to be saying that members of society live propaganda-based lies. In this particular context, Plato does not tell us much about the nature of those lies. Indeed, it is not entirely clear that he is in fact discussing society. But his repeated use of terms like ‘prisoner’, ‘shackles’, and ‘blinded’ suggests as much. Also, having read the rest of The Republic as well as large parts of his dialogue The Laws, I do know that Plato was deeply distrustful of every stratum of society. He distrusted non-rulers, thinking them little more than animals; and he also distrusted rulers, mainly because, it seems, he himself wanted to be one (or was one) and therefore knew firsthand how untrustworthy they could be. 
The second interpretation is, I admit, highly speculative, and there is little direct textual evidence for it in the video (which was taken from The Republic, which, of course, contains a number of passages that are overtly political, as well as overtly cynical, both about politics and human nature). The first interpretation is less speculative, since, in the passage in question, Plato is explicitly saying that how reality seems is not how it is and, consequently, that it is only by analyzing appearances that one acquires actual understanding. 
The one interpretation puts this passage in line with the readings we have done from Heraclitus and Thales, the other with the excerpt that we watched from the Matrix. 
An Ethical Dilemma 
Every summer, starting when I was 11, a friend of mine and I worked at a local tennis club. (I will refer to this friend as “Jacob”, even though that is not his real name.) We both performed low-level jobs: cleaning up after club-members, sometimes picking up balls, working in the kitchen, etc. As we got older, our club-related duties began to pull apart. Being an out-doorsy, athletic sort of person, I usually preferred activities relating to golf and tennis. 
I’m a passable tennis player and golfer and have taught both. But mostly my work at the club has been pretty menial and usually involved me working as a caddy and the like—which I don’t mind, since I like being outdoors. 
Jacob’s work was very different. He always had a knack for numbers and was (and, I suppose, still is) a whiz at math and computers. Starting when we were fifteen, the summer after the 9th grade, Jacob started doing office-work at the club. They had tried to hire people to help with Quicken and other softwares, but nobody could do the job, even the ones with college degrees. As for the management, they were hopeless with anything relating to computers, as they were decidedly ‘old school’---as in, they were very old and not computer-literate. (It is a small club, which services a small, close-knit community in upstate New York.) And they didn’t even know where to begin. 
But Jacob had no problem doing this sort of thing. Plus, Jacob was the son of a well-liked club-member; so the management trusted him. And so it happened that a 15-year-old, without any formal training or licenses, was doing the financials for a country club most of whose members were millionaires. It worked out well---for a time. 
Jacob had always lived life ‘fast and loose.’ He had once been caught cheating, and there were suspicions that he occasionally sold drugs. It was common knowledge that he did drugs, but that was brushed aside on the grounds that it was only pot and that it was only occasional. In point of fact, it was very non-occasional, and although it began with pot, it quickly escalated to cocaine and heroin. Oddly, I was the only one at the club who seemed to notice. (I think the other people there were more focused on Jacob’s job-performance, which was always impeccable.) 
One day, Jacob let me in on a little ‘secret.’ He had started using club-member credit card numbers to make purchases on Amazon, sometimes for himself, sometimes for others, usually drug dealers to whom he owed money. He always only made small purchases, he told me, $10 here, $15 there. Because he had access to every club-member’s card-information, he was able to spend thousands of dollars of club-member money without any of them being the wiser, since he spread the purchases out over so many different credit cards. 
I was absolutely stunned. I was also conflicted. Jacob and I went way back. I knew what he was doing was wrong, but he had always been a loyal friend to me. At the same time, my father was a member of that club, and I thought I might have a duty to tell him that somebody else was using his credit card. I told Jacob this, but he immediately replied that he would never use my father’s card information. That later turned out to be a lie, and deep down I think I knew that it was a lie; but it did give me pause. So I bided my time, telling myself that I was trying to figure out what to do: be loyal to Jacob or prevent Jacob from continuing with this fraud. Frankly, I was not strongly inclined to turn Jacob in. The club members had money, and it didn’t seem quite worth it to ruin Jacob’s life over this theft. If it had continued for longer, I might have changed my mind, but I’m not sure. 
Fortunately—or unfortunately, depending on how you look at it— Jacob was found out. No criminal charges were filed—probably to avoid stigmatizing the club. But his parents were kicked out of the club and had to pay around $45,000 in restitution. Jacob then went to another school. I haven’t kept in touch with him, but the rumor is that his life has gone downhill in a huge way. 
This was, in my opinion, a real moral dilemma. It’s easy to say that I simply should have turned Jacob in—and that’s probably what I would have done if it had gone on much longer—but there is more to it than that. He had never wronged me, and although he was technically stealing, the people at the club certainly wouldn’t miss the few dollars Jacob was stealing from them. This was a tough one—which, fortunately, circumstance resolved for me, sparing me a tough ethical decision. 
Answers to questions about 
documentary about how corporations 
are evil (Response to 
https://www.youtube.com/watch? 

v=Pin8fbdGV9Y) 
1. Briefly summarize the basic plot, or issue that the movie addresses. 
The basic point of this documentary is that corporations, while operating under the banner of providing people with necessary goods and services, are the very embodiment of amoral greed. The basic conceit is that, although evil has always existed, corporations managed to turn it into a science, using the free-market, along with the corresponding ready-made slogans, as a cover. 
2. What do you think is the most interesting point in the movie? 
For me, the most interesting point made by the movie was that corporations had had such a profound influence on the making and executing of laws in the United States. The fact that corporations are legally classified as persons is indeed striking. 
3. What is the most controversial statement you’ve heard? 
That IBM was responsible to some extent for the Holocaust, which, speaking on historical grounds (including testimony of relatives who spent time in Auschwitz), is extremely questionable, given that the WW2 was more about Germany’s straitened economic circumstances coupled with centuries-old ethnic conflicts, with none of which IBM or indeed any other company had anything to do. 
4. What is the most important ethical issue that the movie is addressing? Please explain. 
The most important ethical issue is how to control corporate greed without crippling the market’s ability to function. 
Answers to questions about documentary about the the Parable of the Sadhu (https://www.youtube.com/watch? 
v=nxXZEnR_JSM) 

Instructions: Please answer the questions below. The length of your video case assignment form responses should be approximately one page, single spaced. This form will be graded on a pass/fail basis. To pass, you need to provide reasonably detailed and insightful answers to the questions below. 
1. What do you think you would have done were you Buzz McCoy, the narrator of the film (the Morgan Stanley executive), and the Sadhu was dropped at your feet? Would you have left the Sadhu for the next group, as Buzz did? Would you have taken the Sadhu to safety, and likely sacrificed your trip? 
I obviously like to think that I would have rescued the Sadhu. But the reality is that it depends on the circumstances. If the trip were mere recreation for me, then yes, I would have helped. If the trip were the equivalent of my one shot at an Olympic Gold Medal, then no, I would not have helped. I have been in situations that were similar, albeit much smaller, and I did help. But those situations required little sacrifice on my part, so I don’t know how much can be read into them. 
2. No matter how you answer in 1. above, why do you think Buzz did what he did? Why didn’t Buzz take the Sadhu to safety? In other words, if the situation was different, might Buzz have taken the Sadhu to safety? If so, how would the situation have to change? And what about Buzz himself? What things about him or his personality do you think affected his decision? 
I think it’s important to know that we simply don’t know what Buzz specifically would have done under different circumstances. Obviously, we are led to believe that it was only with great reluctance that he did not help that Sadhu. But actions speak louder than words, and he didn’t help. For all we know, if he had been in a situation where the sacrifice to himself was minimal, he still would have come up with an excuse not to help. Nothing can be assumed here. 
3. What do you think is the “moral” of the parable (case)? 
That people always pass the buck and, therefore, that true morality is about doing the right thing, instead of just having an excuse for not doing it. 
Answers to Questions about Walmart-documentary (https://www.youtube.com/watch? 
v=jf-Sr3SjBzk) 
Instructions: Please address each item below for each movie. The total length of your critical viewing form responses should be approximately one-half to one page, single spaced. This form will be graded on a pass/fail basis. To pass, you need to provide reasonably detailed and insightful answers to the items below. 
1. Briefly summarize the basic plot, or issue that the movie addresses. 
The point of the movie is that Walmart is an exploitative as well as deceptive corporation. The larger point is that exploitativeness and deceptiveness are inherent in what corporations are. 
2. What do you think is the most interesting point in the movie? 
The material about the overseas sweatshops was striking and did show a lack of conscience on the part of Walmart’s management. 
3. What is the most controversial statement you’ve heard? 
The most debatable contention in the movie is that Walmart economically devastates communities. That may be true, but it’s hard to square with my personal experience. I myself shop at Walmart and the prices and selection are very good. And I’m friends with Walmart employees, and although it is not stimulating work, they seem happy to have any kind of employment at all, which Walmart, unlike other employers, was more than happy to offer. 
4. What is the most important ethical issue that the movie is addressing? Please explain. 
The issue was how to curb corporate amorality. As a believer in economic freedom, I would like to think that the movie was concerned with how to keep markets free while preventing malfeasance on the part of juggernauts such as Walmart. But there wasn’t really any direct evidence that the writer-director was concerned with that issue, his focus being, as far as I could tell, to indict Walmart with misconduct, without worrying too much about how to do that without over-regulating the economy.

Discussion Post #8 
Advertising Assignment 
1. Identify any advertisement and provide a copy or link to your submission on Canvas 
https://www.lifehack.org/articles/lifestyle/10-natural-ways-have-beautiful­
and-youthful-skin.html 


2. Clearly state the objectives and target audience of the ad 
The immediate objective of the ad is to sell literature published by LifeHack, a company specializing in personal growth. The long term objective is to hook the prospect into becoming a serial customer of LIfeHack goods (mainly books) and services (mainly information and some personal consulting). The target audience is anyone who believes that his professional future is tied less to his to his doing the intellectual equivalent of going to the gym three times/week. 
3. How does this ad fit into the company’s/organization’s marketing communication strategy? 
It fits well. It’s a pop-up ad that appears in an ad on anti-aging skin cream (which, though I am in my early 20s, I sometimes use), and the two products (the cream and the book) do fit with each other psychologically and also fit with the fact that the company is online-centered and has an amicably aggressive style. 
4. Explain the tone, appeal and format of the ad 
Friendly but ‘in-your-face’: you can be a slacker or you can buy our products---your call. 
5. Assess the ad’s level of effectiveness 
Pretty effective, largely because LifeHack has independent credibility, but it the positioning works: I think I’m going to buy the product right now 
6. How would you make improvements to the ad? 
I would ad an image that draws the person in: I like pictures, and the ad in its current form has none and is too abstract. I want to see an image of a smart person or a good-looking person. As it stands, I have only the text to tell what I’m buying, which isn’t quite enough to seal the deal. 
Does good employee treatment (SAS movie) usually mean more profit? If not, why not? If so, why, and why don't more companies treat their employees well? 
Yes, there is no doubt about it. Happy employees work hard and work intelligently. Unhappy employees cost the firm more than they are worth. The use of force in business contexts is expensive, since it results in mediocre work. Adam Smith said it well: 
The experience of all ages and nations, I believe, demonstrates that the work done by slaves, though it appears to cost only their maintenance, is in the end the dearest of any. A person who can acquire no property can have no other interest but to eat as much and to labour as little as possible. Whatever work he does beyond what is sufficient to purchase his own maintenance, can be squeezed out of him by violence only, and not by any interest of his own. 
Smith, Adam. An Inquiry into the Nature and Causes of the Wealth of Nations (Kindle Locations 5871-5874). University Of Chicago Press. Kindle Edition. 
In other words, people do the bare minimum when it's all stick and no carrot. I myself make money by doing very labor-intensive, highly skilled labor, and the minute a client tries to low-ball me, I immediately put them on a blacklist on my mind. I will fulfill existing contracts, but then they are on their own. If you want results, you have to treat workers well and pay them enough to be happy, since without happiness the emotional basis for good work is absent. 
Should corporate executives be held criminally liable for deaths or injuries in the workplace? Discussion Post 
It really depends on the nature and circumstances of the injury. Suppose that an idiotic employee pours hot coffee on another employee: Should the owner of the company be held criminally responsible? Obviously not. What about if the CEO insists on observance of safety-protocols, which are then ignored, resulting in an accident? Again--no. Of course, you could say that these injuries resulted not from work proper but from workplace goofery. Very well, But what if the stress of being a young lawyer causes Smith to have a heart-attack? In that case, the heart attack is the result of Smith's work: so are the partners of law firm guilty of criminal negligence? Or should they be? Doesn't seem like it. If safety-hazards are not disclosed and the accident is demonstrably a result of such non-disclosure, then yes, there is criminal and civil liability. But it wont' do to say that the owner/CEO should categorically be held responsible for any and all injuries incurred while at work or even as a result of work: the economy would grind to a halt. 
Is Money a Drug? Discussion Post 
I don't agree with Bevilaqua and Singh, and Polk. What is being described is not the effect of money itself, but of money obtained too easily and in questionable ways. I have received some money from parents, and worked for some of money, and I cannot say that any of it was drug-like, especially not the money that I worked for. It may well be that what investment bankers do is on the meaningless side, and it may also be that when large amounts of money are given in exchange for meaningless work, a certain moral vertigo ensues. There are definitely some dodgy ways to make a living, but I'm not sold on the 'money-is-the-root-of-evil' idea just yet. And frankly, I don't think it's a concern my generation will have. The economy is shrinking in some ways (not all), and I don't think there is any 'easy money' out there anymore. For better or for worse, my generation probably won't have the luxury of wrestling with moral perplexities of excessive wealth. Tell somebody who works 12 hours a day for $200 that the feeling of satisfaction is a drug-induced illusion---won't fly. Maybe if he fails to work and gets $20,000, the story will be different. But frankly given only what I know, I can't quite throw my weight the idea that money in and of itself is to be treated with the same contempt as crack or PCP. 
The “Heaven Help Her” Scenario: Utilitarian and Universalist Perspectives 
Section I. In this context, my (Jiu’s) two basic options are (i) lie to the person selling the manufacturing plant (henceforth ‘the seller’) and (ii) do not lie to that person. In this context, a number of assumptions are being made that must be made explicit if it is to be possible to put forth an analysis of the moral complexion of this situation. These are assumptions are: 
(1) 
If I take option (i), Rev. Smith will get a better price and better financing terms than he would get were I to take option (ii). 

(2) 
If I take option (i), my boss will be happier with the outcome than he would be were I to contravene his instructions and take option (ii). 

(3) 
If I cannot find a way to convince Tyler, my boss, to be happy about letting me be truthful with the seller. 

(4) 
Rev. Smith simply cannot or will not raise more than $150,000. 

(5) 
There is no way for me to get the seller to lower the price to within Rev. Smith’s reach except by lying to him about Smith’s caring about wanting to fix the machines. 


If any one of (1)-(5) is rejected, then there is a ‘moral workaround’ for me, in which case the dilemma is ‘solved’ only by fundamentally changing the ethical structure of the situation. For example, if Rev. Smith wins the lottery, then there is indeed no dilemma---but only because the situation in question has been replaced with a morally very different one. We will therefore not reject any one of (1)-(5). 
The stakeholders in this situation are myself (Jiu), Rev. Smith, Tyler, and the seller. Since we are not introducing additional assumptions, there are no other stakeholders. These being the facts, a stakeholder analysis is readily generated: 
Lie to Seller  Do Not Lie to Seller  
Jiu (myself)  + Keep job, make money.  -Lose job, lose money.  
Rev. Smith  +Able to purchase manufacturing plant.  -Not able to purchase manufacturing plant.  
Tyler  +Effectuates sale, makes money from deal, pleases his client.  -Does not effectuate sale, does not make money deal, loses client.  
The Seller  -Sells manufacturing plant for less desired  +Sells manufacturing plant for desired price.  


Section II. 
Utilitarianism: According to utilitarianism, a given act is the right one if, out of the various possible acts, it does the best job of maximizing utility. So according to utilitarianism, I am right to lie if the utility thereby generated exceeds the utility gained if I tell the truth. It is the other way around if telling the truth generates more utility than lying, and neither course of option is morally superior to the other if neither would generate more utility than the other. 
In order to state a utilitarian analysis of this situation, we have to make the following assumptions: 
(1) 
If I lie, the utility lost to the seller does not outweigh the utility gained by the other three parties. 

(2) 
If I tell the truth, the utility gained by the seller does not outweigh the utility 


If we are not granted these assumptions, then it is simply known what the net utility of my decision is, which makes a utilitarian impossible, since utilitarian says ‘it is right if and only if it maximizes net utility.’ 
Given (1) and (2), the utilitarian says: Lie. The reason: if I lie, three people benefit and only one is hurt, whereas three people are hurt and only person benefits if I tell the truth. 
Universalism: The universalist says that one must only act in such a way that, were others to act similarly, that act would not be self-defeating. So I should not, since lies wouldn’t be credible in a world in which everybody lied and my act of lying would therefore fail of its purpose in such a world. 
The universalist also holds that one should treat people as subjects, not as objects. In other words, people should not be ‘used’ or manipulated, since, as subjects, they should be treated as ends unto themselves, rather than as mere means to ends. 
These principles seem not to be equivalent. If I teach someone how to play the piano, I am not ‘using’ that person; but in a world in which everybody wanted to teach piano, nobody could do so, since nobody would make those pianos—not to mention build houses or enforce laws. That said, they happen to have the same result in this particular context. 
If I lie to the seller, I am treating him as a means, not as an end unto himself; and the universalist (i.e. the advocate of the principle that one should treat others as subjects, not objects) would say that I am acting wrongly. 
If I lie to the seller, then, for the reason given a moment, I am acting in a way in which I could not coherently want everyone else to act; and the universalist (i.e. the advocate of the principle that one should only act in such a way that one’s act would not be self-defeating if everyone else acted similarly) would say that I am acting wrongly. 
Profit maximization: According to this view, I should act in a way that maximizes profit provided that I am not (i) breaking the law, (ii) preventing free and open competition, or (iii) lying or committing fraud. The idea is that, if I act in a way that maximizes profit while complying with these constraints, then the economy and society benefit. If I maximize profit but fail to abide by these constraints, then, so the theory goes, society is a whole is not benefited, even though my firm may benefit. 
Since, according to this theory, lying is categorically verboten, I am wrong to lie and right to tell the truth to the seller, notwithstanding that lying may maximize my firm’s profits. Although Friedman and Mackey disagree as to whether a company should have any obligations other than to maximize profits, Friedman’s position being that it should and Mackey’s being that it shouldn’t, they both agree that companies should lie, restrain trade, or break the law, and they would both hold that I would be wrong to lie. 
Section III. I would not lie. I would not lie because, in this context, to lie would be to commit fraud. That raises the question: Why is fraud wrong? According to universalism (people are to be treated as subjects, not objects, and therefore not manipulated, swindled, coerced or otherwise objectified), fraud is inherently wrong. 
As for the utilitarian, his position would be that a given act of fraud is wrong only if it does more than good and, therefore, that fraud is not inherently wrong; and the utilitarian’s position would be that, given the previously stated assumptions, I should lie and therefore should not tell the 
truth. 
That said, my grounds for choosing to tell the truth would not be strictly non-utilitarian. In my personal experience, fraud always does more harm than good. In other words, setting aside issues relating to guilt and other intangibles, fraud is a losing proposition even from a strictly pragmatic viewpoint. I’ve known many fraudsters, and their victories were always small and short-lived; and the people I’ve known who succeeded in life were honest. In my experience, in a situation like the one in question, there would be a practical downside to lying. First of all, an employer who asks employees to lie is, in my experience, likely to lie—and otherwise abuse—his employees. Second, if Rev. Smith were willing to play along with this lie, then he is a fraudster and his charity is also likely to be fraudulent. 
To be sure, given the previously stated assumptions, the utilitarian would say that I should lie. But if I were actually in this situation, my decision to tell the truth would be influenced by such considerations— however irrelevant they might be to the exact version of the hypothetical we are describing—as well as by universalist considerations; and their respective influences on my decision would not be easily separated.

Do we have free will? Responses to discussions posts (my content in bold­faced italics) 
Colleen Sparkman-van der Werf posted Oct 21, 2019 12:32 PM Subscribe I would say that without a doubt yes, we have free will. I believe that although we condition our bodies and brains to do what is natural and what is the norm for us, we, as human beings still have the ability to choose. I feel that what we are getting caught up in is that we tend to choose what is comfortable and safe. And our brains have been programed to remember what we would typically choose. In the video clip “Neuroscience and Free will” although some brains knew the button was going to be pushed beforehand, it is not enough to prove that they did not have free will. This experiment is subjective. This only proves that the brain knew they wold be hitting the button. It is difficult to positively know whether the subjects really did not think about it before their finger actually went to touch it. This also could prove that it is a conditioning of our brain. I suppose this goes against the position of new materialism. I also think that humans condition their brains for self preservation. And the least resistance for that would be to do what is safe and known. Which, in reality, would not be considered free will because you are then choosing something based on what your brain and body is telling you that you need; whereas if you chose to do something just because you wanted to do it and not worry about the consequences, then that would be free will. 
Hello CW! I greatly enjoyed your post. I’m not 100% clear what our position. First, you say that we clearly have free will. But you immediately thereafter say that our behavior is governed by ‘programming’ and ‘conditioning’, which, if true, suggests that very much lack free will. 
Is your position that, when we aren’t being brainwashed or 
manipulated or otherwise ‘conditioned’ or ‘programmed’, our natural state is one of being free? If so, that is a position worth developing, with the caveat that you would have to make it clear what you mean by ‘free will.’ At one point, you correctly that we make our own choices. But are all choices made freely? Setting aside cases of duress or external compulsion, are choices always made freely? 
The video suggested that our choices ‘make themselves’, as it were, and that our conscious minds are simply to take not of our decisions after the fact. This was argued compelling by Freud over hundred years ago, though not on quite the same grounds; and although I personally agree with you that, under certain circumstances, it is an option to make decisions that are in some respects ‘free’, it would have to said which decisions are made freely, and in exactly what respect they are made freely, before it can be said that are categorically ‘free.’ 
Erik Smallwood posted Oct 21, 2019 7:06 AM Subscribe I had a hard time following interpreting the first video we watched. I feel like the researchers did not give us enough information about how they are conducting their experiments. I was thinking during the beginning and middle of the video that they also need to have their subjects think about hitting a button and then decided not to. This way they can compare the brain usage/ramping of both decisions. The researcher at the end of the video said there are multiple impulses running at the same time and certain ones win out. 
I think that what they are finding is more about how our decision making process works. We starting thinking about something/action before we do it which I think is what they are reading. I don’t feel like they even believe that we do not have free will but that we just don’t know when we start thinking about something. I can relate that to conditioning. We are so used to our brains work the way they do that we don’t think about how they take part in our actions or decisions. 
I do think that it can be a little bit of both. We do have free will to make a decision but some of those are decisions could already be predetermined for whatever reason. I do not think my views are threatened by any of these new findings. I think that it could be some combination of everything that makes the whole thing work. I think this quote from the movie Forrest Gump can sum up this topic well. “I don't know if we each have a destiny, or if we're all just floating around accidental-like on a breeze, but I, I think maybe it's both. Maybe both is happening at the same time.” 
Hi Erik! I agree that the people who made the video could have been more clear about the structure of the experiment. Nonetheless, the basic idea was ultimately pretty clear. When a given person makes a given decision, his body gives off a number of signals, in much the way that a bad poker player gives off ‘tells’ (meaning that he inadvertently discloses his hand); and this experiment seemed to show pretty conclusively that a number of involuntary signals that a given decision has been made are given off before the person actually ‘pushes the button’ on the matter. In other words, the subject knows only when the clicks the mouse; but his body, so this experiment showed, has already given off a lot of involuntary signals that show that he was already locked into a certain path. Cheers, Monzer 
Discussion Post #8 (worth 30 points; at least 200 words): Due by Saturday, Oct 26th, by 11:30 p.m. (my work in boldfaced italics)

For discussion post #8, there are two parts: 
First for part A (worth 15 points).
