Summary and Analysis of The Yellow Wallpaper 
This story is told from the viewpoint of a highly emotionally sensitive woman, who in times past would have been classified as ‘neurasthenic’, meaning, in effect, that she had a persistent case of ‘raw nerves.’ The woman lives in a room by herself. She never seems to leave the room, even though it is a part of a large house, which is also occupied by her husband. Her husband is a medical doctor and is also functioning, ominously, as her personal medical doctor—more specifically, as her psychiatrist. (And, like many psychiatrists, he is not, it turns out, a particularly good one.) It is clear from the beginning of the story that the woman is emotionally troubled and, at the very least, extremely neurotic. 
But there is a great deal about her condition that is not clear. It is not clear whether she is basically a normal, if highly sensitive woman, who has experienced a bit of emotional throttling or, on the contrary, whether she has a had full-on psychotic break with reality. At the beginning of the story, we are led to believe that she is more on the neurotic side of the spectrum, but at the end it is revealed by that she is very much at the psychotic extreme. 
The story consists for the most part of the woman’s pondering her husband’s alleged failure to have a genuinely emotional, as opposed to narrowly clinical, understanding of her condition. Her husband has very clearly written her off as a demented woman, who, for reasons of protocol, must be tolerated and treated with a certain degree of civility but not sympathized with or respected. The woman’s grievances about her husband prove to be justified, and as the story progresses, it begins to emerge that her complaints about him are seriously understated, as he is driven not by cold professionalism but by ill-will and hatred towards his wife. 
At the same time, the woman’s mental condition is increasingly clearly that of somebody who is completely psychotic, as is evidenced by her fixation with, and delusions concerning, the peeling yellow wallpaper in her room, which represents her unravelling soul. 
At the end of the story, it is revealed that, contrary to what the woman herself has believed all along and also to what the reader has assumed, the woman is a prisoner and is simply trapped in that room— much as, so it is suggested, she is trapped in her own madness. 
For part B (worth 15 points). Then RESPOND to the story and other piece and/or the ideas in them in some way--either creatively or critically (for critical, for example, see the handout called "An Inductive/Inquiry Approach to Studying Texts" in week two’s module or the “Glossary of Critical Approaches” on pp. 946-954). You might write several questions (as outlined in the Inquiry Approach) or you might choose one of the critical approaches and analyze the story or stories from that perspective. Or you could compare/contrast the story and the other piece in terms of what they say about the theme of “Culture and Identity.” Or you could write about how you interpreted these pieces. These are just some suggestions for ways you can respond to literature you have read. 
If you want to do a creative response, then write your own "mini-story"or poem about this theme or in the style/vein/voice of one of the pieces you read. 
Or you can answer/address this question: Charlotte Perkins Gilman said that she wrote this piece as a feminist protest against the way women were treated during her time. Do you see this character as protesting or not? Is she trying to create a different kind of identity for herself than the one that was acceptable for women at that time—or is she just mentally unstable? Write about her and this story and compare it to the other piece you read somehow.

Dialogue about Sonny’s Blues and The Yellow Wallpaper 
Smith: Brown! How are you, my friend? How did you like those two stories? 
Brown: I very much liked The Yellow Wallpaper (YP). I’m not sure how I felt about Sonny’s Blues (SB). 
Smith: When you say “not sure…”, do you actually mean you’re not sure? Or are you politely saying that it didn’t work for you? 
Brown: It didn’t work for me. 
Smith: That’s surprising, because I know you like non-fiction, or non-fiction-like, stories about wayward lifestyles and the gritty realities they involve. 
Brown: I do. But SB didn’t work for me. 
Smith: Why not? 
Brown: First of all, the main character---the narrator—was boring. He had a boring outlook, and I got the feeling that his job was just a kind of prop. And I didn’t really know what he was supposed to represent. 
Smith: I can kind of see that. 
Brown: On the one hand, he was supposed to be this square schoolteacher, which I can certainly understand. But then there were these weird flashes of bitterness about racial injustice, among other things, that, although morally justified, had no real foundation in the character. They just came out of the blue. 
Smith: I got that feeling myself. The main character was supposed to be a blank—a mirror that reflected reality---but sometimes he would stop being a blank and would go into an angry political mode, which, however justified historically, didn’t cohere with the rest of the character’s personality. 
Brown: Yes, frankly, I felt that way too. I didn’t really know what the story was about. I mean, there were really only two characters: the main one, who was just kind of a run-of-the-mill narrator, and Sonny, who, apart from his piano-talent, had no particularly good or interesting qualities. 
Smith: Yes, and there seemed to be an understanding to the effect that all of this was a consequence of racism, or some such, and maybe it was. But if the story is just considered on its own terms—if it’s just read as a story about a man and his brother, rather than as a political diatribe—it doesn’t really work very well. 
Brown: No, it doesn’t. Not in my view, anyway. And there was undischarged premise there, something to the effect that Sonny was a lost soul who would not have been lost in a non-racist society. Which may or may not be true and which, in any case, didn’t really make for very engaging reading, I’m sorry to say. 
Smith: What about YP? What did you think of that? 
Brown: That one I very much liked. 
Smith: Which is ironic, given that you’re a male and the main character isn’t. 
Brown: Yes, that’s an irony, I suppose. But I still liked it. 
Smith: Because..? 
Brown: Because it was realistic. Especially the way the woman’s delusions were intertwined with accurate emotional perceptions. 
Smith: Explain please. 
Brown: Ok. So obviously the woman’s belief that she lived in the wallpaper, or whatever her delusion was, was indeed a delusion. But her beliefs about her husband’s emotional disposition towards her was accurate. 
Smith: That makes sense. 
Brown: And I think what drove her insane was her awareness, her unconscious awareness, of her husband’s actual feelings. 
Smith: So was it the fact that she was aware of her husband’s feelings that drove her mad, or was it the fact that she was unconsciously aware of her husband’s feeling? 
Brown: The latter. If she had known, consciously and lucidly, what her husband was really about, it would not have driven her mad, since frank acceptances of ugly realities, though unpleasant, don’t undermine mental health. 
Smith: So it was the fact that she was aware of her husband’s actual disposition towards her but couldn’t admit it. 
Brown: Right, she couldn’t admit it---except by going into a kind of perpetual dream-like state. 
Smith: You’re referring to her psychosis. 
Brown: Yes, psychosis being, as Freud said, a prolonged waking dream. 
Smith: Hoo boy! 
Brown: Yes, it’s heavy. That’s why I liked the story. 
Smith: You liked it because it was heavy, or because it was accurate? 
Brown: It wouldn’t have been heavy if it had not been psychologically accurate. 
Smith: Good point, Brown. Good point.

The Yellow Wallpaper by Charlotte Perkins Gilman (page 442-454) 
From the beginning this story will open the doors to a psychological thriller that will leave you wanting more all the way to the end. It begins by detailing the daily struggles of a Physician named John and his wife who seem to operate strangely in their personal relationship. John is his wife’s physician and that will hinder her ability to cope with her mental illness throughout the story. John seems to be in true denial of his wife’s mental condition. This story will lead you to believe that John’s wife is going through postpartum depression in many ways. He has taken her away to a beautiful yet older rental home to heal, and ultimately this home will begin to unravel her mental illness. She becomes obsessed with things she sees and begins to write about them in her journals. The story will explain how John’s wife is hiding a lot of her fears and feelings. She begins to write a secretive journal detailing the things she is experiencing and seeing in the rental house. The yellow wallpaper in the house will have a major significance to the story and will add to the wife’s perception of reality. She begins to see an imprisoned woman within the yellow wallpaper which will leave the reader wondering. At times this story will go from documenting the first person struggles of anxiety and depression to something that is a bit evil and full of suspense.

A Poet To His Baby Son by James Weldon Johnson (page 504-505) 
This poem is about a father who wants his son to grow up to be something great. As his son lays in front of him as a baby, he begins to think of the future and what his son will grow up to be. His son is described as being blessed with his mother’s face and cursed with his father’s mind. He wants his son to grow up to be a banker, a politician, leader or a type of go getter. The father seems to have strong opinions on what he does not want is son to be. He states, “Take the advice of a father who knows.” He clearly does not want his son to become a poet. He defines in the poem that it is no time or place for poets today. He views the language of poets as an unknown tongue today. 
Great post! What are your thoughts on the father’s values? Is the father right to have such specific views as to what constitutes success? And why does he have them? Is it because he is himself unfulfilled? Or is it just that he is ‘old school’ and has rather retrograde beliefs about how life is to be lived? Finally, what are your own views on these matters? Are you a poet who others tried to force into some pre-existing professional template? Cheers, Monzer

Poetry is a great way to communicate love, but it isn’t a good way to pay the bills. A friend of mine is a poet, and he always had to ask other people for money, including me. One day I lent him my car, and he just drove away. With my dog and birth-certificate.

Legal vs. Substantive Freedom and the Paradox of the Free Market Reflections on works by Hall, Debord, and Morgan & Tierney 
This paper is a critical response to three articles: “The Commodity as Spectacle”, by Guy Debord; “The Spectacle of the Other”, by Stuart Hall; and “An Illustrated Guide to Guy Debord’s ‘The Society of the Spectacle’”, by Tiernan Morgan and Lauren Purje. Each one of these articles had most, if not all, of the following five characteristics: 
(a) 
It correctly points out that a society that is market-based, as opposed to culture-based, inevitably turns people into objects; 

(b) 
Despite making this important point, not a single one of these articles discusses pornography, which is the best conceivable illustration of the fact that market-based societies turn people into objects or, to use Debord’s term, ‘mere spectacles’; 

(c) 
Up to a point, each of these articles is itself an example of the very commodity-fetishism that it is its very purpose to expose and heap scorn on; 

(d) 
Although each of these papers does expose a deep problem with all economy-based societies--namely, that human beings are turned into 


objects it does so much as attempt to identify a constructive way of 
responding to this fact; and finally, 
(e) Although, as just stated, each of these papers identified this important problem with market-based societies, there is an important corollary that each of them hints at but not a single one of them clearly states, namely, that the various freedoms that people necessarily have in a given market-economy---in particular, the freedoms to own and exchange goods and services---inevitably lead to complete mutual enslavement on the part of that society’s members, albeit under the pretexts of ‘universal legal protection of individual rights’ and ‘economic and individual freedom.’ 
Let us now clarify and substantiate each of these points, and relate it to the articles in question. First of all, what better example of “the Commodity as Spectacle”, to use Guy Debord’s term, could there be than a stripper or pornographic actress? Debord himself does not mention these professions, and yet they are the best conceivable examples of his point that the free-market turns people into mere ‘spectacles.’ For here we have a human being who has been turned into a commodity that is in the most literal possible way a spectacle. This woman is paid to be ‘stripped’ of her clothing—and dignity —and turned into a pure spectacle—something that exists only to be consumed and that is consumed by being looked at. 
This is not to say that strippers and pornographic actresses are forced into their professions: in most cases they are not. But in a way, the fact that they enter these professions ‘freely’ only shows how insidiously and deceptively destroying of freedom the so-called ‘free market’ really is. First of all, if a woman is abducted and forced to become a pornographic actress, that does not redound to the discredit of capitalism. Capitalism is about people transacting with one another. In other words, it is about people having and using the legal right to dispose of their own assets in a manner of their own choosing. Since the woman was forced into prostitution, her being a prostitute is not the result of a transaction (being kidnapped and forced into a certain line of work is not a way of transacting, after all), and it therefore falls outside the scope of what a capitalist system permits. 
So let us consider the case where the woman ‘freely’ becomes a stripper or pornographic actress. Why has this woman chosen this path? Very simple. She needs money; in a market-economy, she has no value over and above what people are willing to give her in the form of money; and unless she has the wherewithal to become a high-level professional, such as a medical doctor or computer-programmer, her options are either (i) earn a paltry income doing time-consuming, soul-destroying menial work (e.g. work as a waitress or telemarketer) or (ii) make real money by literally turning herself into a spectacle. 
Pornographers and consumers of pornography will say that this woman ‘did this to herself’—that she ‘did it freely.’ And after a fashion, they are right. Like all members of a market-economy, this woman started off with certain legal ‘freedoms’; and in order to eat and pay rent, she disposed of those freedoms in a manner that, given the options available to her, was the most ‘rational’ and that she consequently ‘freely’ chose. And yet all of this ‘freedom’ and ‘rationality’ has been turned against itself and made its possessor into a slave: this woman may be legally free, but how much more freedom than an actual slave does she actually have? If a slave tries to escape, he may be hunted down and beaten, maybe even killed. If this woman refuses to either work ten hours a day in a cubicle or spend those same ten hours dancing naked under neon lights, she will be out on the streets and either die of exposure or be jailed for vagrancy. Incidentally, supposing for argument’s sake that she does choose to live on the streets, the various petty convictions that she will wrack up as a result will make it impossible for her to get any kind of job that pays enough money to live on. So no—her situation is not, at least not unambiguously, different from that of an actual slave, even though her every move is carried out ‘freely.’ 
Debord writes: 
The spectacle subjugates living men to itself to the extent that the economy has totally subjugated them. It is no more than the economy developing for itself. It is the true reflection of the production of things, and the false objectification of the producers (Debord, 142). 
How much more impactful this statement would have been had it been followed by a specific example! And what better illustration of it could there be than the just-discussed completely literal one! 
Debord’s article is the work of someone with a powerful intellect. It reads much like Marx’s “Theses on Feuerbach” and seems to have been modeled on them, both consisting of deep but obscure epigrams that would be a lot less obscure if concretely illustrated. And not a single one of the forty epigrams composing this work involves a concrete illustration-even though illustrations abound, as we have seen. 
Presumably, it was presumably for this very reason that Morgan and Purje wrote their article “An Illustrated Guide to Guy Debord’s ‘The Society of the Spectacle’”. Though brilliant, this article suffers from one major flaw: it doesn’t contain any illustrations. Yes—it contains ‘illustrations’ in the sense that it there is art-work in it; but it is quite as devoid of specific examples of the phenomenon in question (that the ‘free market’ turns people into two-dimensional spectacle-commodities) as Debord’s work itself. And its attempt to clarify Debord’s meaning do not in my view do Debord’s work justice: 
As spectators, we regularly experience advertisements for rival products — Pepsi and Coca-Cola, Delta and US Airways, The X-Factor and The Voice. Often we’re presented with conflicting desires or messages. For instance, a television drama depicting an AA meeting might be preceded by a glamorous vodka advertisement. Such logical inconsistencies are buried by the spectacle’s relentless proffering of goods and imagery. Gradually, we begin to conflate visibility with value (Morgan and Purje). 
Yes, technically speaking, it is ironic that the same consumers are being shown ads for booze are also being shown ads for ways to get off of booze. But this isn’t exactly a ‘logical inconsistency.’ It is, indeed, just a humorous irony: advertisements are seen by millions of people; some of them want vodka and others want to drop drinking vodka---just as some of them want to buy sneakers and others want to buy dress shoes. 
Also, Morgan and Purje are interpreting Debord overly literally. In their view, Debord is making the pedestrian point that, in a market-society, people focus almost as much on packaging—on the wrapper and the ribbons and the like—as they do on the actual product. But Debord is trying to make a much deeper point—one to the effect that capitalistic transactionalism drains turns human beings into soulless widgets who, as such, have nothing on the ‘inside’ and are therefore pure ‘outside’—pure appearance in other words. 
As for Stuart Hall’s “The Spectacle of the Other” (Hall, 2001), this work was a combination of very sharp and accurate and also very misconceived. Hall points out that, thanks to predatory capitalistic practices, human beings were enslaved and that this was subsequently rationalized on free-market grounds. This is true, of course. Hall then insightfully says that capitalism ‘otherizes’ human beings—which it does, in that, in a ‘free’ market system, people do not interact, so much as they transact, with the result that kinship and moral ties are dissolved in an acid bath of acquisitiveness. 
Where, in my view, Hall goes quite astray is his contention capitalism reinforces distinctions of race and gender. As Marx and Engels themselves said, capitalism does quite the opposite: capitalism replaces the biological with the financial: biologically rooted instincts---both wholesome ones, such as feelings of warmth for friends and family, as well ugly offshoots, such as chauvinism and racism---are replaced by cold calculations, with friends and family and even children having only as much worth as an accounting program says they have. So yes—historically, just as Hall says, capitalism led to acts of profiteering that involved exploiting racial differences. What Hall fails to say is that, when capitalism is allowed to run its course, it dehumanizes everybody: all people—man, woman, black, white—are equally required to toil in cubicles for paychecks that barely cover their rent; and if, in order to tend to a sick child or make a much needed visit to the doctor’s office, they take so much as a day off without ‘written permission’ from their supervisor, they are rewarded for it by having their livelihood cut off—none 
Debord, G. (2006). The commodity as spectacle. Media and cultural studies: Keyworks, 117-21. 
Hall, S. (2001). The spectacle of the other. Discourse theory and practice: A reader, 324-344. 
Morgan, Tiernan and Purje, Lauren (2016). “An Illustrated Guide to Guy 
Debord’s ‘The Society of the Spectacle’”. Retrieved from: 
https://hyperallergic.com/313435/an-illustrated-guide-to-guy-debords-the­

society-of-the-spectacle/

Methodology  QUANTITATIVE  



1.1 Research Topic 
The proposed research study topic is implicit bias 
on the part of Nursing Faculties against African Americans who are not first-generation immigrants. It is therefore important in this context to distinguish between native born African Americans and first-generation African Americans, since the current study is concerned with implicit institutional biases that are direct towards native born African Americans but are not, at least not with equal intensity, directed towards first-generation immigrants of African Ancestry. A case will be made that native born (Muronda, 2015). 
Less than 10% of Nursing Faculty members are 
of African (Sub-Saharan) descent, even though African Americans are over 13% of the population (US Census Bureau, 2018). At the same time, first-generation immigrants of African descent disproportionately represented in Nursing Faculties (South, 2018). The population of the United States is approximately 330 million, approximately 42 million of whom are of African ancestry. Of those 42 million, approximately 3.4 million are first generation immigrants, approximately 95% of whom come from one of the following countries: Jamaica, Haiti, Nigeria, Trinidad & Tobago, the Dominican Republic, Ghana, Guyana, Kenya, and Somalia (US Census Bureau, 2018). Immigrants from these countries are twice as likely as native born Americans to be employed as medical professionals, over three times as likely as native-born African Americans to be employed as medical professionals, over four times as likely as native-born African Americans to be employed as nurses, and over five times as likely as native-born African Americans to be employed as members of Nursing Faculties (South, 2019). 



1.2 Research Problem (1 Paragraph) 
Although there is a voluminous scholarly literature concerning racism towards African-Americans, there is a paucity of literature concerning the effects of such literature in the context of Nursing Faculties, and there extremely little literature concerning how the attitudes of nursing faculty members towards first-generation immigrants of African descent differ from their attitudes towards native-born African Americans. 
There does exist purely statistical data relating to the rates of employment in Nursing Faculties of 
both native-born African Americans as well as first-generation immigrants of African descent. But this data has not been interpreted (Akintoye, 2019). It has been observed that, in many different professional context, including nursing and nursing-instruction, first-generation immigrants of African descent do in fact ‘outperform’ native-born African Americans, both when it comes to being hired and also when it comes to being promoted. But this has not been treated as a sign of bias or prejudice. In fact, it has been celebrated as evidence of their absence (White-means, 2009).

2.1 Research Problem Background 
American laws and mores have changed 
enormously over the last sixty years. Jim Crow laws, which explicitly discriminated against African Americans, were abolished and replaced with laws designed to ensure equality of treatment and opportunity (Akintoye, 2019). Also, during this period, the United States has transitioned from being 90% white, has become highly diverse and multicultural, with whites being expected to become a minority by 2043 (Lieberman, 2019). 
It is difficult, however, to track the 
psychological realities underlying these massive demographic shifts and, in particular, to know whether people’s private attitudes have kept pace with the just-mentioned legal and institutional changes. Racism and ethnic bias no longer assume overt forms, but that doesn’t mean they don’t exist. For example, even though the white population has shrunk by almost a third over the last sixty years, nursing faculties are still over 90% white, even in areas, such as Detroit and Chicago, where they make up less than one third of the population (Phillips, 2014). 
Also, the fact that first-generation immigrants 
of African descent are so much more likely than equally qualified native-born African Americans to be hired and promoted by such faculties suggests that their members are still uneasy about native-born African Americans (Sabin, 2009). 
A related fact is that native-born African 
Americans are culturally and even to some extent ethnically distinct from first-generation immigrants of African descent (Hamilton, 2019). The upbringing and outlook of a given immigrant from Lagos, Nigeria is likely to be very different from that of an African American who was raised in Baltimore or Los Angeles; and in addition to speaking with different accents, the two are likely to have different preferences in relation clothing and other personal matters (Hamilton, 2019). Researches have established that racial bias is triggered not by skin color alone but by skin color in conjunction with distinctive accents, clothing styles, and personal manner, and first-generation immigrants of African descent may therefore fail to trigger the biases that operate against his native-born counterpart (Smedley, 2003). 




2.2 Research Question(s)  Is implicit bias on the part of nursing faculty members towards native-born African Americans responsible for the fact that, compared to equally qualified first-generation immigrants of African descent, they are disproportionately unlikely to be hired and, if hired, disproportionately unlikely to be promoted?  
If so, is this bias conscious?  
What non-disruptive and politically feasible measures can be taken to counteract the effects of  
this bias?  
What are the economic consequences of this bias and what would the economic consequences of their elimination be?  
2.3 Purpose of the Study  The purpose of this study is to identify and delineate the social consequences of the psychological forces responsible for the fact that native-born African Americans are less likely than members of all other ethnicities, including first-generation immigrants of African descent, to be recruited/promoted by nursing faculties.  
2.4 Methodology and Basic Design Overview  This study will be divided into two parts, and the methodology used in each part will be quantitative. In the first part, the relevant statistical data will be analyzed, so as to establish that the above-stated discrepancies are indeed a function of implicit bias. Since this will involve correlating hiring-rates and promotion-rates with applicant-credentials, the methodology in question, being causal-correlational, will be quantitative (Cresswell, 2015). In the second part, the nature and causes of the biases in question will be investigated. This will be done through a carefully constructed survey, which will be administered online. The survey will use the standard six-point Likert scale, making it possible for results to evaluated automatically and objectively (Bachmann, 2015).  
2.5 Dissertation Title .  Identifying Implicit Racial Barriers and their Impact on Changing the Face of Nursing Faculty  



3.1 Theoretical Foundations 
Ultimately, the foundations for this study lie in 
Levi-Strauss’s structural approach to 
anthropological research and in James Gibson’s 
ecological approach to psychological research. 
According to Levi-Strauss’s structuralism, 
human interrelations are governed by rules, most of them implicit, that are defined in terms of binaries, such as black/white, good/bad, boss/underlying, and male/female (Levi-Strauss, 1963). It is to be stressed that the rules in question are not laws or even conventions. Rather, they are largely unconscious understandings that, although having some biological roots, are changeable and only assume rigid forms when given external reinforcement in the form of statutes and other expressions of institutional force. 
According to Gibson’s ecological 
methodology, interactions among organisms, including human beings, are guided by implicit understandings as to how maintain viable equilibria in a sustainable manner (Gibson, 1977). Oftentimes, these understandings give rise to enlightened practices, but they may also lead to behavior that, although productive in times past, has long since ceased to be so (Gibson, 2002). 
Structuralism has been used with great advantage in the study of racism, including in the 
medical profession, by a number of scholars, 
including Bonilla-Silva (1996) and Viruell-
Fuentes (2012), who have shown that otherwise 
inexplicable and highly invidious forms of 
racism, such as Jim Crow and Apartheid, can be 
understood, though obviously not justified, if 
mapped onto Levi-Strauss’s structuralism Bonilla-Silva (1996). 


As for Gibson’s ecological school of 
psychological research, Anderson (1999), Williams (2001), and Krieger (2014) have done extensive work showing that racial institutions, as well as their psychological residues, can be understood along Gibsonian lines by regarding them as over-deployed defensive-mechanisms, comparable to those that mediate asthma, lupus, and other auto-immune disorders (Krieger, 2014).

3.3 Theoretical Implications 
Describe the theoretical 
In this work, we will show that the just-
mentioned contextualizations of the theories of Gibson and Levi-Strauss not only make it possible to interpret irrationally discriminatory behavior on the part of otherwise rational professionals but also help identify feasible policy-recommendations that would help reconcile the various parties afflicted by the 
racial biases examined in the current study. 
The primary theoretical implication of the 
present study is that racial attitudes, along with the social damage thereby done, can be mitigated without overhauling the institutions implications you believe your study could have for your field AND your specialization area. This will be the theoretical framework(s) to be used in conceptualizing the study and analyzing the data.

APA style in citing all resources. 


that host them or otherwise engaging in extreme measures that are likely to lead to hate, as opposed to reconciliation (LeGrande, 2018). 
Thus far, racism has been combatted either 
through heart-wrenching but ultimately empty pleas for ‘compassion’ or through massive social changes requiring prohibitively large amounts of political will and financial capital (Kumi-Yeboah, 2019). The current study will identify measures that are both non-ideological and also enviro-friendly and, for those reasons among others, would be loss costly and less likely to meet with emotional or political resistance (LeGrand, 218). 



3.4 Practical Implications 
Please describe the specific practical implications that may result from this research that can be used by any or all of the following stakeholders: the population being studied, professionals for whom this topic pertains, and/or the wider community itself. 
•Use 
current (within 5-7 years), scholarly, PRIMARY resources to support statements. 

•Use 
APA style in citing all resources. 


Discriminatory behavior is never innocuous, 
but it is especially harmful in the context of decisions relating to the hiring and promoting of healthcare employees. Researchers have established that discriminatory hiring-practices correlate with lower-quality healthcare being given to members of the group thereby discriminated against (Sen, 2018). 
Also, economists in the United Kingdom, 
Germany, and the United States have estimated that biases on the part of healthcare providers, including racial biases, increases health-care costs by as much as 20%. Any insight that can be provided as to how to mitigate such bias is therefore likely to improve quality of healthcare while stabilizing costs (Simon, 2019)

Then read any ONE other piece of fiction or poetry or drama or nonfiction from the “Culture and Identity” theme from pp. 428-575. Again, though, you may NOT use the same piece for post #8 as you did for post #7 
For discussion post #8, there are two parts: 
First for part A (worth 15 points). SUMMARIZE “The Yellow Wallpaper” and the other piece you read with one FULL paragraph of 6-8 sentences (at 
least 50 words) given to each piece for a total of at least 100 words for this part (remember, your summaries should be detailed enough to SHOW you read well . . .). Make sure when you do your post that you give the title of the piece you are responding to, as well as the pages it is on in our text. 
For part B (worth 15 points). Then RESPOND to the story and other piece and/or the ideas in them in some way--either creatively or critically (for critical, for example, see the handout called "An Inductive/Inquiry Approach to Studying Texts" in week two’s module or the “Glossary of Critical Approaches” on pp. 946-954). You might write several questions (as outlined in the Inquiry Approach) or you might choose one of the critical approaches and analyze the story or stories from that perspective. Or you could compare/contrast the story and the other piece in terms of what they say about the theme of “Culture and Identity.” Or you could write about how you interpreted these pieces. These are just some suggestions for ways you can respond to literature you have read. 
If you want to do a creative response, then write your own "mini-story"or poem about this theme or in the style/vein/voice of one of the pieces you read. 
Or you can answer/address this question: Charlotte Perkins Gilman said that she wrote this piece as a feminist protest against the way women were treated during her time. Do you see this character as protesting or not? Is she trying to create a different kind of identity for herself than the one that was acceptable for women at that time—or is she just mentally unstable? Write about her and this story and compare it to the other piece you read somehow. 
For the response part, I am looking for at least 100 words for this part also. The more detail the better; the more thoughtful/thought-provoking, the better! 
Jennifer Johnson posted Oct 19, 2019 
The Yellow Wallpaper by Charlotte Perkins Gilman (page 442-454) 
From the beginning this story will open the doors to a psychological thriller that will leave you wanting more all the way to the end. It begins by detailing the daily struggles of a Physician named John and his wife who seem to operate strangely in their personal relationship. John is his wife’s physician and that will hinder her ability to cope with her mental illness throughout the story. John seems to be in true denial of his wife’s mental condition. This story will lead you to believe that John’s wife is going through postpartum depression in many ways. He has taken her away to a beautiful yet older rental home to heal, and ultimately this home will begin to unravel her mental illness. She becomes obsessed with things she sees and begins to write about them in her journals. The story will explain how John’s wife is hiding a lot of her fears and feelings. She begins to write a secretive journal detailing the things she is experiencing and seeing in the rental house. The yellow wallpaper in the house will have a major significance to the story and will add to the wife’s perception of reality. She begins to see an imprisoned woman within the yellow wallpaper which will leave the reader wondering. At times this story will go from documenting the first person struggles of anxiety and depression to something that is a bit evil and full of suspense. 
Hi Jennifer! I very much enjoyed your post. But I feel that you simply stated the ‘cold facts’ of the case, as it were. Yes, this woman is obviously cycling into psychosis, possibly caused by post-partum depression, and yes she does start to project onto the wall-papers. But what, in your opinion, does all of this mean? In other words, what is it that isn’t said but lies beneath the surface of these events? For example, is the husband deliberately torturing her? If so, why? Is there a larger conceit at work concerning gender relations? If so, what? I would be very interested on your thoughts on these matters! -Monzer 
A Poet To His Baby Son by James Weldon Johnson (page 504-505) 
This poem is about a father who wants his son to grow up to be something great. As his son lays in front of him as a baby, he begins to think of the future and what his son will grow up to be. His son is described as being blessed with his mother’s face and cursed with his father’s mind. He wants his son to grow up to be a banker, a politician, leader or a type of go getter. The father seems to have strong opinions on what he does not want is son to be. He states, “Take the advice of a father who knows.” He clearly does not want his son to become a poet. He defines in the poem that it is no time or place for poets today. He views the language of poets as an unknown tongue today. 
Great post! What are your thoughts on the father’s values? Is the father right to have such specific views as to what constitutes success? And why does he have them? Is it because he is himself unfulfilled? Or is it just that he is ‘old school’ and has rather retrograde beliefs about how life is to be lived? Finally, what are your own views on these matters? Are you a poet who others tried to force into some pre-existing professional template? Cheers, Monzer 
Questions about the Yellow Wallpaper 
What type of physician is John? Does he have any experience with mental illness or postpartum depression? Is a psychiatrist? What type of physician is her brother? 
Probably a general practitioner who, if given the choice, would specialize in psychiatry. 
How did she really feel about the yellow wallpaper? She used words like flamboyant, repellent, revolting and unclean. Did she really hate the yellow wallpaper? 
She does not like it. It is clinical and fake. 
The story mentioned that Mary is good with the baby. Is this their first baby? Has Mary been there as the caregiver for more than one child for John and his wife? Does Mary know she has postpartum and that’s why they are at the rental house? 
I don’t know that she thinks in quite such logical, transactional terms. 
In the story she states she is angry enough to do something desperate. She also mentions she has a hidden rope. Does John take her suicide signs seriously? Does John know that his wife could be suicidal? 
He is probably deliberately causing her to be suicidal, or so the subtext suggests. 
John takes it as far as to take his wife to a rental house to recover from postpartum depression. Has John seen signs that would make him think his wife would harm their child? 
Great suggestion, but his objective seems to be to hurt, not to protect. 
Questions A Poet To His Baby Son 
Why does the author think becoming a poet is such a bad choice? Is the author a poet himself? 
He thinks it’s a bad choice, because, financially speaking, it is a bad choice. Pan-handlers make more money than poets. 
Why does the author think that poets are unfortunate fellows? Why doesn’t he want his son to grow up to be like one? Is that because you cannot earn a living being a poet? The poem says that poetry is unknown tongue? Does he think that no one reads or respects poetry anymore? 
He thinks this, because they have no earning power and are therefore defenseless. Plus, there are great poets who have day-jobs, such as Wallace Stevens, who was an investment banker. Even Shakespeare made his living as an actor, not as a poet or playwright. 
It seems the author wants the best for his son. Why would he be critical of what he does not want his son to be when he grows up? 
Asked and answered. See above. 
Why would the author think that poetry is not a good way of communicating love for his son if he is writing his own son a poem like this? 
Poetry is a great way to communicate love, but it isn’t a good way to pay the bills. A friend of mine is a poet, and he always had to ask other people for money, including me. One day I lent him my car, and he just drove away. With my dog and birth-certificate. 
How would the author feel if his son grew up to become a poet and read this poem that was written for him as a baby? Would be feel regretful that he has such harsh feeling on poets knowing his own son became one? less 
That really depends. It depends on the father and also depends on the son’s situation. Is he writing the poem from his mansion or from his jail cell?

“Man seeks ‘the truth’: a world that is not self-contradictory, not deceptive, does not change, a true world---a world in which one does not suffer; contradiction, deception, change—causes of suffering!” Will to Power, Section 585. 
[37] 
“Contempt, hatred for all that perishes, changes, varies---whence comes this valuation of that which remains constant? Obviously the will to truth is here merely a desire for a world of the constant.” Will to Power, Section 585. 
[38] 
“Obviously the will to truth is here merely a desire for a world of the constant….Happiness can be guaranteed only by being; happiness and change exclude each other…[T]he world as it ought to be exists: this world, in which we live, is in error---this world of ours ought not to exist….What 
kind of man reflects in this way? An unproductive, suffering kind, a kind weary of life.” Will to Power, Section 585.

[136] 
Cleckley (1950), Kernberg (1984, 1984b, 1985), Hare (1993). Be it noted that there are different degrees of psychopathy: a given person might have a high degree of professional integrity, and to that extent by non-psychopathic, but otherwise be highly psychopathic. This was the case with Jeffrey MacDonald, the Army doctor who slaughtered his whole family and had no remorse at all doing so. Jeffrey MacDonald's performance as a physician was consistently exemplary, there being no indication that it was in any way corrupted by psychopathy, even though there is no doubt that Dr. MacDonald was otherwise a textbook case of a psychopath. Dr. MacDonald's case is in no way a singularity, there being many cases of psychopaths who, like Dr. MacDonald, are non-psychopathic in specific contexts. See McGuiness (1983).
